Tiger Woods, the 14-time major champion, returned to the course after a 15-month absence, during which he underwent surgery twice. The 40-year-old American is nine strokes behind leader JB Holmes, who set the pace at the event. Woods had four birdies in his first eight holes but bogeyed the ninth after hitting a wayward drive and poor chip. He also hit another shot on the 11th, and double bogeyed the 16th and 18th, leaving him a stroke over par. Despite feeling "pretty good" and looking forward to the next few days, Woods expressed frustration with his performance. "I hit the ball into three bushes and the water, so it could have been something really good," he said. Woods got off to a solid start but made several mistakes, struggling with the par-fives. He had some awkward shots, which highlighted the importance of driving well at the course. Woods' first double bogey came after he pulled his approach to the green on the 16th and had to chip out short of the green. He then over-hit his fourth and two-putted from the back edge for a six. Woods pulled his drive on the 18th into a lake, but managed to hit the pin with his fourth shot, still two-putting from 12 feet for the fourth six of his round. He briefly tied for the lead at four under after a run of three birdies from the sixth hole, but ultimately cancelled out his bogey on the 11th with a birdie on the 15th. However, his two late double bogeys cost him the lead. Holmes leads the 18-man event with an eight-under-par 64, featuring seven birdies and an eagle. Hideki Matsuyama of Japan is a shot further back on seven under, while Dustin Johnson, the US Open winner, is on six under. Henrik Stenson, Matt Kuchar, and Louis Oosthuizen are tied for fourth on five under. Justin Rose, the Olympic champion, rounds out the field on two over.
The official ONA news agency reported that the three American hostages were flown out of Yemen's capital, Sanaa, on Wednesday. The US government had asked Oman for assistance in locating them, according to the agency. No details about the Americans' identities were provided, but a Yemeni security official told Reuters that the hostages were detained by the Houthi rebels, who suspected them of spying. The official made the statement by phone from Sanaa. In an earlier report, the US state department stated that an American contractor for the United Nations, who was detained at Sanaa's airport last month, had passed away. John Hamen and a colleague - believed to also be American - were held while arriving on a UN plane from Djibouti. In September, two Americans held captive by the Houthis for months were released and flown to safety in Oman, along with a British citizen and three Saudi nationals. The Houthis, who seized control of Sanaa this year, are facing a military campaign by a coalition of countries led by Saudi Arabia and supported by the US. Oman, not part of the coalition, has been actively involved in efforts to negotiate an end to the conflict. The ongoing fighting has resulted in at least 5,700 deaths, with about half of them being civilians, according to the UN, since March.
Before officially starting his military service, Prince Harry laid a wreath at the Tomb of the Unknown Australian Soldier in Canberra. He also presented a letter from the Queen, which expressed her hope that her grandson would greatly benefit from spending time with Australian troops. As Captain Wales, he will be leaving the British army in June after 10 years of service. In Australia, he will be paired with Aboriginal soldiers and train with the country's elite forces. Upon reporting for duty, he met with Air Chief Marshal Mark Binskin, Chief of the Defence Force, after visiting the Australian War Memorial and placing a poppy. The Queen's letter also praised the long-standing bond between the Australian and British armies, stating that the military secondment of her grandson would bring the two forces together in a shared commitment to uphold and defend their common values. The Queen wrote: "I am delighted that the long and enduring association between our armies will be strengthened by the military secondment of my grandson, Prince Harry. "Together, our armed forces share skills, resources, and resolve in order to uphold and defend our common values. "When we commemorate the sacrifices of our countrymen at Gallipoli a century ago, it is fitting that we can also reflect on the strength and persistence of those common values and our professional military ties. "I know that Captain Wales will greatly benefit from spending time with the Australian soldiers, and I thank you for welcoming him into your ranks." The Australian military has described the placement as "challenging and hectic", and Prince Harry has expressed his excitement for the opportunity. The placement will be briefly interrupted when he travels to Turkey later this month for commemorations marking the allied campaign at Gallipoli in 1915. Following his deployment, Prince Harry plans to engage in voluntary work for charities as he considers his future options.

Rugby League Club Owner Koukash Threatens to Challenge RFL Leadership In 2014 and 2015, Koukash's club was penalized with six points and fined £5,000 by the Rugby Football League (RFL) for breaching the salary cap. Koukash had previously vowed to take the RFL to "war" if they were found guilty. Since then, he has been discussing an emergency meeting with other club chairmen to express his dissatisfaction with the RFL's leadership. Koukash believes the RFL is "not fit for purpose" and that the sport is suffering financially. He claims that since joining the sport four years ago, the game has seen a decline in commercial success. Koukash, a millionaire racehorse owner, would need the support of six out of the 12 Super League clubs to bring a no-confidence motion. The RFL responded by stating that their organization is dedicated to working for the clubs and the broader rugby league community. They emphasized the existence of a democratic process that allows clubs to voice their concerns and that they are committed to improving the financial fortunes of all clubs. However, the RFL noted that an independent benchmarking report has shown that the sport is in a stronger financial position than it was two years ago. Koukash is set to hold a press conference on May 18, where he will likely discuss the recent salary cap charges in more detail.
In my assessment, I've only spoken to those directly impacted: a leading appliances manufacturer's top executives and stock market investors. My view on the economic changes may evolve as I meet others, but one thing is clear: relief that the US Federal Reserve has delayed the inevitable end of record-low interest rates on dollar debts. When the Fed raises rates, borrowing becomes more expensive and less accessible globally, particularly with the massive $9 trillion in dollar-denominated debt accumulated since 2009. However, Fed Chairman Janet Yellen and her colleagues on the Open Market Committee did not delay their first rate hike due to concerns for Chinese stock market speculators and businesses. Yet, they acknowledged that interest rates would have increased if they weren't worried about fragile global market and economic conditions, especially the slowdown in China's economy, which is severely impacting emerging economies, particularly commodity producers and manufacturers reliant on Chinese demand. The current economic downturn has already tightened monetary conditions in the US, with a 15% rise in the dollar over the past year as investors shifted capital from struggling emerging markets to the US. This has made American investors feel poorer and made it harder for American exporters to generate sales. Ms Yellen's point is that this indirect impact on US prosperity is equivalent to the modest interest-rate rise she would have announced yesterday. In terms of inflation, the slowdown in China's economy may have eased concerns, taking the edge off America's recovery. However, this raises two concerns. Investors may worry that the Fed's caution about China's growth slowdown is a sign of a more serious issue, which could make markets even more volatile. Moreover, the prolonged period of low interest rates since 2008 has created a precarious bubble in global debt markets, which could eventually burst in a painful manner.

Bircham has since coached in the US at Chicago Fire and Arizona United. Dundee United chairman Stephen Thompson met the outgoing head coach, Raivis Paatelainen, on Tuesday after the team's relegation from the Scottish Premiership. With Paatelainen's departure, United will now start looking for a new head coach, with Ray McKinnon, the boss of Raith Rovers, already being considered a strong candidate. Paatelainen expressed his gratitude to the players, staff, and supporters, saying he had enjoyed his time at the club despite the challenges they faced. His contract was set to expire in the summer of 2018, and he had been in charge since October, when he replaced Jackie McNamara. However, he was unable to turn the team's fortunes around, securing just five wins in 25 Premiership matches before their relegation was confirmed with a 2-1 defeat to city rivals Dundee. Chairman Thompson apologised "unreservedly" to fans, promising "radical changes" in the near future to help the team return to the top flight. United's next match is against Inverness Caledonian Thistle on Friday. The Tannadice team has identified McKinnon as their top target to replace Paatelainen, but Raith Rovers has stated they will not be approached for the former midfielder while they are involved in the Premiership play-offs. Raith is set to face Hibernian in their quarter-final on Wednesday, and their director Eric Drysdale said they had not received any contact from Dundee United or any other club regarding McKinnon.
A London pub car park was the gruesome scene where Daniel Morgan was found dead in 1987. Despite five separate police investigations, no one has ever been convicted of his murder. His brother Alastair Morgan, joined by investigative journalist Peter Jukes, is now recounting the story of his brother's tragic fate in weekly episodes. Alastair Morgan expressed his frustration that the full story has never been presented in a coherent and logical way, so the public can truly understand what happened. He wants the public to be able to hear the truth about his brother's death. When Daniel Morgan's body was discovered in March 1987, he had been brutally attacked with an axe after a night of drinking at the Golden Lion pub in Sydenham. The police investigations have consistently failed to identify anyone responsible for the crime, and the case has been marred by accusations of corruption and cover-ups. An independent panel has been set up to examine the circumstances of Daniel Morgan's death and the police's inability to secure a conviction. Daniel Morgan, a 37-year-old man from Llanfrechfa near Cwmbran, died under tragic circumstances. Peter Jukes, the journalist presenting the new podcast, was astonished by the details of the story once he began his research. He hopes that witnesses will come forward to jog people's memories, and that open-source material will help people piece together the story and make new connections. As part of the podcasting process, Alastair Morgan revisited the scene of his brother's murder with the production team. Jukes praises the podcast format for telling in-depth and complicated stories. "A podcast is great because you're limitless in what you can do," he said. "You can move around, and you can rely on a single voice - a presenter or, in Alastair's case, the brother of a murder victim." The producers are aiming to replicate the success of the Serial podcast, which tells a single true story over the course of a series. The Daniel Morgan Murder podcast will be released exclusively to registered subscribers before Christmas, and will become more widely available for download in the new year.
Elmbridge Museum unveiled its "museum without walls" initiative, aiming to deliver its services in a more user-friendly, engaging, and cost-effective manner. The gallery in Weybridge closed on the last Saturday of the month, while the new website was launched on the following Sunday. Surrey County Council owns the museum building, with the borough council set to make a decision regarding its future. The revamped website offers a comprehensive online experience, providing a holistic view of the museum and its services to diverse audiences. The site features an "object in focus" section, which offers detailed information on select exhibits, as well as a "what we're up to" section showcasing the museum team's current projects. Although physical objects will continue to be displayed at temporary exhibitions across the borough, the museum will launch a school programme in September. Currently, two exhibitions are being held at Thames Ditton Library and Walton Library. The museum's collection is being relocated to a purpose-built storage facility, where visitors can view artefacts by appointment.
Scarborough Hospital, plagued by financial and care quality issues, has reached out to its neighboring York Hospital in a bid for potential partnership. The move comes as the government aims to transform all hospitals into foundation trusts by 2013, granting them greater independence from the NHS. However, to qualify, hospitals must demonstrate good financial and clinical standing, a task Scarborough believes is impossible to achieve within the three-year timeframe. The hospital is struggling with £4m in debts and recent criticism from the NHS regulator over subpar care standards. Scarborough's board acknowledged the challenge, stating that seeking assistance from York is the best course of action. The trust's preferred partner for this partnership is York, which was granted foundation status in 2007. A spokesperson for the Scarborough and North East Yorkshire NHS Trust explained that becoming a foundation trust requires meeting stringent clinical and financial standards, ensuring long-term viability. The requirement to achieve this status by 2013 puts Scarborough in a tough spot, as it is unlikely to meet the necessary criteria. Without foundation trust status, the hospital risks compromising its ability to deliver consistent clinical services and staff certainty across its sites. Following a meeting, the board considered various options to secure sustainable services for Scarborough and its surrounding areas. The decision was made to approach York Hospital Foundation Trust to explore a potential partnership. Patrick Crowley, chief executive of York Teaching Hospital NHS Foundation Trust, expressed hope that a collaboration with Scarborough can be maintained, enabling continued provision of health services for the local population.
A scathing letter has criticized the UK government's handling of the Dubs resettlement scheme, which was introduced to bring in unaccompanied child refugees from Europe. Only about 200 children have entered the UK under the scheme last year, and none arrived in 2017, according to the Home Office. The government had promised to honor its commitment to refugees, a pledge made in honor of Labour peer Lord Dubs, who himself fled the UK as a child refugee before World War II. Lord Dubs' amendment required the UK government to provide assistance to a specified number of unaccompanied child refugees. The UK government has agreed to take up to 480 unaccompanied children under the Dubs scheme, but it will eventually close, with ministers arguing that an open-ended commitment would encourage human trafficking. However, a joint letter from the Scottish and Welsh secretaries for equalities, Angela Constance and Carl Sargeant, has expressed frustration with the scheme's management. They state that they have struggled with the lack of information provided by those running the operation, which has hindered their ability to plan ahead. The letter acknowledges that the emergency clearance of the Calais refugee camps led to difficult circumstances and that the UK government is also facing a legal challenge over the scheme. The authors believe that the lack of planning and information sharing from coordinators has been a major issue. Immigration Minister Brandon Lewis has assured that the UK government is committed to fulfilling its commitment under the Dubs scheme as soon as possible and that other mechanisms are available to help vulnerable children. He also emphasized that the UK government is part of a wider response to help people in conflict zones, having committed to resettling 23,000 people directly from regions of conflict and granting protection to over 8,000 children last year. Lewis expressed gratitude for the support from Scotland and Wales and announced plans to extend the National Transfer Scheme to the rest of the UK.
The Scottish Environment Protection Agency (Sepa) has discovered that Dawnfresh, the UK's largest trout producer, used the pesticide azamethiphos excessively at one of its sites in Loch Etive. This pesticide is used to combat sea lice, parasites that can weaken fish health and hinder growth. Dawnfresh acknowledged a "very small number of errors" and stressed that it is working with Sepa to minimize the environmental impact of its operations going forward. The regulator, alerted by the conservation charity Friends of Loch Etive, has been investigating the breach of rules. Dawnfresh produces approximately 3,000 tonnes of rainbow trout at Loch Etive each year. According to Sepa, the company is facing scrutiny for carrying out consecutive bath treatments with azamethiphos less than 24 hours after the initial treatment, which is not in compliance with its CAR licence. Sepa will conduct further investigations into this matter. To control sea lice, which can harm fish, various methods are employed, including chemicals. Dawnfresh Farming director Stewart Hawthorn stated that last year the company experienced a "historically low number of sea lice" in Loch Etive after implementing a more proactive approach to the issue. However, he also admitted that there were six instances where the company did not meet its usual standards in treatment implementation, resulting in a lower-than-expected environmental rating. Hawthorn expressed frustration that the errors could have prevented the site from receiving an 'excellent' rating in environmental compliance, as confirmed by Sepa. Despite this, the company remains committed to environmental protection and is working with Sepa to minimize its environmental impact. Three of the company's Loch Etive sites achieved 'excellent' ratings, while one received a 'good' rating. Two sites will not operate again due to concerns about meeting the highest standards of environmental protection. Dawnfresh is already implementing new treatment methods to further reduce its reliance on chemicals, aiming to effectively control sea lice in the future.

Staff at Claythorpe Watermill near Alford, Lincolnshire, expressed concern over a feisty rooster named Stan, who has a reputation for chasing toddlers and pecking ankles. As the summer season approaches, the owner, Rebecca Agate, suggested it might be best to find Stan a new home. She explained, "We have a lot of young children visiting the watermill, and if Stan is chasing them or pecking, it can be quite intimidating. We want our visitors to have a pleasant experience, not one that's marred by a negative encounter." Rebecca noted that Stan, who is believed to be at least part Dutch bantam, has a lively personality and enjoys the company of people. However, she also acknowledged that he has a grumpy side. Unfortunately, this isn't an isolated incident - there have been numerous reports of birds attacking people, animals, and objects. For instance, last year in Cambridgeshire, a pheasant was dubbed a "terrorist" after attacking vehicles and chasing cats and dogs at a farm in Hail Weston. One driver was trapped for 20 minutes when the bird blocked his path, flew at the bonnet, and chased his van. Another pheasant, named Phil, became aggressive towards a Shropshire family, forcing one of them to defend themselves with a badminton racket. Claythorpe Watermill and Wildfowl Gardens is home to a diverse range of animals, including waterfowl, wallabies, chipmunks, peacocks, and a family of otters.
Around 20,000 Syrians are set to resettle in the UK over the next five years. To prepare, discussions have centered on housing, education, and healthcare, as well as how additional services will be funded. The Local Government Association is calling for more resources to house people rather than utilizing their limited housing stock. BBC correspondent Daniel Sandford said that the UK plans to accept about 400 refugees each month, selected by the UN Refugee Agency. He stated that the plan aims to begin taking in people from camps bordering Syria, rather than those already in Europe. Sandford said: "Many local authorities are hesitant to use their own council housing stock, so they're searching for landlords willing to take in Syrian refugees, funded by the state." Prime Minister David Cameron has emphasized that people will come from camps near Syria, not from those already in Europe. Unlike other asylum seekers, these individuals will be eligible to start work immediately and claim benefits. Local Government Association's David Simmons said that councils need reassurance about long-term plans to support families and children. "This isn't just about short-term funding but ensuring resources are available so families and children can properly integrate into life in the UK, access education, job opportunities, and housing opportunities." Meanwhile, Central European ministers have rejected a mandatory quota system for sharing migrant arrivals. In recent weeks, tens of thousands of migrants have attempted to reach Europe from war-torn Syria and Libya. Many have traveled through Hungary to wealthier EU nations like Germany, Austria, and Sweden, which have more liberal asylum laws. European Council President Donald Tusk has announced that he will call an emergency summit later this month if a solution to the crisis isn't found soon.

New York's attorney general has issued a request for Exxon Mobil's emails and financial records, following recent allegations that the company's scientists first sounded the alarm on global warming decades ago. Exxon has denied these claims, calling them "inaccurate distortions." Kenneth Cohen, the company's vice-president for public affairs, stated on Thursday that Exxon "unequivocally rejects" the allegations of suppressing climate change research. The investigation focuses on whether Exxon misled investors this year about the climate change risks raised by its own scientists. Exxon is also accused of funding anti-climate change groups to mislead the public about the threat of global warming. US presidential candidates Hillary Clinton and Bernie Sanders have both called for investigations into Exxon's actions. Other US attorney generals may also join New York in investigating the company.
A group of men broke into a house on Rouken Glen Road in Giffnock, near Glasgow, at around 5:30 pm on Monday. The burglars made off with cash and jewellery once the woman's family arrived at the scene. Two of the men were driven away in a dark grey Volvo by a fourth man. The third member of the gang was seen walking towards Eastwood Toll. The woman who was threatened was unharmed but visibly shaken. Police are working to identify the men, who were all wearing dark clothing. Det Con Craig Faskin said: "This was an extremely frightening experience for the woman who was in her own home and the perpetrators must be brought to justice." The investigation is ongoing, with officers still gathering information on the crime, including what was stolen and the exact circumstances. With Rouken Glen Road being a busy area at the time, Faskin is urging any witnesses who have not yet spoken to police to do so, as well as anyone with information that could help the investigation.
China's Purchasing Managers Index plummeted to 50.8, lower than predicted, following a 51.1 reading in the previous month. Any score above 50 signifies growth, but analysts had forecast a slight increase in expansion. The latest data reveals the Chinese economy is experiencing its slowest growth in over five years, with July to September growth at 7.3%, down from 7.5% in the previous quarter. "There's still downward pressure on the economy, and monetary policy will remain accommodative," said China International Capital analysts after the PMI data was released on Saturday. Beijing has recently introduced measures to boost consumer spending, including easing home purchase restrictions and injecting billions into major banks. China's central bank has also cut certain interest rates. The government aims for 7.5% economic growth this year, but many analysts believe it will struggle to meet this target. Speculation is growing that further measures may be needed to boost growth. Given China's massive and increasingly educated population, high growth rates are necessary to ensure sufficient job creation. However, China's growth ambitions far exceed those of more developed economies, with the US economy growing at an annual rate of 3.5%.
David Tinsley, a UK economist at UBS, believes Brexit has led to a sharp decline in growth, a significant drop in the pound, and further monetary easing from the Bank of England. He forecasts two interest rate cuts from the central bank over the next six months, which would take rates from their current record low of 0.5% down to zero. Rates have been at 0.5% since 2009, as Britain has struggled to recover from the 2008 financial crisis. In contrast, UBS now expects the US Federal Reserve to delay rate hikes, contrary to initial predictions that Janet Yellen, its governor, would raise rates in September. Mr Tinsley anticipates that the UK's growth rate will plummet to zero in the second half of the year, with the economy having grown at around 2% previously. In the long run, there is a risk of even greater damage. The outcome will largely depend on future trade negotiations and the UK's access to the European single market. According to Tinsley, Brexit could result in a 3.0% decline in long-term UK GDP, assuming limited access to the single market.
Harry Gregg, 84, was one of the few survivors of the 1958 Munich air disaster, which claimed 23 lives including eight of his teammates. When the news broke, Gregg told his wife he wanted to be alone in the TV room, overwhelmed by the tragedy. "I didn't know how to react," he said. "It's hard to put into words." Gregg shared this on his charitable foundation's Facebook page, revealing that a grieving Brazilian journalist reached out to him after the tragedy in Colombia, where 71 people died, including 19 Chapecoense players. "At Munich, I lost friends and fellow players," Gregg said, "but there were others on board, including a renowned journalist at the time." The journalist told Gregg, "I'm glad you remembered everyone who died in the plane because I lost three friends on that aircraft who were sports journalists." Gregg emphasized the importance of remembering all those who died, not just the players. "I hope that brought some comfort to that Brazilian journalist, who was grieving too." Gregg stayed with the burning plane in Munich and helped rescue several people, including a mother and baby. Just six people survived the crash near Medellin, Colombia, with goalkeeper Jackson Follmann losing his right leg. Gregg credited getting back onto the training field with helping him cope with the trauma. Just a few months after the Munich disaster, Gregg was named the world's best goalkeeper in recognition of his performances for Northern Ireland at the 1958 World Cup in Sweden.
Angolan human rights activist Rafael Marques de Morais has accused seven high-ranking military officers of involvement in murder, torture, and land grabs in the country's lucrative diamond fields. His claims have sparked widespread protests, with several people reportedly arrested for speaking out against the trial. De Morais has long been a vocal critic of President Jose Eduardo dos Santos' 35-year rule, and in 1999, he was jailed for 43 days, including 11 days in solitary confinement, for publishing an article that criticized the government. In Angola, dissent is rarely tolerated, and critics of the authorities often face intimidation, imprisonment, or even disappearance, according to BBC Africa analyst Mary Harper. The latest charges against de Morais follow his publication of a book, Blood Diamonds: Torture and Corruption in Angola. The army denies any involvement in the crimes exposed in the book. In Luanda, the trial was held behind closed doors, prompting reports of scuffles between police and protesters who chanted "Free Rafael" and "Imprison the Generals." De Morais, who was in the UK last week to receive a freedom of expression award, faces nine charges of criminal defamation, plus up to 15 additional ones. He had warned that the trial would be a test of his resolve to fight for justice. If convicted, de Morais could face up to nine years in prison and a fine of $1.2m. The unregulated diamond trade fueled Angola's 27-year civil war, which ended in 2002, and since then, the country has experienced an economic boom, but critics say the wealth has mainly benefited a small elite.
Pavel Durov, the former head of VKontakte, had initially announced his intention to leave the company but later rescinded his resignation. The company disputed his claim, and Durov's decision was seen as a way to maintain control. He had previously refused to censor posts on his platform, even when asked by the Russian government. In a statement, Durov expressed his surprise at being fired as general director of VKontakte. He noted that the shareholders had not been willing to take the necessary steps to terminate his employment directly, instead choosing to announce his departure through press reports. Durov's replacement, Igor Sechin and Alisher Usmanov, now have full control over the company. While the situation may have been inevitable, Durov expressed gratitude for the seven and a half years he spent with VKontakte and acknowledged the significant impact they made. Sechin is the CEO of state-owned oil company Rosneft and served as President Putin's former deputy chief of staff. Usmanov, the richest man in Russia, made his fortune in the iron ore and steel industries and held a stake in Facebook until recently. He also owns a significant share of VKontakte through his internet company Mail.ru. Durov had announced his resignation publicly on April 1 but later revealed it was an April Fool's joke. However, VKontakte reportedly took Durov's resignation letter into consideration after the one-month grace period expired. In an interview with TechCrunch, Durov stated that he was no longer in Russia and had no intention of returning. He expressed his concerns about the country's compatibility with internet business and feared that he would face repercussions for his past actions. Durov had previously refused to cooperate with the authorities, including providing information about Ukrainians who used VKontakte to create groups related to anti-government protests. He also refused to close down a group that supported Russian opposition leader Alexei Navalny. Durov sold his shares in the company to maintain his independence and defend his ideals. He emphasized that he had a clean conscience and was willing to stand by his principles. Reports suggest that a new general director will be elected at the next VKontakte board meeting.
Egypt's state prosecutor has referred former President Mohamed Morsi for trial, charging him with inciting murder and violence. Morsi, who was ousted in July, has been held in secret since then. The charges stem from violent clashes outside the presidential palace in Cairo last December, where at least seven people were killed and hundreds more injured. Morsi faces trial alongside 14 other Muslim Brotherhood members, who will also stand trial for the same charges. The clashes took place in early December 2012, when tens of thousands of demonstrators gathered outside the palace to protest Morsi's decision to issue sweeping decrees and change the constitution. The protesters were met with resistance from the Republican Guard and police, who were allegedly ordered to break up the sit-in but refused. Morsi's supporters claim they were defending the palace against opposition activists, while the government says they were attacking the building. Among those charged with Morsi are prominent Brotherhood leaders, including Mohammed al-Beltagi and Essam El-Erian. Morsi has previously been accused of ordering the murder of prisoners, officers, and soldiers during a breakout at a Cairo prison in 2011. He has also been linked to plotting attacks on jails and conspiring with Hamas.

British comedian John Cleese drew inspiration for his TV sitcom Fawlty Towers from a stay at the Gleneagles Hotel in Torquay with the Monty Python team in 1973. The hotel, now slated for redevelopment, was once home to former owner Donald Sinclair, whose likeness inadvertently influenced Cleese's character Basil Fawlty. The 41-bedroom hotel, which ceased operations this year, was purchased by Churchill Retirement Living, who plan to convert the site into a 36-apartment complex. Initially, Torbay Council rejected the proposal, citing concerns that the development was too large and out of sync with the area. However, a revised plan was accepted, involving the demolition of the hotel and construction of 21 one-bedroom and 11 two-bedroom apartments, along with a guest suite and communal lounges. The council allowed the change of use from tourist accommodations to residential, citing the hotel's commercial viability as a factor. Fawlty Towers, ranked number one in the British Film Institute's 100 Greatest Television Programmes in 2000, aired only 12 episodes.

Every year, seventy people lose their lives and hundreds of thousands are injured due to fires caused by household appliances such as cookers, refrigerators, and tumble dryers. The Electrical Safety Council says manufacturers need to improve their ability to contact customers when there are safety issues. The council believes the current guidelines are inadequate. In December, Catherine Paterson of Middlesbrough experienced a devastating fire that destroyed her garage. The fire started in her newly purchased tumble dryer, which was only four months old. After searching online, she discovered that the model had been recalled due to a problem. "About ten minutes after the dryer was switched on, we received a call from the neighbor, informing us that the garage was on fire," she recalled to the BBC. The fire raged for fifteen minutes, causing £10,000 worth of damage. "They knew the product was faulty from the end of November, but no one contacted us," Catherine said. It wasn't until February that someone informed us of the issue, two months after the fire. Trading Standards revealed that the manufacturer of Catherine's tumble dryer made efforts to inform consumers about the product's defect, including advertising in the media and online. However, Steven Curtler, product safety manager at the Electrical Safety Council, believes Catherine's case highlights the need for improved guidelines for manufacturers. Curtler is now calling for changes to industry procedures to ensure consumer safety. "One area we believe needs significant improvement is in the traceability of products," he said. The ESC wants more information about consumers to be collected at the time of purchase. "The more information you have about the person who buys the product, the easier it will be to provide critical information about the product's safety," he added. According to the Electrical Safety Council, on average, seventy people die and 350,000 are seriously injured each year due to household appliances like refrigerators, gas cookers, and washing machines. In 2011, six members of the same family, including a mother and her five children, perished in a fire involving a refrigerator in north London. The London Fire Brigade warned that if people notice unusual noises coming from their refrigerators or freezers, they should contact the manufacturer or an electrical repair expert immediately. The problem lies at the back of the refrigerator, where a large amount of foam insulation is highly flammable.

The Bombay High Court granted Khan interim bail for two days after his lawyers appealed against his sentence. Noor Ullah Khan, 38, was one of five people run over in the incident, with three others suffering serious injuries and another person receiving minor injuries when Khan's Toyota Land Cruiser hit the American Express bakery in Mumbai's Bandra area on September 28, 2002. Khan had maintained that his driver was at the wheel, but the judge ruled that the actor was driving and was under the influence of alcohol. Following his interim bail, Khan returned home, where several Bollywood stars met him. According to reports, Khan will not be required to attend court on Friday when his bail plea is heard. The case has been a major issue in Bollywood and India for years, with Judge DW Deshpande stating in his 240-page ruling that Khan "failed to visit the hospital to meet the injured or provide aid" and "could have contacted the police immediately if he was innocent".

The Queen Street Mill Museum in Burnley is among five Lancashire County Council museums set to close, although they will reopen for school visits. The museum, which is the last remaining example of Lancashire's cotton spinning and weaving industry, will join Helmshore Mills Museum in shutting its doors. Lancashire County Council aims to save £65m by 2018 and has made the decision to close the museums, citing the county's severe financial difficulties. Councillor Marcus Johnstone, cabinet member for environment, planning, and cultural services, said: "Closing museums is never our first choice, but the scale of our financial challenge means we must make tough decisions to protect services for the most vulnerable members of our communities." Other museums that will close include the Museum of Lancashire in Preston, the Maritime Museum in Fleetwood, and Judges' Lodgings in Lancaster. The council had initially planned to close the five museums in the spring, but this deadline was delayed to allow talks with groups interested in taking over the museums. Although discussions are still ongoing, over 10,000 people signed a petition to save the museums, describing them as "a vital part of Lancashire's industrial heritage" during the county's pivotal role in Britain's Industrial Revolution.

The song's message encourages people to step forward and provide a home for a child at a vulnerable moment in their life. The choir has been performing in various settings across Fife, including halls and shopping centers. The Scottish government estimates that around 800 new foster families are needed within the next year to meet the growing demand. Rae Ormiston, from Fife Council's children and families department, highlighted the choir's role in raising awareness about fostering: "We're always looking for innovative ways to recruit and promote fostering to the wider public, and the choir is doing just that." Ormiston noted that there has been a decline in people coming forward to become foster carers, resulting in a need for more frequent publicity about the shortage. Nikki Perry, a foster carer with over 100 children in her 17-year career, expressed her hope that the choir's performance would highlight the rewards of fostering. "It's an incredible experience watching the children grow and thrive, eventually becoming parents themselves and securing a stable life they may not have had before," she said. Perry also expressed her desire to take on more children, but emphasized the need for more foster carers in Fife. Nine-year-old Brooklyn, a member of the choir, shared his personal experience of finding a foster family, stating: "I feel safe and loved having a family to care for me." The Foster for Fife Choir is believed to be the first of its kind in Scotland, using music to promote fostering and raise awareness about the need for more foster families.
Ultra-high-net-worth individuals surged by 6,340 to 193,490 worldwide in 2016, rebounding from a similar decline the previous year, according to Knight Frank. The jump was attributed to strong performances on stock markets. The report focused on individuals with net assets exceeding $30m (approximately £24.2m). The year was marked by significant political and economic uncertainties, including the UK's Brexit vote and Donald Trump's election, but many developed economies still experienced growth. Stock markets in the US and UK reached record highs in the final weeks of the year. "While uncertainty exists, many economies have strong fundamentals, with progress being made on regulation and policy, which will drive growth in some areas," noted Andrew Amoils, head of research at New World Wealth, the company behind the report. Knight Frank predicted a 43% growth in ultra-high-net-worth individuals globally over the next decade, with significant regional variations. The US is expected to see faster growth, with a 12% increase in the rest of Europe. However, Asia is set to experience a 91% rise, narrowing the gap with the US by 2026. Currently, the US has a 27,020-person lead over Asia in ultra-wealthy individuals. Asian cities, including Singapore, Shanghai, and Beijing, are predicted to surpass current concentrations of wealth in cities like San Francisco. Several African countries, including Ethiopia, Rwanda, and Tanzania, are also forecasted to experience significant increases in their wealthy populations.

Researchers at the University of Edinburgh have found that Scots aged 45 to 54 spend an average of 7.8 hours per weekday sitting down, a significant amount compared to the over-75s, who sit for 7.4 hours on average. This sedentary time includes activities like working, eating, watching TV, or using a computer. According to the study group, these figures highlight the potential health risks associated with excessive sitting at work. Experts agree that prolonged periods of inactivity - more than seven hours a day - increase the risk of early death, cardiovascular disease, type 2 diabetes, and certain cancers, even if people engage in physical activity at other times of the day. Interestingly, only the youngest group surveyed, aged 16 to 24, are less sedentary on weekdays than the over 75s. The findings, published in the Journal of Sports Sciences, are based on data from over 14,000 people in Scotland, gathered from the 2012-14 Scottish Health Survey. Notably, at the weekend, the situation reverses, with those aged 25 to 54 being the least sedentary, sitting for 5.2 to 5.7 hours a day. The over 75s, however, are the most sedentary, with 7.3 to 7.4 hours of sitting time per day. For the youngest group, most of their sitting time is spent in front of a TV or screen. Men tend to spend less time in front of screens as they age, with women peaking in middle age. According to lead researcher Tessa Strain, "large parts of the population are dangerously sedentary, something we have underestimated." Strain emphasizes that changing habits in early and middle age, when patterns may develop, is crucial. The researchers suggest that modifying workplace habits could be a suitable starting point, given the significant amount of time people spend sitting at work every day.

China views Taiwan as a breakaway province it's willing to take back by force if necessary. Before her win, Ms Tsai expressed a desire to maintain the "status quo". Ms Tsai stressed the need to respect Taiwan's democratic system, national identity, and international space, warning that any suppression would harm cross-strait relations.
Ocado, a UK-based online grocery retailer, has been working on a range of innovative technologies to improve its warehouse operations. One such project involves a humanoid maintenance robot called SecondHands, which will work alongside human colleagues to maintain the company's automated warehouses. In another area, Ocado is collaborating with Disney on a five-year EU-funded research project called Soma, which focuses on the development of robotic arms that can mimic the human hand. The project, led by five European universities, aims to create a gripper that can pick and handle various items, including fruit and vegetables, with precision. Currently, the project is in its early stages, and only a demonstration device is being showcased. However, the ultimate goal is to create a robot that can learn to distinguish the ripeness of fruit through machine learning and handle different types of items, such as wine bottles and detergent. According to Ocado's spokesman, Alexandru Voica, the company has already made significant progress in this area, with the demonstration of a gripper that uses air pressure to control the movement of robotic fingers. This technology will eventually be combined with computer vision to enable the robot to recognize products by looking at them. Professor Chris Melhuish, director of the Bristol Robotics Laboratory, notes that building a general-purpose robotic device that can perform multiple tasks is a challenging task. He emphasizes that understanding the flexibility and adaptability of the human hand is crucial to developing effective robotic manipulators. Currently, many robotic manipulation projects are focused on specific tasks, such as picking up objects or performing surgery. However, Melhuish suggests that the development of more versatile manipulators that can adapt to different tasks is essential for advancing the field.
Truro FC has secured a Section 106 planning order, which sets a deadline of July 26, 2017, for the team to move into its new stadium at Silver Bow on the outskirts of the city. Chairman Peter Masters told BBC Sport that the planning process is now complete, and work on the site will begin in October this year. The agreement mirrors one already in place for the rival Stadium for Cornwall, a 10,000-seat rugby union ground for Cornish Pirates. Both stadiums received planning permission at the end of July 2015 and required a Section 106 order from the Department of Local Government before proceeding. Truro had initially planned to share Torquay United's Plainmoor ground, but has decided to remain at Treyew Road for one final season. The new stadium will be built to 'A' standards, with a capacity for 4,000 fans, and the potential to increase to 5,000, making it suitable for top-level non-league football. It will feature a synthetic 3G surface and conference facilities. Masters and fellow businessman Philip Perryman took over the club in 2012, when it was on the brink of liquidation. The team was promoted to the second tier of non-league football last season after winning the Southern Premier League play-offs. Masters said, "Given our journey from being £4.5m in debt, selling the ground to a third party, and relegation, we've made significant progress. Royal photographer Kate Middleton was honored by the Royal Photographic Society for her "talent and enthusiasm" behind the lens. At 34, Middleton captured the first official photograph of Princess Charlotte when the princess was born in 2015. She had previously released photos from her and Prince William's 2012 Asian and Pacific tour. Since becoming a mother, Middleton has released numerous family photos, including Prince George's first day at nursery school and Princess Charlotte's first birthday. Older shots include a photo of Mount Kinabalu, the highest point in Borneo, and a black-and-white image of an orangutan from her 2012 trip to the region with Prince William. The society recognized Middleton for her "long-standing" interest in photography and its history, noting that she is the latest in a line of royal photographers. The society has also been supported by Queen Victoria and Prince Albert, who were patrons of the society when it was founded in 1853. Middleton joins fellow lifetime members Annie Leibovitz, who has photographed the Queen, and Sir Don McCullin, a recently-knighted war photographer. The Queen herself has also used her camera to capture family memories and royal trips, filming her own cine films. Middleton, a History of Art graduate from the University of St Andrews, is also a patron of the Natural History Museum and National Portrait Gallery. Her first commission came in 2008 for her parents' company, Party Pieces.
Before the May general election, Conservative MP for Oxford West and Abingdon, Miss Blackwood, publicly disclosed that she has Ehlers-Danlos syndrome. This genetic condition affects the joints and muscles, often causing chronic pain. Miss Blackwood is now actively campaigning to reduce the stigma surrounding chronic illnesses. She was diagnosed with the condition two years ago, after experiencing joint pain, migraines, and asthma. Miss Blackwood chose to share her diagnosis before the election, believing it was essential for people to be aware of her condition. She also wanted to demonstrate that individuals with hidden conditions can successfully hold high-profile, stressful jobs. Miss Blackwood stated: "One of the reasons I decided to speak out was to ensure it was clear that Ehlers-Danlos syndrome is not a terminal illness, and despite being chronic, it is possible to lead a normal life and manage the condition effectively." She believes that the UK still struggles with a stigma surrounding disability and chronic illnesses, which needs to be overcome. Miss Blackwood made lifestyle changes, including adjusting her diet and taking up Pilates, to help manage her condition while speaking to BBC Oxford.
Barnes has been appointed an MBE after retaining his Commonwealth Games light-flyweight title in Glasgow in August. Dame Mary Peters, a former Olympic gold medallist, has been made a Companion of Honour for her services to sport and the community in Northern Ireland. Fifa vice-president Jim Boyce has become an OBE, while Salto Gymnastics Club founder Tony Byrne has received an MBE. Boyce, who is stepping down as Britain's vice-president of the world football governing body in May, has spoken about the importance of football in Northern Ireland, saying he wants to dedicate his honour to those who kept the sport going during difficult times. Peters won gold in the 1972 Olympics and the Commonwealth Games, and has worked to promote both sport and the tourist industry in Northern Ireland. As Lord Lieutenant of Belfast, she escorted Queen Elizabeth on a visit to the city in the summer. Peters received an MBE in 1973, a CBE in 1990, and a Damehood 10 years later. Barnes excelled as an amateur boxer, winning the Glasgow gold, his latest international triumph. He was captain of Northern Ireland's Commonwealth Games boxing team, which won nine medals in Scotland. Barnes has also won two Olympic bronze medals and a gold in the 2010 European Championships. Byrne founded the Lisburn club, which was Ireland's first full-time gymnastics facility, in 1982, and was its CEO. The UK Communities Secretary, Sajid Javid, approved plans for shale gas extraction at Cuadrilla's Preston New Road site in October 2016, prompting the Preston New Road Action Group to label the decision "fundamentally flawed" and a "top-down abuse of power". A court date has been set for 15 March in Manchester, where the group plans to challenge the ruling. In 2015, Lancashire County Council refused permission for shale gas extraction, citing concerns over noise and traffic, but Cuadrilla appealed to the secretary of state, who ultimately sided with them. This decision marks a significant change, as UK shale rock will be fracked horizontally for the first time, potentially increasing gas yields. Construction work at the site began in January, amidst continued protests from anti-fracking campaigners. The group's Claire Stephenson described Javid's decision as "not democracy in action, but a top-down abuse of power" to benefit the corporation over the community's wishes. She argued that the secretary of state, who is not local, has no connection to the area and has not been present during planning inquiries or hearings. The parish council, Fylde Borough Council, and Lancashire County Council all rejected the application, with the county council clearly stating its opposition. The Department for Communities and Local Government declined to comment on the upcoming case, which will be heard at Manchester's Civil Justice Centre on 15 March.
The Prison Officers Association has reported an "ongoing disturbance" at HMP Swaleside, a category B training prison on the Isle of Sheppey. Specialist "Tornado" teams have been deployed to address the issue, according to the Prison Service. POA chairman Mike Rolfe stated that the extent of the disturbance was unclear, but it did not appear to be on the scale of the recent riot at HMP Birmingham, which lasted 12 hours and involved hundreds of inmates. Riot police were used to quell the disturbance, which began when staff raided cells and confiscated prisoners' possessions. A Prison Service spokesman said that the incident involved 60 prisoners on one landing of A Wing, while the rest of the prison remained secure with all prisoners locked in cells. The situation remains under control and is being resolved, he added. Rolfe described Swaleside as a challenging environment due to a significant staff shortage and high levels of violence. An inmate at the prison contacted the BBC, claiming that the disturbance started after staff confiscated prisoners' belongings and that tensions between inmates and prison officers had increased following the introduction of inexperienced staff and reduced family visits. The BBC's Danny Shaw reported that the disturbance had been contained, but prisoners had refused to return to their cells. "Tornado" teams were also used to quell the riot in Birmingham, where prisoners set fires, stole keys from guards, and one inmate was seriously injured. While up to 240 prisoners were relocated following the riot, the Ministry of Justice has not commented on whether any were transferred to Swaleside. Guardian prison correspondent Eric Allison had previously reported problems at the prison during a research visit, describing it as a "troubled jail" with high levels of violence and access to illicit substances. However, Allison noted that Swaleside has implemented innovative education schemes, housing around 1,100 inmates serving terms of more than four years. An HM Inspectorate of Prisons report in July found the prison to be "dangerous" and reported high levels of violence, with many incidents classified as serious. In a recent letter to the Times, former deputy prime minister Nick Clegg, ex-Home Secretary Ken Clarke, and former Labour Home Secretary Jacqui Smith called for the prison population in England and Wales to be reduced to 45,000, citing the increasing danger posed by jails. They pointed to the recent violent unrest at Birmingham Prison as a "wake-up call" for the country.
Rag N Bone Man, whose real name is Rory Graham, won the Critics' Choice award, beating out newcomers Anne-Marie and Dua Lipa. The singer's victory comes after his soul ballad Human reached the charts for the first time just a week ago. Graham expressed his surprise and happiness, saying, "I thought they'd made a mistake, I was up against two pop giants. I'm absolutely thrilled." The award, which has previously been won by Adele, Sam Smith, and Emeli Sande, is considered a key indicator of success in the music industry. Since its inception in 2008, all but one winner has achieved platinum status for their debut album. The exception is last year's winner, Jack Garratt, whose album Phase is currently certified silver. Graham's journey began as a jungle MC before his parents encouraged him to pursue singing. Inspired by blues legends like John Lee Hooker, he combines soulful melodies with hip-hop beats and gospel elements. In 2012, Graham supported his musical idol, Joan Armatrading, after his girlfriend shared his early recordings with promoters. Graham made some of his early recordings while on the toilet, which surprisingly gained attention. He chose his stage name after watching an episode of Steptoe and Son, thinking it sounded like a classic blues singer. Graham's current single Human was a hit in Europe before reaching the UK, where it spent 10 weeks at number one in Germany. The song was propelled into the UK's top 40 after being covered by X Factor contestant Emily Middlemas. Graham's debut album is set to be released in February, and he's also nominated for the BBC's Sound of 2017. He'll perform live on the Brit Awards launch show and headline tour in April, including two shows at London's Shepherd's Bush Empire. The Critics' Choice award is chosen by a team of experts, including music journalists and programmers at major TV and radio stations. More than 100 artists were considered for the shortlist. Graham will take the stage at the Brit Awards on February 24 at the O2 Arena in London. Singer Michael Buble is set to host the main ceremony, but it's unclear if he'll be able to fulfill the role due to his son's cancer diagnosis.
Belfast City Council has approved two major redevelopment projects, with estimated costs of £35m and £20m. The Robinson Centre and Brook Activity Centre will be transformed, with the former set to be replaced by a state-of-the-art facility featuring an eight-lane pool and leisure waters, following its closure in 2015 due to asbestos concerns. The £15m Brook Activity Centre rebuild will focus on outdoor leisure, including a new Gaelic football pitch, two football pitches, and several five-a-side pitches. These developments are part of the council's £105m programme to enhance leisure facilities across the city. The chairman of the council's planning committee, Cllr Peter Johnston, described the projects as "an investment in the future health and well-being of both the city and its citizens". Construction on both projects is expected to begin over the summer, with the facilities set to open in early 2019. In related news, revised plans for Windsor House, Ireland's tallest office block, have also been given the green light. The Grand Central Hotel, which will feature 304 bedrooms plus serviced apartments and offices, is set to become the city's biggest and tallest hotel.
Senator David Leyonhjelm, a libertarian, has made a complaint under Australia's Racial Discrimination Act, citing Section 18C as a prime example of the law's overreach. The section prohibits insulting someone due to their race, and Leyonhjelm, who has lodged the complaint, is attempting to demonstrate why he believes it should be repealed. In an article, newspaper columnist Mark Kenny made derogatory comments about Leyonhjelm, labeling him a "boorish, supercilious know-all with the empathy of a brick". Leyonhjelm acknowledged he wasn't offended, but saw the opportunity to illustrate his point. According to Section 18C, Kenny's article is unlawful because it was reasonably likely to offend, insult, or humiliate another person due to their colour. Leyonhjelm argued the comments were "reasonably likely" to offend or insult some white males. If the complaint succeeds, Leyonhjelm expects the law to be repealed, allowing Kenny to express his opinions freely. The law prohibits publicly offending, insulting, or intimidating someone because of their race, colour, or national or ethnic origin. Critics, including conservatives, have long campaigned against the law, citing its use to prosecute high-profile right-wing commentator Andrew Bolt. Former Prime Minister Tony Abbott initially vowed to repeal the law in 2013 but abandoned his attempt amid strong opposition. In recent weeks, Abbott reignited the debate when he suggested a more limited reform that would remove the words "offend" and "insult" from the law.

At the inquest, it was revealed that Chapman had dropped off two passengers just before the accident in the Baldwin Valley. Coroner John Needham stated that the cause of Chapman's death had yet to be determined. The court heard that Chapman had departed from Milton Keynes at around 6 am with two passengers on board his Bell Jetranger aircraft. He then flew to the island, where he dropped off the two passengers at the Creg Ny Baa, a popular spot for watching the TT races. Chapman had planned to refuel at Ronaldsway Airport before returning to the area to join friends and watch the races. However, his helicopter crashed at around 9 am in a field on a hillside adjacent to the 33rd milestone on the Mountain Road. Chapman's body was found "trapped in wreckage" and pronounced dead at the scene. A paramedic's report noted "multiple trauma consistent with a helicopter crash." Chapman's body was found to be "trapped in wreckage." Inquiries into the crash are being conducted by inspectors from the UK's Air Accidents Investigations Branch. The inquest has been adjourned to a yet-to-be fixed date.
Graham Ovenden, a resident of Bodmin Moor in Cornwall, was found to have abused children in the 1970s and 1980s, using them as subjects for his paintings. In 2013, District Judge Elizabeth Roscoe ordered the destruction of Ovenden's indecent paintings and photographs, citing their explicit nature. Ovenden was subsequently jailed after the Court of Appeal deemed his suspended sentence too lenient. Judge Roscoe acknowledged that the decision would likely spark outrage within the art community. Lord Clancarty, an independent peer, expressed concerns in the Lords, stating that this "groundbreaking judgment has significant implications" for the freedom of expression of many contemporary artists. Following Ovenden's conviction, the Tate removed over 30 prints from its online collection. Labour's Shadow Culture spokesman, Lord Stevenson, questioned the government's stance, saying "Should we be saying it's acceptable to destroy art in totalitarian regimes, but not within our own borders?" Internet Safety Minister Lady Shields clarified that the court's decision was final, but noted that Ovenden could appeal the ruling. She emphasized that some of Ovenden's works were explicit child abuse images, which should be destroyed. Ovenden was found guilty at Truro Crown Court of six counts of indecency with a child and one count of indecent assault, but a jury acquitted him of three counts of indecency and two counts of indecent assault during his trial.

The 42-year-old Goldsmith took to the pre-event briefing, delivering a humorous rap that left runners in stitches. "My phone has been flooded with messages," Goldsmith said, "It's amazing what people find funny." Parkrun, the free weekly runs that took place at the Kesgrave venue for the 90th time on Saturday, has been a global phenomenon since its inception in September 2014. "I'm a white, middle-class rapper from Suffolk, rapping in a Suffolk accent," he joked, "It's about time the world took notice of that."
A range of suspects, including judges, lawyers, academics, and student leaders, are being targeted by the attorney general who claims they formed a secret society with ties to the Muslim Brotherhood, with the aim of overthrowing the government. Human rights groups have denounced the trial as a farce, saying it is a gross miscarriage of justice. In the UAE, where seven sheikdoms are ruled by powerful families, opposition to the government is strictly prohibited, with political parties and demonstrations banned. Several Gulf nations, including Bahrain, Yemen, Oman, and Saudi Arabia, have witnessed pro-democracy protests inspired by the Arab Spring uprisings in Tunisia and Egypt in 2011. The activists were arrested last year during a campaign against civil society activists suspected of dissent. Many have ties to the Islamist group al-Islah, which the authorities claim has links to the Muslim Brotherhood, but al-Islah maintains it advocates for peaceful reform and denies any connection to the Brotherhood. The group allegedly used social media and other channels to incite public opposition to the UAE's system of government and the ruling families. According to the attorney general, the defendants were part of an organisation that sought to undermine the UAE's fundamental principles and seize power. Human rights groups argue that the defendants were denied access to lawyers until just days before the trial, and were not provided with the charges and evidence against them. They also claim the defendants were subjected to harsh treatment, including bright lights, hoods, and insults, while in custody. Human Rights Watch has condemned the trial as a sham, saying it amounts to a grossly unfair judicial process that mocks justice. The organisation has urged US Secretary of State John Kerry to raise concerns about a fair trial during his visit to the UAE this week. The 94 defendants are being tried in the UAE's highest court, which means the decision is final and there is no right of appeal.
A probe by the standards committee is underway to consider tougher regulation of lobbying in Wales. Scotland and Westminster have introduced lobbying registers, but Wales has not. A campaigner believes that by opening up lobbying to scrutiny now, Wales can maintain its reputation. Lobbyists are typically companies or individuals hired to influence government decisions. Plaid Cymru AM Neil McEvoy has been a vocal advocate for regulating lobbying in Wales, arguing that transparency would be essential. However, the outgoing standards commissioner, Mr Elias, stated that there had been no complaints regarding lobbying during his tenure. He also pointed out that setting up a statutory register of lobbyists would come with significant resource implications. Tamasin Cave, who leads the Alliance for Lobbying Transparency, expressed concerns that lobbying is often done covertly, without transparency. She suggests that a lobbying register would be a straightforward way to address this issue. In Wales, unlike Westminster, there have been no major scandals involving lobbyists. However, Cave believes that opening lobbying up to scrutiny now would be the best way to maintain this reputation. Huw Roberts, chairman of the Welsh lobbying company Deryn, has stated that his company publishes a list of clients and employees and adheres to a strong code of conduct. The Association of Professional Political Consultants, the industry's self-regulatory body, also follows strict guidelines. Mark Glover, chairman of the APPC, emphasized the importance of any new register providing consistent levels of transparency for both consultant and in-house lobbyists without being overly bureaucratic or costly. In 2013, the assembly standards committee concluded that AMs should face stricter scrutiny but not a full official register. First Minister Carwyn Jones has stated that lobbyists do not have access to Welsh ministers. The committee has agreed to hold a written consultation on the issue.
The Vatican City is nestled within the Italian capital, Rome, and its residents include priests and nuns from many different countries. Compared to the vast Papal States of central Italy, the Vatican City is a tiny territory that was once under the control of the Italian unification forces in the mid-19th century. For nearly a century, the popes were effectively "prisoners in the Vatican" until the Lateran Treaty in 1929, which created the current mini-state. The Vatican City is home to numerous impressive buildings, including St Peter's Basilica, a famous pilgrimage site. The Vatican Museums and Art Galleries house an extensive collection of priceless art, once owned by the popes. However, the Vatican has also made efforts to increase transparency following an investigation into the Vatican Bank in 2010, which revealed violations of money-laundering rules. With a population of around 800 and an area of just 0.44 square kilometers, the Vatican City is one of the world's smallest countries. The official language is Latin, and Italian is the most widely spoken language. The majority of the population adheres to Christianity, with a life expectancy of 74 years for men and 81 years for women. The official currency is the euro, and the head of state is the Pope, currently Pope Francis. In 2013, Pope Francis was elected, becoming the first Latin American pope in history. He was chosen despite being 76 years old, which was seen as unusual given the age of the previous pope. Additionally, he has only one lung, raising questions about his ability to handle the demands of the job. Despite this, he has broad appeal among the College of Cardinals, who were drawn to his conservative views on homosexuality but liberal stance on issues like poverty and inequality. Vatican Radio, which broadcasts to a global audience as "The Pope's Voice," has faced allegations that its transmissions have been exceeding Italy's electromagnetic radiation standards, a claim the Vatican denies. The papacy also maintains a Twitter account, @pontifex.
The Brexit could fundamentally alter the rules governing the signing of players from other EU countries. Canning expressed uncertainty about how these changes would impact his team. "We might have seven or eight players who fall under this category, but how would they go about it?" he asked. "Would they secure a work permit right away or when their contract is renewed?" The BBC reported that none of the 53 non-British EU players currently in the Scottish Premiership would qualify for a work permit solely based on their international career. Professor Raymond Boyle, a sports industry expert from the University of Glasgow, suggested that the rules might be relaxed to make it easier for non-EU players to join the league, similar to Norway and Switzerland. However, Canning acknowledged that the changes would likely alter his club's thought process when considering potential signings. "One of the first things we do when evaluating a player from abroad is ask if they need a work permit," he explained. "If that's the case, we usually don't pursue that route. The new rules would definitely have a significant impact on the club. For us to attract an international player who would qualify for a work permit would be challenging, making it harder to bring in foreign players. We've always tried to develop our own talent, so this could affect our approach. I think there needs to be a balance struck."

Dan Tan, also known as Tan Seet Eng, has been held in detention without trial since October 2013. A judge ruled that his detention was unlawful because he posed no public threat. Tan, who maintains his innocence, is sought by several countries including Italy, but Singapore does not have extradition treaties with them. He has been implicated by Interpol in rigging hundreds of sports events, mostly football matches, and is suspected of rigging matches in Italy's Serie A and Serie B in 2011. In the past, he was referred to as "the mastermind and leader of the world's most notorious match-fixing syndicate". Singapore's law allows for indefinite detention of suspected criminals without trial. These detentions are reviewed annually. The judge, Sundaresh Menon, stated that while Tan's alleged actions were "reprehensible" and should not be condoned, there was "no evidence" to suggest they harmed public safety and peace. "The matches he allegedly fixed, whether or not successfully, took place outside of Singapore," he said, adding that there was no indication that he worked with international crime syndicates or that such activities would be based in Singapore.

The Scottish paper outlined its demands for Scotland to remain in the single market and increase devolution. Scottish Minister for UK Negotiations on Scotland's Place in Europe, Mike Russell, expressed hope that the paper's options, particularly Scotland's membership in the single market and increased devolution, would be taken forward. The Northern Ireland paper focused on what would happen to the border between it and the EU after Brexit. Drakeford stated that the Welsh Government would use the JMC's findings to inform its own position and ensure that Wales's interests were represented. The Welsh Government would also consider the Scottish and Northern Irish governments' positions, as well as Prime Minister Theresa May's views, when developing its paper. In contrast, Mark Drakeford made clear that the Welsh Government would not abandon the JMC if the other governments did.

Scott Van Zyl, a South African, was killed last week while on a hunting safari in Zimbabwe, according to Sakkie Louwrens, director of a South African crime-fighting NGO. Van Zyl disappeared during the trip with a local tracker and a pack of dogs. His death is the latest in a series of fatal crocodile attacks in the country. Louwrens told the BBC that Van Zyl left his vehicle with the tracker and dogs to search for crocodiles, but his dogs returned to camp without him. A search and rescue operation was launched after Van Zyl's dogs returned. The hunter's footprints led to a riverbank, where a discarded rucksack was found. Van Zyl was married with two children and took foreign clients on hunting trips. Louwrens said staff from the Heritage Protection Group, an organization he heads, helped the Zimbabwean authorities search for Van Zyl. "Permission was given to shoot three Nile crocodiles in the area, and one contained Van Zyl's remains," he said. Subsequent DNA tests confirmed the remains were Van Zyl's. At least four fatal crocodile attacks have been reported in Zimbabwe this year. A conservation group condemned the circumstances of his death, calling it "senseless." The group stated, "Animals in the wild are living, thinking beings with instincts for survival," and criticized Van Zyl for hunting in the first place.
A US investor, Wilbur Ross, helped the struggling Bank of Ireland avoid a state rescue during the financial crisis by providing much-needed funding. The bank's shares plummeted by 5% in early trading, but Ross downplayed the move, saying it wasn't a negative comment on the bank.
