Another prominent US investor, Prem Watsa, is expected to remain a significant shareholder, holding a 5.8% stake in the bank.
In March 2015, the tug Asterix was attempting to move the chemical tanker Donizetti off a berth at the Fawley marine terminal when it was suddenly pulled over sideways. The vessel capsized, trapping the coxswain inside, who was rescued by a colleague just before the Donizetti sank. The Marine Accident Investigation Branch (MAIB) concluded that the crew's training was inadequate. The investigation revealed that the coxswain lost control of the vessel in strong winds shortly after 8 pm on 30 March 2015. The report found that the coxswain lacked training on using a 'gog' rope, which is used to prevent the tug from being pulled over sideways. The investigation also identified communication problems between the Donizetti and Asterix. Furthermore, it was discovered that the launch crews were not adequately experienced in using the tug's emergency release mechanism for the towing hook. The report made safety and training recommendations to the tug's operator, Ã˜stensjÃ¸ Rederi AS, ABP Southampton, and the National Workboat Association. Following the incident, a crew member of the tug boat received a bravery award for saving the coxswain. Both men required hospital treatment for shock and hypothermia.
Gian Piero Conte has previously been banned for not reporting match-fixing allegations involving his former club Siena during the 2010-11 season. As part of a broader probe, prosecutors have identified 104 individuals, including the 45-year-old Conte, who they believe should face charges of sporting fraud. Conte has consistently denied any wrongdoing. The next step is for the judiciary to schedule a preliminary hearing where a judge will decide whether to press charges as requested by prosecutors. Conte gained international recognition as Juventus coach, winning three consecutive Serie A titles before becoming Italy's national coach last year. The inquiry began following alleged match-fixing attempts in lower divisions, including Serie B, Lega Pro, and some Coppa Italia matches during the 2010-11 season. Italy's football federation had already investigated and banned Conte for 10 months in 2012, which was later reduced to four months on appeal. Over 50 players were banned for up to five years, and several clubs, including Atalanta and Siena, had points deducted from their league standings as part of the case.
The Council of Mortgage Lenders has announced a significant decline in loan demand during April, with mortgage borrowing totalling £18.4 billion, a 11% decrease from the previous month. However, the figures are often volatile, and spring typically sees a drop in activity. After accounting for this seasonal trend, the CML noted that the amount borrowed last month was higher than the average over the past year and 4% higher than in April 2016. According to the CML's senior economist, Mohammad Jamei, "The housing market seems to be moving sideways." Despite this, other indicators suggest a slowdown in the market. This week, HM Revenue and Customs reported a 22.5% drop in property transactions in April compared to March. One possible reason for this decrease could be that landlords brought forward purchases to avoid recent tax changes, which reduced the amount of tax relief they could claim on mortgage payments starting from April 1. The CML also reported that first-time buyers are now taking out more mortgages than people moving house for the first time since 1996. Their research shows that while the number of home movers and landlords taking out mortgages is declining, the number of first-time buyers is increasing. "First-time buyers and remortgage customers appear to be supporting the market," said Jamei. "Home movers, however, are facing challenges, as their activity has been subdued for some time and the low number of movers means fewer properties for sale, resulting in a supply and demand imbalance that will continue to influence house prices, even as price growth slows."
Sarah Lowndes, a resident of Derby, tied the knot with her fiancé David Moore at Makeney Hall in Milford, Derbyshire. In 2010, Lowndes was diagnosed with breast cancer, which later progressed. Despite the illness, the couple, who met while driving buses, initially struggled to afford the wedding. However, they were overwhelmed with support from charity Wish for a Wedding, which helped fund the ceremony. Lowndes, a mother of two, described the event as "smooth" despite experiencing a moment of forgetfulness. She expressed her gratitude to Wish for a Wedding, stating that without their assistance, the couple might not have been able to celebrate. David Moore, who met Lowndes two years after her initial diagnosis, felt "absolutely brilliant" following the wedding. He called it "the best day of my life." Wish for a Wedding's Alison Walker, who first met David and Sarah nine weeks prior, described the ceremony as "very, very emotional." She praised the event, saying it was "beautiful," and noted that David's emotional response surprised her, as she had expected Lowndes to be the tearful one.
Moses, a massive 1,500-pound animal, was rescued from a deep mud pit at the rural Oregon City property of the Clackamas County owners. The owners, who had tried but failed to free Moses, contacted the firefighters late on Tuesday, fearing the animal would injure its legs if it attempted to extricate itself while still partially buried. After hours of digging, the firefighters finally managed to slip straps under Moses's belly and pull him to safety. During the ordeal, Moses continuously cried and wailed. After the rescue, Moses was forced to lie on his side for about an hour to rest. Eventually, he got up on his knees, rose fully, and slowly made his way to his barn.
Macsen Oatway from Neath has a rare condition called LAD1, which affects his immune system and is so rare that only 400 people worldwide have it. He spent weeks in a specialist isolation unit in Newcastle, known as a "bubble," where only his parents, doctors, and nurses were allowed to visit. Macsen underwent a series of blood transfusions, chemotherapy, and bone marrow treatment - without which he would have had to take antibiotics for the rest of his life. His parents, Richard and Zoe, described the treatment as "life-changing," and are now urging more people to register on the bone marrow transplant list to ensure other children born with the condition can live a full life. Macsen himself emphasized the importance of the treatment, saying, "It's crucial - even if you're never called, if you do get the opportunity, seize it with both hands. "It's a lifesaving opportunity, and I want to thank the donor who made it possible for me." Doctors have confirmed that Macsen is now well enough to attend school, and his parents report that he thoroughly enjoys it.

Kainos has secured funding to further develop its mobile software for the healthcare division, building on its existing platform, Evolve, which stores patient records and integrates healthcare data. The investment will also be used to boost the company's global sales efforts, with a particular focus on the US market. According to Invest NI's chief executive, Alastair Hamilton, this grant will contribute to economic growth and support skill development. In 2017, Evolve generated around £10m in revenue, and its records management system was used by 35 NHS Trusts. Brendan Mooney, Kainos's chief executive, notes that Evolve Integrated Care software will enable healthcare professionals to securely access, amend, and store patient information as they work, maintaining confidentiality. Kainos employs approximately 1,000 people worldwide and is one of the few Northern Irish companies listed on the London stock exchange.
A house fire broke out at a home on the Llangwm estate in Penlan, near Penplas, early on Monday morning. The property was initially deemed too hazardous to search, but the body of the woman who lost her life - identified locally as Linda Merron - has now been removed from the scene. Two people, a man and a woman, were taken to Morriston Hospital for treatment of severe burns and smoke inhalation, while a pet dog was also rescued. Neighbours have left floral tributes at the front gate, paying their respects. The cause of the fire is currently being investigated by police and the Mid and West Wales Fire and Rescue Service. Gina Norwood, a neighbour, revealed that the woman who lived at the house had been in poor health and had moved to the area just two years ago after her daughter began studying in the city. "She adored her animals and was a kind and friendly lady who always made time for her neighbours," Norwood said in an interview with BBC Wales. "I was awoken by my son's screams to leave the house. I looked out the window and thought the flat was ablaze. My son emerged from the front door, and the flames were engulfing everything." Norwood initially called the fire service and later an ambulance to treat the woman's daughter, who had suffered burns to her body. Her son Karl reported hearing a series of loud bangs that alerted him to the fire just after midnight.
In Pontypridd, Christopher Langley joined forces with James Lovegrove, a publican, to compile a collection of paintings of Wales' pubs. According to the Campaign for Real Ale, two pubs in Wales close each week. Langley has already completed 13 paintings and aims to finish another 20 over the coming months, expressing concern that "so much history could be lost". Pubs featured so far include The Vulcan, recently saved by St Fagan's Museum, as well as the York Hotel, Pantmawr Inn, and Glendower. Langley's collaboration with Lovegrove has broadened his understanding of pubs, a culture he was not familiar with before. He notes, "There's so much history in Cardiff that could be lost." Langley views his paintings as a way of preserving and sharing that history. When creating the paintings, he often relies on drawings or photographs of the buildings, which no longer exist. Initially, Langley had not appreciated the architectural details of pubs. However, through his painting process, he realizes the significant effort that goes into building these establishments.

The Elephant and Castle roundabout in south London was plagued by the worst cycling accidents between 2009 and 2013, with a staggering 80 incidents reported. Data from the Department for Transport (DfT) and insurance firm Aviva revealed that accidents involving cyclists within the M25 area were attended by police. Transport for London acknowledged the issue, stating that "any accident on London's roads is one too many." In response, the city is investing nearly £1 billion in upgrading its existing Cycle Superhighways, introducing new segregated cycle routes, and improving dozens of junctions on both major roads and borough streets. In 2014, the London mayor's office unveiled a £4 billion plan to revamp the city's roads, including a new layout at Elephant and Castle roundabout with additional cycle lanes. However, critics argue that the city has failed to adequately address cycling safety, with Liberal Democrat London Assembly Group leader Caroline Pidgeon stating that Transport for London has consistently underinvested in junction improvements and cycling infrastructure. Tragically, four cyclist deaths occurred in London in 2015, all involving lorries, including a recent fatality in Victoria. The recent surge in road fatalities prompted Scotland Yard to reintroduce its highway safety operation, Operation Safeway, which will deploy 600 officers at 166 junctions during peak hours for six weeks to target traffic offenders.
People can now call 111 for medical advice, replacing the previous lengthy number. In the past, those making the call from a landline would pay the local rate, but mobile users often incurred much higher charges. This change aligns Scotland with England, which has been using the 111 number since February. The NHS 24 service remains unchanged, with nurses and healthcare professionals available 24/7 to offer advice on non-urgent health matters. The introduction of a freephone number was first announced last January by Scottish Health Secretary Alex Neil, who stated it would help overcome barriers to healthcare services. The move was welcomed by poverty campaigners, including Caroline Mockford, who was initially charged nearly Â£8 by her mobile provider for a call to the helpline. She later submitted a petition to the Scottish Parliament, advocating for the phone charges to be abolished.
The Dominican Republic is a major tourist destination, with free-trade zones contributing significantly to its economy. This shift away from traditional exports like sugar and coffee has made the country a major employer and a key source of revenue. The nation is predominantly inhabited by people of mixed European and African ancestry. Western influences can be seen in the colonial architecture of its capital, Santo Domingo, as well as in its art, literature, and music. With a population of over 10 million, the country boasts an area of approximately 48,072 square kilometers. The official language is Spanish, and the majority of the population practices Christianity. The life expectancy in the Dominican Republic is around 71 years for men and 77 years for women. The official currency is the Dominican peso, and the president is Danilo Medina, who was re-elected in May 2016. Medina, an economist, first took office in 2012 and succeeded President Leonel Fernandez Reyna. He has been a long-standing member of the Dominican Liberation Party (PLD) and has overseen several significant changes, including the relaxation of abortion laws and the introduction of a naturalization law that has left thousands of Haitian immigrants stateless. The country's media landscape is concentrated in the hands of a few economically and politically powerful individuals, with numerous terrestrial TV channels and multichannel cable TV operators. Over 300 radio stations are on the air, with most being commercial. The government operates its own TV and radio networks, and press freedom is guaranteed by law, allowing diverse political views to be represented. The Dominican Republic has a rich history, with key dates including Christopher Columbus's visit in 1492, the establishment of the first Spanish colony in the Western Hemisphere in 1496, and the country's declaration of independence in 1844. The nation has experienced periods of independence, occupation by foreign powers, and authoritarian rule, including a 30-year dictatorship under Rafael Leonidas Trujillo Molina, which ended with his assassination in 1961.
A month ago, the Police Service of Northern Ireland (PSNI) uncovered that a law firm had been given confidential documents from their office without authorization. The discovery led to the arrest of a 69-year-old man who had previously worked as an investigator for the ombudsman in England. He was later released on bail. The PSNI has now confirmed that an independent police service will be appointed to oversee the investigation. According to Assistant Chief Constable Stephen Martin, "steps have been taken to ensure that independent advice has been available to the PSNI since the investigation began." Additionally, the PSNI is actively seeking to appoint another police service to lead the investigation independently. The new team is expected to be appointed within weeks. The decision to appoint an independent investigation team was made due to concerns about a conflict of interest, as the ombudsman is responsible for investigating allegations of wrongdoing by PSNI officers and former members of the Royal Ulster Constabulary. At least eight former RUC officers are named in the documents, which are part of an ongoing investigation into police misconduct. The PSNI and MI5 believe the material could pose a risk to lives. The police declined to comment on whether the investigation is focused solely on one case or if it has been expanded to include other cases. "The PSNI is conducting a criminal investigation, so it would be inappropriate to comment further," ACC Martin said. The PSNI has suspended the release of sensitive material linked to dozens of cases currently being investigated by the ombudsman's office until a review of security protocols is conducted. In addition to the police investigation, the ombudsman, Dr Michael Maguire, has referred the alleged theft and unlawful disclosure of documents to the information commissioner. The Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure has also been asked to conduct a health check on the handling of information within the ombudsman's office. The Police Ombudsman's office stated, "It appears that the theft at the heart of this investigation occurred around 10 years ago. Even so, we are now seeking an independent review by the Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure to ensure that our current systems for handling sensitive material are robust."

The image went viral, and the student later apologized, expressing regret for his actions. Leanna E, an Indianapolis Colts cheerleader, responded with forgiveness, tweeting a photo of herself with the student and saying she felt stronger after witnessing his apology. The incident began on December 14 when Leanna posed for a picture with the student and another cheerleader at an event promoting a blood donation campaign. The student had jokingly included a Snapchat photo that referenced a racial slur, which sparked widespread outrage. The principal arranged for Leanna to meet the student, which took place in a conference room on Wednesday night. The student's mother was also present, expressing gratitude for Leanna's forgiveness and stating that the language was not used at home. Leanna said she was at peace with the incident, encouraging people to reflect on their own mistakes and acknowledge that nobody is perfect.
Renowned sand sculptors Remy and Paul Hoggard are utilizing approximately 80 tonnes of sand to bring to life a monumental sculpture of King John at Runnymede in 1215. The artwork, set to stand 4m (13ft) tall and 9m (30ft) wide, is part of the anniversary Festival800, which celebrates democracy and freedom. The couple, who have created sand masterpieces across the globe, including Mr Hoggard's wife from the Netherlands, considers this project to be one of his most ambitious in terms of scale. Born in Beverley, East Yorkshire, but now residing on a farm in Bulgaria, Mr Hoggard reflects on the significance of the event. The Magna Carta, a historic treaty between King John and the barons who rebelled against him, laid the groundwork for freedom under the law. Although only three of its 63 clauses remain in law, the document has had a lasting impact. One clause protects the English Church's freedom and rights, while another preserves the privileges of the City of London. The third is believed to have established the right to a fair trial by jury. A copy of the Magna Carta is displayed at Lincoln Castle, and Festival 800 will run from August 28 to September 6, featuring performances from notable artists such as Billy Bragg and the Levellers, alongside comedy, poetry, street theatre, lectures, and debates.
New plans for Wales' health and social care services aim to enhance quality and patient voices. The Health Secretary, Vaughan Gething, stated that these plans would ensure the services are prepared for the future. A three-month consultation period has been announced. The plans, revealed in a White Paper jointly written by Mr Gething and social services minister Rebecca Evans, build on criticisms from a prominent international organisation, the OECD. The OECD previously highlighted that health boards and social care services in Wales were not working effectively together. The OECD also advocated for a more robust system of inspection and regulation within the NHS and social care in Wales. Mr Gething expressed confidence that the proposals would yield tangible improvements by prioritising people's needs in healthcare delivery. He also noted that the plans would facilitate collaboration among health and care organisations to deliver exceptional care across Wales.
In Siberia, local politician Konstantin Senchenko had criticized Russian strongman Ramzan Kadyrov on Facebook. However, he soon posted a deeply contrite apology, sparking speculation that he had been coerced into doing so. Kadyrov also shared a video of Senchenko's apology on Instagram, in which the politician profusely apologizes for his past comments. Senchenko says, "I was wrong - my emotions got the better of me." The controversy started on Tuesday when Kadyrov, an ally of President Vladimir Putin, denounced some opposition figures as "enemies of the people and traitors" and called for them to be put on trial. Senchenko responded with a Facebook post criticizing Kadyrov, labeling him a "disgrace to Russia" and urging him to leave. He also insinuated that Kadyrov was corrupt and uneducated. Kadyrov responded to Senchenko's apology by saying "I accept" - accompanied by five smiling emojis. Despite this, the incendiary statement Kadyrov made about Russia's opposition remains on his official website unchanged. Kadyrov took control of Chechnya with Kremlin backing in 2007 and has continued his fight against Islamist rebels. In exchange for his loyalty, Kadyrov has been allowed to maintain his own security force and has enjoyed a significant degree of autonomy in governing the southern Russian republic. However, human rights groups have accused Kadyrov's security forces of abuses, including torture and extrajudicial killings.

A SpaceX Falcon-9 booster successfully returned to a drone ship off the Florida coast shortly after launching a Japanese satellite into orbit. This is the second time SpaceX has achieved an at-sea touchdown, following a similar feat last month. What makes this landing particularly impressive is that the rocket was carrying significantly more speed at the time of its return. According to SpaceX officials, recovering boosters used on missions that require orbiting geostationary satellites will be even more challenging due to the high velocity required to place these platforms in the correct orbit. Following confirmation of the successful landing, SpaceX CEO Elon Musk tweeted a joke: "May need to increase the size of our rocket storage hangar." The primary objective of the latest Falcon launch was to orbit the JCSAT-14 satellite, which will relay telecommunications across the Asia-Pacific region for the Sky Perfect JSAT Corporation. Lift-off from Cape Canaveral Air Force Station in Florida occurred at 01:21 local time (05:20 GMT), and separation of the satellite from the Falcon's upper-stage was recorded 32 minutes later. The mission had been delayed a day due to poor weather over the Cape. This marks SpaceX's third successful recovery of a booster under control, following its first success in December when it returned a stage to hard ground near the launch site. However, the company is particularly keen to master ocean landings on special barges, as many of its missions will require this type of return location. Launching satellites to geostationary transfer orbit requires a lot of performance from the Falcon-9 rocket, and the energy must then be removed before it can make a landing. It's not just the extra speed that the booster is traveling at that must be reduced; the extra loading and heating on components also need to be taken into account. For SpaceX, the goal is clear: if it can recover, refurbish, and re-fly rockets, it should be able to offer its customers lower-cost launches.

A 42-year-old man, Ali Qazimaj, was arrested in Luxembourg in connection with the disappearance and murder of Sylvia and Peter Stuart. Peter Stuart, 75, was found dead near their home in Suffolk on June 3, stabbed to death. His wife, Sylvia, 69, has not been found yet. Qazimaj, a 42-year-old Kosovan immigrant from Essex, is believed to have left the UK on June 4, the day after Peter's body was discovered. He caught a ferry from Dover to France and later traveled to Luxembourg, where he was found by a worker at a hostel in Luxembourg City. He is currently being held in custody in Luxembourg. The Stuarts were last seen on May 29 when they were filmed by CCTV walking into a shop in Pulham Market. Police confirmed they were treating Sylvia's disappearance as murder and are continuing to search for her. Despite the search, there is little hope of finding Sylvia alive. Steve Paxman, the Stuarts' son-in-law, was arrested earlier this month and released on bail in connection with the investigation.
The European Commission examined whether tech giants were meeting their promise to swiftly remove hate speech after it was reported. It found that only 40% of reported hate speech incidents were removed within a day. The pledge was made in May when the companies signed up to a "code of conduct" brokered by the Commission. "The past few weeks and months have highlighted the need for social media companies to live up to their role in combating online radicalisation, hate speech, and fake news," said Justice Commissioner Vera Jourova in a statement. The research tracked the responses of 600 reported hate speech incidents over a six-week period in October and November. The study began in early October, when the monitoring system to track tech firms' responsiveness was set up. Campaign groups and NGOs used the notification systems on various social media platforms to report hate speech and track the time it took for the tech firms to act. Of the 600 reports, 270 were made to Facebook, 163 to Twitter, 123 to YouTube, and none to Microsoft. The remaining seven reports were made to social media groups that had not signed up to the May pledge. The Commission noted that the 600 reports represented a sample of racist, sexist, and misogynistic content and did not represent all hate speech on the tech firms' sites during the study period. The study found that about 20% of the messages seen were anti-Muslim and 23% were anti-Semitic. In 169 cases, the tech firms removed the content flagged as hate speech. YouTube removed 48.5% of the reported content, Facebook 28.3%, and Twitter 19.1%. In 40% of cases, reports were reviewed within 24 hours, while 43% were reviewed within 48 hours. "It is our duty to protect people in Europe from incitement to hatred and violence online," said Ms Jourova. "While IT companies are moving in the right direction, the first results show that they will need to do more to make it a success."
Festival-goers heading to Winchester's Boomtown Fair were met with sweltering temperatures, dehydration, and a host of other issues, including fainting and inadequate facilities. Organisers initially attributed the congestion to increased searches and the delayed opening of the site gates, which were caused by inclement weather. However, many attendees took to social media to express their frustration and described the situation as "hazardous". Some, including Charlie Hadley, who had been waiting for three hours, expressed concerns about the welfare of others, such as his friend recovering from chemotherapy. Richard Evans warned that the festival had become a "serious safety concern" due to the long queues and heat. Tommy Palmer noted that the sun was beating down on the queue, which was expected to last around five hours. The event, which features headliners like The Specials, M.IA., and Cypress Hill, is marketed as a fully immersive experience. A festival spokesperson acknowledged that the delay in opening the gates and the extensive searching process had caused delays, but assured attendees that the welfare of the crowd was their top priority. They stated that additional provisions, including water, were being distributed to those in the queues and that security, medical, and welfare teams were on site to address any issues.
At just 29, the Spanish tennis star has made a strong start to the European clay-court season, winning his first 10 matches. Kei Nishikori won this tournament in 2014 and 2015, but ultimately fell short due to missed break-point opportunities. Argentine legend Guillermo Vilas holds the record for 49 clay-court titles, achieved in the 1970s and early 1980s. Rafael Nadal is on the cusp of breaking this mark, with upcoming appearances in Madrid and Rome before attempting to secure his 10th French Open title at Roland Garros. In Barcelona, Nadal demonstrated his dominance, saving seven of eight break chances in the opening set and converting both opportunities he was presented with, as Nishikori suffered his first set loss of the tournament. Nadal took a commanding lead of 4-1 in the second set, only to be pushed back to 4-4 before securing the victory for a ninth time and adding to his impressive career tally of 69 professional titles. "I think my serving was key, as it allowed me to play more effectively," Nadal explained. "I was able to capitalize on a good first-serve percentage, which was crucial. In the second set, I let a number of opportunities slip away, but I'm thrilled to have held my nerve and come out on top."

Carly Fiorina, the first female Republican to throw her hat into the ring, is a political newcomer, unlike the seasoned senators and governors vying for the nomination. She's not alone; Ben Carson, the first African American to do so, also joined the fray. Both Fiorina and Carson are outsiders, setting them apart from their Democratic counterparts. Hillary Clinton and Jeb Bush, although not yet officially in, are front-runners in the Republican field. Fiorina took aim at Clinton from the get-go, citing Clinton's use of a private email server during her time as secretary of state. Fiorina's own public service record is limited; she lost her 2010 Senate bid in California by a narrow margin. Her claim to fame lies in her stint as CEO of Hewlett-Packard, which she left after six years amidst shareholder discontent. What drives her candidacy? Some might say it's a publicity stunt, with her new book set to hit shelves the day after her announcement. However, supporters argue that Fiorina's unique position as the only woman seeking the Republican nomination gives her an edge in attacking Clinton without being accused of sexism. Fiorina has won over Republican audiences with her appearances, but now it's time to see if she can turn those positive vibes into tangible support. Ben Carson made his announcement at Detroit Music Hall, accompanied by a choral rendition of Eminem's "Lose Yourself." He painted a picture of the US as a place where people can thrive when they escape government restrictions. "It's time for people to rise up and take the government back," he said. Carson's campaign has had its stumbles, particularly in media interviews, but his responses to hot-button issues like evolution and gay rights have done little to tarnish his image among core conservative voters. If enough of them rally behind Carson, he could potentially become a spoiler for other socially conservative candidates, such as Ted Cruz and Mike Huckabee. Three other Republicans have already entered the race. On the Democratic side, Hillary Clinton will need to contend with left-wing Senator Bernie Sanders.

Honey Rose, 35, was convicted of gross negligence manslaughter over the death of Vincent Barker in July 2012. The boy had died five months after an eye test at the Ipswich branch of Boots on February 15, 2012, where Rose was the optometrist. The trial had found that Rose had failed to detect swollen optic discs, a symptom of hydrocephalus - fluid on the brain, during the examination. Rose's family said the appeal court's decision had left them "devastated". In a statement, they expressed their sadness and felt that Rose's manslaughter conviction should have been upheld. They believed that if Rose had not breached her duty of care to their son, he would still be alive today.
East Lindsey District Council is considering transferring ownership of the So Festival, the Embassy Theatre in Skegness, and the Meridian Leisure Centre in Louth to a charitable trust. This move would enable the facilities to tap into new funding opportunities, with officials citing potential savings of at least £1m over five years. The decision comes as the council has faced significant funding cuts, including a £1.9m reduction in central government funding this financial year, on top of a £1.2m decrease the previous year. The Meridian Leisure Centre, which opened in 2010, relies on an annual £250,000 subsidy, with about £350,000 spent on its events programme, including the So Festival. Councillor Adam Grist, responsible for culture, leisure, and tourism, emphasized the need for action, stating that "the status quo is not an option." He argued that by proactively transferring ownership, the council can protect its current services. The proposal would grant the trust access to grants, as well as business rate and VAT relief. The council would pay a management fee to the trust, which would decrease over time, and maintain ownership of the buildings. The authority would also ensure guarantees for employees' pay and conditions. The plans are set to be debated by the full council next month.
In a significant move, the US government announced its intention to release more classified military and intelligence files from the 1970s, shedding light on its role in the 1976 coup in Argentina. The US had been criticized for its slow response to human rights abuses during that period, with estimates suggesting over 30,000 people were killed and thousands more detained and tortured. The country's former leader, General Jorge Rafael Videla, died in prison in 2013 while serving a sentence for human rights violations. President Obama acknowledged the US had learned from its past mistakes, saying its foreign policy now prioritizes transparency and human rights. By releasing these secret files, Obama hoped to help mend relations with Argentina, a move that comes after decades of controversy over US policies in the country. "Democracies must have the courage to acknowledge when we don't live up to our ideals," Obama said, referencing the US's slow response to human rights abuses in Argentina. As the country marked the 40th anniversary of the coup, tens of thousands of people took to the streets in Buenos Aires. The US had previously released over 4,000 secret files, but this latest move marked a significant step forward. Following a meeting with President Mauricio Macri, Obama announced the US would declassify military and intelligence files for the first time. The decision came after Obama visited Argentina's Remembrance Park and expressed his commitment to confronting the past with honesty and transparency. Macri welcomed the move, saying it was an opportunity for Argentina to move forward and build new relationships with the US. The visit marked a significant shift in US-Argentina relations, which had been strained during previous left-wing governments. The two leaders hailed the visit as the beginning of a new era of cooperation, with Obama praising Macri's government's reforms and Macri saying the visit marked a new chapter in their relationship.
A £282m relief road at junction 6, near the National Exhibition Centre, could be the end for Pairc na hEireann, home to the Warwickshire Gaelic Athletic Association. The site was built with funds raised by the Irish community who purchased the land in the 1950s. Treasurer Martin Grogan expressed his concerns that Highways England was "taking memories away". Martin Morgan, a 30-year-old member of the club, said: "This is the hub of Gaelic sport in Britain. It's a no-brainer." He added that the club would not be leaving its current location, citing its prime position and the fact that it was "one of the biggest and best" facilities outside Ireland. Every weekend, hundreds of people play hurling, camogie, and Gaelic football there. Three alternative route options are being considered by Highways England, one of which would pass directly through the association's land. Highways England stated that junction 6 is nearing capacity and the upgrade is necessary to alleviate severe congestion and prepare for the arrival of a new HS2 station, which will also increase traffic. A spokesperson said the decision had not been made yet, but they were in touch with the club and would keep them informed. Highways England assured that they would work to minimise the impact on the environment, local communities, local businesses, road users, and residents.

The Cormorant, a $14m (Â£11m) aircraft, has been developed over 15 years for rescue or military use and can carry 500kg (1,100lb) at speeds of up to 185km/h (115mph). According to Urban Aeronautics, the internal rotors make it safer than a helicopter, and it can take off and land vertically, as well as fly between buildings and below power lines. "This technology marks the beginning of the Cormorant's transition from prototype to near-term production and eventual commercialization," said Urban Aeronautics chief executive Rafi Yoeli. The rise of commercial drones is evident, with Amazon announcing Prime Air, a delivery-by-drone service, in the summer, and another passenger drone, the Ehang 184, receiving approval for test flights in Nevada in June. Dr Mirko Kovac, director of the Aerial Robotics Lab at Imperial College London, believes the proposed vehicle has the potential to revolutionize personal drones, not just for delivery and environmental sensing, but also for transport. However, he notes that significant technological work remains to be done to make such vehicles safe and easy to use. Drone expert Ravi Vaidyanathan from Imperial's department of mechanical engineering agrees that the recent flight of the Israeli drone could be a "landmark" moment in low altitude navigation and targeting it for humanitarian rescue and military use is a wise move. "Finding a niche application and establishing a safety record is crucial," he said. Nevertheless, the timescale for civilian drones remains uncertain due to regulatory hurdles, such as where to take off, land, and fly.

George Osborne claimed that 98% of homeowners in England and Wales would see their stamp duty bills decrease after the changes take effect. Only those who purchase homes worth more than £937,000 will face higher tax rates. "It's time we revamped this poorly designed tax on aspiration," Mr Osborne told MPs. So, how will these changes work? Currently, stamp duty operates throughout the UK and charges progressively higher rates on the entire purchase price. This is why it's often criticized as a "slab tax," with sudden jumps in rates when the price exceeds the next threshold. For instance, someone buying a home for £250,000 would pay £2,500, or 1%, in stamp duty. However, if the price increased by just £1, they would face an additional £5,000 in tax, as they'd pay 3% on the entire purchase price. From December 3rd, the new rates will only apply to the portion of the purchase price within each band, making it more comparable to income tax. This means someone buying a house for £200,000 would pay £1,500 in stamp duty, including 2% on the next £75,000. Previously, they would have paid £2,000 in stamp duty, despite the higher percentage rates appearing in some cases. Although the percentage rates may seem higher, the overall charge will be lower in most cases. On average, a family home buyer in England and Wales will pay £4,500 less in stamp duty under the new system. The new rates will also eliminate the sudden jumps in existing stamp duty thresholds. Until April 1st, 2015, Scotland's stamp duty is being replaced by Land and Buildings Transaction Tax (LBTT). During this period, the new stamp duty rates will apply in Scotland as well. The new system in England and Wales is similar to LBTT, with buyers only paying higher rates on the portion of the property price within that band. Under LBTT, people buying houses priced below £324,280 will pay less than under the previous stamp duty system. Those spending more will be worse off. The government has created a stamp duty calculator to help buyers, but here are some examples: The rates for LBTT in Scotland are lower than the equivalent stamp duty in England and Wales, with Scottish purchasers starting at £135,000. As in England and Wales, richer buyers will lose out, while more modest buyers will benefit. From April 1st, the LBTT rates will be

Abubakar Shekau publicly rejected the Islamic State's (IS) declaration that Abu Musab al-Barnawi was the new leader, accusing al-Barnawi of attempting to stage a coup against him. Boko Haram is fighting to overthrow Nigeria's government and establish an Islamic state in the north. Over the past 18 months, the group has lost most of its previously controlled territory, largely due to the efforts of Nigerian forces and their neighbors. In an August audio message, Shekau claimed to be alive and had not been replaced, contradicting an IS video released in April. In a 10-minute audio message, Shekau distanced Boko Haram from IS, while still referring to Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi as the caliph. However, he also expressed frustration with IS, stating that some within Boko Haram had manipulated his communications with al-Baghdadi to further their own interests. Shekau alleged that he had sent letters to IS leaders, but they failed to act on them, leading him to describe Abu Musab al-Barnawi and his followers as polytheists. This split marks the most serious division within Boko Haram to date. The split highlights deep disagreements within the group, which could lead to clashes between loyal followers of the two leaders. This internal weakness may signal the group's eventual collapse, although some security analysts caution that the internal power struggles could make the group more deadly and unpredictable. Nigeria and regional forces will need to capitalize on this factionalism to gain an advantage. The split is likely to have a significant impact on Boko Haram's operations and could be a turning point in the fight against the insurgents. Shekau took over as the group's leader after the death of its founder, Muhammad Yusuf, in 2009. Under his leadership, Boko Haram became increasingly radical, carried out more killings, and swore allegiance to IS in 2015. In numerous videos, Shekau taunted Nigerian authorities, celebrating the group's violent acts, including the abduction of over 200 Chibok schoolgirls in 2014. Nigeria's army has previously claimed to have killed him, and he has not appeared in a video since Boko Haram's alignment with IS.

St James Theatre in Victoria's Palace Street was constructed on the site of the former Westminster Theatre, which burned down in 2002. The owners are optimistic that the £7m complex will attract new audiences and contribute to the growth of commercial theatre beyond the West End. The inaugural season will feature Bully Boy, a play by comedian Sandi Toksvig. Originally a chapel since 1766, the building was transformed into a cinema in 1924 and later converted into a theatre in 1931. For many years, the building was owned by the Moral Re-Armament movement, which used it to stage shows with moral or spiritual messages. The new complex boasts a 312-seat main house and a studio space that can accommodate up to 150 people, as well as apartments. The theatre will be equipped with multi-media broadcast facilities to broadcast performances on the venue's own BSkyB satellite channel.
Rates have plummeted by 3.5% since June 24, according to Tom McPhail, head of retirement policy at Hargreaves Lansdown. For a 65-year-old with £100,000 in savings, the annual income that could be generated would have been £5,069, but that amount has now dropped to £4,890, reaching a new record low. Before the Brexit vote, experts had predicted this outcome, yet a majority of people over 60 voted to leave the EU. "Annuity rates are vanishing from the market," said Mr McPhail. "Just six months ago, a 60-year-old could secure a better deal than the one being offered to a 65-year-old. Despite rates being at historic lows, there's no guarantee that they'll rise again anytime soon." Annuity rates have been declining for years as life expectancy increases, but the drop has been accelerated by the recent decline in bond yields following the Brexit vote. Mr McPhail advises those considering purchasing an annuity not to wait for future rate increases. "If the question is, 'should I buy an annuity today?', the answer is to not delay simply because today's rates are lower than in the past."

The test's success would be noteworthy since submarine-launched ballistic missiles (SLBMs) are notoriously difficult to detect. This comes as North Korea prepares for a rare and significant party congress next month. The country is prohibited from conducting nuclear tests and activities involving ballistic missile technology under UN sanctions that date back to 2006. According to a South Korean defense ministry spokesman, "North Korea launched a projectile believed to be a submarine-launched ballistic missile around 6:30 pm (0930 GMT) in the East Sea (Sea of Japan) near the north-eastern port of Sinpo." The spokesman also stated that South Korea is closely monitoring the North Korean military and maintaining a full defense posture. Notably, North Korea has not reported the test in its official media. The secretive state has previously claimed to have conducted similar tests, but some question the validity of these claims. The US has disputed photographs purportedly showing a launch in December, suggesting they were manipulated. Others believe the North may have fired missiles from submerged platforms rather than submarines. In a statement, South Korea's Joint Chiefs of Staff said the missile traveled approximately 30km (19 miles), whereas a typical SLBM can travel at least 300km (186 miles). North Korea has conducted four nuclear tests, including the first in October 2006 and the latest in January this year. The UN Security Council responded to the latest test by imposing its strongest sanctions to date on North Korea's nuclear weapons program. Last month, North Korea claimed to have developed nuclear warheads small enough to fit on ballistic missiles, although experts have expressed skepticism about these claims. Analysts believe the North may be preparing for a fifth test as a demonstration of strength ahead of the North Korean Workers' Party Congress, the first since 1980.
At least one person was treated in hospital and several were injured as police used tear gas and rubber bullets to disperse protesters in Siyazan. The demonstrators were angry about the worsening economic conditions caused by a sharp decline in oil prices. Azerbaijan's economy is heavily reliant on oil, with nearly half its GDP in 2014 coming from the oil sector. Oil prices have plummeted by 70% over the past 15 months, currently trading at $31 per barrel on international markets. The manat, Azerbaijan's currency, has also seen a dramatic devaluation. The interior ministry claimed the protests were organized by opposition groups and extremist elements. The government has responded by reducing the price of flour, effective from Friday, according to Reuters. VAT was waived on wheat imports and the sale of bread and flour. The central bank also banned the sale of foreign currency in commercial banks' bureaux de change, in an effort to prop up the faltering manat. Azerbaijan's economy, heavily dependent on oil revenue, has been severely impacted by declining oil prices. Small businesses have suffered greatly in recent years due to alleged bribes demanded by officials. Corruption has been a significant obstacle for businesses in Azerbaijan, according to the International Monetary Fund. The manat was initially nearly equal to the euro but was devalued against the dollar in February 2015. In December 2015, the Central Bank unpegged the manat from the dollar, leading to a sharp price hike that affected low-income families and those with large bank loans. Azerbaijan relies heavily on imports, importing most basic consumer goods, although some agricultural products are produced locally. Despite warnings from international and local analysts about the risks of oil dependency, the government has done little to diversify the economy. In a country with strict media control, critical voices are often met with force. Discontent was relatively low during the oil boom, but the volatile currency has made price increases feel like a universal concern, affecting almost everyone. As more people suffer, it may become increasingly difficult for the government to appease the wider public.

The celebration, which takes place in Edinburgh, Scotland, has collaborated with the Edinburgh International Science Festival, the UK Space Agency, and the European Space Agency. Over 80 countries will be represented at the street party, with revellers from around the globe coming together to celebrate the start of 2016. According to Pete Irvine, director of Edinburgh's Hogmanay, the event is truly global, with participants joining from more than 80 countries worldwide. Irvine also revealed that this year's Hogmanay will feature a special message from Tim Peake, a UK astronaut currently on board the International Space Station. The message will be broadcast just before midnight, and revellers are advised to keep an eye on the event and stage screens at this time. Dr. Simon Gage, director of the Edinburgh International Science Festival, noted that during the few hours the revellers enjoy the Hogmanay street party, the International Space Station will complete three orbits of the Earth at a speed of five miles per second. Gage expressed the hope that viewers may be able to spot the station passing overhead. With Peake on board, 2016 is set to be an exciting year for UK space science, offering many opportunities for inspiration and learning. Gage praised the UK Space Agency and the European Space Agency for their involvement in the event, saying they will bring a bit of science to the celebration.
Ciaran McClean, a Green Party member, claims the confidence and supply agreement breaches the Good Friday Agreement and the Bribery Act. The DUP has agreed to support the minority Conservative government in key votes in exchange for funds for Northern Ireland. A former government lawyer described the bribery claim as "baseless". The government believes the agreement is within the law. Ciaran McClean has launched a crowdfunding campaign to fund a judicial review. His solicitor, David Greene, said an application would be submitted on Monday or Tuesday. In a statement on his crowdfunding page, McClean says the government is "endangering hard-won peace" with its deal. "The Tories are propped up by the DUP to cling to power after the recent election. This is a blatant case of vote-buying, where money is exchanged for votes." McClean is pursuing the legal challenge as an individual, not on behalf of the Green Party. Greene, a senior partner at Edwin Coe solicitors, told the BBC that public outcry has been significant over the Tory-DUP deal. "This isn't about imposing views, but about upholding the rule of law," he said. "It's about a citizen's right to challenge the deal in court and have it meaningfully addressed." Alberto Costa, a former government lawyer and current MP for South Leicestershire, said the investment in Northern Ireland was not a personal incentive and Prime Minister Theresa May had a constitutional duty to form a government. He described the deal as "transparent and lawful" and the bribery claim as "frivolous" and "without merit". The confidence and supply arrangement includes the DUP guaranteeing its 10 MPs will vote with the government on key legislation, while Northern Ireland will receive an additional £1 billion over two years. While some parties in Northern Ireland have welcomed the funding, concerns have been raised that the deal could undermine the peace process and devolution negotiations. Opposition parties, including Labour, have heavily criticized the deal, with Labour describing it as "shabby and reckless". The Welsh First Minister, Carwyn Jones, called it a "straight bribe" and said it "kills the idea of fair funding".

Researchers found that taller individuals have a slightly elevated risk of breast and skin cancer, among other types of cancer. The study discovered that for every additional 10cm (4in) of height when fully grown, the risk of developing cancer increases by 18% in women and 11% in men. However, experts noted that the study did not account for numerous risk factors, and therefore, tall people should not be overly concerned. To reduce the risk of cancer, experts emphasize the importance of several key actions: Previous studies have consistently shown a link between height and an increased risk of developing cancer, but the underlying cause remains unclear. A preliminary report from the Karolinska Institute in Stockholm, presented at the European Society for Paediatric Endocrinology conference, revealed that researchers tracked a large group of Swedish adults for over 50 years. Taller women experienced a 20% greater risk of developing breast cancer, while taller men and women showed a 30% increased risk of skin cancer (or melanoma). These findings are remarkably similar in size to previous studies. Dr Emelie Benyi, the study's lead researcher, stated that the results could help identify risk factors that could lead to the development of treatments. However, she cautioned that "as the cause of cancer is multi-factorial, it is difficult to predict what impact our results have on cancer risk at the individual level." While it is clear that adult height is not a direct cause of cancer, it is believed to be a marker for other factors related to childhood growth. Scientists suggest that taller individuals may have more growth factors, more cells in their bodies due to their size, and a higher food intake, which increases their risk of developing cancer. Prof Dorothy Bennett, head of the molecular cell sciences research centre at St George's, University of London, argued that it is "very plausible" that the risk of cancer in a person should be related to the number of cells in their body. "Bigger people have more cells (not bigger cells)," she explained. "So, melanoma risk, for example, might be expected to increase with surface area (amount of skin), which is related to the square of height." Sarah Williams, health information manager at Cancer Research UK, noted that the study did not account for factors such as smoking or breast screening. She emphasized that "whatever your height, there are lots of things you can do to reduce the risk of cancer - not smoking, cutting down on alcohol, eating healthily, being active, having a healthy
Syrian President Bashar al-Assad has told Egyptian newspaper al-Ahram al-Arabi that dialogue is the only solution to the country's conflict, in a move that has been described as a rare public statement. However, Syrian officials have disputed the claim, saying that the interview was informal. Meanwhile, fighting continues in Aleppo, with the Syrian Observatory for Human Rights reporting clashes between government forces and rebels in the north-eastern Arkoub district. The conflict has claimed at least 225 lives, including 140 civilians, 39 rebels, and 46 security personnel, with a government warplane bombing a petrol station in Raqqa province killing over 30. The president's comments have been seen as a sign that he remains committed to his course of action, despite a period of silence. In the interview, Assad claimed that armed groups were responsible for terrorism and would ultimately be defeated. He also dismissed the idea of foreign intervention, saying that change would not come through the removal of leaders, and that Syria would not follow the path of Libya. Instead, he argued that the overthrow of Arab regimes had not led to the expected outcomes of freedom, democracy, or an end to social injustice, but had instead created chaos. Assad reiterated that dialogue was the only solution, and that violence would not be tolerated. He also criticized the role of Saudi Arabia and Qatar in supporting the opposition, accusing them of using their wealth to try to buy influence. However, Syrian Information Minister Umran al-Zubi later denied that the president had granted an exclusive interview to al-Ahram al-Arabi, saying that it was an informal conversation with nine Egyptian journalists whose comments had been taken out of context.

India currently holds the top spot, but they'll relinquish it to Pakistan if they fail to secure a win in their upcoming final Test in the West Indies. Sri Lanka declared their second innings at 347-8 on day five in Colombo, setting Australia a challenging target of 324 to chase. However, only David Warner managed to resist, scoring 68, as spinner Rangana Herath took a impressive 7-64. A week ago, four teams were in contention for the top spot in the rankings, but England's series draw with Pakistan ended their chances, while Australia's hopes rested on avoiding a whitewash. Now, Pakistan must rely on India failing to triumph in their fourth Test in Trinidad, which begins on Thursday. India leads the series 2-0. Sri Lanka's success has also led to a significant drop in South Africa's ranking, who initially started as the number one team in 2016 but lost to England in January. Rangana Herath achieved the biggest haul for a left-arm bowler in a three-Test series, taking 28 wickets at an average of 12.75. Australia's collapse was devastating, with the team being bowled out for just 44.1 overs against an all-spin attack in the second innings at the spin-friendly Sinhalese Sports Club. Only 17 of the 169.2 overs they faced were bowled by seamers. Shaun Marsh, who scored a century in the first innings, added 77 for the first wicket with Warner, but both fell to off-spinner Dilruwan Perera. The rest of the Australia team struggled to cope with Herath, who returned match figures of 13-145. Herath was not only named man of the match but also chosen as man of the series as Sri Lanka regained the Warne-Muralitharan Trophy. Australia have not lost a home Test series since December 2012, but they have struggled in foreign lands. This was their third successive series whitewash in Asia, following defeats to India in 2013 and Pakistan in 2014. Their only series wins away from home in that time were in the West Indies in 2015 and New Zealand in 2016.
World number one Victoria Azarenka edged past Serena Williams 3-6 6-4 6-2 in just over two hours to reach the fourth round of the French Open. The intense match took a toll on both players, particularly at the end of the second set when Azarenka and Williams got into a heated exchange over a disputed line call. "I was really down and out, but I managed to refocus and want to win that set," said Williams. "We've talked about this before and promised to leave everything on the court and be cool when we're not." Williams also addressed the argument with Azarenka, saying they had previously made that promise. "I love her, she's a great person and we get along really well," Williams added. Azarenka will face fellow American Sloane Stephens in the last 16. Azarenka is seeking a third French Open title and 20th Grand Slam win, while Williams is trying to win a third French Open and 20th Grand Slam title. It's been two years since Azarenka's best run in Paris, when she reached the semi-finals before injury derailed her 2014 campaign. Azarenka had shown earlier this month that she was back in form with a win over Williams in Madrid, but ultimately blew her chance after earning three match points. The Belarusian was in control at 4-2 up in the second set, but let the lead slip as Williams mounted a comeback. Azarenka saved three set points but reacted angrily when her shot was incorrectly called long, leading to a replay of the point. Williams insisted she was distracted by the call, but Azarenka angrily waved her down and lost the point. The Belarusian took a bathroom break to cool down ahead of the final set, but came out firing again. Williams, meanwhile, was pumped up and eventually sealed victory with a brilliant forehand winner. After the match, Azarenka called for a review system to deal with disputed points. "I'm not making excuses, but without a review system, it causes problems," she said. Azarenka also downplayed the row with Williams, saying they didn't have time to clear the air, but had exchanged gifts - including a pair of Azarenka's shorts, which Williams had liked.

The crash occurred on the southbound carriageway of the M1 near junction 14 at 03:15 BST on Saturday. Wagstaff has been charged with eight counts of causing death by dangerous driving and four counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving and is due to appear at Milton Keynes Magistrates' Court on 11 September. Six men and two women died in the collision, which happened shortly before 03:15 BST on the motorway between junctions 15 and 14. He was taking a group of people from India to London, from where they were due to start a tour of Europe. A widespread malware attack affected 51 US franchises across 24 states, exposing sensitive information including clients' names, addresses, email addresses, and payment card details. The company claimed the malware had been eradicated, and its services were now secure. However, the breach was compounded when a large US hospital chain revealed it had also fallen victim to the malware. Approximately 4.5 million healthcare patients associated with Community Health Systems facilities had their private information stolen. In a separate incident, the US grocery chain SuperValu reported a breach, further highlighting the scale of the issue. Founded in 1907 as a messenger company, UPS now boasts over 4,450 franchised locations across the US. Each franchise is independently owned and responsible for installing its own network. The breaches, which were only discovered due to a notification from the US government, occurred between January and August. While UPS had not received reports of the stolen data being misused, customers were advised to monitor their account activity for signs of intrusion. "We urge you to remain vigilant by reviewing your account statements and monitoring your free credit reports," said Tim Davis, president of The UPS Store. He added: "Our customers can be assured that we have identified and fully contained the incident." The company has initiated an internal review to investigate the breach.
Andrey Melnichenko's luxury yacht, A, has been stuck in Gibraltar, a British territory, since Wednesday, with his spokesman expressing confidence that the order to release the vessel will be lifted soon. The 143m (469ft) long yacht, built by Nobiskrug, left the Kiel shipyard in northern Germany two weeks ago. Constructed with a gross tonnage of 12,600, it boasts a reported cost of at least €400m and features an underwater observation pod, hybrid diesel-electric propulsion, and state-of-the-art navigation systems. Designed by Philippe Starck, the vessel is registered to Valla Yachts Ltd, a Bermuda company. A top Gibraltar court official confirmed that the yacht is under arrest and is currently moored in British Gibraltar Territorial Waters. Melnichenko's spokesman described the situation as a "technical problem" and stated that the yacht will be handed over to the owner's project team in the coming days, resolving the issue. As a wealthy industrialist with a $13.2bn fortune, Melnichenko also owns another superyacht, Motor Yacht A, built by Blohm & Voss shipyard and launched in 2008. It is 119m long, smaller than Sailing Yacht A, and was also designed by Philippe Starck. The Gibraltar Port Authority explains that ship arrests occur when banks and creditors seek recompense from shipowners who are unable to pay debts, with the arrested vessels typically sold at a sealed-bids auction within six to eight weeks after the claim is proven and judgment is given. The port authority also provides crew members with basic necessities, including food, water, and shore leave, while ensuring the vessel's security.

Currently, students in Northern Ireland receive their maintenance loan and grant payments three times a year, typically at the start of each academic term. However, student leaders argue that switching to monthly payments would make it easier for students to manage their finances. DEL estimates that implementing this change would cost between £250,000 and £350,000. Many students in Northern Ireland rely on loans to cover living expenses, but some also receive additional support grants. Unlike loans, grants are means-tested and do not need to be repaid. In the 2013-14 academic year, nearly 42,000 students in Northern Ireland borrowed a total of £131 million in maintenance loans. Meanwhile, around 25,000 students received a support grant, which cost DEL over £68 million. The amount of grant a student receives is determined by their household income. Those with an income of £19,203 or less are eligible for the maximum grant of £3,475 per year. However, students from households with an income between £19,203 and £41,605 receive a partial grant. In the 2013-14 academic year, 38% of students in Northern Ireland received the full grant, while 22% received a partial grant. Like maintenance loans, grants are paid in September, January, and April. However, Scotland already operates a monthly payment system, where students receive a larger initial payment in September and smaller monthly payments from October to June. NUS-USI President Fergal McFerran believes that monthly payments would be more beneficial for students. "Monthly support payments, with a larger September payment, could help students pay for essential expenses such as accommodation deposits, household equipment, or study materials at the start of the academic year," he said. However, there is no indication that the level of maintenance loan or support grant is set to increase. DEL Minister Stephen Farry launched the consultation, stating that while there is limited flexibility in the current financial context, there is scope to consider the profiling of support. The consultation closes on September 27. Tuition fee loans, currently £3,805 per year for Northern Irish students studying at Queen's or Ulster University, are paid directly to the university and would not be affected by any changes.

A government review found that the producers were aware of Mallah's criminal convictions but not his more recent derogatory social media posts when he was selected to ask a question.

South Wales Fire and Rescue reported a significant increase in incidents involving verbal and physical abuse against its firefighters between April 2016 and March 2017, with 31 incidents compared to just 12 the previous year. The number of attacks on firefighters was highest in Blaenau Gwent (13%), Caerphilly county (16%), Cardiff (19%), and Newport (23%). In response to the issue, South Wales Fire and Rescue is now working closely with Crimestoppers and the Gwent Police and Crime Commissioner to reduce attacks and bring offenders to justice. Matthew Jones, head of the fire crime unit in south Wales, emphasized the importance of protecting firefighters, saying, "Firefighters risk their lives to save lives and protect property." He described the lack of respect shown to firefighters as "appalling." North Wales Fire and Rescue also reported a low number of attacks on its firefighters, despite attending a large number of calls each year. However, the service maintains a zero-tolerance approach to abuse and will report and deal with any offenders. Similarly, Mid and West Wales Fire and Rescue has a zero-tolerance policy towards attacks on its firefighters, with those responsible facing police action.

Germany has fully opened its labor market to workers from Poland and seven other countries that joined the EU in 2004, a decision that marks a significant shift after holding out for years. The country had previously resisted allowing workers from former communist countries to enter its economy. To reassure German workers, ministers have promised to protect their jobs from cheaper labor. According to estimates, around 100,000 people will migrate to Germany each year. In response, Labor Minister Ursula von der Leyen told the Bild newspaper that stricter checks would be put in place on industries such as construction and catering, where German unions are concerned that pay will be undercut by immigrants. She promised that inspectors would ensure minimum wages were paid and that newcomers would be registered. Finance Minister Wolfgang Schaeuble announced that his ministry would deploy an additional 150 officials to monitor industries and detect any illegal workers. In addition to Poland, workers from the Czech Republic, Hungary, Slovenia, Slovakia, Estonia, Lithuania, and Latvia will also be eligible to join Germany's labor market. Ministers chose to make their promises in popular newspapers, possibly hinting at the unease among ordinary Germans, despite employers believing that the economy needs outsiders to fill skilled positions. However, what employers don't openly say is that they also want unskilled workers at lower rates of pay.

British Paralympic athlete Jonnie Weir was left feeling let down after a row with his coach, Louise Banks, at the Rio Paralympics. Weir, 37, didn't win a medal at the Games, his fifth, and said he was "gobsmacked" by the way he was treated. Banks allegedly told him he was a "disgrace to the country", which Weir took as a personal attack. Weir, who will compete in the London Marathon next April, said Banks didn't even check in on him after his disappointing performance. "Not once did she come and see if I was okay that week," Weir said, adding that he felt it was poor given his extensive medal haul. Weir was unable to confirm reports that Banks had thrown his racing wheelchair during a row in Brazil, saying he hadn't seen it himself and had only heard it from others. Banks joined British Athletics as the lead wheelchair racing coach in 2012 and previously worked with the Australian Paralympic Committee. The UK government has secured a 25-year lease for 265,976 sq ft of space, marking the largest office deal ever made in Wales since 2020. The former Cardiff bus station site, which began construction in 2014, is set to become a major hub. BBC Wales is scheduled to relocate from 2019, while HM Revenue and Customs will also move from its current Cardiff base in Llanishen. The site, situated next to Cardiff Central railway station, is already home to several businesses, including Hugh James solicitors and Hodge Bank. It's also set to become one of 13 regional tax office centres in the UK. Other government departments, such as the Office for the Secretary of State for Wales, will also relocate to the site. Cardiff council leader Huw Thomas described the move as an essential part of "the jigsaw" of the city's development plans, which include a new bus interchange and the redevelopment of the nearby Brains Brewery site. He stated that Cardiff is competing with major UK cities to attract jobs and investment. A UK government spokesperson said the deal is part of an effort to significantly reduce the number of government buildings from 800 to 200 by 2022. Secretary of State for Wales Alun Cairns said the move demonstrates the government's commitment to being visible, accessible, and improving people's lives. HMRC chief executive Jon Thompson announced that the agency will collaborate with Cardiff council to redevelop its current base at Ty Glas, which houses 2,800 staff, following the relocation.
The claim that at least 6,700 mental health nurses and doctors have been cut from the NHS in England since 2010 appears to be accurate. However, Labour's former shadow minister for mental health, Luciana Berger, tweeted that the actual number of doctors and nurses working on mental health had decreased by 6,700. An answer from Health Minister Philip Dunne to Ms Berger's parliamentary question revealed that the number of nurses working in mental health in England dropped from 45,384 in 2010 to 38,774 by July 2016, resulting in a 6,610-person decrease. For doctors, the monthly NHS England workforce statistics showed a decrease from 8,699 in May 2010 to 8,676 in January 2017, a drop of 23. Nevertheless, this figure may not account for all doctors working in mental health.
Stars Jim Parsons, Kaley Cuoco, Kunal Nayyar, and Simon Helberg did not report to work on the first day of production for The Big Bang Theory. The show's production has been delayed due to ongoing contract negotiations with Warner Bros. Television (WBTV). The sitcom, which revolves around a group of high-functioning science "geeks," is the most-watched TV show in the US, averaging 23.1 million viewers in the 2013-14 season. In an earlier decision, WBTV extended the show for three more series, ensuring it will air until at least 2017. In a statement, WBTV said the production has been postponed "due to ongoing contract negotiations." This unexpected move caught the studio off guard, as it had previously expected the eighth series to proceed without any issues. The delay affected a table read, where the cast would have reviewed the script for the comeback episode. According to The Hollywood Reporter, the main cast members, including Parsons, Galecki, and Cuoco, currently earn $325,000 (approximately £190,000) per episode and are seeking up to $1 million (approximately £584,000) per show. Such a deal would make them the highest-paid actors on US television, surpassing Ashton Kutcher and Jon Cryer from Two and a Half Men, who are currently the highest-paid TV actors. Both shows were created by Chuck Lorre, who recently stated that he did not expect the contract dispute to be overly disruptive. "There are people at Warner Bros. Television and people representing the actors who have done this before," he said. "This will work itself out. I think it's great; I want them all to be crazy wealthy because nobody deserves it more than this cast. It'll work out." However, salary negotiations on top-rated comedies have previously turned contentious. In 2012, several actors on Modern Family staged a walk-out and took legal action to have their contracts declared void in a dispute over pay. After Fox relented and doubled their salaries, the dispute was resolved.

Bignot, who joined Solihull in 2011, successfully led the Moors to promotion in April. A 42-year-old Birmingham native and former QPR defender, Bignot has worked without a contract for the past five years at Solihull. However, in September, he expressed his readiness to sign his first deal at Damson Park. Solihull, who sit 15th in the National League table, released a statement saying: "The board wishes to make it clear that, while we wouldn't want to hinder an individual's ambitions, we're keen to retain Bignot's services to build on the progress he's made during his five-year tenure.
