Michel Dussuyer's team will face Ghana on Sunday after a draw at a Malabo hotel. Guinea and Mali had finished level in Group D after drawing all three pool matches 1-1, a result that has not been seen since 1988 when Algeria benefited from Ivory Coast's loss. This time, Ivory Coast topped the group after beating Cameroon 1-0, the only match that wasn't a 1-1 draw. Representatives from Guinea and Mali took part in the draw, which involved picking balls from a bowl. Mali's football association president Boubacar Diarra was the first to pull out the ball, placing his team third in the group, leaving him in tears. Amara Dabo, Guinea's financial director, then drew the ball that secured his side's second place. Dabo expressed his gratitude, saying, "The god of football fortune has smiled upon Guinea after this long and hard journey." Former Mali international and Tottenham and West Ham striker Frederic Kanoute, 37, expressed his disappointment at the use of draws to decide who would advance, tweeting, "This is no longer sport. But I have courage for our Guinean brothers." Both Guinea coach Dussuyer and Mali boss Henri Kasperczak had previously said that using draws to separate teams was unfair. Dussuyer stated, "Mali does not deserve to be eliminated in this manner, just as we do not." Kasperczak added, "We need to find a way to solve the problems of qualification without relying on draws." The Confederation of African Football (Caf) acknowledged that alternative systems would be considered in the future. Caf media director Junior Binyam said, "We have to stand by the regulation, but also think about how to improve it. It's not fair to have a team eliminated in this way. This decision is the worst - to have to make it this way - because everyone expected it to be decided on the field." In Euro 2008, Uefa had ruled that Turkey and the Czech Republic would have been separated by a penalty shootout if they had drawn, but Turkey won 3-2.
Researchers at Imperial College London analyzed national health survey data spanning from 1994 to 2011. According to a study published in The Lancet, the results showed a significant increase in the number of people receiving effective treatment for high blood pressure over the past two decades. The team concluded that if the current trend of improvement continues, it could lead to even more lives being saved. Their findings revealed that the proportion of people with high blood pressure who were receiving treatment nearly doubled from 32% in 1994 to 58% in 2011. Meanwhile, the percentage of people whose blood pressure returned to normal levels tripled from 11% to 37%. "If we had treated people in 1994 to the same standard as we do today, it's estimated that we could have prevented up to 100,000 major health events – including heart attacks, strokes, and deaths," said Prof Neil Poulter, one of the researchers, in an interview with the BBC. He also emphasized that the progress is "extremely encouraging" and "has saved a huge number of lives." The study projects that by 2022, 80% of patients will be able to control their blood pressure if the current rate of improvement continues. Experts attribute the improvement to better medications, increased awareness, and financial incentives for doctors. Despite this progress, heart disease remains the UK's leading cause of death, accounting for approximately 82,000 deaths each year.
The coffin of King Richard III was placed in a vault at Leicester Cathedral during a reinterment ceremony on Thursday, and the tomb was unveiled the following day. However, some service booklets have now been offered for sale on eBay for as much as £200. Cathedral officials have made available a set of all three booklets for £12.50, suggesting that fans could purchase them as a memento. The king's skeleton was discovered in a Leicester car park in August 2012, but despite a court challenge, it was reburied in the cathedral during a series of events. There were three main services held in the cathedral - one to receive the coffin, one for the reinterment, and one to unveil the sealed tomb. The reinterment, attended by the Countess of Wessex and actor Benedict Cumberbatch, took place on Thursday. The following day, the Diocese of Leicester warned on social media against purchasing Orders of Service, with the Dean, David Monteith, supporting this stance. He explained that dealing with death requires acknowledging the darker aspects of individuals, adding that the cathedral had initially printed more booklets than needed, but all three are now available for £12.50, with no one being exploited - the costs will cover the cathedral's expenses and a small portion will go towards its upkeep.
In Cuba, private ownership of broadcast media is not permitted, and the government controls all major outlets. According to Reporters Without Borders, Cuba is the only country in the Americas that does not allow a non-state, independent press. The official media is designed to disseminate propaganda for the government, with the state maintaining a strong grip on the media. Despite this, the US has been attempting to reach Cuban audiences through various channels. Washington-backed Radio-TV Marti claims to provide "balanced, uncensored" news to Cubans, although its independence has been questioned. The number of internet users in Cuba was 3.1 million by 2014, according to Internetlivestats.com. However, US-based NGO Freedom House notes that access to the internet is tightly controlled, making it difficult for most Cubans to connect from their homes. Bloggers who support dissidents have faced harassment and detention. As a result, Cuba is one of Reporters Without Borders' "Enemies of the Internet". Furthermore, the authorities have been unable to attribute internet connection problems to the US embargo, as a submarine cable linking Cuba to Venezuela has been operational since its activation.
Tragedy struck when 20-year-old Sam Pickering lost control of his Yamaha motorbike in Oughtibridge on Sunday. He was on his way to participate in the annual bikers' charity egg run for Sheffield Children's Hospital, an event he had attended for 15 years, when the accident occurred. The event, organised by the Three Shires Honda Owners Club, has raised over £40,000 for the charity since its inception. In a poignant tribute, fellow bikers revved their engines in Sam's honour at the event. Sam's mother described him as a "beautiful son" and paid tribute to his "huge personality" and "smile that lit up the room." She also remembered him as a person who "only ever wanted to do good in life." A minute's silence followed by 30 seconds of revving was held at the start of the event, after organisers became aware of the fatal crash. Mr Pickering's father, Stephen, also paid tribute to his son, describing him as "my son and friend" who will be deeply missed. The incident is currently under investigation, with officers urging anyone with information to come forward.
The West of England Local Enterprise Partnership has agreed to contribute £53m from the City Deal to support the development of a new arena in Bristol. Bristol City Council has committed to funding the remaining £38m, which will be recouped from operator rental and other sources. The 12,000-capacity venue will be built in the heart of the Temple Quarter Enterprise Zone. A final decision on the project is set to be made at the city council's budget meeting on 18 February. The Mayor of Bristol, George Ferguson, welcomed the LEP's decision, saying it would give a significant boost to the local economy. The LEP's Colin Skellett highlighted the economic benefits of the arena, citing its potential to create jobs and drive growth in the region. The fund is designed to unlock opportunities for growth in the west of England, and the Bristol Arena is one of several projects that will achieve this. The city council will begin searching for an independent company to operate the arena next month. This project is part of Bristol's broader plan to regenerate 70 hectares of land surrounding Bristol Temple Meads station. The council aims to complete the arena by June 2017. The City Deal scheme grants local authorities more control over their area, allowing them to make decisions on economic growth and public spending.

The Kremlin had denied any involvement, but Vovan and his accomplice, Alexei Stolyarov, or "Lexus," admitted to the hoax. Vovan had used his friend's excellent English to impersonate the presidential press secretary, Dmitry Peskov, and pass himself off as the Russian leader. Elton John was in a London recording studio at the time of the call and was reportedly delighted to be speaking with Putin, saying that the conversation made his day and that it was the most wonderful of his life. A part of the recording would be aired on a Russian state TV show on Wednesday. The prank follows an interview Sir Elton gave to the BBC, in which he expressed a willingness to meet with Putin, despite knowing that the Russian leader might mock him behind his back.

Leicestershire Police confirmed that the perpetrators did not gain access to the building, but caused damage to the exterior. Sandra Weir, 41, must serve at least 21 years in prison before she can be considered for parole. Weir brutally attacked 82-year-old Mary Logie, also known as Rae, with a rolling pin at her Leven home on January 5, 2016. Logie suffered multiple skull fractures and 31 head and neck injuries. As she tried to defend herself, Logie sustained defensive wounds. Weir left her victim seriously injured but alive for hours before delivering the fatal blows, prosecutors believe. The killing occurred after Weir had been stealing significant sums of cash from Logie over time to fund her own drug habit. Weir was convicted of murder by a jury at the High Court in Edinburgh last month. A family member, believed to be Logie's son Ronald, said: "Justice was served, but we would have wanted more." Judge Michael O'Grady described the manner of Logie's death as "breathtakingly wicked." Passing sentence, he said that nothing could diminish the "callous, cruel, and utterly heartless" nature of the crime. He noted that Weir had been a troubled young woman and may have had underlying issues. Judge O'Grady said: "You have clearly been a troubled young woman, and some of your problems may not be of your own making." He did not want to repeat what he said during Weir's conviction, but emphasized that the crime's nature remains unchanged. In his view, nothing can diminish the callous and cruel nature of the crime. The crime is beyond any meaningful mitigation. Regular churchgoer Mary Logie was found battered to death in the living room of her first-floor flat in Green Gates. She had planned to visit a friend in the hospital but never showed up. Logie spent Christmas with one of her sons in England not long before her death. Weir had become close to Logie and acted as her unofficial carer, but she had been a drug addict since her 20s and had accumulated significant debts. Weir regularly exploited Logie for financial gain. Detective Chief Inspector Keith Hardie described Weir as a "callous and uncaring individual" who demonstrated a complete disregard for Logie's wellbeing. He said: "The level of violence inflicted upon Mary, coupled with the prolonged period of bullying and intimidation, demonstrated Weir's complete disregard for her victim." This was a brutal and horrific attack committed by a despicable individual.
The party has heavily criticized Labour for a 2011 promise of annual health checks by GPs for people over 50, calling it a "massive climb-down". Tory AM Darren Millar described the Add To Your Life self-assessment scheme as a "complete folly", urging it to be scrapped. In response, the Welsh Government stated that lessons learned from the program will be incorporated into a review of health advice. The Add To Your Life survey, launched in April 2014, aimed to provide an overall picture of people's health and well-being, along with suggestions for better lifestyle choices. However, the Tories claimed the program had a dismal take-up rate, with only 12,939 out of 1,177,000 eligible over-50s in Wales participating in the 23 months following its launch. Mr Millar attributed this to the digital skills gap faced by many older people, while Angela Burns, the current health spokesperson for the Welsh Conservatives, pointed to poor phone and internet connections in rural areas. "People over 50 are vulnerable to various health concerns, including cancer, high blood pressure, and diabetes," she said. "The scheme has been an abject failure, and future funding should be redirected towards GP recruitment and retention initiatives." The Welsh Government defended the scheme as an "innovative approach", citing relatively low running costs of £70,000 in 2015-16. They also highlighted the involvement of charity and voluntary organizations in using the service as a basis for group discussions on health and lifestyle. According to the Government, evidence suggests that providing tailored health information to the public is more effective than other approaches. Public Health Wales is currently reviewing its approach to providing information to the public, with lessons learned from Add To Your Life playing a key role in this review.

In a report focusing on women in England and Wales, researchers found that about one in 14 women born in 1995 had their first child before turning 20. The Family Planning Association hailed this decline as a sign that teenagers are developing the skills to make informed choices about their reproductive health. However, others argued that the decrease could be attributed to increased pressure to use contraceptives. Data from the Office for National Statistics (ONS) shows a gradual decrease in teenage motherhood rates, which have fallen to a level comparable to that of women born in the 1920s. The 7% figure represents a significant drop from the peak of one in five (20%) for women born in 1952. This news follows a decline in teenage pregnancies, which the ONS reported earlier this year as the lowest level since records began. While the ONS measures this data differently, it does show a decrease in family size among women born in 1970, with an average of 1.91 children by age 45, compared to 2.24 children for women born in 1943 by the same age. However, the ONS cautions that this decline may not necessarily indicate fewer children being born, but rather a delay in childbirth. Paul Casey from the Family Planning Association believes the figures indicate that teenagers have easier access to contraception and support services, and the confidence to use them. He praised the 10-year Teenage Pregnancy Strategy, saying it has helped bring about this change and urged caution against losing momentum. However, Dr. Ellie Lee from the University of Kent expressed concerns that the decline may not be entirely positive. She argued that teenage births were already at historically low levels and that the trajectory was in a downward direction before the strategy was implemented. Lee also questioned the focus on long-term contraceptives, saying that teenagers should have the freedom to make their own choices about their reproductive health. She emphasized that negative attitudes towards teenage mothers are unwarranted and that they are not inherently worse than other mothers.
The Metropolitan Police Service is currently dealing with up to 18 ongoing investigations into historical allegations that span several decades. However, Deputy Assistant Commissioner Steve Rodhouse expressed concern about the lack of victims coming forward to share their experiences. The appeal for witnesses comes at a time when the Met is facing criticism over alleged cover-ups of high-profile sex offenders. The Independent Police Complaints Commission is investigating 14 separate referrals, all of which date back to the 1970s and 1980s. In a statement to BBC Radio 4's Today programme, Rodhouse acknowledged that progress is being made through wider inquiries, but admitted that police are struggling to find victims. He urged anyone who has been a victim of non-recent sexual abuse to come forward. Despite the IPCC's investigation into claims of a long-running cover-up, Rodhouse assured the public that the Met has a good track record of cooperating with internal investigations. He pointed out that it was the Met itself that referred the case to the IPCC, and that it will continue to follow the evidence wherever it leads, without fear or favour. The IPCC has described the allegations as "high-level corruption of the most serious nature", involving suppression of evidence, hindering investigations, and covering up offences due to the involvement of politicians and police officers. One of the ongoing investigations, Operation Midland, was launched in November and is examining claims of widespread abuse by powerful men in politics, the military, and law enforcement agencies in the 1970s and 1980s. The investigation has focused on the Dolphin Square estate in Pimlico, near the Houses of Parliament, and is also looking into allegations of three boys being murdered. Rodhouse revealed that the Met has worked with one young man for some time, who he believes is a credible witness. However, he declined to comment on whether any further witnesses had come forward, saying that the cases can only progress if people come forward with confidence. He also emphasized that the Met thoroughly tests all evidence and accounts, and ultimately leaves it up to the courts to determine the credibility of witnesses.
A damning report has concluded that a "deadly combination" of mistakes led to the deaths of 11 babies and one mother over nearly a decade at Furness General Hospital. The UK's Health Secretary, Jeremy Hunt, has called for a thorough examination of the role of the Nursing and Midwifery Council (NMC) in light of the tragedy. The Professional Standards Authority (PSA) has been tasked with conducting the review, following the preventable deaths that occurred between 2004 and 2013 at the hospital, which is part of the Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust. An investigation later revealed that the maternity unit at Furness General had been severely dysfunctional, with substandard care provided by staff lacking essential skills and knowledge. Poor working relationships between doctors and midwives were also uncovered, with midwives referring to themselves as "the musketeers" in their pursuit of normal childbirth, regardless of the risks. Mr Hunt has already suggested that the NMC should no longer be responsible for supervising midwives in the UK, proposing a new model similar to that used for other healthcare professionals. The PSA review will be carried out as soon as possible, according to Mr Hunt. He added: "Given the NMC's crucial role in ensuring high standards of care in nursing, midwifery, and health visiting, this review will provide the public and the NMC itself with independent assurance that all lessons have been learned from the Morecambe Bay tragedy." The NMC's chief executive, Jackie Smith, welcomed the PSA's contribution to identifying areas where the NMC could improve its handling of similar cases in the future. While acknowledging that the NMC cannot alter the past, she emphasized the need to learn from its mistakes and move forward with improved procedures. In 2014, the NMC was criticized by the PSA over its handling of disciplinary cases related to the Mid-Staffordshire NHS Trust scandal.

From the White House, President Obama made a statement saying the pipeline would not align with the US's national interests. The construction of the pipeline has been highly contentious for seven years, with environmentalists arguing that it would cause irreparable harm. However, the president claimed that the pipeline had taken an overly prominent role in the climate change debate. The proposed pipeline, spanning 1,179 miles from Alberta, Canada, to Steele City, Nebraska, would transport 800,000 barrels of oil daily. But the president stated that it would not have a significant long-term impact on the economy. Republican presidential candidates strongly criticized the decision, with Jeb Bush labeling it an attack on the US economy. In February, the Republican-led Congress voted to initiate construction, but President Obama vetoed the bill, awaiting environmental reports. It took more than seven years, but President Obama has finally made his stance clear. The timing may have been influenced by the election of a Liberal government in Canada, the recent decline in oil prices, or the announcement that the US unemployment rate has dropped to 5%, the lowest of his presidency. The president decided to end the pipeline now, a move that will be welcomed by environmentalists and criticized by Republicans on the campaign trail. This decision puts Democratic presidential frontrunner Hillary Clinton in a favorable position, as she had recently expressed opposition to the pipeline. The decision comes ahead of the Paris climate conference, where world leaders will meet to finalize a major global climate agreement. The agreement, which could be reached at the UN Climate Change Conference or COP 21, is a significant part of the president's legacy. While the pipeline is currently dead, the controversy surrounding it will not end anytime soon. Proponents will likely challenge the decision in court, and if a Republican is elected president next year, construction could still be approved. The pipeline also generated controversy outside of Washington, straining relations between the former Canadian Prime Minister Stephen Harper and the US president. President Obama stated that he had spoken to newly-elected Canadian Prime Minister Justin Trudeau and they agreed that addressing climate change was more important than the pipeline issue.

The Airports Commission's consultation on the matter closes on Friday, with a recommendation to the government on expansion plans expected soon. Following the Supreme Court's deadline for the government to submit new air pollution reduction plans, the commission ordered a further air quality assessment. The commission is set to recommend one of the three shortlisted schemes - two at Heathrow and one at Gatwick - for expansion. However, local councils in the Heathrow area and the Gatwick Area Conservation Campaign have argued that the commission has underestimated the environmental impact of airport expansion. Keith Taylor stated: "Building any major new runways would be catastrophic for air quality, making it impossible to meet our climate change targets and leading to unprecedented noise levels." He said, "While Heathrow currently breaches air quality limits, it doesn't make sense to assume that a third runway will solve the issue. Northern Ireland's nightlife took a hit after the government introduced a new closing time, forcing clubs to shut their doors by 2am. The change came after a 20-year-old died from drowning after a night out drinking £1 vodka shots. Music promoter Lyndon Stephens said the shift has caused confusion, particularly among tourists who often go out at 1am. Stephens explained that there's a difference between alcohol and entertainment licenses, with alcohol licenses ending at 1am, but entertainment licenses allowed events to run until 3am. However, the new 2am closure now applies to all events. Stephens expressed concerns that the earlier closure would harm tourism, as some European visitors were accustomed to only going out at 1am. Colin Neill, of industry body Pubs of Ulster, said the 2am closure was a voluntary measure agreed upon by his members. The decision followed controversy over irresponsible promotions and allegations of illegal trading. Neill stated that the industry was taking steps to self-regulate, and the new time was a sign that people were ready to leave by 2am. Neill emphasized the need for modernized licensing laws, stating that Northern Ireland's current laws were outdated. He suggested a model where some clubs could stay open slightly later, rather than pursuing 24-hour licensing. Last month, nightclub owners met with Environment Minister Alex Attwood to discuss irresponsible drinks promotions. Attwood plans to make it tougher for pubs and clubs to obtain and retain entertainment licenses, following the death of 20-year-old Joby Murphy, who drowned in the River Lagan after a night of drinking £1 vodka shots. Murphy's father had previously criticized cheap drink deals.

Following a £24.3bn offer from Japanese firm Softbank for chip designer Arm Holdings, concerns were raised that British companies are now vulnerable to takeovers. A Vocalink spokesperson claimed the deal has been in the works for eight months and denies it's driven by the decline in sterling's value. The Payment Systems Regulator had previously recommended that the banks sell their stakes in Vocalink to boost competition and innovation. Hammond stated that this would benefit challenger banks, fintechs, consumers, and small businesses. Mastercard will acquire 92.4% of Vocalink and the remaining stake will be held by the banks for at least three years. Mastercard will pay £700m, plus an additional £169m based on performance targets. The targets were not disclosed, but Vocalink's spokesperson confirmed discussions with the Treasury, Bank of England, and PSR.

Regardless of the method, these claims will likely be misleading. Even if we try to understand the value of a large sum in Euros, such as "X billion Euros," when spread across a population of 503 million people living on 4 million square kilometers of land, it's difficult to grasp the true impact on each of the 28 countries. As a result, we'll have to rely on our instincts. Amtrak CEO Joe Boardman vowed to restore limited service to New York to Philadelphia by Monday, with the possibility of full service on Tuesday. He also pledged to install Positive Train Control, a system that may have prevented the accident, in the entire Northeast Corridor by the end of the year. Amtrak has been working on the system since 2008, and Boardman is confident it will be the only railroad in the Western Hemisphere to implement it.
A jury had previously cleared Harbinder Khatkar of raping a woman in 2011. Just six weeks later, he attacked three women on the streets of Derby and invaded the homes of three other women on February 1. The 37-year-old was retried and found guilty of the 2011 attack, thanks to double jeopardy laws. This was the third time Khatkar had faced trial over the same attack, as jurors had been unable to reach a verdict in his previous trials. One of Khatkar's victims on February 1 was repeatedly raped in her own home for several hours, and he also threatened to kill her children. Judge John Burgess described Khatkar as a "highly manipulative man with no regard for others". "The damage he has done to these women is appalling, particularly to the one woman who had to give evidence three times since 2011," he said. "During the trial, Khatkar demonstrated contempt for the court, the prosecutor, and anyone else in his way. If he can behave like this in court with everyone watching, what must he be like with a vulnerable woman alone in her own home?" Khatkar, from Sinfin in Derby, was found guilty of 18 offences, including five rapes, on Wednesday. He will serve a minimum of 14 years before being considered for parole. Judge Burgess said: "I've never been more convinced that there's no other option than life in prison. "There's a real danger to women if he's released - he'll do this again. This is an exceptional case, especially since he invaded the homes of four women and attacked them." Khatkar's crimes included rape, robbery, causing actual bodily harm, assault, sexual assault, and trespass with the intent to commit a sexual offence. One victim told the court that her life had been "completely devastated" by what Khatkar had done to her in her own home. The Crown Prosecution Service successfully overturned the verdict at the Court of Appeal, paving the way for Khatkar's retrial under the amended double jeopardy law, which came into effect in 2005.

A dazzling mosaic of Ulster life is revealed in unseen archive footage, showcasing the region's history from Winston Churchill's 1912 visit to Belfast to a 1963 Viking invasion re-enactment in a County Down fishing town. Hundreds of unique films have been released online for free as part of the British Film Institute's From Britain On Film project. The archive contains footage of royal visits, Lord Mayor's shows, and Churchill's address to nationalists in Celtic Park, where he supported Home Rule. The collection also features travelogues that promoted Northern Ireland to tourists in Great Britain and the US, boosting tourism before the Troubles. Sport is documented in films of the 1930 Ulster Tourist Trophy, which drew thousands of spectators and made headlines in the motorsport world. However, the real gems are found in footage of ordinary people achieving extraordinary things. Films of people working in spinning mills and constructing the M1 in Belfast showcase Northern Ireland's thriving industry. The 1959 World Ploughing Championships in Armoy, County Antrim, are captured on camera, with a local man, Lawrence McMillan, winning the Golden Plough. A 1938 recording of the Royal Victoria Hospital in Belfast provides a rare glimpse into the casualty department and operating theatres, 10 years before the National Health Service was founded. This footage also highlights aspects of healthcare that remain unchanged, including an appeal to reduce waiting lists. Francis Jones, of Northern Ireland Screen, gathered much of the footage, stating that the project's aim is to preserve delicate film that might otherwise be lost. He notes that film has a unique nostalgic value, striking a chord with people who see it, as it offers a visceral experience that reading or history books cannot match. The full From Britain On Film archive is available to view on the BFI Player.

The UK's mobile companies have been ordered to improve their coverage, with a new rule requiring each to provide voice coverage to at least 90% of the country by the end of 2017. While they already meet the 90% target for 3G coverage of UK premises, many users still experience dropped calls when they're outside. In December, Ofcom published a report detailing the 2G coverage of three of the four operators, showing the extent to which they can let people make and receive calls and texts. The Culture Secretary, Sajid Javid, said that the watchdog couldn't provide a figure for Three's 3G and 4G coverage, but welcomed the change, calling it a step towards world-class mobile phone coverage. The DCMS expects the deal to lead to a two-thirds reduction in "not-spots", areas with poor coverage that can leave people unable to make calls or send texts. Javid had previously warned the operators that if they didn't improve, he might force them to automatically transfer customers to rival networks without charge. However, the networks agreed to the change, despite concerns that it could reduce handset battery life and security risks. The 2017 target should lead to fewer black spots when travelling across the countryside or visiting rural areas. The consumers association Which? welcomed the government's action, saying that the companies must now take swift action and face financial penalties if they fail to meet their commitments. Ofcom noted that EE, Three, and Vodafone are on track to match O2's 4G coverage of 98% of UK premises by the end of 2015. EU regulators still need to approve the plan, while Three may benefit from its parent company's attempt to buy O2 from its current owner, Telefonica.

Fairy circles, which range in diameter from 2 to 15 meters, are found in barren ground surrounded by grass. Researchers at the universities of Strathclyde and Princeton have proposed a new theory explaining their formation, suggesting that plants take advantage of the moist conditions created by termite activity. Until recently, these circles were thought to be unique to the Namib desert in southern Africa, but observations in western Australia in 2014 revealed similar patterns. For years, scientists have debated the possible causes, with some attributing them to sand termites eating the roots of vegetation, while others suggested competition between plant species was to blame. The new theory attempts to unify these explanations by positing that it is the interaction between the termites and vegetation that leads to the formation of fairy circles. Termites remove vegetation above their mounds to conserve water, allowing surrounding vegetation to grow taller and take advantage of the increased moisture. According to Dr Juan Bonachela of the University of Strathclyde's Department of Mathematics and Statistics, the findings provide a harmonious explanation for the regular patterns observed around the globe, particularly in fairy circles. The process begins with termites removing vegetation on their mounds to increase moisture, which is essential for their survival in dry environments. This creates a bare disk, which is then surrounded by taller vegetation that grows as a result of the water accumulation. The repetition of this pattern is due to competing termite colonies, and this behavior has a significant impact on the ecosystem, enabling it to survive harsh conditions and recover from droughts more quickly. The research, which drew on field data from four continents and computer simulations, provides a comprehensive understanding of the phenomenon.
Sudan's government has supported the creation of an independent South Sudan, where the predominantly Christian and Animist population had long been fighting against the Arab Muslim north's rule. However, unresolved issues, particularly the sharing of oil revenues and border demarcation, continue to strain relations between the two nations. Sudan has been plagued by conflict for many years, with two civil wars in the north-south divide claiming 1.5 million lives, and ongoing violence in Darfur displacing 2 million people and killing over 200,000. The country has a population of 45.7 million, with Arabic and English being the major languages and Islam the predominant religion. Life expectancy is 60 years for men and 64 years for women. The Sudanese pound is the national currency, and Omar Hassan al-Bashir has been president since a 1989 coup, with a 25-year rule marked by re-elections in 1996, 2000, 2005, and 2010. Bashir faces two international arrest warrants for genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity. In Sudan, broadcasting is heavily restricted, with state-run media reflecting government policy and private press outlets often censored. The internet has around 9.3 million users, with blogging heavily monitored. Key milestones in Sudan's history include the emergence of the Nubian Kingdom of Kush around 2000-1500 BC, the spread of Islam in 651, and the Ottoman Empire's conquest of northern Sudan in 1820-21. Sudan was under British-Egyptian rule from 1899 to 1955 and gained independence in 1956. In 1983, President Numeiri introduced Sharia law, while the Darfur conflict began in 2003. An international arrest warrant was issued for Bashir in 2009, and South Sudan gained independence in 2011.

A 62-year-old man from Thetford in Norfolk has confessed to raping a 22-year-old woman on Wells Road in Knowle in 1981. The confession came 34 years after the crime, after police matched DNA from a burglary scene in Bristol to the victim's clothing. The man, Stephen McCafferty, handed himself in and confessed on December 30, 2015, a fortnight after officers visited his home to take a DNA sample. He will serve half of his sentence in custody, according to Bristol Crown Court. In a recorded statement, McCafferty expressed remorse, saying, "I hate myself, I wish I hadn't done it. You have your family, you don't want to upset them, you're scared of that." The victim, speaking through the police, said: "I had lost all hope of ever being caught, but thanks to the police's persistence, he was. Even after so many years, the effects of what happened still linger, but a conviction will bring some relief." Avon and Somerset Police said that McCafferty was unaware that his DNA sample was confirmed to match the DNA from the crime scene the day before he traveled to Bristol to make his confession. The crime occurred on July 15, 1981, when the victim was attacked while riding a bicycle along Wells Road. No one was charged at the time, but DNA taken from the victim was matched to DNA recovered from a burglary scene in Long Ashton, Bristol, in May 1999. However, there was no match on the national database. Six months later, McCafferty's name was given to detectives investigating the burglary, leading to his full confession to both the rape and the burglary. The impact of the crime on the victim's life has been immense, with her still living in fear 35 years later. Det Con Scott Westbrook-Smith said, "Her bravery is inspiring, and we appreciate all the support she's given us throughout the re-investigation of this case. The lives of Stephen McCafferty's family have been ruined by his confession, which came completely out of the blue."
In 2012, a staggering 668 rhinos were killed for their horns, a nearly 50% increase from the previous year. The majority of these tragic deaths occurred in the Kruger national park, South Africa's largest wildlife reserve. The demand for rhino horn in Asia is thought to be driving this slaughter, with experts pointing to growing interest in the country as a key factor. South Africa is home to around three-quarters of the world's rhinoceros population, numbering approximately 28,000 animals. In stark contrast to 2007, when just 13 animals were lost to poachers, the killing has skyrocketed since then. The notion that powdered rhino horn possesses medicinal powers, particularly in countries like China and Vietnam, is also fueling the slaughter. With horns selling for around $65,000 per kilogram, the rewards are too great for many to resist. Sophisticated technologies are being used by poachers to capture and dehorn the animals, with organized crime groups profiting from this lucrative trade. The South African government has responded by deploying soldiers and surveillance aircraft, but the numbers suggest they are struggling to stem the tide. According to a recent report from TRAFFIC, South Africa's rhinos are facing a severe poaching crisis that is likely to lead to a population decline. "Rhinos are being brutally killed, their horns hacked off, and the animals left to bleed to death, all for the sake of a hangover cure," says Sabri Zain, director of advocacy for TRAFFIC. Five more rhinos have been killed this year alone, according to the South African government. However, South Africa is not alone in facing this threat. India, home to over 2,200 rhinoceroses in the Kaziranga reserve, has also been plagued by poaching. Despite the presence of around 900 armed rangers and guards, the poachers remain a persistent threat, with 18 animals killed in India last year, up from 10 in 2011.

The appointment of Ajit Pai as chairman of the FCC has raised concerns among internet rights activists, who fear that laws ensuring net neutrality are now under threat. Net neutrality refers to the principle that all internet traffic should be treated equally, rather than allowing internet service providers to charge extra for "fast lanes" that favor data-heavy services like Netflix. Proponents of net neutrality, including Google and Facebook, believe it's essential to maintain competition among online companies. Without access to the "fast lane," a new video service would struggle to compete with established players like YouTube or Vimeo. In the past, Republican members of the FCC have voted against net neutrality, and with President Trump's Republican majority, the commission's stance is likely to shift. Under President Obama, the laws over net neutrality were regularly debated, sparking strong public responses. In 2015, landmark laws were passed to protect net neutrality, a victory for advocates who had feared corporate pressure would undermine the rules. After President Trump's election, Ajit Pai stated that the commission would "fire up the weed whacker" and repeal regulations that hinder investment, innovation, and job creation. Pai, who previously voted against strengthening net neutrality laws, can now take on the role without a Senate confirmation hearing. Free Press president Craig Aaron criticized Pai, saying he has consistently opposed major issues and undermined public safeguards. However, Pai's appointment was welcomed by telecom giants Verizon, Comcast, and AT&T, including his former employer Verizon. For AT&T, the move could be extremely beneficial, as Pai has expressed support for the mega-merger between AT&T and Time Warner, which contradicts President Trump's assurances that the merger would not be approved. Time Warner is also the parent company of CNN, which has faced intense criticism from President Trump. AT&T stated that Pai would work with commissioners to restore a regulatory framework that supports the president's agenda.
A survey commissioned by Lord Holmes of Richmond found that 63% of people rated their experience of shared spaces as poor. Lord Holmes, who is blind, believes that an immediate halt to shared spaces is necessary. He hopes that the survey will serve as a wake-up call to councils that are introducing these spaces. The survey also found that more than a third of people actively avoid shared spaces, and there is significant underreporting of accidents in these areas. Lord Holmes, who has personally experienced the dangers of shared spaces, is adamant that they are not safe. He argues that councils are prioritizing aesthetics over public safety, leading to confusion, chaos, and unnecessary costs. According to the Department for Transport, a shared space is a street or area designed to improve pedestrian movement and comfort by reducing the dominance of motor vehicles. However, Lord Holmes believes that these schemes do not achieve their intended purpose. The survey's findings are being challenged by a group of visually impaired individuals who are suing local authorities over the removal of pedestrian crossings. The visually impaired argue that councils are failing to make reasonable adjustments to the shared spaces, in breach of the Equality Act. In response, a DfT spokesperson emphasized that local authorities must assess the suitability of introducing shared spaces and consider the needs of the whole community, particularly disabled people.

Young Park, an analyst at Hyundai Securities, attributes Samsung's dwindling profits to the declining margins of its smartphone business, as well as a slowdown in the growth rate of the sector. The success of Samsung's Galaxy smartphone range has been a key driver of its growth in recent years, but the company is now facing increased competition from rivals such as Apple, HTC, and Chinese manufacturers like Lenovo, ZTE, and Huawei, all vying to tap into the sector's growth. According to Greg Roh, an analyst at HMC Investment and Securities, Samsung may need to cut costs to sustain its earnings growth. "To achieve additional growth, Samsung will need to focus on cost reduction and reducing marketing costs," he said. "In essence, Samsung has limited options to prevent a decline in its earnings without improving internal efficiencies."

To understand the daily struggles of India's working class, the BBC's Rupa Jha met five individuals in Ahmedabad, one of the country's largest cities. One such person is Denim King, a Mars engineer turned entrepreneur. "People often treat you poorly when you're out working," she says, recounting instances of verbal abuse and dog bites. "It's gotten worse over time, especially for women." Her anger is palpable, yet she chooses to let it go, acknowledging that some things are simply out of her control. Living in a two-room apartment with her unemployed son and daughter-in-law, Ms. Parmar's life has become increasingly difficult due to rising food prices. To make ends meet, she works two shifts a day, earning around $20 a month, and supplements her income by donating blood for clinical research. However, despite India's overall progress, she believes her life has deteriorated. "The city's cleanliness efforts have reduced the amount of trash on the streets, making it harder for us to find work," she says.
Disney and other theme parks are banning selfie sticks, a decision that comes into effect immediately for Disney parks in the US and on Wednesday for Paris and Hong Kong. The move is aimed at reducing safety risks and preventing accidental injuries. The long arm of selfie sticks can pose a hazard on rides, and they can also cause problems during photo opportunities. A spokeswoman for the parks explained that the ban is necessary to protect guests and staff. The gadgets have already been prohibited on rides, but now visitors will be asked to leave their selfie sticks at the parks' entrances, where they can be stored in lockers. Several institutions, including football clubs, the National Gallery, and the All England Lawn Tennis & Croquet Club in Wimbledon, have already banned selfie sticks due to concerns about injury and disruption. In response to the ban, St John Ambulance, a first aid charity, offered advice on how to treat selfie stick-related injuries. Training officer Clive James said that if someone is hit on the head with a selfie stick, they should sit down, apply a cold compress, and seek medical help if they experience signs of a worsening injury, such as dizziness, nausea, confusion, or memory loss.

Colombia, Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica advised pregnant women to delay their pregnancies until more information was available about the mosquito-borne Zika virus, following an outbreak in Brazil. Brazil reported nearly 4,000 babies born with suspected microcephaly, or abnormally small heads, since October. US health authorities warned pregnant women to avoid traveling to more than 20 countries in the Americas and beyond, where Zika cases had been registered. However, the link between microcephaly and Zika has not been confirmed, and a small number of babies who died showed the virus in their brains without any other explanation for the surge in microcephaly. The virus is not contagious and typically causes flu-like symptoms. Health Minister Alejandro Gaviria of Colombia urged women to delay pregnancies for up to eight months, stating that he believed this was a way to communicate the risk and inform people of potential serious consequences. Similar warnings were issued in Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica. However, women's rights campaigners criticized the recommendations, arguing that women in the region often had little choice but to become pregnant. Monica Roa, a member of the Women's Link Worldwide group, said it was "incredibly naive" for governments to ask women to postpone getting pregnant in a context like Colombia, where over 50% of pregnancies are unplanned, and where sexual violence is prevalent. In Brazil, 49 babies with suspected microcephaly have died, with Zika virus found in five cases. Brazil is experiencing the largest known outbreak of Zika, primarily in the north-east, with cases also detected in the south-east, including Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo. Other Latin American countries have seen a sharp increase in Zika cases. In Colombia, over 13,500 cases have been reported. The US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention initially warned pregnant women to avoid traveling to certain countries and added eight more places to the list on Friday.
Crime in London decreased by 6% over the past year, with 45,000 fewer reports of crime filed between 2012-13. The Metropolitan Police Service attributed the drop to proactive measures, including the launch of the Trident Gang Crime Command, which aimed to tackle an estimated 250 active gangs in the city. The unit's efforts have shown a significant impact, with offences involving weapons decreasing by 20%. Specifically, knife crime fell by 20%, while gun crime also declined by 20%. However, some crimes have seen an increase, including personal thefts, which rose by 17%. Of these, 70% involved the theft of mobile phones. Former Met officer Mike Pannett expressed concerns about sustaining the crime reduction, saying he would be monitoring the situation closely over the next two to three years. The police force cited the Trident Gang Crime Command as a key factor in the reduction. Homicides remained stable, with 106 deaths, a 3% increase from the previous year. Victim Support welcomed the potential for increased reporting of rape, citing a 16% rise in the number of cases. The charity attributed this increase to a growing confidence in reporting rape cases. Mayor Boris Johnson praised the efforts of London's police force, saying the city's low crime rate is a testament to their work.
Kinnell died on Tuesday at their home in Vermont after a long battle with leukemia. The celebrated poet, known for his prolific output and five-decade career, had written more than a dozen books. His 1982 collection, Selected Poems, earned him the Pulitzer Prize, while the National Book Award for Poetry shared the honor with contemporary poet Charles Wright. Other notable works include The Book of Nightmares, inspired by the horror of the Vietnam war, and The Bear, a poem about a hunter who identifies with his prey after consuming animal blood and excrement. Kinnell's poetry also explored themes of love, loss, and mortality, as seen in After Making Love We Hear Footsteps and When the Towers Fell, which commemorated the 9/11 attacks in New York. Born in Providence, Rhode Island, on February 1, 1927, Kinnell was the son of Scottish and Irish immigrants. In an interview, he once revealed that he was a "very silent child" who developed a strong sense of isolation, which eventually led him to poetry as a means to find happiness. Kinnell attended Princeton University, where he befriended fellow poet WS Merwin, with whom he shared a love for the works of WB Yeats. Merwin described Kinnell as a "very generous soul" and praised his work as "warm-hearted" and "independent but sympathetic to others." Kinnell's breakthrough poem, The Avenue Bearing the Initial of Christ Into the New World, appeared in 1960 and marked a significant milestone in his career. He served as Vermont's poet laureate from 1989 to 1993 and moved to the state in 2005. The Academy of American Poets awarded him the Wallace Stevens Award for lifetime achievement in 2010. Kinnell married twice, first to Spanish translator Ines Delgado de Torres, with whom he had two children, before divorcing 20 years later. He married Barbara in 1997.
Dr Nihal Weerasena, a doctor employed by Leeds Teaching Hospitals NHS Trust, was found to have severely neglected the care of six patients, including five children and one adult. He was sanctioned after being found guilty of misconduct by the Medical Practitioners Tribunal Service (MPTS). The MPTS found that Dr Weerasena showed a "reckless disregard for patient safety" and had displayed "substandard practice" during a complex surgery performed on a seven-year-old patient in 2012. However, he was cleared of intentionally omitting a crucial event from his report, which had been recorded in a handwritten note. Dr Weerasena was also found to have failed to maintain accurate records of surgery for a six-year-old patient in November 2010. An allegation regarding his care of a sixth child was not proven. The MPTS concluded that Dr Weerasena's misconduct, lack of insight, and failure to take effective corrective action were incompatible with his continued registration. As a result, the MPTS determined that Dr Weerasena should not practice medicine again due to public safety concerns and to protect the reputation of the medical profession. In 2014, the Trust referred Dr Weerasena to the General Medical Council following a review of paediatric care services, which aimed to assess clinical outcomes. The review occurred a year after a temporary halt was placed on children's heart operations at Leeds General Infirmary due to safety concerns. A subsequent report found that the unit did not have excessively high mortality rates. Dr Weerasena was absent from the hearing and explained his absence in writing, claiming he was a victim of a "cover-up".

Other top-selling British albums included Coldplay's Mylo Xyloto, Susan Boyle's Someone to Watch Over Me, and two albums by Florence and the Machine. BPI chief executive Geoff Taylor predicted that boybands like One Direction and The Wanted would continue the trend. "These results show that investing in British talent by UK labels can help artists break internationally and generate important exports that boost the UK economy," he said.
Over the past three years, the number of homes sold within five miles of the new railway stops has increased, although the growth is less pronounced than in the wider areas of Midlothian and the Scottish Borders. House prices have also risen along the route since 2006, but the council-wide figure has been higher. What's happening in Scotland today? Stay up-to-date with our live coverage. Since 2012, the volume of residential sales within five miles of the new stops has risen by 50.2% in Midlothian and 26.3% in the Borders, with the whole council areas experiencing a 60.6% and 30.1% increase respectively, and a 28.2% rise across Scotland as a whole. Interestingly, house prices have remained largely unaffected. In the Midlothian catchment area, average prices have grown by 6.1% to £166,639, while houses within five miles of the new Borders stations have seen an average price increase of 4.5% to £143,283. Council-wide area figures for the same period are 13.4% in Midlothian and 9.5% in the Borders, with the Scottish increase at 16.1%. According to RoS' head of data, Rhona Mackay, "We have seen an increase in the volume of sales around the new Borders railway stations, but this is slightly lower than the volumes seen in the local authority areas as a whole. Prices are also up, but again this is lower in the five mile catchment areas than the wider local authority areas where the increase is more substantial." Glyptodonts roamed South America for millions of years, until the last Ice Age, and some grew as large as cars. Their distinctive physical attributes, particularly their impenetrable shell, already suggested a close relationship to armadillos. However, researchers now believe that glyptodonts are not just cousins, but a subfamily. According to Frederic Delsuc, from the National Centre of Scientific Research and Montpellier University in France, glyptodonts should be considered a subfamily of gigantic armadillos. To test this theory, Delsuc and his colleagues used computer simulations to reconstruct DNA sequences of armadillo ancestors, based on the genes of living species. They then used this information to create RNA "bait" and use it to search for glyptodont DNA in a small, fossilized shell sample from a Buenos Aires museum. This technique allows scientists to confidently identify real DNA sequences from ancient species without worrying about contamination. The team eventually managed to reconstruct the entire mitochondrial genome of a glyptodont, which came from Doedicurus, one of the largest members of the family. This fearsome but vegetarian beast grew up to 4 meters in length and weighed about 1.5 tonnes. Researchers say that these creatures got progressively larger over time until their extinction at the end of the Ice Age 10,000 years ago. This is not surprising, as South America was home to other giant animals, such as elephant-sized ground sloths and giant, sabre-toothed cats. The team is confident that they have finally resolved the position of glyptodonts in the tree of life, and they are found in the "cingulata" order, among the many branches of armadillos. According to co-author Hendrik Poinar from McMaster University, Canada, glyptodonts represent an extinct lineage that likely originated 35 million years ago within the armadillo radiation. One of the main differences between this ancient group and their modern cousins is the glyptodont's massive, dome-shaped shell, which was not articulated like the iconic, layered bands of the armadillo. Researchers believe that this single, rounded shield might have evolved alongside the spectacular increase in the glyptodonts' size, which also explains why some older members of the family have banded shells.
In January, the Home Office awarded thousands of posthumous pardons to men who have since passed away, but those still alive must apply to have their convictions expunged. Stonewall Cymru described the men as having been "unjustly persecuted" and many were unaware they could apply for a pardon. Wednesday marks 50 years since the Sexual Offences Act of 1967 decriminalized consensual sex between men aged over 21 in England and Wales. Approximately 65,000 men were convicted under the old law, with about 15,000 still alive. Mabli Jones, campaign and research manager at Stonewall Cymru, believes men should not have to live with convictions that are no longer considered valid. She stated: "The change in the law was a significant step forward, but many men were unjustly persecuted, and they deserve their convictions to be removed." Jones also noted that some people remain unaware they can apply for a pardon, and the charity would welcome automatic removal from records. A Home Office spokesperson clarified that convictions are not automatically deleted, as the government aims to ensure pardons are granted only to those convicted of consensual offenses involving individuals over 16. The spokesperson emphasized the government's record in improving equality. According to 2015 ONS figures, 1.6% of people in Wales identified as gay, lesbian, or bisexual, compared to 1.8% in the UK. Jones acknowledged that attitudes towards LGBT individuals have improved in Wales, but many, particularly young people, still face discrimination. The 2017 School Report by Stonewall Cymru showed a decrease in anti-LGBT bullying and language in British schools, but 45% of LGBT pupils reported facing bullying for being LGBT. Jones expressed her desire for mandatory teaching of all types of relationships in schools, along with information on safe sex within same-sex relationships. She is working with Education Secretary Kirsty Williams, who has established an expert panel to advise the Welsh Government on addressing the issue and making schools more inclusive.

China's $180m satellite project is set to revolutionize space exploration and aid in the search for extraterrestrial life. A survey of 250 school, youth, and health staff in England found that many staff members witness children going hungry during the school day. The Children's Food Trust's poll revealed that 68.1% of respondents had noticed an increase in families struggling to provide meals for their children over the past two years. As a result, lunchboxes now contain less fruit and more junk food, according to the survey. Of the staff working in schools, 47.5% reported seeing a change in the food in children's lunchboxes as household budgets tightened. One staff member noted that they had seen "poorer quality sandwich fillings, often just margarine." Another mentioned that there were "fewer processed items, more leftovers, or store-bought items." However, they added that "in some ways, it is healthier, but some families only provide cold cooked rice, cold chips, or similar." The survey also found references to junk food, sweets, and chocolate appearing in lunchboxes, along with less fruit. The survey revealed that 84.6% of the professionals who participated had seen children without enough to eat during their work. Of those who said this, 84.8% reported that this issue applied to about a third of the children they worked with. Linda Cregan, the upcoming CEO of the Children's Food Trust, stated that many staff members who work with children have to go above and beyond to protect them from the effects of hunger and poor diet. She emphasized that while parents are ultimately responsible for their child's nutrition, the reality is that this can be a significant struggle. Cregan noted that staff members in these jobs are at the front line of helping parents with this issue, and therefore need the right support. She suggested that local authorities could develop public health plans that include ring-fencing funding for children's nutrition, which could be used to support various initiatives such as cooking skills training, subsidizing school meals, or providing breakfast clubs. Cregan emphasized the importance of making this commitment explicit. In contrast, pupils at Priory School in Lewes reported that their lunches were generally quite good. Flora, 14, said she hadn't seen people with chips in their lunchboxes, but noted that the school serves chips on Fridays. Ellen, 13, agreed that most people were quite healthy, saying she takes a piece of fruit to school every day. Maidstone Museum's mummy, Ta-Kush, has been a fan favourite for years, but now scientists plan to use a £78,700 grant to reconstruct her face and uncover more about her life. Believed to be a 14-year-old girl who died around 2,700 years ago, Ta-Kush's story is about to be rewritten thanks to a CT scan on the mummy and coffin. The museum is set to launch a new ancient civilisations gallery, combining its Egyptian and Greek collections, with Ta-Kush as the star attraction. However, until recently, her name was thought to be Ta-Kesh, a name revealed by an Egyptologist in Cairo. "For over 150 years, we've been calling her Ta-Kesh," said Samantha Harris, the museum's collections manager. "But she's now reinterpreted her name as Ta-Kush, meaning the Kushite lady." Ta-Kush has been at the museum since the 1850s, and experts will use the grant to scan her using the Kent Institute of Medicine and Surgery's equipment, which will then be analysed by experts at Liverpool John Moore's University. The results will digitally reconstruct her face, head, and shoulders, allowing visitors to see what she looks like. The analysis should also reveal her age and even confirm her sex, although scientists are cautious, saying that previous scans of females have sometimes turned out to be male. Work so far has helped narrow down her origins to the area around Thebes, and details about her parents have been gleaned from hieroglyphics on the coffin. The imaging manager at Kims, Matt Travail, explained that the scan itself will only take five minutes, but the analysis and reconstruction will take a lot more time.

GP Energy has submitted a planning application to explore beneath a plot of land near Commonwood Farm in Borras, with the company insisting the operation is purely exploratory drilling and not aimed at fracking. However, North East Wales Against Fracking Action has expressed concern among local residents, who fear that the planned test drilling could eventually lead to hydraulic fracturing, or fracking. According to Luke Ashley, spokesperson for the newly-formed action group, the drilling could mark the beginning of a larger industrialization of the countryside to the east of Wrexham. Ashley explained that the test borehole will be used to assess the amount of coal bed methane present in the underground coal seams that stretch from Flintshire's Point of Ayr down to Cheshire and Shropshire. He warned that if full-scale extraction of coal bed methane were to follow, it would involve pumping large quantities of water and chemicals into the ground to extract the gas. Ashley expressed particular concern that this could contaminate the nearby River Dee, which supplies drinking water to the area. The group plans to lobby council members before the plans are discussed by the council's planning committee, possibly later this year. A public meeting was held on Tuesday evening to discuss the proposal. Fracking involves pumping water and chemicals into shale rock at high pressure to extract gas, a process that was temporarily banned in the UK after two earth tremors in Blackpool in 2011. Opponents argue that water used in fracking could contaminate domestic supplies, but a UK government review has concluded that fracking is safe if adequately monitored. Dart Energy, which owns GP Energy, has stated that it has no plans to frack in the Wrexham area and that the well at the drilling site will be sealed over once exploration is complete. A company spokesperson added that the drilling is temporary in nature and will be fully cemented once the operation is finished. The purpose of the well is to extract a core sample from the coal seam to test the methane content in a laboratory.

About 21,000 British soldiers lost their lives in the 1915 Battle of Loos, with around 7,000 of them being Scottish. A significant number of casualties came from the 4th Black Watch, a Dundee-based regiment. To honor their memory, Prince Charles and Camilla attended a service in the city, along with about 1,000 people. The prince is the Royal Colonel of the 3rd Battalion (The Black Watch), The Royal Regiment of Scotland, and his great-uncle, Captain Fergus Bowes-Lyon, died on the third day of the battle in France. On September 25, 1915, the first day of the battle, around 8,000 lives were lost within the first four hours. To mark the centenary, events began on Friday with a beacon lighting ceremony at the top of Dundee's war memorial. On Saturday, a service started with a parade of about 250 current service personnel and 300 veterans, followed by the transformation of the City Square into a "cathedral" with ministers from various churches. Charles, known as the Duke of Rothesay in Scotland, laid a wreath against a specially-commissioned memorial stone alongside the first minister and Lord Provost of Dundee. He also unveiled a commemorative plaque, saying, "The units involved in the Battle of Loos represent Scotland's finest regiments. They left their homes to fight for our freedom in a foreign land, and their example is deeply humbling." Scottish First Minister Nicola Sturgeon said, "With around 30,000 Scots serving at Loos, its impact was felt across every village and town in Scotland." Lord Provost Bob Duncan added, "The battle affected every Scottish regiment, and it was also Dundee's darkest hour, with significant losses among local Black Watch battalions." He continued, "This weekend presents a chance to remember their heroic sacrifice and honor their memory." Charles and Camilla will later attend a reception for forces personnel and veterans and view an exhibition on the Battle of Loos by pupils from Dundee schools.
A new plan aims to enhance the management of type 1 diabetes. The condition affects approximately 30,000 people in Scotland. The government's priority will be to boost the use of insulin pumps, which can reduce the need for multiple daily injections. There are currently 3,200 insulin pumps in use in Scotland. In addition to the pumps, the government will provide access to continuous glucose monitoring equipment. Karen Addington, chief executive of the diabetes charity JDRF, stated, "Research indicates that this type 1 diabetes technology, when used alongside support and training, can significantly improve people's lives. "We appreciate the government's decision to increase funding to improve access to it." Health Secretary Shona Robison noted, "Insulin pumps and continuous glucose monitoring kits can greatly simplify the management of type 1 diabetes for some individuals. "Maintaining proper control is crucial in preventing complications and improving outcomes. "This new investment will expand access to the equipment, making it possible for more people to lead healthier lives."
The Sun is the largest object in our Solar System, accounting for 99% of its mass. Its enormous size is staggering, with a diameter roughly 109 times that of Earth, and an incredible one million Earths could fit inside it. Scientists believe the Sun, along with the planets, formed from the solar nebula around 4.59 billion years ago, making it a truly ancient body. The surface temperature of the Sun is a scorching 5,500 degrees Celsius, while its core is a sizzling 15.6 million degrees Celsius. Interestingly, the temperature varies across the Sun, with 'sun spots' appearing in areas that are significantly cooler than others. Composed mainly of hydrogen, with smaller amounts of helium, oxygen, and heavier gases like iron, the Sun is a complex and fascinating celestial body. What's more, light from the Sun takes just 8 minutes to reach our planet. A crucial reminder is that we should never look directly at the Sun, as it can cause serious damage to our eyes.
French philosopher Anne Dufourmantelle died after being pulled underwater by a strong current while trying to rescue two children at a beach near St Tropez. The children, who were later rescued by lifeguards, were unharmed. Culture Minister Françoise Nyssen described Dufourmantelle as a "great philosopher who helped us live". Witnesses said Dufourmantelle, 53, was 50m away from the children when an orange warning flag was replaced with a red flag, signaling the ban on bathing due to hazardous conditions. She tried to reach the children but was swept away by the current. Attempts to revive her after she was recovered were unsuccessful. Dufourmantelle's funeral is set to take place in Ramatuelle, southern France, on Tuesday. The philosopher, known for her essays on the importance of taking risks and accepting the presence of threats in everyday life, had written extensively on the subject. In 2011, she published the book "Praise of Risk", which explored the need to confront risks as part of living. Nyssen praised Dufourmantelle on Twitter, describing her as "a great philosopher, a psychoanalyst, who helped us live and think about the world today". Fellow philosopher Raphaël Enthoven expressed his sadness at Dufourmantelle's death, saying she "spoke so well of dreams". In a 2015 interview with Liberation, Dufourmantelle argued that the idea of "absolute security" is a fantasy and that life is inherently risky. She believed that the desire for security can lead to control and diminish freedom. Dufourmantelle also warned that security measures, such as armed officers on the streets, can generate fear and paralysis. She had earned a doctoral degree in philosophy from Paris-Sorbonne in 1994 and later practiced psychoanalysis, winning the Raymond de Boyer Prize for philosophy in 1998.

Ecometrica, based in Edinburgh, has developed software to rapidly interpret satellite imagery, enabling the company to identify threats to tropical forests such as deforestation and agricultural encroachment. Under a contract awarded by the UK Space Agency, Ecometrica will collaborate with experts from six other countries to safeguard vulnerable ecosystems. The "Forests 2020" project, which spans 300 million hectares of tropical forests, aims to enhance management and conservation efforts. Ecometrica will work alongside partners in Brazil, Colombia, Ghana, Indonesia, Kenya, and Mexico, establishing earth observation laboratories to assess threats to rainforests and guide conservation efforts. The company will subcontract experts from the University of Edinburgh, the University of Leicester, and Carbomap, another Edinburgh-based company. Funding for the project comes from the UK Space Agency's International Partnership Programme, which fosters collaboration between British space expertise and developing nations. Ray Fielding, head of the UK Space Agency's International Partnership Programme, stated, "This programme will explore innovative ways space technology can contribute to this critical issue, identified by the UN as crucial for sustainable development. We aim to make a tangible difference on the ground, preserving the world's forests." Dr. Richard Tipper, Ecometrica's executive chairman, noted, "Tropical rainforests are vital to the global ecosystem, yet we're just starting to realize the need for technology to ensure conservation efforts are effective and targeted. The Earth Observation platforms will enable quicker detection of threats like fires and deforestation, leading to faster and more cost-effective responses on the ground."

A staggering 360 million account credentials, including email addresses and passwords, were recently uncovered, according to Hold Security. The company also found an additional 1.25 billion email addresses without passwords. The origin of these credentials, which were discovered over the past three weeks, remains a mystery, but they are believed to include major email providers. Experts have described the batch as enormous, with Graham Cluley, an online security consultant, likening it to a "Godzilla-sized" treasure trove for cybercriminals. Cluley warned that the sheer size of the data could be used to access not only the directly associated accounts but also potentially other accounts, as people often reuse the same passwords across multiple sites. This could lead to a significant increase in spam and phishing attacks, as well as the ability to crack into accounts using common passwords. The discovery has raised concerns that the risk to consumers and companies may be greater than stolen credit card data, as it could potentially compromise online bank accounts, corporate networks, and health records. Alex Holden, chief information security officer of Hold Security, described the volume as "overwhelming" and stated that the credentials were stolen in breaches that have yet to be publicly reported. The companies affected may remain unaware until they are notified by third parties who discover evidence of the hacking. Hold Security has a team working around the clock to identify the victims and mitigate the damage.

An investigation into a library in Lake County, Florida, found that fake borrowers had "read" thousands of books. Library staff have been suspended while officials investigate whether the fictitious readers led to the library receiving more state funding. The staff claimed that their actions had helped save money by keeping popular titles that would have had to be purchased again if they had been removed. Officials from the Lake County public resources department, which oversees the libraries, told local newspapers that they had been alerted to the existence of fake readers at the East Lake County library by an anonymous person. An internal investigation by Lake County municipal staff found that several fake identities had been created by senior staff at the library, including one named Chuck Finley, after a retired baseball player. Reports showed that Mr. Finley had checked out over 2,600 books in nine months, with some titles being checked out and returned within an hour. The investigation suggests that the fake readers boosted East Lake's readership numbers by almost 4%, which is important because it counts towards the amount of official funding the library receives. Last year, libraries in and around Lake County received about $1m from local government. The head librarian at East Lake said that the false borrowers were created only to keep popular titles that staff knew were always in demand. He claimed that if the computer system recommended getting rid of unread books, libraries would have to re-purchase titles they had just thrown out. He also alleged that many other libraries used "dummy" reader cards to achieve the same result. Digital activist Cory Doctorow said that the logging system had become a "straitjacket" that disregarded the views of staff who knew what readers wanted. He denied that the librarians had fooled the software for personal gain, saying they did it to make the system better, to teach it to weight the circulation data to reflect the on-the-ground intelligence.

Patients diagnosed with the disease are entitled to follow-up eye examinations, but a shocking discovery has revealed that nearly 500 were not given the necessary tests since 2010. These tests can detect retinal damage caused by high blood sugar levels, a complication of diabetes that can lead to blindness if left untreated. In fact, diabetic retinopathy is a common issue for people with diabetes, resulting from damaged cells at the back of the eye. Unfortunately, it has come to light that systems meant to refer patients with the disease to specialist eye examinations at local clinics failed to function. The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Trust is currently investigating the errors. Diabetes UK expressed concern that patients had not been regularly checked. According to a spokesperson, diabetic retinopathy often lacks noticeable symptoms in its early stages, leaving patients unaware of the damage until it's too late. Sheila O'Brien of Cumbria Societies for the Blind noted that while it's disappointing that the issue was not identified sooner, the fact that it's now being addressed is a positive step. What's crucial is ensuring that such mistakes don't happen again. The affected areas include 160 people in Morecambe Bay, 143 in North Cumbria, and nearly 200 in Blackpool. George Nasmyth, the deputy medical director at The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust, acknowledged the issue and emphasized the need for collaboration with the Diabetic Eye Screening Programme, NHS England, and other NHS Trusts to prevent similar incidents in the future. All affected patients have been notified and invited to attend a screening appointment, and those with concerns can contact 0845 0500 109.
The Bank of England's Prudential Regulation Authority and the Financial Conduct Authority aim to curb irresponsible risk-taking and short-termism. Under the new regulations, senior managers may face penalties for up to 10 years for misconduct, with their bonuses potentially being clawed back. FCA chief Martin Wheatley sees these rules as part of a broader effort to instill an accountable culture in the financial sector. He believes they are essential for rebuilding public trust in financial services. The rules apply to banks, building societies, and some investment firms, following a nearly year-long consultation. The main changes include restrictions on buyouts of unpaid bonuses. However, the Bank of England notes that new employers won't be barred from buying out unpaid bonuses, but managers won't be able to access the funds until they remain with their current company. Andrew Bailey, deputy governor for prudential regulation and head of the PRA, emphasizes that effective regulation relies on creating incentives that encourage individuals to take responsibility for their actions. His goal is to reward those in positions of power for promoting a culture of prudent risk management, which in turn safeguards the stability of individual financial institutions.
