"We cannot say that if they had done so, the abuse would not have happened, but we can say that many of our agencies could and should have responded differently." The report found that Baby D's mother was vulnerable, having been taken into care after suffering neglect and violence. She was 17 when she became pregnant and the father was 34. The baby also lived with its grandfather and a 14-year-old uncle, both of whom had a history of violence and criminal behavior. Although the baby's family was suspected of being involved with gangs and drug-related activity, social workers failed to take these risk factors seriously enough, a serious case review found. The report also discovered that "a culture of meeting targets and timescales had resulted in a lack of critical analysis when conducting assessments." Top wheelchair tennis players, including Grand slam winners and Paralympic champions, gathered for the first-ever Surbiton Wheelchair Tennis Tournament. Thirteen of the world's best players competed in the warm-up event, preparing for Wimbledon, where three British players will be defending their titles. The tournament marked the first time players had a chance to practice their grass-court game before heading to the All England Lawn Tennis Club. Wheelchair tennis is played on a standard-sized court with the same balls and rackets, but athletes use specialized wheelchairs and the ball can bounce twice on each side of the net. Lucy Shuker, a double Paralympic bronze medallist, spoke about her journey to the sport after a motorbike accident left her paralyzed from the waist down. She has represented Great Britain at three Paralympics and won bronze medals in the doubles at both London 2012 and Rio 2016, alongside Jordanne Whiley. "When I first started, everyone said I was too disabled to compete," Shuker said. "Despite being the most disabled girl on the tour, I've developed a specialized chair and straps to help me compete, making it challenging every day." "However, it's made me stronger and my body better, so dealing with my disability is easier." Top players travel the world for the four Grand Slam tournaments, which take place in Melbourne, Paris, Wimbledon, and New York. Britain's Alfie Hewett, fresh from winning the men's singles at the French Open, will defend his title after winning the men's doubles at Wimbledon last year with fellow Briton and world number one Gordon Reid. Hewett started playing in 2005 and made his Paralympic debut in Rio, where he won two silver medals in the doubles and singles. "Playing on grass requires a different approach," Hewett said. "We haven't had the opportunity to play competitive matches on grass before Wimbledon, so this tournament will be crucial for my preparations." Reigning Australian Open champion Gustavo Fernandez beat Hewett in a reverse match in Surbiton, just days after the Briton's triumph in Paris. The Tennis Foundation, which organized the event, hopes the warm-up tournament will give the players an edge for Wimbledon, where the wheelchair events start on July 13.
Phil Everly, one half of the iconic duo the Everly Brothers, passed away in his Los Angeles suburb of Burbank due to complications from lung disease. His wife Patti revealed that the disease was a direct result of his lifelong habit of smoking. As a key member of the Everly Brothers, Phil and his brother Don were one of the most popular acts of the 1950s and early 1960s. Don expressed his deep sadness at Phil's passing, saying, "I loved my brother very much. I always thought I'd be the one to go first." Phil's death has sent shockwaves, with Don mourning the loss of his brother, stating, "The world might be mourning an Everly Brother, but I'm mourning my brother Phil Everly." The Everly Brothers had a string of close-harmony hits including Wake Up Little Suzie, Cathy's Clown, Bye Bye Love, and All I Have To Do Is Dream. Rock pioneer Duane Eddy, a close friend of Phil's, called his death a "terrible, terrible loss" for everyone. Phil's son Jason confirmed that he died on Friday from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease. Rolling Stone magazine considers the Everly Brothers "the most important vocal duo in rock". The duo, who were the children of Midwestern country music singers Ike and Margaret Everly, performed on their family radio show while growing up. In their prime between 1957 and 1962, the Everly Brothers achieved 19 Top 40 hits. They also influenced notable acts such as the Beatles and the Beach Boys. The pair had a highly publicized onstage breakup in 1973, leading to a decade-long estrangement. However, Phil later stated that despite their split, the brothers' relationship had endured. "Don and I are infamous for our split," Phil said, "but we're closer than most brothers." The Everly Brothers were inducted into the Rock 'n' Roll Hall of Fame in 1986 and received a lifetime achievement award at the Grammys in 1997. Singer-songwriter Charlie Daniels paid tribute to Phil, tweeting, "Rest in peace Phil Everly. You guys brought us a lot of pleasure back in the day."

Dozens of people demonstrated outside Walsall Council House earlier, expressing their opposition. The council claimed that it was necessary to enforce the regulations to prevent graves from becoming disorganized and chaotic. However, a section of the cemetery is a special memorial for children, and the council had allowed the edgings and ornaments to remain intact in that area. Matloob argued that the current rule discriminated against older residents and the wider community, stating that "one rule for everybody" should be applied. In a statement, the council acknowledged that most people would understand the need for proper management of the existing regulations. The council emphasized the importance of maintaining the cemeteries in good order to protect the rights of its residents. It stated that it had undertaken a two-month consultation period, made some minor changes to the rules, and confirmed these changes at its June Cabinet meeting.

According to local media reports, the militant was overpowered by two civilians he had taken hostage, and then handed over to the police. A senior police official confirmed that the man was a Pakistani national who had crossed into India through the Line of Control, the informal border between the two countries. Unlike Qasab, who was captured by a Mumbai policeman and executed in November 2012, the arrested gunman is being referred to as "Kasab II". Indian media outlets are highlighting the arrest as evidence of Pakistan's backing for "anti-India" terrorist groups, with some calling it a "big catch" for India. However, a news report in the NDTV website notes that the capture could lead to new evidence of repeated terror attacks against India, organised across the border. The Times of India sees the arrest as a significant breakthrough, while a Firstpost article argues that it may not be enough to prove Pakistan's involvement in terror attacks. In contrast, Pakistani media coverage has been low-key, with few outlets mentioning the story. Geo News, which did cover the story, has criticized the Indian media for making claims about the arrested man's nationality without concrete proof.
Healthcare professionals are set to face significant changes in September 2017, with plans to overhaul the funding system and introduce university fees for students studying to become nurses, midwives, and other healthcare staff. The Royal College of Nursing and Unison have expressed concerns that this move could deter people from pursuing careers in the NHS, exacerbating existing shortages. Currently, student nurses and healthcare staff are eligible for bursaries ranging from £4,500 to £5,500, in addition to a grant of £1,000 per year. The government also covers course fees, but now proposes to scrap these benefits and introduce university fees. Ministers argue that this change will increase the number of nurse students, with around 10,000 more applicants anticipated. However, unions warn that this could have the opposite effect, potentially reducing the number of students starting courses by around 6.5%. The Royal College of Nursing has warned that the fees could deter older students, who may not have the same flexibility to pursue careers in the NHS due to the demands of the job and the lack of paid holidays. RCN general secretary Janet Davies stated, "Our members tell us that the fees could put people off, particularly older students who come into nursing later in life. They are crucial for the diversity of the workforce, and this seems a risky move at a time when we are severely short of staff." The Public Accounts Committee has previously warned that the NHS is facing a shortage of around 50,000 front-line staff. Davies called on the government to pause its plans and conduct a full risk assessment to gauge the potential impact. Unison head of health Christina McAnea expressed concern that the move could make the situation worse, highlighting the potential risks to patient safety. A Department of Health spokesperson defended the changes, stating that they aim to increase the number of home-grown nurses, which would reduce reliance on expensive agency staff and overseas nurses.

The latest Markit/CIPS service sector purchasing managers' index (PMI) jumped to 54.9, up from 53.3 in the previous month. A reading above 50 signifies growth in the sector, a milestone that hasn't been achieved in four months. Markit described the growth rate as "relatively subdued". Sterling surged to its highest level against the euro since mid-August at 70.78 pence, and against the dollar it rose to $1.5446. The survey revealed that services companies, accounting for about three-quarters of the UK's private sector activity, experienced a five-month high in job growth. The services figure followed strong results from comparable surveys of the manufacturing and construction sectors. Markit's chief economist, Chris Williamson, noted that this improvement, combined with the recent uptick in hiring, might prompt policymakers to consider raising interest rates before the end of the year. However, he cautioned that dovish policymakers would point to the lack of inflationary pressures in October, suggesting no need to rush into rate hikes. Most economists do not foresee the Bank of England raising rates until the second quarter of next year.

Archaeologists in Cambridgeshire have unearthed Britain's best-preserved Bronze Age dwellings, dating back to around 1,000-800 BC, at a quarry site near Whittlesey. The homes, consisting of two or three circular wooden houses, were remarkably preserved in silt after falling into a river during a devastating fire 3,000 years ago. The painstaking process of excavating the site has only just begun, but initial finds have been hailed as "amazing artefacts". The structures, built on stilts, were part of a settlement that was largely destroyed by fire. Unlike most Bronze Age sites, which have little to no timber remaining, the site has yielded intact stilts, roof structures, and walls. It's believed the roof collapsed during the fire, protecting the contents of the houses. Much of the site was likely preserved as it sank into the Fenland silt. Currently, only a small section of one of the house interiors is being examined. One of the first items discovered was a tiny, delicate wooden box that is remarkably complete. The team was thrilled to find such a well-preserved artefact, but the contents are yet to be examined. An intact fineware pot and animal bones have also been found, which must be meticulously cleaned and documented. The cluster of fish and animal bones uncovered could have been the kitchen waste of the time, the team said. The excavation, which is expected to last eight months, is currently halfway through. Despite being in the early stages of examining the house interior, the quality and quantity of what has been uncovered so far have left archaeologists "very excited". The site has the potential for more uncommon household objects, including tools, cutlery, and even furniture. The excavation is being jointly funded by Historic England and quarry owner Forterra.
Mayor Boris Johnson took his dispute to the Supreme Court after a lower court ruled that the police were responsible for the £60m bill under the 1886 Riot Damage Act. Insurance companies claim the losses from the Enfield building are covered under the 19th Century law. The court said a final decision is expected "in a few months". The Sony warehouse was targeted on August 8, 2011, during the riots that followed Mark Duggan's death in Tottenham. Over 1.5m CDs were destroyed in what lawyers described as the largest arson attack in Europe. At a High Court hearing, Justice Flaux stated that total losses exceeded £60m, including £9.8m for lost profit and £1.6m for lost rent. The Supreme Court judges were told under the Riot Damage Act that damage caused by rioting should be paid out of police funds. However, the High Court had previously ruled that the police should only cover the damage, not consequential losses like lost profit and rent. Insurers won a subsequent appeal at the Court of Appeal, which found the Metropolitan Police should cover all costs. After the ruling, Clive Coleman, the BBC's legal correspondent, noted that the decision could have implications for other businesses affected by the riots. Mayor Johnson, who funds the police, requested a Supreme Court ruling after the two previous hearings.
A UK athlete and campaigner hopes to meet the Russian Minister of Defence to discuss the creation of a marine reserve around the Ross Sea, a move that could help the world reach an agreement on the proposed protected zone despite international tensions. The Ross Sea, which covers a slice of Antarctica claimed by New Zealand, is one of the last pristine regions of the Earth's oceans untouched by human activities. However, there is a deadlock over giving it a special designation. Speaking of his upcoming encounter with the Russian, athlete and UN Environment Programme Patron of the Oceans, Pugh, said: "This is the first time Antarctica has been discussed at this level." He expressed his hope that the meeting could lead to dialogue and peace, adding, "When you take off your clothes, there are no barriers. You can talk person to person." Despite his optimism, Pugh warned that the process would be lengthy, particularly given the current international disagreements over Ukraine. As part of a series of Antarctic swims, Pugh recently broke his own record by swimming further south than anyone has before. He described the Bay of Whales in the Ross Sea as the most terrifying place he had ever swum, with temperatures as low as -1C and -37C. The Ross Sea Marine Protected Area (MPA) has been discussed for four years but has failed four times, with the last proposal vetoed by Russia and China. The area would be the size of the UK, Germany, and France combined, requiring the agreement of 24 countries and the EU. Pugh referenced the 1959 Antarctic Treaty, which was signed at the height of the Cold War, and expressed his hope that Antarctica could be used as a bridge to find common ground. He believes that this could have a ripple effect, leading to greater cooperation in the region.

Bradford was one of the UK's first places to host regular screenings of Hindi language films. Singh, who boasts over 3.6 million followers on Twitter, attributed his visit to the large concentration of South Asian diaspora residents in the area. "I was told it's been a while since anyone has visited Bradford," Singh said, "but it's a great move to come here because there are so many people interested in Hindi films." Alongside co-star Vaani Kapoor, Singh unveiled a statue of the pair based on a scene from the movie. Befikre is a romantic crime comedy set in Paris and is set to open on December 9. Bradford's long history with film was recognized by the UN in 2009 when it became the first city to be named City of Film by Unesco. Bollywood's presence in Bradford dates back almost 50 years, when it became a go-to destination for screenings for South Asian immigrant communities. Several cinemas opened specifically to show all-singing, all-dancing Bollywood movies, providing escapism for Pakistanis and Indians who'd settled in the UK. For many, these films were the only source of entertainment. Singh's father and brother were among the first men in Bradford to show these films in the cinema after importing reels from London. They would sometimes host actors of the time, such as Waheeda Rahman, Vaijayanti Mala, and Helen. Singh attended his first Bollywood premiere at just six months old. The love for Bollywood remains strong among Bradford's South Asian diaspora and has spread to other communities.
Hull FC kicked off the game with a 12-0 lead in the first half after a clever cross by Mark Minichiello and Gareth Ellis setting up Josh Bowden. However, Darnell McIntosh and Jermaine McGillvary quickly replied, with Danny Brough missing a conversion attempt. The match became a tight contest in the second half, with Marc Sneyd kicking the only points for Hull. If Hull had lost, Castleford would have secured a spot in the semi-finals without playing a match in the Super 8s. In the second half, Ellis showed off his skills after a three-month absence due to a sternum injury, creating space for Bowden to score. Sneyd successfully converted both of his attempts, while Brough failed to replicate this feat, ultimately proving costly. The match remained goalless for the majority of the second period until the 75th minute, when Sneyd opted to kick a penalty instead of running it. As a result, Hull moved a point above Wakefield Trinity, who host St Helens on Sunday. Hull FC coach Lee Radford praised his team's intense performance, saying it was not pretty but achieved their goal of securing a top-four spot after 23 rounds. He highlighted the improvement in their front-line defence, which was crucial in straining Wakefield Trinity's possession throughout the game. Radford expressed his satisfaction with the team's performance, particularly in the first half. In contrast, Huddersfield coach Rick Stone acknowledged the effort his team put in but criticized their poor execution. He noted that despite their courage, they made too many errors and struggled to complete tackles at a rate of 63%. Stone also praised Hull's proud team spirit, which played a significant role in their victory. He attributed Huddersfield's poor performance to their poor kicking and selective approach throughout the game. The match became a real arm-wrestle, with both teams having their fair share of chances but ultimately unable to score tries in the second half.
HP is suing Autonomy's former management, including Michael Lynch and Sushovan Hussain, for allegedly making "false and negligent statements" in connection with the $11 billion purchase of the software firm in 2011. The US-based company had bought Autonomy with the goal of expanding its software business, but shortly after the acquisition, HP claimed it had been misled about the true value of the firm. The Serious Fraud Office had investigated Autonomy's sale but found insufficient evidence to pursue a case, and jurisdiction was handed over to US authorities. Lynch and Hussain have consistently denied any wrongdoing. HP filed a claim form in London's High Court, seeking damages of approximately $5.1 billion. Meanwhile, Autonomy's former management has announced plans to sue HP, with Lynch's claim expected to exceed £100 million and filed in the UK. In the past, Autonomy had accused HP of "mismanagement" of the takeover and alleged that HP sought to cover up its integration issues.
Norfolk North MP Nick Lamb vowed to restore trust in the Liberal Democrats, which have been eroded in recent years. Following his party's dismal performance in the general election, Lamb announced he would stand for the leadership, despite having reduced his majority. Lamb cited the election as "absolutely punishing" and acknowledged he had been "completely exhausted" after the campaign. He stated that he decided to run for the top spot as the party needed to reconnect and find an effective advocate for modern liberalism. As health minister, Lamb demonstrated his commitment to the cause, saying he would apply the same level of dedication to the leadership role. Lamb's party had suffered a significant setback when it reneged on a pledge to oppose higher university tuition fees before the 2010 election. He voted in favour of the fee increase when he was a whip in the Conservative-Lib Dem coalition government. Lamb admitted that the party's decision had severely damaged public confidence, but he believed they had learned a valuable lesson from the experience. He vowed that the party would never make that mistake again. A two-month contest to succeed outgoing leader Nick Clegg is underway, with former president Tim Farron seen as a frontrunner. Farron, who opposed the tuition fees increase, called for his party to "turn anger into action" and rebuild from the ground up. He criticized the election, which was fought on the basis of fear, saying it was "on the politics of fear." Farron noted that the SNP's success was largely due to its opposition to the Conservative-Lib Dem coalition. Nominations for leader open on May 13 and close on June 3. Ballot papers will be sent out on June 24, and the winner will be declared on July 16.
US President Donald Trump has made the latest move in his protectionist trade agenda, aiming to overhaul the North American Free Trade Agreement and imposing tariffs on foreign products, including steel. He also withdrew from the 12-nation Trans Pacific Partnership agreement in January. As one of the US's major trade partners, South Korea has been the recipient of approximately $144.6 billion in US goods and services last year. However, the 2012 free trade agreement has failed to boost American exports, with exports declining by nearly 3% to $42.3 billion, while imports have risen by about 23%. US Trade Representative Robert Lighthizer stated, "We can and must do better." The US trade deficit with South Korea was around $17 billion last year, although services exports yielded a surplus. In a letter, the administration described South Korea as a crucial ally and key trading partner, while expressing concerns over the significant trade imbalance. Mr Trump has been more critical, labeling the agreement a "horrible deal" and a "one-way street." The administration has called for a joint session to address the issue within 30 days, although South Korean representatives were unavailable for comment.

The awards honour the best acts signed to the UK's independent record labels. Collins, best known for his work with Orange Juice and the 1995 hit A Girl Like You, received an outstanding contribution award, despite being left severely impaired by a stroke and two haemorrhages in 2005. The 53-year-old Scot was able to recover enough to record again, work as a producer, and publish a book of his bird illustrations. Collins was in attendance at the event to collect his award, which he shared with friends and collaborators Vic Godard and Frankie and the Heartstrings. Daniel Miller, founder of Mute Records, also received an honorary award at the ceremony, which was hosted by BBC DJs Huw Stephens and Steve Lamacq. The Prodigy were named the best live act, while Enter Shikari's A Flash of Colour was named independent album of the year.

Teo described himself as "tired and indignant" and lashed out at "whiners" who were demanding his attention. Notably, Teo had already offered to step down, which he did after Caldbeck left the firm in June following similar allegations. Caldbeck later tweeted an apology, acknowledging his wrongdoing. He argued that talent is universal and that choosing a partner based on gender is "grandstanding for a personal agenda." Teo also denied reports that investors were trying to buy back shares, calling the rumors "dishonorable." Only one firm has announced its intention to sever ties with Binary Capital. Teo took aim at the "entitled" individuals in Silicon Valley, asking if those who complain about being neglected truly have it tough. His email was first published by Axios and was described as "unapologetic" by journalist Erin Griffith on Fortune, who noted that it was "angry" and "barely coherent" at times.
Pilar Carbonell, head of tourism for the islands, including Mallorca and Ibiza, has urged Spain and the European Commission to set a limit on the amount of alcohol allowed in airports and on flights. This proposal was introduced in Brussels on Tuesday, following a string of high-profile incidents involving intoxicated passengers. One such incident, which occurred on a Ryanair flight from Manchester to Palma, Majorca, saw passengers report members of a stag party fighting in the aisles. Three people were subsequently arrested upon landing on the island. In a statement, Ms Carbonell stated that introducing a limit would "ensure security and tackle anti-social tourism". However, it was unclear whether the measure was specifically aimed at flights to the Balearic Islands or the wider European Union. According to Ms Carbonell, the aim of the proposal is to enhance passenger security and that of security forces in airports and planes in the islands, where they often encounter intoxicated passengers.
Lee Goddard, 40, from Hambridge, pleaded guilty to opening the sluice gate at Slabgate between December 20 and 23. During his court hearing, it was revealed that his actions led to approximately 45,000 cubic meters of water flowing towards the village of Thorney. Goddard claimed he wanted to redirect floodwater away from his property, but prosecutors argued that his actions increased water levels in Thorney, which could have indirectly caused damage to properties in the village. The private prosecution, brought by the Parrett Internal Drainage Board, alleged that Goddard breached a local by-law and the Land Drainage Act. Nick Stevens, from the drainage board, stated after the court case that such unauthorized activity would lead to chaos, contradicting the way Somerset manages flood risk. Goddard was also ordered to pay £1,500 in prosecution costs and the cost of repairing the sluice gate. In the 2013-14 winter, severe storms hit the Somerset Levels, forcing hundreds of people to evacuate their homes.
At the 2015 British Fashion Awards, Jonathan Anderson made history by being named both the menswear and womenswear designer of the year. The exhibition, Disobedient Bodies, will showcase his designs alongside those of renowned designers such as Christian Dior and Jean Paul Gaultier, as well as artworks by artists like Barbara Hepworth and Henry Moore. Anderson, 31, has always been fascinated by 20th Century British art, drawing inspiration from the likes of Moore, Hepworth, and Graham Sutherland. His designs will be paired with provocative combinations, drawing parallels with works by artists Louise Bourgeois, Sarah Lucas, and Dorothea Tanning, alongside garments by Rei Kawakubo, Helmut Lang, and Issey Miyake. Anderson expressed excitement about bringing together the works that have long influenced his creative practice, stating: "This is a rare opportunity for me to combine art and fashion in a way that mirrors our fast-paced, image-driven world, where unexpected encounters are the norm. I also want to explore the ongoing themes of gender and identity that have been central to my work." The exhibition, set to run from March 18 to June 18, 2017, reflects Anderson's mission to blur the boundaries between men's and womenswear, a trait he's also showcased through his menswear shows, such as the one he streamed on Grindr in January.
The Court of Appeal has upheld an eviction order against a group protesting over several issues, including the Afghanistan war. London Mayor Boris Johnson, who took legal action to evict the demonstrators, expressed his delight at the decision. However, the protesters' solicitors claim they won't appeal the ruling. According to protest organiser Chris Knight, this eviction is part of a broader protest movement that won't be easily stopped. The protesters transformed the central London green into a makeshift village, dubbed Democracy Village, complete with tents and flags. Critics argue that the square has been vandalised and other groups are unable to use it for protests due to concerns about public health, particularly the lack of toilet facilities. Lord Neuberger, who made the ruling, stated that while the Crown owns the land, the Mayor of London has the authority to act over the square. He noted that the protesters have been allowed to express their views and assemble freely at the chosen location for over two months. The Mayor urged the protesters to leave the site peacefully and respect the court rulings. Mayor Johnson expressed his support for peaceful protest, but also highlighted the damage being done to the lawn. He warned that police will have to handle the eviction with sensitivity, avoiding the kind of confrontations seen during the G20 protests. Last month's High Court eviction orders were delayed pending an appeal to three judges. The protesters' counsel argued that the Mayor lacked the right to evict the demonstrators since the land belongs to the Queen. The Mayor's team countered that Parliament Square Gardens is an open space where the public has a right to use it. Colin Barrow, leader of Westminster City Council, welcomed the decision, saying it will allow ordinary Londoners and visitors to use the square once again.
A new report calls for a shift away from the current guidelines, which recommend prescribing medication only when blood pressure exceeds a certain threshold. However, experts recognize that lifestyle factors also play a crucial role in reducing blood pressure. The study, published in The Lancet, has found that high blood pressure is a significant risk factor for heart disease and stroke. According to current guidelines, issued by England's National Institute for Health and Care Excellence, medication should only be prescribed when blood pressure reaches 140 mmHg. Even those at the highest risk, such as individuals who have had strokes, are typically offered monitoring but not medication. Now, a team of global experts is urging doctors to focus on an individual's specific risks rather than adhering to arbitrary blood pressure thresholds. Experts analyzed data from over 100 large-scale trials involving more than 600,000 people between 1966 and 2015. The results showed that those at the highest risk, including smokers with high cholesterol and people over 65 with diabetes, would benefit most from treatment, which would lower their risk of heart attacks and strokes. The report also suggests that once on treatment, blood pressure levels could be reduced even further than the current targets. Additionally, the study provides further evidence that patients may benefit from lowering their blood pressure, regardless of their initial levels, through lifestyle changes or medication. However, the findings also indicate that the lower a person's blood pressure to start with, the lower the benefit they gain from reducing it. While the report does not advocate for medication for everyone, it emphasizes the need to weigh the potential side effects of medication against the benefits. Prof. Liam Smeeth, of the London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine, acknowledged the importance of the findings for those at the highest risk. However, he cautioned: "One important caveat is that not everyone will be able to tolerate having their blood pressure reduced to low levels, and there is a need to balance the possible drug side effects and likely benefits." Heart specialist Dr. Tim Chico, of the University of Sheffield, added that medication is not the only way to address the issue. He said: "We can all reduce our blood pressure. We can do this safely, cheaply, and as effectively as medication by eating healthily, taking more physical activity, reducing alcohol intake, and maintaining a healthy weight."
A group of six climbers, consisting of three men and three women, were caught in the avalanche on Bidean Nam Bian at approximately 14:00. One of the male members alerted the authorities, while a sixth climber, a woman, remains in a serious condition at a hospital in Fort William. It's understood that the group was descending from a peak on the south side of the valley when the slope they were on broke away. The incident occurred near the Church Door Buttress, where the group was close to when the snow slope gave way. Five of the climbers were swept down the mountain and buried under ice and snow, while one female climber was recovered alive but sustained serious head injuries. John Grieve, leader of the Glencoe mountain rescue team, which coordinated the search, explained that the initial alarm was raised by two climbers who discovered one of the casualties lying in the snow. However, soon after, police were contacted by the male survivor from the climbing party, who reported that more people were missing. Grieve, who was not part of the search and was in Spain, said: "The first call to police was from two other people who had been on the mountain; they found someone lying next to where they were climbing." Initially, it was assumed that there was only one casualty, but the information from the man who was safe soon revealed that there were others missing. Prayers will be held for those who were killed and injured during a Sunday service at the nearby St Munda's Church. Reverend Moira Herkes described the incident as the worst accident to occur in the area for many years, saying: "It's very distressing, as people come here for pleasure, and when something like this happens, it hurts everyone involved." Scotland's First Minister Alex Salmond called it an "appalling tragedy," stating: "Our immediate thoughts and prayers are with the families of those who have been lost." He added: "To lose four people from a party of six is truly devastating." The Scottish government has promised to provide any support necessary, and Salmond thanked the police and mountain rescue team for their efforts. Anyone concerned about relatives climbing in the area is advised to contact Northern Constabulary's control room on 01463 715555.
Markus Reichel confessed to handing over more than 200 documents to the CIA over four years in exchange for at least €80,000. The Munich court heard that these documents included the real and cover names and addresses of German agents operating abroad. The 32-year-old was also found guilty of treason for giving three documents to the Russian secret service. Reichel had been working at the Federal Intelligence Service (BND) since 2007, initially in the mail room where he had access to classified documents. He also had clearance to handle top-secret information, according to the Munich-based Sueddeutsche Zeitung. During his trial, which started in November, Reichel claimed that he handed over the intelligence not only for the thrill and adventure, but also for recognition. "No-one trusted me with anything at the BND," he told the court. "At the CIA, it was different. I felt valued and respected." His department was responsible for protecting soldiers serving abroad, the Reuters news agency reported. Between 2008 and 2014, he received between €10,000 and €20,000 annually for sharing secrets at secret meeting points in Austria. Reichel used to copy sensitive documents at work, smuggle them home, scan them, and send them to his handler. After several years, he sought "something new" and offered his services to the Russian consulate in Munich. However, his email with classified documents was intercepted, leading to his exposure. In his closing remarks, Reichel apologized and asked for forgiveness, Sueddeutsche Zeitung reported. His arrest in 2014 followed strained relations between the US and Germany after former CIA agent Edward Snowden revealed the extent of US spying activities in Germany.

Valeant plans to go ahead with its plan by bypassing Allergan's board through a webcast aimed at the company's shareholders. Its goal is to become the fifth-largest pharmaceutical company by 2016. In a letter to investors, Valeant stated: "We acknowledge that yesterday we received Allergan's letter rejecting our offer. We also note that our offer was not discussed with Allergan before being rejected." Valeant's CEO, Michael Pearson, said: "We will not give up our pursuit of this merger until we hear directly from Allergan shareholders that they prefer staying with the current strategy to partnering with us." Allergan rejected the offer due to concerns about Valeant's long-term growth prospects and its business model. According to the company, Valeant's proposal poses significant risks and uncertainties for its shareholders, and its business model is unsustainable. However, Allergan's largest shareholder, hedge fund Pershing Square, which is managed by activist investor Bill Ackman, has previously expressed support for the bid.
At 29 years old, ranked 196 globally, Hadwin is the second golfer this year to break 59 on the PGA Tour, following Justin Thomas's achievement at the Sony Open in Hawaii. However, this feat is only the fourth time it has been achieved on a par-72 course. David Duval holds the record for the last player to achieve a 13-under round, which he posted at the Bob Hope Classic in 1999. Hadwin's impressive score features 13 birdies, tying Chip Beck's record from the 1991 Las Vegas Invitational. When asked about his remarkable performance, Hadwin attributed it to getting away with a few shots on the back nine and having an unusual streak of putts going in. He even considered walking off the green on 11, thinking he might have shot 59. As the tournament enters its final round on Sunday, Hadwin aims to secure his first PGA Tour win, leading Dominic Bozzelli by a single stroke. A group of eight players is close behind, within four strokes of the lead. The Australian former US Open champion Geoff Ogilvy is five strokes behind, while Scotland's Martin Laird and England's Greg Owen trail by a further shot.
BP, the oil giant, has announced it's exploring the use of existing equipment on site and offshore to process gas from the west of Shetland, reversing plans to build a "sweetening" plant. This facility, which would have employed around 300 workers in the construction phase and created about 30 full-time jobs, is now on hold. Local contractor DITT Construction, which has been preparing the terminal, is set to complete its work next month. DITT currently employs about 20 staff, with around a dozen potentially facing redundancy. Shetland councillor Andrea Manson expressed her disappointment, saying she wasn't surprised by the decision, given the current low oil prices. "I think BP is looking to cut costs wherever possible, so maybe the gas sweetening plant is a casualty of that," she stated. Manson hopes BP will focus on maintenance and repairs, rather than building new facilities, and maintain existing infrastructure, which would require increased work to keep it running.
New rules adopted by the Football Association at its Annual General Meeting this week will govern player betting on all competitions, not just those in which their team participates. Currently, players are only prohibited from betting on games involving their own club. The new regulations will prevent players from sharing confidential information with a third party who uses it to place bets. Notably, Tottenham's Andros Townsend, Cameron Jerome, who is currently on loan from Stoke to Crystal Palace, and Newcastle's Dan Gosling, who is set to join Bournemouth next season, have faced fines for breaching existing betting rules. Townsend was fined £18,000 in June 2013, Jerome £50,000 last August, and Gosling £30,000 in March. The new rules, which come into effect on August 1, cover betting on international games, as well as football-related matters like transfers, managerial appointments, and team selection. They will apply to players in the Premier League, Championship, Leagues One and Two, Conference, and top Northern, Southern, and Isthmian leagues.
Scotland held a massive march ahead of the United Nations' Paris meeting, organized by Stop Climate Chaos Scotland. The campaign is pushing for politicians to agree on reducing carbon emissions and also advocating for support for developing countries severely affected by climate change. The event began at The Meadows before moving to the Ross Bandstand in Princes Street Gardens for a rally. Despite the rain, the marchers donned bright colors and rallied around the slogan "Climate, Justice and Jobs." Approximately 5,000 people attended the event, which was also attended by Environment Minister Aileen McLeod. McLeod emphasized that Scotland is seeking an ambitious and fair climate treaty at the conference, stating that the Scottish government has set a world-leading target of a 42% emission cut by 2020 compared to 1990. Tom Ballantine, chairman of Stop Climate Chaos Scotland, a coalition of 60 organizations, said: "It's heartening to see so many people in Scotland coming together to demonstrate their concern for the world and to protect our planet." He noted that Scotland and the UK benefited greatly from the fossil fuel era, and now it's time for the country to take responsibility for the growing global climate crisis. At the upcoming United Nations talks in Paris, international leaders will attempt to agree on a deal to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Patrick Harvie MSP, co-convener of the Scottish Greens, stated that Scotland could lead the transition from fossil fuels to a low-carbon economy. He emphasized the need for a bold deal to protect society, citing the rising global temperatures and the impact on the world's poorest people. Harvie also highlighted the scientific consensus that we must leave fossil fuels in the ground to limit climate change damage. Scottish Labour's environmental justice spokeswoman, Sarah Boyack, pledged that her party in government would deliver regulatory changes to make warm homes that address climate change a 21st-century reality. Boyack warned that without strong support for low-carbon heat and power, the SNP government will struggle to tackle fuel poverty and miss out on becoming leaders in the commercially viable renewable technology.
The State of Britain's Dormice report reveals that hazel dormice are now extinct in 17 English counties. Researchers have analyzed over 100,000 records gathered across the UK over the past 25 years. The report warns that the dormouse is facing a high risk of extinction in Britain. Trained volunteers have been collecting data on these tiny creatures since 1998, making it one of the longest-running small mammal monitoring projects globally. Hazel dormice primarily inhabit hedgerows and woods, building ball-like nests from bark in summer and hibernating near the ground between October and May. Due to their elusive nature, only a small area the size of two football fields can contain four dormice, making them extremely hard to locate. A telltale sign is a nibbled hazelnut. The latest report shows a sharp decline in their population over the past 16 years, with a 40% drop. Today, their populations are restricted to the Welsh borders and southern England, with no records in Scotland or Northern Ireland. Ian White, dormouse officer at the People's Trust for Endangered Species, has been monitoring the species for the past decade and describes the decline as "dramatic." He notes: "Hazel dormice have been present on our planet for 40 million years, but their future in Britain is now precarious, and urgent action is needed to ensure their long-term survival." In 1885, dormice were found in 49 English counties; today, they are present in 32 counties, excluding those where they have been reintroduced. Mr. White attributes the decline to several key factors, including the loss of woodland and hedgerows, fragmented habitats, and changes in farmland and woodland management, which hinder their dispersal and survival. Additionally, they are vulnerable to changes in the weather, particularly wetter springs and summers, and warmer winters that disrupt successful hibernation. Mr. White comments: "This is a sad tale, but the raw data is indeed gloomy, however, I believe there is a lot of interest in finding solutions to this problem and a great deal of enthusiasm from the public to help." Efforts to reintroduce hazel dormice to the wild have been underway, with over 850 animals released at 26 sites. While some sites have shown promising results, such as breeding or dispersal to new areas, others have seen the population die. The People's Trust for Endangered Species monitors 400 sites regularly and builds a national dataset from public submissions,

However, the Windsor council claims it was constructed without adhering to the planning permission granted in 2014, and it had ignored the council's requests for changes. BAE is set to carry out 17 flights, utilizing a Jetstream 31 aircraft that can fly itself. Human pilots will be in charge of takeoff and landing, but the plane will operate autonomously during its 300-mile (482km) journey from Lancashire to Inverness. The trial aims to assess the performance of piloting software and sensor systems that monitor clouds and other aircraft. To navigate on its own, the Jetstream aircraft relies on data from satellites and onboard identification systems that track radio signals emitted by other planes. It also features a camera that can detect other air users, even if they're not broadcasting warning signals. This camera not only scans the skies for other planes but also identifies bad weather or heavy cloud, allowing the aircraft to adjust its route to avoid turbulence and other challenging conditions. During its autonomous flight, the aircraft will follow a non-congested route at an altitude of about 15,000ft (4.6km), with the journey expected to take around 90 minutes. Maureen McCue, head of BAE's research and technology, described the trials as "exciting" and noted that the company is collaborating with the UK's National Air Traffic Services to evaluate how well the autonomous aircraft handles sharing the skies with human-piloted craft. She said the company is working towards the possibility of operating its own unmanned systems in a highly controlled environment in the UK.
The body of a 20-year-old woman who went missing in April was found at a location provided by a 32-year-old man. The man admitted to abandoning the body, but police suspect he may have killed her, although they have not made any charges. The case is likely to fuel discontent among Okinawa residents over the large US military presence and crimes by personnel. It comes at a time when US President Barack Obama is set to visit Japan for a G7 summit next week. The man, Kenneth Franklin Shinzato, was a contractor at the US Kadena Air Base. Prime Minister Abe expressed his strong anger, saying he had no words to describe the feelings of the family. "We urge the US to take decisive measures to prevent such incidents from happening again," he added. Foreign Minister Fumio Kishida also summoned the US ambassador, Caroline Kennedy, to formally protest the incident. "I felt strong indignation and told her that an incident like this is unacceptable," he said. The US ambassador promised to cooperate fully with the Japanese authorities and pledged to double efforts to prevent such a tragedy from occurring again. However, Japan's ambassador to the US, Kenichiro Sasae, argued that the tragic event should not overshadow the fundamental objective of the alliance. The US currently has around 50,000 military personnel in Japan, with about half based in Okinawa. Prime Minister Abe recently agreed to suspend construction work on relocating a US Marine Corps base from one part of the island to another, following years of opposition from locals. Many locals want the base removed entirely, as they believe the relocation will harm the environment and associate US bases with accidents and crime.
The F-Rating was first introduced by Holly Tarquini, director of the Bath Film Festival, in 2014 and has since been adopted by over 40 UK cinemas and festivals. Col Needham, the boss of IMDb, praised the F-Rating, saying it's a great way to highlight women on screen and behind the camera. Tarquini noted that over 21,800 films have been tagged with the F-Rating on IMDb, which attracts more than 250 million visitors each month. Tarquini's goal is to encourage people to discuss the representation of women in film, both on and off screen. She believes that by increasing awareness, the film industry can move closer to providing equal opportunities for women. Ultimately, the F-Rating aims to become redundant as 50% of films on screen are told from the perspective of women, a demographic that has historically been underrepresented in the industry. The F-Rating is a classification for any film that meets specific criteria, which include having stories told by and about women. The Barbican Cinema in London has recently joined the campaign, with curator Gali Gold describing the rating as a "simple and effective way" to highlight films with female protagonists and address the issue of gender imbalance in the industry. Anna Navas from the Plymouth Arts Centre added the F-Rating as soon as she heard about it, citing its clarity and accessibility as reasons for its appeal. The concept behind the F-Rating is inspired by the "Bechdel Test," a comic strip created by Alison Bechdel in 1985 that asks whether a film features at least two women who talk to each other about something other than a man. Some films have all three criteria met, with significant women on screen and in writing and directing roles.
Many Labour MPs see Momentum as a faction within their party, a front for the far-left, and a means to oust those opposed to Jeremy Corbyn's revolution. But who are these individuals and what do they want? I met a group of Momentum supporters in Brixton, who don't fit the typical hard-left stereotype. They lacked the stereotypical Trotskyite beards and didn't try to sell me a newspaper. What unites Steve, Samira, Nadine, and Roland is their enthusiasm for Corbyn's leadership campaign. They are Corbyn's foot soldiers, having joined the Labour Party shortly after his victory. Steve, a self-proclaimed "middle-aged BBC Radio 4 addict," joined the party "about 20 seconds" after Corbyn's win. Samira also joined after Corbyn's victory, saying for the first time she felt she could stand behind someone she could trust. Momentum currently boasts around 60,000 supporters, charges no membership fee, organizes mostly through social media, and has approximately 50 local groups across the UK. Its declared aim is to create a mass movement for real progressive change. However, the fact that anyone can join Momentum has raised concerns that it may be vulnerable to infiltration by far-left groups like the Socialist Workers Party (SWP) and the Trade Unionist and Socialist Coalition (TUSC), who are opposed to the Labour Party. Labour MP Emma Reynolds describes Momentum as a "parallel organisation to the Labour Party," saying it has questions to answer. "What is it they're seeking to do?" she asks. "Who will be involved?" Reynolds suspects that Momentum may be working to de-select MPs and force them out of the party. Some far-left groups, like the TUSC, have already expressed interest in collaborating with Momentum. TUSC chairman Dave Nellist says Momentum was established to provide Corbyn with an alternative powerbase to pursue a left-wing agenda and "democratise" the Labour Party. This could involve making the party conference the supreme policy-making body, restoring Clause 4, committing to public ownership of industry, and re-introducing mandatory re-selection, a tactic seen as a means to purge the party of "moderate" MPs. The issue of re-selection has caused anxiety among Labour MPs. Nadine insists that Momentum is not planning to de-select sitting MPs, but rather that there may be opportunities for MPs to step down due to boundary changes and retirements. It's clear that Momentum wants to bring about change, creating a broad anti-austerity movement outside the Labour
First Minister Carwyn Jones told the assembly that ministers will proceed with the £600m Metro project despite warnings it may take longer to complete. However, he acknowledged that there are no guarantees the UK government will protect EU funding, crucial for the scheme. Welsh Conservative leader Andrew RT Davies accused Mr Jones of using Brexit as a "smokescreen" for the government's lack of ideas. Later, Economy Secretary Ken Skates revealed that construction on the metro will start from 2019, with services launching as soon as possible, depending on the solution. The Metro aims to improve trains, buses, and light rail services in Cardiff and the valleys. Last year, it was announced that £600m would be spent on the current phase, with £125m coming from the UK government and £150m from European regional development funding. In a statement on his government priorities, Mr Jones stated that despite uncertainty over EU funding, his government will "press ahead" with the development. "Although the form may change, we will explore alternative funding models and it may take longer to reach our goal," he said. Mr Jones initially planned to publish a detailed programme for the government's five-year term before the summer recess but is delaying it until September due to Brexit. He reiterated his call for the UK government to guarantee replacing the EU funding currently enjoyed by Wales, estimated at around £600m a year. Without this assurance, Wales faces a significant funding gap in its future budgets, he argued. However, Welsh Conservative leader Andrew RT Davies countered that Mr Jones should focus on laying down his Programme for Government instead of using Brexit as an excuse. Mr Davies claimed that Mr Jones's statement failed to mention the steel crisis among the government's priorities. Economy Secretary Ken Skates later clarified that construction on the Metro will begin in 2019, with services launching as soon as possible, depending on the solution. Earlier, Welsh Secretary Alun Cairns suggested that tackling the root causes of economic problems in poorer parts of Wales would be more effective than replacing EU grants with Westminster cash.
Ethan McKen, 24, from Nottingham, was tragically struck by a wave while on holiday at a resort in Bulgaria on August 8. A moment of silence was observed at all Notts Senior League and Central Midlands league matches in tribute to him. McKen was a defender for Bulwell FC's reserve team and had also taught physical education at several primary schools in the Ashfield district of Nottinghamshire. Ben Moore, manager of Bulwell FC reserves, praised McKen, saying that the league's moment of silence was a mark of respect from the teams he had played for. "He was a determined and confident young man who made a lasting impact on our team in his short time with us," Moore added. Darrell Hatton, captain of Bulwell FC reserves, described McKen as "a talented player" who shocked the team with his untimely passing. Friends remembered McKen as a kind and intelligent individual who had achieved academic success, earning a first-class honours degree in sports science and management from Nottingham Trent University.
Spoken by Spike Lee, the acclaimed director of 'Do the Right Thing,' the former head of 40 Acres and a Mule film production company shared his thoughts on the disparity in opportunities for black people in the film industry. Speaking at a ceremony in Los Angeles, Lee expressed his concern that being a black person is easier in the White House than it is in the leadership of a major film studio. At the Governors Awards, Lee received an honorary award, while actresses Debbie Reynolds and Gena Rowlands also received accolades. However, Reynolds was unable to attend due to health reasons, so her award was accepted by her granddaughter, Billie Lourd. Lee, who received his award from actors Denzel Washington, Samuel L. Jackson, and Wesley Snipes, used his acceptance speech to address the racial imbalance he perceives in his industry. He noted that many in the audience likely voted for Barack Obama, but when he visited studios, he rarely saw black people except for the security guard who checked his name off the list. Lee urged a serious discussion about diversity and the need for more black representation. He has been nominated for Oscars twice, for writing 'Do the Right Thing' in 1990 and for his documentary '4 Little Girls' in 1997. Rowlands, who received her award from her director son, Nick Cassavetes, has also been nominated twice for her films 'A Woman Under the Influence' in 1974 and 'Gloria' in 1980. When asked about her award, she joked, "I think I'll take him home with me. I know a wonderful place for him to sit." Actresses Cate Blanchett and Laura Linney paid tribute to Rowlands before she received her award. The recipients of this year's awards will be invited to present awards at next year's Oscars. Meanwhile, the recent attacks in Paris that left 129 people dead weighed heavily on the minds of those in attendance. Director Danny Boyle expressed his sadness, saying, "It's tragic, absolutely tragic. The suffering of innocent people is just terrible." Rapper-turned-film producer Ice Cube also condemned the violence, stating, "I don't understand how that helps any cause, killing innocent people." Lee revealed that his daughter Satchel had been studying in Paris before the attacks but had returned to the US before they occurred.
Food Utopia announced it is consulting on closing Avana Bakeries in Rogerstone by the end of January. The bakery, which has been struggling in a highly competitive market, cited "insufficient progress" as the reason. Staff were informed of the proposal on Wednesday. Food Utopia stated that the site has been operating at a loss and is "financially unsustainable". The Bakers, Food and Allied Workers Union said a 45-day consultation period will begin on Thursday. In a statement, Food Utopia said it would explore all options and seek external support as it discusses the bakery's future. The company acknowledged the proposal's impact on employees, stating it would focus on supporting them during this challenging time. Rogerstone councillor Chris Evans expressed concern, saying the bakery has been a vital part of the community for decades and will be felt throughout south east Wales. A Welsh Government spokesperson expressed concern for affected staff, noting the timing is particularly difficult. He pledged to work with the employer and partners to provide support for the employees. Food Utopia took over Avana Bakeries in 2014 after its previous owners threatened to cut 650 jobs due to losing a contract with Marks and Spencer. At the time, the bakery employed 552 people, and in the following month, it was reported that Food Utopia planned to cut 390 jobs.

A blaze at a rental property in Mill Hill, a town in the UK, broke out at around 04:30 GMT on Saturday without causing any injuries. The fire quickly spread to two adjacent family homes, destroying the rental property and damaging the other two. A family was able to escape one of the houses after a neighbour alerted them. The family lost several possessions, including Christmas presents for their children. Thirty-five firefighters and seven appliances were deployed to tackle the blaze. It's thought to have started accidentally, possibly due to smoking materials. Upon arrival, firefighters found a well-developed fire in a first-floor bedroom, and the occupant had already left the premises. To prevent any further harm, the crew began evacuating adjoining houses. Fortunately, the fire was contained to the rental property, and the cause is believed to be accidental. Due to the potential structural damage, council officials are currently cordoning off the affected properties.
Twitter is ending support for its short-video app Vine, which it acquired in 2012 for $30m. The decision comes after the company announced it was cutting 9% of its workforce due to slow growth. In a blog post, Twitter stated that the Vine mobile app will be discontinued, but users can still access and download their existing videos. The website will remain online, allowing users to watch all the Vines that have been created. Twitter had tried to limit video content to six-second loops, but it couldn't compete with other platforms like Snapchat and Instagram. Analyst Ian Fogg says that Twitter's attempt to replicate short video content was a mistake. The company may consider integrating Vine-like features into its main app, but it's unclear if this will happen. Vine still has a dedicated fan base, but its content is often shared on other platforms like Facebook.
Cardiff Foodbank distributed 14,568 three-day food supplies in 2016-17, with over a third of these going to children. The figure increased by 11% across Wales, with 43% of recipients citing problems with benefits as the main reason. The Department for Work and Pensions described the reasons for food bank use as complex, cautioning against linking them to a single issue. Statistics from the Trussell Trust food bank network showed a rise in emergency food parcels handed out across the UK. In Wales, the number of emergency food parcels increased from 85,656 in 2015-16 to 95,190 in 2016-17. The top three reasons given for food bank referrals in Wales were delays in benefit payments, low income, and changes to benefits. Cardiff Foodbank echoed the concerns of other Trussell Trust food banks in areas where Universal Credit was being rolled out, citing the six-week wait for a first payment as a potential cause of debt, mental health issues, and rent arrears. The trust welcomed a co-ordinated approach to Universal Credit rollout in some areas, such as Torfaen, where local authorities invited food banks and charities to form working groups ahead of the rollout. Wales was the first nation to complete Universal Credit rollout for single jobseekers and is now starting with couples and families. Tony Graham, director of devolved nations at the Trussell Trust, said the trust was monitoring the Scottish Government's plans to ease Universal Credit's effects and welcomed the possibility of discussing how to support people in Wales during the rollout's next phase. Cardiff Foodbank's partnership and fundraising manager, Helen Bull, expressed concern about the increase in emergency food supplies provided to local people in crisis. She noted that anyone could find themselves in need of food bank assistance, citing examples of people being hit by unavoidable circumstances such as illness, delayed benefit payments, or unexpected bills. A spokesman for the Department for Work and Pensions reiterated that the reasons for food bank use were complex and cautioned against linking them to a single issue. He added that the department worked closely with local authorities to provide support, including budgeting assistance, benefit advances, and direct rent payments to landlords.
A 34-year-old tennis star, the 17-time Grand Slam champion from Switzerland, was set to make his comeback on Friday after suffering a knee injury in January while running for his twin daughters. The injury required arthroscopic surgery to repair a torn meniscus. His spot in the tournament was taken by Argentine Horacio Zeballos, a lucky loser. Federer's last match was his Australian Open semi-final loss to world number one Novak Djokovic. Federer revealed on Thursday that he had spent 12 days on crutches following his surgery and had only begun full training about a week ago. "If I feel anything, I won't play," he said, but just 24 hours later, it was a virus, not the knee, that forced him to withdraw. Federer's original comeback plan had been to focus on the clay-court season in Europe next month, and that now appears to be his likely schedule.

Halani Aulika scored a consolation try for London Irish in the final minutes of the game. The match was watched by new England head coach Eddie Jones.

It's also the one-year anniversary of his plumbing business, and England prop Hill, a destructive forward in rugby league, has found a new passion in combining his duties as Wire captain with running Premier Plumbing Ltd. Hill, 28, has always been interested in plumbing, and it's something he can do after rugby. "It's a hobby I've always enjoyed, and I think it's something that will keep me busy after rugby," he told BBC Sport. "It's a great way to take my mind off the sport and hopefully, it will be running smoothly by then, and I can sit back and relax." Hill approached player welfare manager Karl Fitzpatrick about learning the trade and started a three-year course around his training and game commitments. He co-owns the company with partner Christian Froggat, whom he met during his training. Froggat handles the manual labor, while Hill focuses on administration. They both work on quotes on their free evenings. Hill joked that Froggat often teases him about still having tools with the packaging on. With training taking up only a small part of the day, Hill generally has afternoons and evenings to himself. When asked about Warrington coach Tony Smith's thoughts on his job, Hill said, "He thinks it's a great idea because it can take your mind off rugby league, which makes you a better player." Hill highly recommends it, as it allows you to focus on something else and even helps you concentrate on rugby. Hill added that it gives you life skills, particularly in dealing with people. Hill couldn't have envisioned a better start to his career when he moved to Warrington from Leigh in 2012. The Wolves won the Challenge Cup for the third time in four years, and he was named player of the season, players' player of the season, and vice president's award winner in a sweep of Warrington's awards night. While it hasn't been as rewarding since, Hill is focused on making this visit to Wembley a winning one. "I banned the Wembley word last week," he told BBC Radio Merseyside. "All my family has been preparing for the trip, but I'm trying to stay out of it."
Rollins, a 25-year-old Olympic gold medalist, missed a test to attend her hometown's 'Brianna Rollins Day' in September. Her 100m hurdles victory in Rio was marred by a doping ban until December 18. As a result, she will miss the World Championships in London in August. Rollins expressed her frustration in a statement on Instagram, saying, "This is one of the most difficult times in my career, especially after having such a great 2016 season." The United States Anti-Doping Agency (Usada) stated that Rollins failed to properly file her whereabouts information for drug testers. According to World Anti-Doping Agency rules, athletes cannot miss three tests within a 12-month period. Rollins had missed one test in April 2016 due to travel and two others in September - one when she visited the White House and the other when she returned to Florida. Usada ruled that her results from September 27, the date of her third whereabouts failure, would be disqualified, allowing Rollins to keep her Olympic medal won in August. Usada noted that this case presents a difficult situation, as it involves imposing a serious penalty on a talented athlete without any evidence of banned substance use.
Actor Shia LaBeouf was set to star alongside Alec Baldwin and Tom Sturridge in the March 19 previews of the play Orphans. However, the production was put on hold due to "creative differences" cited by the producers. LaBeouf later shared emails suggesting he and Baldwin had a difficult time working together. The story, set in Philadelphia, revolves around two orphaned brothers who kidnap a wealthy man, played by Baldwin. According to the Hollywood Reporter, director Daniel Sullivan expressed concerns over LaBeouf's "performance choices." LaBeouf posted a video of himself auditioning for the role, as well as emails between Sullivan, Baldwin, and himself. LaBeouf apologized to Baldwin, describing the situation as "disagreeable." Sullivan responded with a message saying he was too old for disagreements and that LaBeouf was "incompatible" with Baldwin. He also mentioned that he should have known better and that the experience would haunt him. Another email showed Baldwin reassuring LaBeouf that he had nothing but kind words for him. LaBeouf later tweeted an email from Tom Sturridge, who was perplexed by the situation. Sturridge praised LaBeouf's performance, saying it elevated the play beyond its potential. The revival of Kessler's 1983 play is set to open officially on April 7 at the Schoenfeld Theatre, although producers plan to announce a replacement for LaBeouf soon.
Parking charges at Glan Clwyd Hospital in Bodelwyddan have been cancelled following the end of an external contract. This move follows a promise made by the Welsh Assembly Government that patients would be able to park for free at almost every NHS hospital in Wales by the end of 2011. For hospitals where private companies manage parking, the cost-cutting measures will come to an end once contracts expire. At Glan Clwyd, the changes coincide with a new parking system designed to make it easier for patients and visitors to find a spot. Under the new system, patients and visitors will still be able to park in the front of the hospital near main entrances, with additional parking spaces available on the right-hand side of the access road. Special designated parking areas for renal, cancer, and maternity units will continue without time limits or charges. The hospital's accessible parking arrangements for the disabled will not be affected by the changes. New signs will be installed to guide visitors to these designated areas. The remaining parking spaces will be available to staff. A hospital spokesperson said, "We acknowledge that the new system will take time to settle in. Our immediate priority is to minimize disruptions to patients and visitors while the new arrangements are implemented."
Monitor launched an investigation into The Christie NHS Foundation Trust on March 8. The investigation was prompted by concerns over the trust's governance. Stephen Hay, managing director of provider regulation at Monitor, stated that the trust's care provision had not raised any concerns. However, Hay did express that the trust's governance issues were a serious matter. The Christie has chosen not to comment on the findings. Hay explained that the trust's board was not operating effectively, citing concerns about its governance standards. He emphasized that the trust's care provision was not a concern, but rather the need for a well-run organization. The investigation was triggered by the resignation of the trust's chairman, Lord Bradley, in February. The trust's chief executive, Caroline Shaw, had been placed on suspension since November as part of an internal investigation. Shaw was not implicated in Monitor's inquiries. The investigation led to the appointment of Sir Hugh Taylor, chair of Guy's and St Thomas' NHS Foundation Trust, as the interim chairman of The Christie. Monitor has the authority under the Health and Social Care Act 2012 to make senior appointments if it deems them in the best interests of patients. The trust had faced allegations of breaching its licence to provide healthcare services due to governance issues. Monitor had expressed concerns that the trust's board had not adhered to corporate governance standards. The Christie has declined to comment on Monitor's findings but welcomed its new chairman. In a statement, the hospital expressed hope that Sir Hugh's appointment would bring a swift and satisfactory resolution to the situation. The trust assured that patient care remained unaffected by the ongoing issues, stating that its activities continued to meet high standards. Manchester MP John Leech described the situation as a "mess," emphasizing the need to prioritize patient care. Patient Kathryn Wood, who received treatment for cancer at The Christie, expressed her gratitude for the hospital's care, stating that it "saved her life." The Christie treats over 40,000 patients annually.
A military source told the Yonhap news agency that every part of Pyongyang will be completely destroyed by ballistic missiles and high-explosives shells. Yonhap, which has close ties to South Korea's government and is publicly funded, has been a key source of information. On Friday, North Korea carried out what it claimed was its fifth and largest nuclear test. The international community is weighing its response. The US has said it is considering its own sanctions, in addition to any imposed by the UN Security Council, Japan, and South Korea. Pyongyang responded by calling the threats of "meaningless sanctions... highly laughable". A South Korean military official told Yonhap that districts in Pyongyang thought to be hiding the North's leadership would be targeted in any attack, with the city set to be "reduced to ashes and removed from the map". The BBC's Korea correspondent Steve Evans says South Korea is using the same aggressive rhetoric that North Korea frequently uses about the South Korean government in Seoul. He notes that there has been growing criticism within South Korea of the government's efforts to isolate the North, which have failed to deter leader Kim Jong-un's nuclear ambitions. News of South Korea's planned attack on the North is believed to have been revealed to parliament following Friday's nuclear test. The US's special envoy for North Korea, Sung Kim, has said that Washington is considering taking unilateral action against Pyongyang. "North Korea continues to pose a growing threat to the region, our allies, and ourselves," Kim said. "We will do everything possible to defend against this threat. In addition to sanctions in the Security Council, the US, Japan, and South Korea will be looking at unilateral measures, bilateral measures, and possible trilateral cooperation." North Korea is banned by the UN from any tests of nuclear or missile technology and has been hit by five sets of UN sanctions since its first test in 2006. The North claimed Friday's test was of a "nuclear warhead standardised to be mounted on strategic ballistic rockets". Estimates of the explosive yield of the latest blast have varied, with South Korea's military saying it was around 10 kilotonnes, enough to make it the North's "strongest nuclear test ever". Other experts believe the yield could be as high as 20 kilotonnes or more. For comparison, the nuclear bomb dropped by the US on Hiroshima in 1945 had a yield of about 15 kilotonnes.
Wales will secure a spot in the quarter-finals if they defeat Australia, with Warren Gatland's team set to face the Wallabies on October 10. According to kicking coach Jenkins, his team's goal is to win the match, regardless of the outcome against England. "Our focus is on the match against Australia next Saturday, whatever happens tomorrow," he explained. "I'm not saying I'm rooting for the Australians. "What will be, will be, so whatever happens, we'll just have to see it unfold. We need to turn up next Saturday and win, as simple as that." With England and Australia also in the running for the quarter-finals, the possibilities are complex. If England beats Australia, the hosts will likely be favourites due to their easier final game against Uruguay. Meanwhile, a bonus-point victory for England would make the match between Australia and Wales, which takes place on the same day, crucial in determining the other team to qualify from the pool. There is a scenario where Wales and England could finish on the same points, with Wales advancing due to their previous win against England. If Australia loses to England and then beats Wales, points difference would come into play, potentially allowing all three teams to finish on the same score. Jenkins, who holds the record for the first player to score 1,000 points in international rugby, will be watching the England-Australia game from home, despite the Wales management team choosing not to watch. "Whatever happens, we're still looking forward to the game next week because it's an important match for us," he said. "We want to win, top the group, and secure a spot as group winners."
Tidal Lagoon Power has revealed to BBC Wales that on the same day an independent review was ordered, the company received a letter from a minister. The UK government won't comment on the letter, but Chief Executive Mark Shorrock said that the cost of the Swansea lagoon could now be spread over 90 years, rather than 35 years. The project, which aims to build six tidal lagoons along Britain's coastline, could generate 8% of the UK's electricity and have a lifespan of 120 years. The company also plans to develop lagoons at five other sites, including three in Wales. Shorrock believes the review will show that tidal energy makes sense for the UK and claims the government is prepared to consider long-term funding for the project and future lagoons. He received a letter from Energy Secretary Amber Rudd on the day the announcement was made, which he says indicates the government's willingness to look at long-term funding. The review, which started this spring, will assess the company's information and negotiate the electricity strike price. Shorrock welcomes the review, saying it will validate the existence of cheap power from UK sources, such as the Severn and Liverpool Bay tides. The review is expected to report in the autumn. Building work on the Swansea lagoon has been delayed by a year due to disagreements over public funding. The company had called for a decision on a guaranteed energy price to be made by next month, but the review won't provide any answers until later in 2016 at the earliest. Energy Minister Lord Bourne said the review will bring clarity on the project's cost-effectiveness and its impact on energy bills.
