Residents of Eglwyswrw, near Cardigan, are hoping it will finally rain soon, as they are currently on track to break a record set in Scotland in 1923, which lasted 89 days. The village has been plagued by rain for nearly a month, with some forecasters predicting dry weather on Saturday. John Davies, a local farmer and Pembrokeshire councillor, said that the prolonged rain has been "grinding" and that he and his fellow villagers are eager to see an end to it. "We're still in the running, but hopefully, this will be the last day," he said. "We don't want to be famous for this record, especially since it's not good for the locals or the livestock." Sales of rain gear have been brisk for shopkeeper Brian Llewelyn, who noted that the rain has made it difficult for people to get about without proper footwear. "It's a bit of an ill wind that brings no good at all," he said. "You can't survive without a good pair of wellies in the last two months." The continued rain has been "depressing" for him and other local businesses, such as builders and farmers, who have had to bring their livestock indoors. While the Met Office doesn't keep official records for consecutive days of rain, its weather stations do record rain data, and the nearby automated Whitechurch station has shown rain every day since October 26. The village's location near the Preseli Hills has contributed to the high rainfall, which is part of a larger pattern of mild and wet weather this winter. In fact, Wales's December rainfall has only been surpassed once before, in November 1929. Davies added, "We're a hardy bunch here in Eglwyswrw, but even we can't handle endless rain. It's been, all jokes aside, quite grinding."
Peaty, 22, broke the 26-second barrier in the 100m event, finishing in 25.95 seconds to retain his World Championship title in Hungary. "I think I can shave about 0.3 seconds off my start time," he said in an interview with BBC East Midlands Today. The Loughborough-based swimmer had already secured the 100m title and took silver in the 100m mixed medley, bringing his total World Championship medal count to six - five golds and a silver. Peaty expressed confidence that he would eventually break the 57-second barrier in the 100m, his long-term goal. He was surprised by his new 50m world record, which he set just hours after also breaking the record in the morning heats. Peaty is currently focused on "Project 56," his goal of becoming the first man to break the 57-second barrier in the 100m. He has no intention of setting limits on his speed, saying "25.9 seconds is outrageous." Peaty admitted he was confident but not overconfident, noting that the next fastest swimmer is 0.6 seconds behind the 50m world record. Peaty emphasized the importance of unwinding after intense training. "The training regime is extremely demanding," he said. "It's crucial for me to relax now and enjoy my free time, whether that's going on holiday, partying, or celebrating. I need to come back next year with a clear head."

A new report from the World Health Organization (WHO) reveals that significantly increasing food prices by 20% or more can lead to lower sugar consumption and improved nutrition. This aligns with the global health group's previous advice to limit sugar intake, although it has not endorsed implementing taxes on sugary products. Several countries, including Mexico and Hungary, have already introduced taxes on added sugar products. South Africa is set to introduce a sugar tax next year, making it the only African country to do so. The WHO aims to reduce "free sugar" consumption, which it believes will lower the incidence of obesity, diabetes, and tooth decay. "Free sugars" encompass all types of sugar in the diet, excluding those naturally found in fruit and milk. According to Dr. Francesco Branca, the WHO's nutrition director, people do not need any sugar in their diet from a nutritional standpoint. He recommends limiting sugar intake to 10% or less of a person's total calorie intake, with an ideal limit of 5%. However, the group suggests that taxes or other financial measures should only be applied to items for which healthier alternatives are available. The report is based on a meeting of global experts and a review of available evidence. It also found that government subsidies for fruit and vegetables, which reduce prices, can increase consumption. Moreover, the same tax measures applied to sugary drinks appear to be effective in reducing consumption of other sugary foods, as well as those high in saturated fats, trans fats, and salt.
A decorative gourd was once believed to contain a handkerchief dipped in the king's blood, following his execution by guillotine in 1793. However, scientists have now sequenced the genome of the sample, and their findings suggest it's unlikely to be the monarch's. The study, published in Scientific Reports, proposes that the gourd and its contents were probably created by an 18th-century con artist seeking to profit from a revolutionary souvenir. According to Prof. Carles Lalueza-Fox from the Institute of Evolutionary Biology in Barcelona, Spain, the guillotine was used frequently during the French Revolution, making it easier for someone to approach the scaffold when non-royal victims were being executed. "They may have thought that the blood wouldn't be easily recognizable as belonging to the king," he said. The blood-stained cloth was stored in a hollowed-out gourd, which had been in the hands of an Italian family for over a century. The squash is decorated with images of revolutionary heroes and inscribed with text claiming it was dipped in Louis XVI's blood after his decapitation. Early forensic tests suggested the possibility of the king's blood, but the new research casts doubt on this theory. To investigate, the scientists examined stretches of the sample's genome related to physical appearance, finding that the individual likely had brown eyes, unlike the blue eyes depicted in portraits of Louis XVI. "The probability of this person having blue eyes is very low, around 3%," Prof. Lalueza-Fox noted. Historical records, including correspondence from Louis XVI's wife, Marie-Antoinette, also stated that the king was exceptionally tall, with an average height of around 167cm (5ft 5in) at the time. However, the King was believed to be at least 185cm (6ft 1in) tall. Prof. Lalueza-Fox said, "While we don't know the full genetic basis of height, we do know the genetic markers associated with extreme cases. This sample doesn't show an excess of markers that could be linked to someone as tall as the king." The scientists also analyzed genetic ancestry, which shows the individual's ancestral roots tracing mainly back to France and Italy, whereas many of Louis XVI's ancestors came from Germany and Poland. The team concludes that it's extremely unlikely that this is Louis XVI's blood, supporting another study published last year that compared the sample to two living relatives of the king and cast doubt on its authenticity.

William Thomas Cosgrave served as Ireland's first prime minister, or President of the Executive Council, from 1922 to 1932. He led the newly independent state through its tumultuous early years following the Irish War of Independence from Britain. His headstone at Goldenbridge Cemetery in Inchicore was smashed, causing significant damage to the other 12 headstones, which date back to Victorian and pre-Victorian times. Unfortunately, this was not the first time Cosgrave's burial plot had been targeted, as his grave had been vandalized in 2014 as well. Cosgrave came to power during the Irish Civil War, a bitter conflict between those who supported the 1921 Anglo-Irish Treaty and the creation of a Free State, and those who wanted a new republic completely independent from Britain. Cosgrave fought for independence in the 1916 Easter Rising rebellion, but later supported the treaty and fiercely defended the new Free State. Under his leadership, 77 republican prisoners were executed during the Civil War, far more than the 16 rebels formally executed by the British after the 1916 rebellion. Cosgrave's legacy has been divisive, with some viewing him as ruthless and others as a strong leader and a principal architect of Irish independence. He died in 1965 and was buried at Goldenbridge Cemetery after a state funeral. His son, Liam Cosgrave, who also served as prime minister from 1973 to 1977, visited the grave to see the damage firsthand and a family statement said the incident was "regrettable" for all those affected. The cemetery, maintained by the Glasnevin Trust, has faced serious anti-social behavior in recent years, with drug addicts and dealers using it to hide drugs. The chairman of the charity, John Green, stated that the attack seemed like a coincidence with the publication of a biography of Cosgrave, but the motive behind the latest incident remains unclear. He said the charity did not have evidence suggesting the damage was politically motivated, despite the centenary of the Easter Rising later this month. The chairman also mentioned that a grave belonging to a child killed during the Easter Rising was targeted in the attack and that the trust was viewing it as vandalism.

Williams, visibly emotional, spoke to the media after the match, saying: "I couldn't have scripted it any better. I managed just to get outside Barnes and cross the line. I'd like to thank everyone at the Millennium Stadium for making it happen." Williams, who was making his 87th and final Wales appearance, admitted to being more nervous than ever before. Despite scoring his 58th try for Wales in injury time, he said: "For me personally, I was more excited than I've ever been for a game, certainly more nervous. I found it difficult sleeping last Friday night and was up at 0530-0600. But I knew there were a lot of eyes on me, and I didn't want the game to be about me – I wanted it to be about Wales winning." Williams praised his teammates for their effort, saying: "We were under the cosh from the start, but we were playing one of the best teams in the world, and the effort was there. We found it really difficult when we were down to 14 men, but we never gave up. That's the kind of spirit I like to see in this Welsh side."

London's mayor is pushing for a ban on pedicabs after he lacks the authority to do so himself. He's seeking government legislation to restrict their operation. This move has been welcomed by the Licensed Taxi Drivers Association, which notes that the number of private hire drivers increased significantly in London in 2014, partly due to the rise of cheaper car booking apps like Uber. The mayor has been advocating for a ban since December 2012, citing concerns over safety and road congestion. In the West End, pedicabs are a common sight, particularly at night, but their popularity is not shared by everyone. While some people enjoy the experience, the authorities have long sought to ban them due to safety concerns and the fact that some operators charge excessive prices. The pedicabs' operation is technically allowed under the metropolitan public carriage act of 1869, which classifies them as stage carriages rather than Hackney cabs. To ban them, the mayor would need to revise this legislation, a process that will take time and may involve legal challenges.
Lindsay Newell, a resident of Burton-upon-Trent, was found guilty of neglecting several animals at the Burton Wildlife Rescue and Animal Centre in Etwall, Derbyshire. At Stafford Magistrates' Court, she was sentenced to a two-year conditional discharge, barred from caring for certain animals, and ordered to pay £2,000 in costs. The judge ruled that Newell's neglect was not intentional, but rather a result of her efforts to help animals, which put a significant strain on her finances and led to her losing her job as a veterinary nurse. Newell had spent £300 a week on food and straw for the animals, which she could no longer afford. Despite being cleared of 25 charges related to failing to feed the animals, the court found that she had not monitored the weight loss of six animals, including a pig nicknamed Babe, who had been rescued from a Derby council house and had made national headlines. The pig's death during a 2012 raid by RSPCA officers and police was witnessed by the judge. Newell's defence lawyer argued that she was now determined to dedicate her life to running the sanctuary, but the court imposed a five-year ban on her from keeping sheep, goats, pigs, and equine animals. The rescue centre remains open, housing hundreds of animals, including cats, pigeons, chickens, rabbits, and crows. The RSPCA spent £20,000 investigating the case and brought Newell to court, and she will have to pay an additional £2,000 in costs.

Dr William Stewart testified at the High Court in Glasgow, where the trial of Steven Jackson and Michelle Higgins was underway. The couple denies murdering and dismembering Kimberley MacKenzie, who was found dead in Montrose on October 27 last year. Dr Stewart, who examined MacKenzie's brain on December 10, revealed that she had suffered severe injuries, including bruising, bleeding, and brain swelling. He also found a segment of bone that appeared to have been "embedded on impact," suggesting that the head injury was extremely severe. Dr Stewart estimated that the survival time between the initial blow and death would be around an hour, or possibly less. He noted that with medical intervention, the head injury could have been potentially survivable. The defense team suggested that changes in the brain caused by decay could have masked Dr Stewart's findings, and that MacKenzie may have died shortly after being injured. Dr Stewart countered that his findings were not consistent with such a scenario. The accused, Jackson and Higgins, are charged with repeatedly striking MacKenzie on the head, neck, and body with a hammer or similar instrument, and also with stabbing her. They are also accused of attempting to conceal the evidence by dismembering her body and hiding parts of it in various locations in Montrose. The trial continues before Judge Lady Rae.

Mark Frost, a 70-year-old retired English teacher, admitted to abusing nine children in Thailand between 2009 and 2012. He pleaded guilty to 23 charges at the Old Bailey, having previously admitted to 22 charges last year. Six of these charges relate to the abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school during the 1980s and 1990s. Frost is set to be sentenced on Thursday. Once known as Andrew Tracey, Frost was brought to justice following an international investigation involving the UK's National Crime Agency and authorities in Thailand, Spain, and the Netherlands. Frost targeted impoverished Asian boys, luring them into sex acts by offering them money, sweets, computer games, and pool time. He evaded prosecution in Thailand, but was later extradited from Spain after Dutch police uncovered his activities on a computer in the Netherlands. Frost used the boys, aged 10 to 14, to create compromising videos, including thumbs-up signs and heart gestures while being filmed on webcam during sexual acts. The court also heard about Frost's abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school in the late 1980s and 1990s. He had sex with the boys in a school storeroom, at his home where he lived with his adopted son, and also at a car park in Woking, Surrey. One of the victims has since passed away, while the other stated he lived in fear every day following the abuse. Frost admitted to charges including multiple rapes, sex assaults, inciting children to engage in sexual activity, and making indecent pictures. Prosecutor Sally-Ann Hales revealed that images and web chats seized from Frost's Dutch contact featured 15 boys, nine of whom were identified and interviewed. The boys' mothers expressed their sorrow upon learning that the man they considered a "kind and generous foreigner" had abused their children. One of the youngsters was too ashamed to tell his mother, and had become withdrawn and aggressive. The court heard that Frost had subscribed to the now-defunct Paedophile Information Exchange, an international organization that traded obscene material. In 1986, Frost tried to adopt a son through the Catholic Church, but was rejected. He later succeeded through a local authority, but the pair are now estranged. Frost had held a senior position in the Scouts before resigning in 1991, before his first conviction. He was banned from teaching by the Department for Education in 1995. The NCA believes he may have assaulted many more youngsters

In contrast, the Samoa match, scheduled for June 24 in the capital city of Apia, has fewer concerns. WRU representatives visited Tonga in December to assess the facilities, but the absence of a completed pitch and ongoing medical cover issues have raised concerns. World Rugby stated in October that a new venue is planned and must be completed in time for the match. As an alternative, the game could be played in Auckland, New Zealand, although the facilities fall short of requirements. Phillips explained that the pitch is crucial, and while Tonga is committed to developing the stadium, the current forecast suggests it might not be ready by May. The team is currently working to make the Tonga match happen, but a deadline must be set. If this doesn't work out, playing in Auckland would be the next option, with an informal agreement already in place with New Zealand. The return of the Wales A team has also been delayed due to a lack of opposition. Initially scheduled for January 2015, the team's comeback was put back due to a six-year agreement between the WRU and the Welsh rugby regions. The team is looking to play England or Ireland, but due to scheduling conflicts, alternative options are being explored. The worst-case scenario is playing one of these teams in January 2018. England and Ireland have both expressed interest in playing, and the team is working to finalize a contract. If another fixture can be arranged, it will be pursued, and discussions are underway with other potential opponents.

North Wales Police PCSO Gareth Davies expressed concern that the anti-social behaviour was having a devastating impact on the community. However, some residents suggested that youngsters in the town had nowhere to go, which might be contributing to their misbehaviour. In response, North Wales Police has issued a dispersal order, allowing officers to order anyone to leave the area if their behaviour is deemed anti-social. Mr Davies explained that the aim of this measure was to prevent individuals from being prosecuted or facing a dispersal order that could affect their future. "It's a puzzling situation," he said. "Youngsters are at an awkward age, feeling too old to attend youth clubs and too young to visit public houses. One resident, who wished to remain anonymous, worried about her child's safety due to the broken glass in the parks. Another resident, however, was skeptical about the effectiveness of the enforcement, fearing it would unfairly target young people in the town. "When kids get bored and have nothing to do, they can become restless," she said. "The police are planning to introduce this legislation, but it's unlikely to succeed. Instead, they should focus on providing safe spaces for kids to go."
The new BBC Three logo, featuring two white bars and an exclamation mark on a pink background, has faced criticism on Twitter for its similarity to Roman numerals that resemble the channel number "BBC 2". The head of marketing for BBC Three, Nikki Carr, acknowledged the concerns but expressed confidence in the logo's boldness. "We wanted to create something that looks forward and will stand the test of time," she said. Carr argued that the logo needs to be versatile, working well both on TV and as an app icon, citing Snapchat as an example of a successful brand without a prominent logo. However, she admitted that the BBC's marketing team is notoriously difficult to please, as evident from her own experience with the satirical show W1A, which poked fun at the corporation's marketing efforts. Carr explained that the three bars in the logo represent three core principles that underpin everything the new channel does: "make me think", "make me laugh", and "give me a voice", which is exactly what the BBC aims to do for young people. She announced plans to involve young people in the decision-making process and include them as content creators, with a collaboration with 50 young creatives launching in the coming weeks. The BBC Trust previously approved a plan to move BBC Three online as part of cost-cutting measures, with the condition that long-form programmes would be shown on BBC One and Two, approximately two hours a week. Starting in February, the BBC Three TV channel will cease to exist, and the channel will shift towards a promotional role, highlighting online content that can be found on the platform.
Researchers presenting findings about the alleged discovery in Poland's Walbrzych claimed there may be a tunnel, but no train. One of the claimants still believed the train was present. The discovery was said to be hidden near Wroclaw as Soviet forces approached in 1945. The Nazis had built extensive tunnels in the area during World War Two. In August, Deputy Culture Minister Piotr Zuchowski stated that ground-penetrating radar images left him "99% convinced" that a German military train was buried near Walbrzych, showing a train equipped with gun turrets. However, Professor Janusz Madej from Krakow's Academy of Mining found no evidence of a train in his geological survey of the site. "There may be a tunnel," he said, "but there is no train." Local folklore suggests that an armoured train carrying gold was lost near Ksiaz castle, 3km from Walbrzych, as the Soviet army closed in at the end of World War Two. This year, Piotr Koper and Andreas Richter, from Poland and Germany respectively, claimed to know the train's location and sought 10% of its value if found. At a press conference, Mr Koper questioned the survey methodology and maintained his belief in the train's existence.
Isabelle Sanders, 51, was brutally stabbed to death by 20-year-old Paul McManus during a robbery at her home in Crookston. McManus was handed a life sentence with a minimum term of 26 years. Sanders' brother James Dougall believes whole life terms are essential in protecting the public from violent offenders. Last year, at the High Court in Glasgow, a jury discovered that Sanders was stabbed 37 times during the robbery. McManus was also found guilty of attempting to murder her partner, Norman Busby, 86, and two other stabbings related to the robbery. Just weeks before the murder, it emerged that McManus had been released from a previous sentence. The judge who sentenced him highlighted McManus's extensive criminal record, which included convictions for theft, assault, and robbery, as well as the use of weapons. The judge noted that the 20-year-old had already served seven sentences of detention. Sanders' family has submitted a petition to Holyrood's petitions committee, hoping to garner widespread public support. Her brother, Mr Dougall, said: "In cases where violent offenders get the chance of early release and then go on to commit violent murder, a whole life sentence is an appropriate option for the judiciary. "Guidance should be given to judges that they can impose a whole life sentence in such circumstances. "That's why we've started our petition - 'life means life' - that judges have the option to impose a whole life sentence in specific cases." Although not used in Scotland, whole life sentences are used by judges in England. The Scottish government has pointed out that judges already possess the power to impose a sentence that exceeds the rest of a person's life. A government spokesman said: "Scotland's courts have the power to impose a 'whole life tariff' in any given case. "The independence of Scotland's judiciary is fundamental to the Scottish legal system. As such, sentencing is a matter for judges who operate independently of Scottish ministers and it's for our courts to decide on a sentence in each case. "The Scottish government has previously announced the creation of a specific Scottish Sentencing Council, which will promote consistency and transparency around sentencing and encourage better understanding of sentences across Scotland, as well as producing sentencing guidelines for the judiciary."
In February 2013, Torfaen council's education department came under the supervision of the education watchdog Estyn due to a lack of progress following a 2011 inspection that highlighted serious failings and an unsatisfactory rating. Subsequent checks were made, and necessary steps were taken to lift the measures on a Friday. By 2013, Torfaen became the sixth Welsh council to be placed in special measures, following Anglesey, Blaenau Gwent, Pembrokeshire, Monmouthshire, and Merthyr Tydfil. Only Merthyr Tydfil remains in special measures. Education Minister Huw Lewis expressed optimism, stating that the progress seen with local authorities being removed from special measures would provide a springboard for future success. In a letter to Torfaen's chief executive Alison Ward, Clive Phillips, an assistant director at Estyn, highlighted seven recommendations from inspections in 2011 and 2013, which included raising standards in secondary schools and supporting school leavers who fail to secure jobs or training. The council was also criticized for its GCSE standards, with over half of schools falling into the bottom quarter for performance in core subjects such as English, maths, and science. A report found that no school achieved above average for pupils achieving the equivalent of five GCSEs at grade A* to C. After being placed in special measures, the Welsh government established a recovery board to support and scrutinize the council in addressing its problems. Phillips's letter noted that the council, elected members, and the recovery board had worked well together to improve these areas. As a result, the Her Majesty's Chief Inspector of Education and Training in Wales has removed Torfaen council from special measures, citing the authority's improved performance. Torfaen council's executive member for education David Yeowell welcomed the news, stating that it reflected the combined efforts of colleagues in the schools and education service.

Det Ch Insp Richard Wall of Cambridgeshire Police described the case as "tragic" where "a woman lost her life and families have been torn apart." Samantha's family released a statement saying she was "well-loved" and had a "bright future ahead of her" with many aspirations. The 39-year-old former student of Nottingham Trent University had worked at Asterand, a bio-science firm in Royston, Hertfordshire, for over 10 years. Prior to that, she worked at Pharmagene, which is part of Asterand, and Cytocell in Cambridge. Her family stated that she "wanted to be a great scientist, travel the world, and spend more time with family." However, on that fateful day, Samantha was "tragically taken away from us."

Russia denied resuming attacks on Aleppo, but claimed to have targeted jihadist groups elsewhere with missiles. The only aircraft carrier Russia has, the Admiral Kuznetsov, was used in combat for the first time, with Su-33 fighters flying sorties from the eastern Mediterranean. Footage from Russian media outlets showed the aircraft equipped with air-to-air missiles rather than bombs. Government forces launched a major assault on rebel-held eastern Aleppo on September 22, just two weeks after placing the city under siege. Since then, troops have pushed into several surrounding areas with the help of Iranian-backed Shia militias and Russian air strikes. Rebels attempted to break the siege in late October with a counter-attack, but their progress slowed after early gains. The UN says that weeks of air strikes and shelling have killed more than 700 civilians in the east, while rocket-fire has left scores dead in the government-controlled west. On Tuesday, activists reported that Russia's three-week moratorium on air strikes had seemingly ended, with warplanes and helicopters bombing several districts. A resident, Modar Shekho, described the bombardment, saying "Our houses are shaking from the pressure. Planes are soaring above us and the bombardment is around us." Russian Defence Minister Sergei Shoigu briefed President Vladimir Putin about a "major operation" against Islamic State and Jabhat Fateh al-Sham, which was known as al-Nusra Front until it broke ties with al-Qaeda in July. The operation involved a frigate firing Kalibr cruise missiles at targets in Idlib and Homs provinces, as well as the Su-33s on board the Admiral Kuznetsov and the Bastion mobile coastal defence missile system. The targets included terrorist training centres, arms depots, and factories producing various weapons with mass destruction capacity. The Local Co-ordination Committees reported that a missile hit the town of Saraqeb in Idlib province, and that Russian warplanes targeted the towns of Ariha, Ihsim, Khan Sheikhoun, and Tal Nabi Ayoub. The LCC also said there had been air strikes on several locations in Aleppo province on Tuesday, including one that damaged a hospital in Awaijel, resulting in at least one death, the third on a medical facility in 24 hours.

A replica stone unveiled in the village of Serre by Bradford's Lord Mayor on Saturday commemorates trenches used by soldiers during the battle. The stone, a replica of one found in Bradford's Memorial Gardens, honors the soldiers of the Bradford Pals and West Yorkshire servicemen who served in World War I. Many Pals battalions were formed in northern towns as friends and colleagues enlisted together. The inscription on the stone reads: "To the memory of the Bradford Pals and West Yorkshire servicemen who served in the Great War 1914-18. 'And lo a mighty army came out of the North.'" The stone was quarried in Bradford and transported to France. The first Bradford Pals battalion was formed at the Bradford Mechanic's Institute in 1914, and a 100-year-old union jack flown above the institute was used in the unveiling. Local dignitaries and representatives of veteran groups attended, including the Commonwealth War Graves Commission. Councillor Geoff Reid, Bradford's Lord Mayor, expressed gratitude: "We owe a tremendous debt to these brave men from Bradford who gave their lives during the First World War."

It's with great sadness that we have to announce the passing of Jaki Liebezeit, who died this morning from sudden pneumonia. He left the world peacefully, surrounded by his loved ones, and we will miss him hugely. As a pioneer of the Krautrock movement, Can's unique blend of rock, electronic, and world music earned them a reputation as one of the most influential bands of their time. Liebezeit played on all of Can's albums, including Tago Mago, Ege Bamyasi, and Future Days, despite the band's prolific output, releasing up to three albums a year in the 1970s. Despite their success, they were destined to be a cult band, influencing artists like Roxy Music, Julian Cope, The Flaming Lips, and The Horrors. Their unexpected UK hit, I Want More, reached number 26 in 1976 and earned the band their sole appearance on Top Of The Pops. Liebezeit's approach to music was unique, saying, "We were trying to find our own way. We thought like artists. Imagine a painter saying, 'I can't suddenly paint like Andy Warhol or like Jackson Pollock, so I have to find something else, something original.'" A student of the free jazz scene in Dresden, Germany, Liebezeit co-founded Can in 1968 with keyboardist Irmin Schmidt, bassist Holger Czukay, and guitarist Michael Karoli. Vocalist Malcolm Mooney joined later that year. Liebezeit had a long and successful career behind the kit, working with artists like Depeche Mode, the Eurythmics, Jah Wobble, and Brian Eno, as well as his work with Can. His dynamic drumming style was the heartbeat of Can's music, providing a precise and hypnotic pulse that tethered their more exploratory compositions. This style, dubbed "motorik," was meant to evoke the repetitive experience of driving on a motorway. In April, Liebezeit was set to perform with Sonic Youth's Thurston Moore, as well as Can's Schmidt and Mooney, at London's Barbican, dubbed The Can Project. Public Image Limited's bassist, Jah Wobble, expressed his sadness at Liebezeit's passing, calling him "a wonderful person and the best European drummer." Brian Eno responded by sharing links to Can's music and interviews with Liebezeit.

A business secretary stated that around 1.2 million small and medium-sized firms in the UK were involved in exporting to the EU, and these businesses would likely be on the front line if the UK voted to leave on June 23. However, Leave campaigners argued that the government's figures were questionable and that EU "red tape" was detrimental to businesses. In a speech in Birmingham, the business secretary claimed that EU regulation was becoming less burdensome due to UK lobbying and predicted a sharp increase in tariffs if the UK were to leave the EU. He was accompanied by Piers Linney from the TV show Dragons' Den, after seven past and present members of the show's panel expressed their support for remaining in the EU. The impact of EU membership on trade and the UK's economy is a significant concern. The business secretary stated that when the UK is part of the EU, small and medium-sized enterprises have the ability to export to the world's largest market, which is located "right on our doorstep". However, he warned of an economic shock that would occur if the UK were to vote to leave. "Small businesses are the backbone of the economy," he said. "Let's not break that backbone by taking a leap in the dark." Leave campaigners argue that small businesses are more sympathetic to their cause than large multinationals, primarily due to the effects of EU regulations emanating from Brussels. The business secretary disputed this claim, stating that it was a myth that only large firms wanted to remain in the EU. He pointed out that 1.2 million small and medium-sized enterprises were involved in exporting to the European Union, including 400,000 who sell directly to the EU. Louise Stewart, of the Federation of Small Businesses, stated that members of her organisation were divided on EU membership and that the organisation was remaining neutral in the debate. She told the BBC Radio 4's Today programme that FSB members "really want more information". "We might be talking about it every day in the main, but there seems to be more heat than light in some cases." John Longworth, of Vote Leave, pointed to the business secretary's previous criticism of the EU, suggesting that his support for Remain was motivated by his desire to advance his political career. "EU rules damage all British businesses, and smaller businesses in particular - and hold us back from trading freely with the rest of the world," he said. Google's new venture, Waymo, will be owned by its parent company, Alphabet. This move signifies a push to commercialize the self-driving car technology that Google has been developing for over a decade. At a recent media event, the company showcased a remarkable story of a blind man who successfully completed a full trip using a prototype self-driving car. The trip took place in 2015, but it was only now being publicly discussed. According to John Krafcik, Google's CEO, "Steve Mahan, a legally blind individual, rode solo in one of our prototype vehicles, cruising through Austin's suburbs." Krafcik further emphasized that "Steve's sensors and software were his chauffeur." When discussing the formation of Waymo, Krafcik stated, "We believe that this technology can start to transform the 10 trillion miles that motor vehicles travel around the world every year, with safer, more efficient, and more accessible forms of transport." Krafcik will serve as Waymo's chief executive. The company plans to apply the technology behind self-driving vehicles to other purposes, suggesting an increased urgency in finding immediate commercial uses for the technology, which has so far been mainly tested by Google. Unlike other companies, such as electric car maker Tesla, Google has taken a more cautious approach. The Waymo announcement could signal a shift in Google's original goal of only offering 100% self-driving vehicles. Krafcik believes that their technology could be useful in personal vehicles, ride-sharing, logistics, or solving 'last mile' problems for public transport. Notably, the company has not responded to reports that it is abandoning its plan to manufacture its own car in favor of collaborating with established carmakers. This move would be a cost-cutting measure at a time when Alphabet is trying to reduce the costs of its "other bets," innovative parts of the business that are not yet generating revenue.

A documentary shoot was underway in the city in November 2015 when Hugh McGrattan lost control of his vehicle, causing severe injuries to some of those who were hit. The 25-year-old from Whiterock Drive has pleaded guilty to five counts of causing grievous bodily injury by reckless driving. The crash occurred on Apollo Road, just off Boucher Road in the southern part of the city, where the speed limit is 30mph. At Belfast Crown Court, it was revealed that CCTV footage showed McGrattan driving at an excessively high speed of between 52mph and 69mph before the crash. His car struck a group of up to 15 people on the footpath, who were filming a programme for Irish broadcaster RTÉ. One woman sustained a spinal cord injury, was left paralyzed from the chest down, and now requires a wheelchair. McGrattan admitted to the police that he was speeding and told a police officer that he thought he would lose control. A prosecutor told the court that some of the injured victims were trapped under McGrattan's car and the fencing it had brought down. Witnesses described seeing his car fail to negotiate a bend due to its excessive speed, with one woman stating that the car was like a "bowling ball" when it struck the group. Another woman described the car as "like a snow plough with people being swept away". The prosecutor noted that some of the injuries suffered by the victims still required medical treatment. A defence barrister said McGrattan had not driven since the crash, had been diagnosed with post-traumatic stress disorder, and suffers nightmares, after which he wakes up believing he has killed someone. McGrattan deeply regrets the incident and "struggles with the enormity of it". However, the barrister added that McGrattan's actions were not reckless or showboating, but rather a significant misjudgement. McGrattan remained at the scene and offered assistance to the injured, and gave a roadside confession to police. Forensic evidence suggested that McGrattan's speed when he hit the kerb was about 25mph. The judge expressed her desire to consider the case before passing sentence next week, and McGrattan was released on continuing bail.

Motorists must now access the DVLA website to obtain a check code, which allows companies to verify if a driver has penalty points. This code was introduced after the switch from paper licences, which stored such information, to a digital format. Initially, the code was only valid for 72 hours, but the checking period has been extended to 21 days. In response to complaints from car hire companies and drivers who lack internet access while abroad, the DVLA has decided to provide an alternative – a phone number, although it's not available 24 hours a day. The British Vehicle Rental and Leasing Association welcomed the change, saying it will help reduce queues at rental desks and give renters more time to plan ahead. However, some motoring organisations noted that many drivers and car hire companies found the new system confusing, with the DVLA assuring that it's actually quite straightforward to use. "It's easy to view and share your driving record – just go to gov.uk and search for 'view driving licence'," said the DVLA's chief executive. As a result of the switch, all driving endorsements and convictions will be stored online, with the eight million remaining paper licences still valid. This system does not apply to drivers in Northern Ireland.

The accident, which occurred when Alonso hit the wall after a right-hand turn at the Circuit de Catalunya, was attributed to gusty winds and there was no evidence of mechanical failure. Alonso, 33, is being kept in hospital for further observation. A photo of the driver sitting up in his hospital bed, accompanied by the message "Lunch time! Thank you for your support!", was posted on social media by his manager. Alonso suffered a concussion after the crash, which occurred at speeds of up to 150mph, although Ferrari's Sebastian Vettel claimed his rival was only going at around 90mph. In the immediate aftermath of the crash, Alonso was given first aid at the circuit's medical centre and then sedated before being airlifted to hospital. A thorough examination of his condition, including CT scans and MRI scans, showed no abnormalities. He is being kept in hospital for further observation and to recover from the effects of the medication used to sedate him. McLaren also released more information about Alonso's treatment. His car ran wide at the exit of Turn Three, causing a loss of traction, which led to instability and ultimately the crash. The team also addressed rumors that Alonso was rendered unconscious by an electrical fault, stating that their data showed he was downshifting while applying full brake pressure at the time of the crash, which would not have been possible if he were unconscious. Alonso left Ferrari at the end of last season to join McLaren and Honda, believing it would give him a better chance of winning a third world title. However, McLaren's preparations have been hampered by repeated engine reliability issues, limiting their testing mileage.
The legendary comedy "Life of Brian" was blocked by councillors in 1980, who gave it an X rating. However, the 19-seat Bournemouth Colosseum has since allowed the film to be shown after the authority relaxed its stance. Cinema boss Paul Whitehouse described the historic showing as "a bit of fun." As part of the special event, he laid on a three-course meal. It is believed to be the first time the film has been shown in public in Bournemouth since the controversy over its certificate. "The council has changed its attitude now, but in the early 1980s, they had a very different view and felt empowered to judge what residents should watch," Whitehouse said. "I remember when it was released, people went to Southampton to see it. The film has become a part of British language, with phrases from it becoming iconic." Dave Harvey, who attended the screening, said: "It is one of my favourite films. "Monty Python films are iconic comedy from the 60s and 70s, and I am looking forward to watching it at the first screening in Bournemouth. "I didn't realise I was making local history tonight." The film, which tells the story of a man mistaken for Jesus Christ, sparked accusations of blasphemy when it was released. Bournemouth council's environmental services committee raised the certificate from an AA to an X, meaning only those over 18 could see it. The distributors had said it could only be shown in areas where it had an AA rating. Train driver Adrian Cox recently asked the council to change its stance, and it was only after licensing officers agreed that it could be shown. Colin Robinson gave the film 9.5 out of 10 and said: "I couldn't understand why they banned such an amusing film. "It is like reliving my student days. "The Monty Python squad gave us the freedom to live life without care."

Price growth in food, alcohol, and tobacco slowed down compared to November, while energy prices dropped less than expected, according to Eurostat estimates. This rate was lower than the 0.3% rise economists had anticipated. The data may prompt the European Central Bank to take further action to boost the struggling European economy, which has been under pressure. Last month, the central bank failed to meet market expectations with its efforts to revive the economy, which were less intense than analysts had predicted. These inflation figures are a preliminary estimate from Eurostat and do not provide detailed information on member states. However, they offer a general idea of which products have experienced price increases or decreases. Food, alcohol, and tobacco prices rose 1.2% year-on-year in December, down from 1.5% in November. Energy prices fell by 5.9% annually, compared to a 7.3% drop in the previous month. Meanwhile, service prices increased by 1.1%, a decrease from the 1.2% rise the month before.
Dublin Airport is set to build a new 3,100-metre runway, which will be constructed 1.6 km north of the existing main runway. The project is scheduled to be operational by 2020, following the airport's receipt of planning permission in 2007. However, the construction plans were put on hold due to the economic downturn and a decline in passenger numbers. After passenger numbers reached a record 25m last year, surpassing the previous record of 23m set in 2008, the latest announcement was made. The airport is considering increasing passenger charges to help fund the new runway. Dublin Airport's chief executive, Kevin Toland, believes the expansion will be beneficial for the business and tourism sectors. "The new north runway will greatly enhance Ireland's connectivity, supporting trade, foreign investment, and tourism," he stated. Toland emphasized the importance of balancing national and business needs with those of local communities. "We will continue to work closely with our neighbours on this project." Paschal Donohoe, Ireland's transport, tourism, and sport minister, views the new runway as crucial for the country's economic recovery. "This project holds significant strategic importance for Ireland as an island economy, with improved connectivity offering the potential to create thousands of jobs in the coming years," he said.
SpaceX's Falcon 9 rocket was ready to lift off from Cape Canaveral when its onboard computers initiated an abort, marking the third attempt in five days to launch a satellite for Luxembourg-based SES. A fourth launch attempt could happen later this week, but engineers need time to analyze the data to determine why the abort occurred. The US Air Force also requires some time to prepare the Florida range. SpaceX CEO Elon Musk explained on Twitter that the abort was triggered by a low-thrust alarm, which he attributed to rising oxygen temperatures due to a pause in the countdown to allow a boat to move out of the restricted zone under the rocket's flight path. This incident was a precursor to the abort, as SpaceX had previously experienced a failure last year when a Falcon 9 broke apart about two and a half minutes into its mission. The company had since returned to routine launches with successful deployments in December for Orbcomm and January for the Jason ocean altimeter mission, which also marked the historic first landing and recovery of an orbiting rocket stage. For these recent launches, SpaceX is using an upgraded Falcon 9 with a higher thrust engine, which achieves extra performance by using super-chilled liquid oxygen as one of its propellants. However, the new LOX has introduced handling challenges, including how to load and maintain the liquid at the correct temperature.

Walter Newall's legacy continues to be felt in Dumfries and Galloway, with many of his 19th-century works still standing. A recent talk at Moat Brae, a building he designed in 1823, is shedding new light on his life and work. The site, which was believed to have inspired author JM Barrie, who lived in the town and wrote Peter Pan, is now undergoing a transformation into a national centre for children's literature. Margaret Elliot, a local photographer, has embarked on a project to document all the existing buildings in the region that Newall was involved with. Born in 1780 at Doubledyke, New Abbey, Newall began his career as a cabinet-maker but eventually turned to architecture after his business failed. Despite lacking formal training, his buildings showcase his understanding of contemporary styles, including Greek Revival and Picturesque Gothic. His diverse portfolio included churches, country houses, villas, and farmsteads, many of which were built for the Buccleuch estates. He also played a key role in the development of Dumfries' New Town and the conversion of a windmill into an observatory, which became a town landmark. Newall remained single and passed away in 1863 at Craigend, New Abbey, where he is buried in St Michael's Churchyard. Margaret Elliot, a lifelong resident of Dumfries, has only recently begun to appreciate the beauty of Newall's buildings. Her talk, which explores his life and work, will take place at Moat Brae on August 25 at 18:30.

The UK government is providing micro-processor limbs, known as "bionic legs," to wounded service personnel from Iraq and Afghanistan. These advanced prosthetics will offer greater stability and mobility, benefiting around 160 members of the armed forces. Defence Secretary Philip Hammond considers it a top priority to provide the best possible care and support to troops. Chancellor George Osborne has allocated funds from the Treasury's special reserve to make this cutting-edge technology available. Osborne stated that it is only right to help injured troops, especially when they suffer an injury. He expressed delight at the funding, which will significantly improve the lives of those who have made sacrifices for the UK. Experts say the "bionic legs" will greatly enhance the quality of life and speed of rehabilitation for amputees. These limbs provide better stability, greater mobility, and improved ability to navigate obstacles, stairs, and walk backwards safely. The technology will be fitted at the Headley Court military rehabilitation centre in Surrey, where clinically suitable. Paralympic rower Captain Nick Beighton, who lost both legs in an explosion in Afghanistan, welcomed the micro-processor limbs as a significant technological advancement. He said that the opportunity to use this technology will give him greater freedom, functionality, and independence. Surgeon General Air Marshal Paul Evans noted that the next generation of micro-processor knee is a fantastic prosthetic development, offering proven benefits for certain amputees. It will improve quality of life and rehabilitation for patients where it is clinically suitable, providing better stability, improved mobility, and reduced back pain. Bryn Parry, chief executive of the Help for Heroes charity, said that wounded servicemen, women, and veterans deserve the best. This announcement will help transform lives, but each individual must receive the best prosthetic for their needs.

Some UK government departments have been instructed to prepare for budget cuts of either a quarter or two-fifths by 2020. Housing Minister Gavin Barwell said the government was shifting its approach to housing, introducing minimum tenancies and more homes for rent. He emphasized that the government has not given up on making home ownership accessible to all. In an interview on the BBC's Sunday Politics, Barwell stated that the government needed to address the rising costs faced by renters. He revealed that a new package of measures would be announced in a forthcoming White Paper. The measures aim to encourage more investment in building affordable rent homes, which he defined as at least 20% below the market rate. Barwell also encouraged councils to get involved. The government has previously announced plans to ban letting agents in England from charging fees to tenants. In its last general election manifesto, the Conservative Party promised that everyone who works hard should be able to own a home. Barwell confirmed that the government remains committed to reversing the decline in home ownership. However, he acknowledged that policies are also needed to support private renters, who number approximately 4.3 million. Barwell argued that the main issue is that governments have not built enough homes, leading to housing becoming less and less affordable. He emphasized that the new White Paper is a step towards addressing this issue. Barwell acknowledged the recent low figures for new affordable homes, but noted that this was due to the start of a new five-year program. He insisted that the government remains committed to building one million new homes in England by 2020, despite being behind schedule. However, Barwell ruled out changes to the current rules on the green belt, which allow building only in exceptional circumstances. He argued that removing large areas from the green belt is not a solution to the housing crisis. Labour's Shadow Housing Secretary, John Healey, disagreed, stating that the proposals fall short of what is needed to support renters from private landlords.

The UK government's reform does not eliminate the tax status of non-domiciled individuals, but those who have lived in the UK for 15 out of the past 20 years will lose the right to claim it. The non-domicile rule allows wealthy UK residents to reduce the tax they pay on earnings abroad. Chancellor George Osborne said changes to the non-dom rules would generate an additional £1.5 billion in annual tax revenue. The changes to the non-dom system would also mean that, from April 2017, a person born in the UK to UK-domiciled parents would no longer be able to claim non-dom status if they moved abroad, then returned, and took up residency in the UK again. Furthermore, new rules would be introduced from April 2017, requiring everyone who owns UK residential property to pay inheritance tax on it, regardless of whether it's held in an offshore company or not. The government claims this move will curb "abuses" of the rule by non-doms who use offshore companies to avoid paying inheritance tax. "It's unfair that non-doms with UK property here can hide it in an offshore company and avoid inheritance tax," Osborne said. "From now on, they'll pay the same tax as everyone else." The chancellor decided not to abolish the non-dom tax status entirely because it would be costly, but he acknowledged that many individuals who take advantage of the tax status make significant contributions to public life. However, he argued that it's not fair for people to live in the UK for extended periods, benefiting from public services, while operating under different tax rules than everyone else. The government will also allocate an extra £750m to HM Revenue & Customs to tackle tax evasion. This funding will help HMRC officials raise an additional £7.2 billion in tax by 2020.

Three firearms agents claimed they were instructed to track the movement of the guns, but not to make any arrests. US lawmakers were outraged by the details of Operation Fast and Furious, a program aimed at monitoring the flow of small-time gun buyers to major traffickers along the US-Mexico border. The news comes after a report revealed that Mexican drug cartels had acquired US-made firearms, with around 70% of recovered guns from 2009 and 2010 traced back to the US. A congressional hearing found that Fast and Furious, designed to track the guns, never led to the arrest of any major traffickers. The guns tracked by ATF agents were used in numerous killings in Mexico. Lawmakers on the House Oversight Committee demanded answers from the Obama administration about why no arrests were made while tracking the firearms. ATF agent John Dodson testified that he had been instructed not to make any arrests, despite wanting to intervene and interdict the weapons. Senator Chuck Grassley said that hundreds of firearms destined for cartels in Mexico were purchased in gun shops in Arizona. The operation was designed to track the movement of guns from small-time buyers to major traffickers, who had often avoided prosecution. In December, two US assault rifles were found at the scene of a shootout where Customs and Border Protection agent Brian Terry was killed. Earlier this year, plans to bring in a non-native species for the first time were unveiled. A public consultation was held, revealing that some wildlife experts believed it could have a "devastating impact", while others supported the idea. The government stated that there were "no conservation grounds" to proceed with the plan. Environment Minister Richard Ronan said after considering all opinions, he concluded that it would be "detrimental" and decided not to move forward at present. Over 100 people responded to the consultation. The Manx Wildlife Trust expressed concerns that introducing the species could have a "devastating impact on habitats already struggling with environmental pressures". Some wildlife experts argued that the red squirrel is not at risk of extinction in the UK and does not have a scientific basis for creating a refuge. Grey squirrels were introduced to the UK from North America in the 19th century and have since threatened the red squirrels, primarily due to competition for habitat and the transmission of the deadly parapoxvirus. Although red squirrels are not currently considered endangered, the Red Squirrel Survival Trust in the UK claims they could disappear within a decade without conservation efforts.

The veteran musician complained to Rolling Stone magazine that folk and jazz musicians are often overly concerned with quotation. However, he felt that he was exempt from this rule. His 35th studio album, Tempest, was released recently. Earlier this year, at the age of 71, he received the Medal of Freedom, the highest civilian honor in America, from President Obama. However, he was accused of borrowing from Henry Timrod, a 19th-century poet, on his 2006 album Modern Times. He also faced criticism for similarities between passages from his 2001 album Love and Theft and lines from the memoir Confessions of a Yakuza by Junichi Saga. When asked about Timrod, Dylan retorted, "Have you even heard of him? Who's been reading him lately? And who's brought him to the forefront?" He suggested that if someone wants to use Timrod's work to enhance their own, they should do it themselves and see how far they can get. Dylan's past has been marred by controversy, including the false accusation that he was Judas, a name considered the most hated in human history. He felt that those who accused him of this were trying to discredit him for playing the electric guitar. Born Robert Allen Zimmerman in 1941, Dylan began his music career in 1959, playing in coffee houses in Minnesota. He adopted his stage name from the poet Dylan Thomas. Much of his most famous work dates back to the 1960s, when he became an informal historian of America's troubles, as seen in songs like Blowin' In The Wind and The Times They Are A-Changin'. The shift from traditional acoustic guitar to electric guitar in the mid-1960s was a bold move that sparked debate among folk fans. When asked about songwriting, Dylan explained, "It's about melody and rhythm, and after that, anything goes. You make everything your own."
To meet the 2C target and avoid severe climate change, crop yields must increase by 40-60% by 2050. This prediction was made by the Global Calculator, an online tool developed by the government to assess various options for reducing carbon emissions. The calculator, led by the Department of Energy and Climate Change, examines different scenarios for achieving the 2C target, a goal scientists believe is essential for preventing catastrophic climate change. The model suggests that living standards can be maintained, but only by making drastic changes to agriculture, transportation, food, and fuel. In 2050, there would need to be hundreds of millions of electric cars on the road, and the amount of CO2 emitted per unit of electricity would need to decrease by at least 90%. Consumers would need to consider adopting diets rich in vegetables or opting for meat from animals raised using intensive farming methods. Energy and Climate Change Secretary Ed Davey stated: "For the first time, the Global Calculator shows that everyone in the world can prosper while limiting global temperature rises to 2C, preventing the most severe impacts of climate change." However, the calculator emphasizes the need to act now to transform how we use energy and land in order to achieve this green growth. Dr. Mike Cherrett of Climate-KIC, the EU climate initiative that co-led the project, added: "The calculator clearly highlights that we can meet our 2C target while maintaining good lifestyles, but we must set ambitious targets on all fronts and utilize innovation to address climate change." Building on Decc's UK calculator, published in 2010, the global calculator is being offered to other governments to use in the lead-up to crucial climate negotiations in Paris at the end of the year.

In the first quarter of 2017, average weekly earnings dropped 0.4%. However, part-time workers' pay had returned to pre-2008 levels. The Resolution Foundation, claiming to be "non-partisan," was established in 2005 with the aim of improving living standards for Britain's 15 million low and middle-income individuals. According to Stephen Clarke, economic analyst at the foundation, the pay squeeze has made a comeback and is expected to persist for the rest of 2017. What's most concerning is that pay packets have yet to recover from the previous squeeze. The foundation, however, notes that the National Living Wage has been a positive force since its introduction two years ago, delivering a 12% rise in pay. In response to the report, an HM Treasury spokesman stated: "We're seeing more people in work than ever before and are helping people keep more of their earnings by cutting taxes for millions of working people. Murugan stopped writing a year ago after protests over his book. The court's decision is being hailed by supporters as a triumph for freedom of expression. There was no immediate comment from Murugan, but the court also stated that a settlement between the author and a peace council in Tamil Nadu, where the author agreed to apologize and withdraw unsold copies, was not legally enforceable. Madhorubagan, set a century ago in southern India, revolves around a childless woman who participates in a sex ritual to conceive during a temple festival. He also instructed his publishers not to print or sell his work and promised compensation for unsold copies. Murugan asked his readers to burn his books and announced he would stop attending literary festivals. Many critics regard Murugan as one of the finest writers in the Tamil language.

Smoking more cigarettes and changing habits such as waking up earlier have been blamed for the significant rise in lung cancer cases. Research published in the New England Journal of Medicine revealed that death rates among women have surpassed those of men. The study analyzed data from over two million women in the US, with the first generation of female smokers starting in the 1950s and 60s. In those early years, women who smoked were nearly three times more likely to die from lung cancer than non-smokers. A 2010 study of medical records showed that women who smoked were 25 times more likely to die from lung cancer than their non-smoking friends. Similar trends have been observed in men, with their risk levels increasing to a comparable level in the 1980s. According to lead researcher Dr. Michael Thun, the increased risk among female smokers has persisted for decades despite the established health risks of smoking. Interestingly, women who smoked lower-tar and nicotine cigarette brands did not benefit from these marketing strategies, as they also faced a significant increase in deaths from chronic obstructive lung disease in male smokers. Dr. Thun explained that the diluted smoke from these cigarettes led to deeper inhalation, resulting in greater nicotine absorption. Research published last year found that lifelong female smokers died a decade earlier than non-smokers. However, those who quit smoking by age 30 greatly reduced their risk, while those who stopped by 40 still experienced a one-year decrease in life expectancy. Sir Richard Peto, a professor at Oxford University, noted that if women smoked like men, they would die like men.

MB Aerospace has acquired Centrax Turbine Components in Newton Abbot, with the Gas Turbines division remaining part of the family-owned Centrax Group. The Devon-based company, which employs 430 people, has been supplying engine parts for nearly 70 years, specializing in rotating components for aero-engines. It will continue to supply components that require automated machining, grinding, and robotic polishing. Centrax currently supplies Rolls-Royce, Snecma, and Solar to engine-makers like Airbus, Boeing, and Embraer. MB Aerospace, with its headquarters in Motherwell, North Lanarkshire, has manufacturing facilities in Connecticut, Michigan, Poland, and the UK. The acquisition marks the first major deal since Blackstone private equity provided additional funding in 2013. Robert Barr, Centrax's CEO, stated: "We've found a custodian in MB Aerospace that shares our values and commitment to the region, as well as our significant pool of high-tech manufacturing expertise."
Business owners who use Wyvern Media have reported being charged for unwanted ads. One entrepreneur claimed she was forced to shut her business after receiving a £5,000 bill, while another said she was charged over £500 for ads worth £40. Wyvern Media has denied ever taking unauthorized payments, stating it only bills customers for ads that have been agreed upon. A dog breeder in Lincolnshire, Jean Wood, initially paid for two ads in company publications to sell puppies, but later claimed she was charged for ads in other publications. "I received two invoices for the two I'd paid for, £40 each," she said. "However, I started receiving invoices with 'paid' on them that I hadn't authorized. "I received six payments from my credit cards that I hadn't authorized, totaling over £500." Polly Zabari, the owner of a bespoke travel business, claims she was forced to close her company due to charges of over £5,000. She agreed to sign up for a single ad, but became confused after receiving numerous phone calls and emails from various companies within the Wyvern Media group. She discovered that the company claimed she had signed up for over £5,000 worth of advertising, despite never seeing a published copy or receipt. Mrs. Zabari's bank has since reimbursed her after she lodged a successful complaint. On its website, Wyvern Media states it runs 28 newspaper titles across the country, with offices in Coventry, Leamington Spa, and Rugby. An ex-employee of Wyvern Media confirmed that they had received complaints from unhappy advertisers, stating that they had received calls from customers claiming that unauthorized payments had been taken from their bank accounts. "Calls started coming in from customers that... I knew our office had sold to, claiming that certain payments had been taken out of their bank accounts without authorisation," she said. Wyvern Media's managing director, Jonathan Rivers, denied the allegations, stating that the company has never taken unauthorized payments. He added that the publications now record all sales calls, and complaints have decreased to three or four a month, out of several thousand sales. Mr. Rivers also stated that if customers feel money has been taken without proper authorization, the payments can be reclaimed through their credit cards.
In the past decade, the Glasgow postcode has claimed the most UK lottery winners, surpassing 21 million pound jackpot holders. Meanwhile, Glasgow itself has a staggering 106 millionaire winners since the first draw in 1994, ranking it second only to Birmingham. The Paisley postcode area, covering Renfrewshire, Inverclyde, and Argyll and Bute, has been named Scotland's luckiest region, boasting the highest number of winners per capita for prizes over £50,000. According to Andy Carter, senior winners' advisor for The National Lottery, Scotland has experienced a recent surge in National Lottery luck, with residents in the Paisley postcode area celebrating numerous big wins. Glasgow has solidified its position as the country's Lottery millionaire hub, producing nearly two millionaires per month over the past couple of years. There are now approximately 4,250 National Lottery millionaires across the UK, with around six new millionaires emerging every week. When it comes to lottery millionaires in Scotland since 1994, Edinburgh ranks second to Glasgow with 62 winners, followed by Motherwell with 43 and Aberdeen with 41. In the last two years alone, 30 top-tier prizes of at least £50,000 have been won by players in the PA postcode area, resulting in the creation of four Lottery millionaires. Since the National Lottery began, the area has seen 182 major prizes of £50,000 or more.

At 19, Steven Thomas reached speeds of up to 100mph before crashing his car into a tree on the A158 near Hagworthingham in Lincolnshire in May. Thomas, from Alfreton, suffered a broken back and the woman in the car had a head injury. Thomas was sentenced to 12 months detention. Prosecutor Phil Howes told Lincoln Crown Court that Thomas was trying to evade a police pursuit on the A158 Horncastle to Skegness road and lost control of his car due to excessive speed. "At one point, the officer involved estimated he was traveling at 100mph, and Thomas seemed to be still pulling away," Howes said. The court heard that Thomas suffered a serious brain injury in the crash and will undergo an operation to insert a plate over his brain, as well as therapy for slow speech and poor balance. Recorder Paul Mann QC said, "Having seen the photos of the wreckage, it's a miracle any of you survived." Thomas, of Scott Drive, Alfreton, pleaded guilty to two charges of causing serious injury by dangerous driving. He was banned from driving for two and a half years and ordered to take an extended driving test.
Over 4,100 years ago, a young woman named Ava was buried with her belongings, including a decorated beaker. Analysis of pollen found on the pottery revealed that Ava had used plants with medicinal properties. The woman's remains, including a skull and teeth, were discovered at Achavanich in Caithness 30 years ago. Archaeologist Maya Hoole, who is managing the research project, noted that the presence of the pollen "raises some intriguing questions." Last year, forensic artist Hew Morrison created a facial reconstruction of Ava. Now, other research has been published, shedding new light on the woman's burial. Analysis of pollen recovered from the beaker identified various plants, including trees and shrubs, as well as grasses, meadowsweet, and St John's wort. The presence of meadowsweet and St John's wort, both known for their medicinal properties, is particularly interesting, as they have also appeared at other Bronze Age burials in Scotland. It is possible that Ava deliberately included these flowers in her burial. Ava's remains are now held at the Caithness Horizons museum in Thurso, alongside other artefacts found with her. Her burial was unique, as she was buried in a pit dug into solid rock. Her skull is also an unusual shape, with some suggesting that it may have been the result of deliberate binding. It is believed that Ava was part of a larger European group known as the Beaker people. Further research is needed to uncover more about Ava and her burial.
Negotiations are focused on reinstating a power-sharing executive by Monday's deadline. The Stormont Estate is typically a peaceful spot, popular with joggers and tourists on weekends, but on Saturdays, politicians and officials will be present, as efforts to reach a deal continue to intensify. With the 4 pm BST deadline looming, discussions went late into the night on Friday. It's expected that Saturday's talks will extend into the evening, and the process is now at a critical juncture. Two key obstacles remain: agreement on legacy issues and the Irish language. A source close to the talks suggested that without a change in positions, it's unlikely a consensus on legacy issues will be reached by Monday. However, they hinted that progress is being made on budgetary matters and a programme for government. Notably, in previous negotiations, deadlines have been missed or extended. This time, there's a sense of urgency, as a deal must be struck by Monday to avoid the Secretary of State, James Brokenshire, having to quickly call a fresh assembly poll if a First and Deputy First Minister are not in place.
Russia was outraged by a US Department of State report that placed it in its lowest category, along with China, Iran, and North Korea. Uzbekistan also fell into Tier 3, a designation that can lead to sanctions in areas such as cultural and educational programs. The US report highlighted the need for these countries to take action against human trafficking, with Russia's foreign ministry arguing that the US methodology was flawed and based on Washington's friendliness with the country. "The idea of raising this issue is infuriating," said Konstantin Dolgov, a Russian foreign ministry human rights envoy. In response to the report, China expressed its support for combating human trafficking, while also calling for the US to take a more objective view of its efforts. The US report noted that Russia had made some attempts to tackle trafficking, but had not established a formal system for identifying and caring for victims. China, on the other hand, had taken steps to raise awareness and cooperate with international organizations, but still faced significant challenges, including a high demand for prostitution and foreign brides among its internal migrant population.

President Obama told ABC News that some Chinese hacking was indeed state-sponsored, although he downplayed the idea of a cyber war with China. This came a day after the US intelligence chiefs said cyber attacks had become the main security threat, surpassing terrorism. China, however, denies any involvement and claims it is the victim of such attacks. In an interview with ABC News, broadcast on Wednesday, Obama was asked about US lawmakers' claims that the scale of attacks on American firms and infrastructure constituted a cyber war with China. "There's a significant difference between state-sponsored cyber espionage or attacks and a full-blown war," Obama said. "What's true is that we've seen a steady increase in cyber security threats, some of which are state-sponsored, while others are the work of criminals." The president made it clear to China and other state actors that the US expects them to adhere to international norms and rules. "We'll have tough talk with them," Obama said. "We already have." China has long been suspected of being involved in cyber attacks, prompting the Pentagon to warn of a potential "digital Pearl Harbor". A US congressional report last year named China as the most significant threat in cyberspace, leading to growing tensions between Washington and Beijing. On Tuesday, Director of National Intelligence James Clapper told a Senate committee that cyber attacks and espionage had replaced terrorism as the top security threat. The day before, National Security Advisor Tom Donilon called on Beijing to take steps to stop cyber crime. Last month, a report from security firm Mandiant revealed that a unit of China's People's Liberation Army had mounted data raids on over 140 mostly US-based organizations. US state department officials have repeatedly raised the issue of hacking with Chinese officials. In January, the New York Times reported being the victim of cyber attacks from China, following a story about the wealth of outgoing Premier Wen Jiabao's family.
Adobe has confirmed evidence of limited, targeted attacks on its Flash software, urging users to update their installations immediately. As a widely used tool for delivering multimedia content across the web, Flash is vulnerable to hackers taking control of computers, affecting Windows, Mac, and Linux systems. To check if their Flash is up to date, users can visit the Adobe website, where the latest version is currently 18.0.0.194. Users browsing the internet with the latest versions of Google Chrome or Microsoft Internet Explorer should have their Flash automatically updated. Adobe's Flash software has a history of requiring security patches, and some security experts view it as a weak point on many websites. Like Java, Flash is frequently targeted by hackers using zero-day exploits, which are previously unknown security vulnerabilities. According to Mark James, a security specialist at ESET, this is partly due to Flash's widespread use. "Since Flash is so widely used, it's logical that it will be a prime target for vulnerability," he explained. "To affect the most people, you need an app that's used by many users, and Flash is one of them." Security blogger Brian Krebs recently disabled Flash entirely on his machine as an experiment, writing on his blog: "It's worth considering whether keeping Flash Player installed is necessary at all."
