A coroner's verdict into the death of Jackie Williams, 42, has found that she was not adequately managed at Royal Blackburn Hospital. The inquest concluded that Williams, who hanged herself in a cubicle at the A&E ward on January 27, was not provided with the necessary care. She had been admitted to the hospital late on January 26 after being found unconscious and intoxicated by a canal in Burnley. The coroner noted that hospital staff were aware of Williams's suicidal thoughts, but it was not made clear that the cubicle door should remain open. The coroner also expressed concern that Williams was placed in an environment where she could act on her thoughts - a room with potential ligatures and ligature points. Furthermore, the coroner highlighted the lack of effective referral to the mental health liaison team and the misunderstanding between nursing and mental health staff, which left Williams unattended and without proper specialist care. The coroner has prepared a Regulation 28 report, which aims to identify concerns that can be used to prevent future deaths. However, the coroner noted that the hospital trust and Lancashire Care NHS Trust have already taken steps to address some issues. Both trusts have reviewed patient areas to reduce the risk of self-harm and improve the observation of high-risk patients. However, Jackie Williams's husband, Wayne, expressed disappointment with the inquest outcome, stating that he was not satisfied with the conclusion.
Inspectors made the discoveries during a surprise visit to HMP Bristol in Horfield earlier this year. Chief Inspector Nick Hardwick stated that a "sense of drift" had returned to the prison following a positive report in 2010. The National Offender Management Service has announced that the prison will receive support to enhance performance. At the 2010 inspection, Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Prisons noted improvements and described the prison as well-led with a "clear sense of direction." However, they found that this progress was not sustained. The recent inspection revealed that staff at the category-B prison, which can accommodate up to 600 inmates, were too dismissive towards inmates. The report stated that staff displayed disreputable behavior, using derogatory and abusive language, and fewer prisoners than at similar prisons reported being treated with respect. Cells were also found to be dirty, with damaged or missing furniture, offensive graffiti, and an infestation of cockroaches. The report noted a lack of clean clothes and adequate bedding for inmates. Furthermore, inspectors observed the arbitrary punishment of a prisoner when a prison officer prevented him from having his full meals. The prison has taken disciplinary action against the staff involved. Hardwick stated that the experience of prisoners was poor, identifying priorities for the prison to improve the environment and staff culture, and provide inmates with something useful to do that will equip them for the future. The inspectors highlighted broadly proportionate security procedures and the prison's largely successful resettlement challenges. Michael Spurr, of the National Offender Management Service, said that the prison had been through a significant period of restructuring and change at the time of the inspection. He also noted that the high turnover of prisoners presented a challenging time for the governor and her staff. "The prison and its staff will receive the necessary support to help raise performance and deliver a safe and constructive environment for the prisoners it holds," Spurr added. The governor of HMP Bristol, Andrea Albutt, was unavailable for comment.
A 34-year-old man from Shanlea Drive appeared before an Antrim Magistrates Court, accused of arson endangering life with intent. On Sunday, a house on the Killyglen Road suffered significant damage when a car was set alight and left at the front door. The incident also involved alleged threats to kill a man, intimidation of a woman and two children, and making a false report of a crime. On April 23, Mr Lynch was charged with wasting police time by making a false report of a crime. The court heard that police had CCTV footage showing Mr Lynch crashing his silver Peugeot into the house, appearing unsteady on his feet, and setting light to a jacket before flames engulfed the car. The footage also showed him kicking the window of the house and shouting at the property. There was no one inside at the time. The police officer testified that Mr Lynch had previously made false reports to police and that the occupants of the house had reported several incidents over the past few weeks, including sightings of men wearing balaclavas outside the house. The officer stated that Mr Lynch and another man had reportedly gone to the home, made a gun sign to the male occupant, and told him, "You're dead." On April 16, the male occupant woke up to find his car and wife's car on fire. The officer argued that bail should be denied due to potential interference with witnesses and a likelihood of reoffending. The officer stated that Mr Lynch's actions showed he had no regard for his own safety or that of others, and that police believed he was involved in the UVF in Larne and was "actively involved" in an ongoing criminal feud there. "There is a real fear that lives will be lost," the officer added. A lawyer for Mr Lynch argued that there was no evidence linking him to other arsons in Larne in recent days. The lawyer said that Mr Lynch accepted the CCTV footage showed him carrying out the arson, but disputed making threats or carrying out intimidation. He claimed that the arson attack was the result of a "heavily intoxicated man" and that Mr Lynch had realized he would likely end up in jail. The lawyer stated that Mr Lynch was not directly involved in the ongoing dispute, but was on the periphery, and that he had been pushed to the edge and broken. The judge denied bail, citing his responsibility to protect the public, and adjourned the case for two weeks.
Lewis Coaches, based in Ceredigion, is set to cease operations on Fridays. The Confederation of Passenger Transport Cymru reported that 40 jobs are at risk. This is the third bus company in Wales to collapse in the past two months, following the administration of Wrexham-based GHA Coaches, which left over 300 staff without jobs. In June, Pembrokeshire's Silcox Coaches, a company that provided school transport, local services, and tours, closed after 134 years. The Ceredigion council confirmed that Lewis Coaches, based in Llanrhystud, has also gone into administration. The 701 bus service from Aberystwyth to Carmarthen and Cardiff will no longer operate. Plaid Cymru's Mid and West AM, Simon Thomas, has written to the Welsh Government seeking assurances about the bus route. "We rely heavily on bus services in the west to connect our communities and allow workers, students, and tourists to travel," he said. "This essential link cannot be lost." Lewis Coaches expressed sadness on its website, stating that it would like to thank its customers for their loyalty and hoping that the closure will not cause any problems.

Samsung's latest financial forecast reveals a substantial jump in operating profit, nearly 80% higher than the same period last year, reaching 7.3 trillion won ($6.29 billion; Â£4.13 billion). This surpasses analysts' expectations for the three months to September, with the figure marking the company's first quarterly profit growth in two years. The world's largest smartphone manufacturer has been struggling to maintain its market share due to intense competition from rival Apple and cheaper Chinese competitors like Xiaomi. However, analysts attribute the firm's success to stronger sales in its chip business and favourable currency exchange rates, which have offset the decline in its mobile business. As the world's largest maker of memory chips, Samsung's semiconductor unit is expected to continue generating significant revenue, with its top spot for the fifth consecutive quarter. The weaker Korean won has also made the company's products more affordable overseas, boosting its earnings when repatriated. Samsung predicts its sales will rise by 7.5% to 51 trillion won in the same period. The announcement sent Seoul-listed shares soaring nearly 9% after the positive guidance. The release of the full results, including division-by-division breakdowns and a net income figure, is expected later this month.

At the time, he was deemed unfit to have safe contact with his children. Ms Throssell emphasized the importance of ensuring children's voices are heard in decision-making processes. Both boys died in the blaze, with Jack succumbing to his injuries six days later. A coroner ruled that both boys were unlawfully killed and determined that their father's actions constituted suicide.

Cox, a former sprinter who suffered a stroke in 2014, set a world record of 34.598 seconds in the C4-5 cycling time trial in Rio. More than 272 people who have had cancer in the UK over the past five years have donated their organs, resulting in 675 transplants, according to data from the Press Association. One area where these donors have made a significant impact is in eye donation, with 1,033 people going on to donate their eyes after being diagnosed with certain types of cancer. However, the Advisory Committee on the Safety of Blood, Tissues and Organs has clarified that not all organs can be donated, even by those with cancer. While there is a common misconception that individuals with cancer cannot be organ donors, officials say that the risks of cancer transmission must be weighed against the risk of not receiving a transplant. The committee states that organs from deceased donors with some cancers can be safely used for transplantation. Currently, the risk of donor-transmitted cancer in the UK is estimated to be just 0.06%. Prof John Forsythe, associate medical director for organ donation and transplantation at NHS Blood and Transplant, advises people not to let a health condition or previous illness deter them from registering as a donor. "We want everyone to register their decision to donate, regardless of their health status, and inform their family of their wishes." NHS Blood and Transplant works to minimize the risks to recipients by carefully evaluating all potential organ and tissue donors. Approximately 70 cornea donations per week are needed to meet the demand for sight-saving transplants. However, one in 10 people on the NHS Organ Donor Register do not wish to donate their eyes. A tragic example of the impact of eye donation is that of Alison Cooney, a midwife who died in 2010 at the age of 28 after being diagnosed with bowel and liver cancer. Her mother, Ann Cooney, agreed to donate her corneas after her death, which helped save the sight of two people. However, her major organs could not be donated due to the aggressive nature of her illness. Ann Cooney reflects on the experience, saying that while it was initially difficult to accept what was happening, they focused on the positive outcome of saving two people's sight.
Shawn Tyson was found guilty of the murder of his friends James Cooper, 25, and James Kouzaris, 24, in Sarasota, Florida, on April 16, 2011. An appeal court confirmed his conviction but overturned his sentence because he was just 16 when the crime was committed. The case was sent back to a lower court for a new sentencing hearing. In his ruling, Associate Senior Judge James R. Case stated that Tyson's guilty verdicts were upheld "without discussion." However, the judge explained that as a juvenile homicide offender, his sentence required re-assessment due to a Supreme Court ruling that prohibited mandatory life terms without parole for juveniles, which would be considered cruel and unusual punishment. Thirty volunteer bell-ringers at York Minster were informed at a meeting on Tuesday that all bell-ringing activities had stopped immediately. Peter Sanderson expressed his desire to know the reason behind the decision. Dean Vivienne Faull stated that the move was made for health, safety, and security reasons. The bells would remain silent until Easter, with a new head bell-ringer and team recruited in the new year. A petition calling for the bell-ringers to be reinstated for special occasions such as Remembrance Sunday, Christmas Day, and New Year's Eve has garnered over 12,000 signatures. Sanderson, who has led the Minster's bell-ringing team for 10 years, was "devastated, frustrated, and bewildered" after the meeting. He urged the Dean to meet and discuss a plan that addresses the Minster's concerns. This would allow the bells to be heard across the city again. Recruiting a new team and training them to meet the Minster's standards within three months is considered a challenging task. In an interview with BBC Radio York, the Dean stated that the bell-ringers were an autonomous organisation that needed to be brought back under the Minster's control. She emphasized that ensuring the safety and security of the bell-ringers was crucial.

GlaxoSmithKline, the British pharmaceutical giant, is facing mounting allegations of bribery across multiple countries. The company, which is already under investigation in several nations, has pledged to cooperate fully with the UK's Serious Fraud Office. Earlier this month, Chinese authorities accused GSK staff of bribing government officials and hospital administrators in the country. GSK declined to comment on whether the SFO's investigation was linked to a specific incident, but the company insisted it operates its business according to the highest ethical standards. The multinational firm is also under scrutiny in Poland and Iraq over similar allegations, and if proven, could have broken the UK Bribery Act and the US Foreign Corrupt Practices Act. As a US and UK-based company, bribing foreign government officials is strictly prohibited. The Serious Fraud Office confirmed it has launched a criminal investigation into GlaxoSmithKline's business practices and welcomed any information from whistleblowers or individuals with inside knowledge. A former GSK sales representative in Poland, Jarek Wisniewiski, revealed to BBC Panorama that doctors in the country had been paid to promote the company's asthma medication Seretide. Eleven doctors and a regional manager were charged with corruption between 2010 and 2012. Chinese authorities began investigating GSK in July, detaining four executives. GSK had previously informed the UK's SFO, the US Department of Justice, and the US Securities and Exchange Commission about the Chinese probe.

About 800 officers have been trained to hand out on-the-spot punishments, which help first-time offenders avoid the criminal justice system. Since 2011, restorative justice has been in use, with 190 offenders dealt with through the system to date. The approach is only used if the victim agrees, and it's based on three core principles. According to Insp Dominic Human, "You acknowledge what you've done is wrong, make amends, and reintegrate into society." This leads to a quick resolution, as victims are given a chance to accept responsibility and move forward. For many, an apology is sufficient, and restorative justice allows this to happen. Trained officers work with victims of minor crimes to suggest punishments and determine if they're fair. "We're not judges or juries," said Insp Human. "We're simply facilitating a chance for someone to acknowledge their wrongdoing and make amends." As part of the project, offenders were required to compensate for their actions. A 16-year-old thief had to clean 100 shopping baskets, a 12-year-old who damaged a car had to write an apology letter, and the parents paid for the damage. Insp Human notes that the system saves a significant amount of time compared to traditional court proceedings. "We can achieve the same outcome in a fraction of the time," he said. "The goal of restorative justice is to do what's right for the victim." Since its introduction, the system has saved over 1,000 operational policing hours, allowing officers to focus on other community issues. Cambridgeshire Constabulary plans to train all officers in restorative justice methods in the future.

Italy's third-largest bank faces a deadline to raise €5 billion in new capital by the end of the month. Without a private sector bailout, the Italian government may intervene as early as this week, given the bank's struggles with bad loans and its status as the weakest major EU bank. Italian Prime Minister Paolo Gentiloni is under pressure, as any losses incurred by private investors under EU bailout rules could have severe consequences. He described the potential move as a "precautionary measure", stating that it's the government's duty to protect savings. Gentiloni hopes that all political parties in parliament will share this responsibility. However, Economy Minister Pier Carlo Padoan clarified that the funds will be used to maintain liquidity in the banking system and support other struggling banks. The government is also exploring a plan to compensate retail investors for any losses they may incur. Gentiloni's predecessor, Matteo Renzi, resigned following a referendum defeat and was often accused of being too close to the banks.
Over 400 complete skeletons were uncovered during a four-year study at St John's College between 2010 and 2012. The remains, dating from the 13th to 15th centuries, belonged to the hospital's burial grounds. The £1.2m project aims to investigate the health of urban poor during the devastating epidemic. In addition to the skeletons, nearly 1,000 body parts were discovered in what a St John's College spokesperson described as one of the largest medieval hospital burial grounds ever found in Britain. The majority of those buried on the site were ordinary townspeople, rather than clergy, and included men, women, and some children. The plague, which ravaged Europe, claimed millions of lives. Researchers from various disciplines, including archaeology, history, and genetics, will collaborate to examine the impact of the Black Death on a medieval town's population before, during, and after the epidemic. The Hospital of St John the Evangelist, an Augustinian charity, provided care to the public between 1200 and 1500. The research is being funded by the Wellcome Trust and St John's College.

Last week, the Police Service of Northern Ireland (PSNI) announced that some of its officers will participate in the Belfast Pride parade for the first time, wearing their uniforms. While officers have attended the parade in the past, they have never done so in full attire. Garda officers have also been granted permission to wear their uniforms at the Belfast parade, although their attendance will be voluntary and unpaid, as they will be participating in a different jurisdiction. The event is scheduled to take place in Belfast city centre on August 5. According to a Garda spokesperson, members of An Garda Síochána, their families, and friends are invited to join the event, alongside members from the PSNI and colleagues from Great Britain. Garda members are permitted to participate in uniform, with their attendance at the event being their own responsibility. The PSNI will also be present at the event with vehicles and signs to encourage the public to report homophobic hate crimes. In addition, the 'Policing with Pride' liveried PSNI vehicles will be used at Pride events in Newry and Londonderry this year.
A pilot study involving 261 teenage girls at three secondary schools found that a six-lesson course on body image significantly improved their self-esteem and body image compared to the regular curriculum. According to psychologists at King's College London, nearly one in three teenagers expresses dissatisfaction with their body. Poor body image has been linked to depression, eating disorders, excessive cosmetic surgery, obesity, and unhealthy weight loss behaviors. Dr. Helen Sharpe and colleagues at the Institute of Psychiatry developed a six-part course focused on promoting positive body image, tackling unhealthy peer interactions, and practical measures to boost mood and self-esteem. The course, taught by teachers in three state-funded girls' schools, showed promising results in their research published in The British Journal of Psychiatry. Dr. Sharpe stated that their study demonstrated the effectiveness of teacher-delivered lessons in improving body esteem. She expressed hope that further research could enhance the program's effectiveness and ultimately reduce disordered eating behaviors such as binge eating and unhelpful weight loss. The researchers noted that interventions by teachers would have a wide reach and be cost-effective, especially in comparison to unproven programs that may be harmful. In response to growing concerns, MPs recommended that all schoolchildren participate in compulsory body image and self-esteem lessons last year. An inquiry by the All-Party Parliamentary Group on Body Image revealed that over half of the public harbors a negative body image, with girls as young as five now worrying about their appearance. The MPs' report also noted a 20% increase in cosmetic surgery rates since 2008.
A flight search engine uses stopovers to find cheaper flights. Airlines, United and Orbitz, claim the service is unfair competition and seeks to recoup lost revenue. The site's developer says he's exposing airline inefficiencies, not breaking any rules. The lawsuit was filed in Illinois. Skiplagged works by finding longer flights that include a stop in a major city on the way to another destination. For example, flying from New York to Lake Tahoe with a stop in San Francisco might be cheaper than booking a direct flight to San Francisco from New York. Some travellers can save up to 40% on ticket fares by booking a stopover flight instead of a direct one. However, this trick only works for one-way flights and doesn't allow checked luggage, as it would go to the final destination. Skiplagged's 22-year-old creator, Aktarer Zaman, says he hasn't made any profit from the site. He declined to comment on the case. Zaman has started a fundraising campaign to raise money to fight the lawsuit against United and Orbitz. So far, he has raised $10,538 of the $15,000 needed. United and Orbitz claim Skiplagged is "intentionally and maliciously" interfering with their business and breaking contracts with partners. They argue that using "hidden city" tickets poses logistical and public safety concerns and that Skiplagged is breaching these rules. The firms are seeking damages of at least $75,000 in revenue they claim they have lost due to Skiplagged's operations.
A Langstone memorial marks the culmination of a four-year project that began with a plan to tell the stories of six World War Two servicemen. Following research by Langstone council, which uncovered the details of 10 additional men from the previous conflict, the memorial was dedicated by the Bishop of Monmouth, the Right Reverend Richard Pain, on a Saturday in May. The project started when two members of Langstone council sought to learn more about six men listed on a World War Two memorial plaque in the village hall. Chairman of the community council, Edward Jeffery, not only delved into the stories behind the original six but also discovered 10 more men from Langstone, Llandevaud, Llanmartin, and surrounding areas who lost their lives in World War One. The memorial, built from granite, pays homage to the area's history of quarrying, while a time capsule filled with items by children from Langstone Primary School reflects the lives of the local community. The memorial also features a QR code that allows visitors to learn more about the men whose names are inscribed on it.
A groundbreaking discovery is helping researchers better understand the "strong force," which holds atomic centers together. The new particle, Xi-cc++, was theoretically predicted but has now been identified for the first time. The details of the particle were presented at a high-energy physics conference in Venice. The study, led by Dr. Patrick Spradlin of Glasgow University, marked a significant breakthrough. Dr. Spradlin noted that the discovery would "shed light on a long-standing puzzle and open a new avenue of investigation." His colleague, Prof. Paul Soler, also from Glasgow University, described the development as "a new frontier in understanding the strong force." At its core, atomic matter is composed of neutrons and protons, which form atomic centers. These centers are made up of three smaller particles called quarks, which can be either light or heavy. However, researchers have discovered six different types of quarks, which combine in various ways to form other particles. So far, particles containing no more than one heavy quark have been detected. The discovery of a particle with two heavy quarks is a significant milestone. According to Prof. Guy Wilkinson of Oxford University, there's an interesting difference between this new particle and others of its kind. "Unlike other particles, which have quarks performing a complex dance, a particle with two heavy quarks is expected to behave like a planetary system, with the heavy quarks orbiting each other and a lighter quark orbiting around the binary system." The research team will now investigate the properties of the Xi-cc++ to understand how this arrangement of quarks behaves and how the strong force holds it together. They also anticipate finding more double-heavy quark particles. Another unique feature of the particle is its double-positive charge, which is twice that of the proton, and its massive size, four times that of the proton. The researchers have submitted a paper detailing these findings to Physical Review Letters.
A chaotic scene erupted at the Lava and Ignite club in Northampton on October 18, 2011, when 19-year-old Laurene-Danielle Jackson and 22-year-old Nabila Nanfuka became trapped in a crowded stairway. Both girls were found unconscious and died from asphyxiation. Laurene-Danielle's friend, Leonel Oliviera, 25, told the Northampton County Court hearing that he tried to perform CPR, but she had no pulse. Oliviera described a scuffle that broke out as people rushed to leave the club following repeated announcements that coaches were departing. He stated that this altercation triggered a change in the crowd's mood, leading to a "domino effect" of chaos. Oliviera recounted carrying a semi-conscious man back inside and then picking up another girl, who was fully unconscious, and carrying her out. Laurene-Danielle, a psychology student at Kingston University, died at Leicester's Glenfield Hospital on November 6. Nabila, who was studying a leisure and tourism degree at Northampton University, died at Northampton General Hospital on October 19. Her friend Olabanji Gellatly-Olanrewaju reported seeing five people fall on top of Nabila, who rolled onto her back in an attempt to free herself. Someone then came to her aid and carried her outside, where she was motionless. The club's former manager, Spencer Fitzgerald, testified that he attempted to cope with the situation by deploying additional doormen to the cloakroom area. He described trying to calm the crowd but ended up becoming overwhelmed. On the night of the incident, there were approximately 1,700 people at the club, who had traveled from across the UK for the "Wickedest Wickedest" RnB night. The inquest continues.
A Spanish magistrate has ruled that insufficient evidence exists to prosecute a 62-year-old man for manslaughter by negligence in connection with a fatal crash in Catalonia. Among the 13 victims of the crash, seven were Italian, with one of their parents, Alessandro Saracino, expressing his distress again. In an interview with La Repubblica, Saracino stated that the families may decide to file an appeal. Initially, Catalan police had suggested that a black box on board indicated multiple changes in speed prior to the crash. However, the court in Amposta, in the southern region, concluded that there was no proof of speeding and no mechanical fault. Additionally, the court found that the driver was not impaired by substances. The accident occurred on March 20 near Freginals, approximately 150 kilometers south of Barcelona. The majority of the passengers were exchange students returning to Barcelona from a fireworks festival in Valencia. The victims were all between 19 and 25 years old, with seven being from Italy, two from Germany, and one each from Romania, Austria, France, and Uzbekistan. The court's decision can be appealed.

Microsoft plans to distribute its computing resources over the next three years to around 70,000 non-profit organizations and 900 university research projects. In essence, cloud computing refers to storing data online rather than on a personal computer. This approach not only makes data more accessible but also promises to provide non-profits with vast computing power that would typically be out of reach for smaller organizations. According to a blog post by Microsoft's chief legal officer Brad Smith, cloud services have the potential to unlock new insights from data, leading to breakthroughs in various fields, including science, technology, and addressing economic and social challenges. The processing of "big data" is seen as a significant opportunity for non-profits addressing complex social issues that require substantial computing power. In this context, Microsoft's pledge is not a tangible product or cash but rather access to servers and services that larger businesses would typically need to pay for. The funds will also be used to improve internet connectivity, particularly in countries with limited access, with the aim of providing basic internet services, such as broadband at home, to those who lack it. Other companies, including Facebook, have pursued similar goals through initiatives like the Internet.org project, which aims to increase internet access in developing countries. However, such efforts have not always been well-received, as seen with Facebook's Internet.org project, which has faced criticism in India for favoring local businesses over competitors. Microsoft's donation may also raise concerns that the company is seeking to establish long-term partnerships with non-profits. Despite this, Microsoft's CEO Satya Nadella has been praised for his efforts to use the company's resources to support development in countries, including India, where he is a native. In a recent blog post, Nadella emphasized the need to ensure that cloud computing benefits are accessible to all, regardless of geographical location or economic status.
A proposed tunnel linking Slough's Great Western main line to Heathrow Airport's Terminal 5 has been delayed, with a planning application now set to be submitted in late 2017, rather than in the next few weeks as initially planned. The link, which would stretch for 5km, is seen as crucial for attracting more people to Reading, a view shared by local business owners. Nick Halstead, owner of Datasift in Green Park, described the delay as "disappointing" and warned that it would make it harder to attract staff to his business. Slough Labour MP Fiona Mactaggart criticized the delay, calling it a "failure of leadership" by the government. Tim Brownstone, who runs sportswear company Kymira in Reading, emphasized the need for the rail link to bring in external investment to the area. A review of Network Rail's investment programme by Sir Peter Hendy found that several projects, including this one, would take longer than planned. Network Rail officials attributed the delay to the need for extensive planning and changes following the Hendy Report. They also pointed out that consultations with landowners and interested parties in the area were required. Originally, the new service was set to launch by 2021, with the tunnel saving passengers up to 30 minutes on their journey from south Wales, the west of England, and the Thames Valley.

Hawkins acknowledged that the start was more aggressive than he anticipated, saying, "It started off a lot more aggressive than I thought." He had intended to employ a different strategy and aim for a medal, but this didn't pay off. Matt Bond finished 42nd, with fellow Englishmen Lee Merrien and Jonny Hay coming in 52nd and 77th. Derek Hawkins did not participate due to an injury, while Tewelde pulled out overnight due to illness. In the women's race, Gemma Steel secured the first British finish in 10th place, with a time of 1:12:19. Alyson Dixon finished 13th, while Lily Partridge and Tina Muir came in 51st and 59th, respectively. Charlotte Purdue failed to complete the event.

Thieves broke into Pip and Jim's Church in St James Place, Ilfracombe, Devon on Saturday night, making off with a safe containing silver plates, chalices, and other valuable silverware. The safe was found ripped open nearby, and police were called to investigate. In the meantime, churchgoers held their Sunday morning service in a nearby pub. Vicar's wife Alison Rogers, who discovered the burglary, explained: "I was going to lead an act of worship and found the back door was badly damaged. "I called the police and just 10 minutes later, they told me they'd found the safe. The thieves had moved it to a trolley and carried it about 300 yards to Capstone Hill." The service took place at Wetherspoon's pub, which is just outside the church gates. "It was a lovely service," Mrs Rogers said. "The locals have always been very kind to us." While the value of the stolen silverware remains unknown, Mrs Rogers was relieved that no one was hurt. Insp Ian Dawson of Devon and Cornwall Police emphasized that the public is at low risk, as the safe was left in an open area, which may have exposed the offenders to potential asbestos when they forced it open. As such, they advise the perpetrators to prioritize their own safety and that of any family members or associates they may have visited after the crime.

Dr. Keith McNeil left Cambridge's Addenbrooke's Hospital this week, following the Care Quality Commission's likely "inadequate" rating on September 22. Paul James, the hospital's chief finance officer, who has been struggling with a £1.2m weekly deficit, has also departed. McNeil described Addenbrooke's as "phenomenal," stating that people across the country would be envious of its results. "We save lives every day at that hospital," he said, expressing his disbelief at the CQC's assessment. When asked if the commission's rating was wrong, McNeil replied that he believed so. He was appointed chief executive in 2012 but stepped down on Monday, along with James. McNeil rejected the idea that the hospital's operations were inadequate, saying it was a "sane and rational interpretation" that wouldn't apply. The hospital faced criticism last year for implementing a £200m electronic hospital system, but McNeil defended the move, calling it "absolutely the right thing to do." He explained that the system has its value, especially as staff become more familiar with it and customize it. McNeil argued that the system's high cost was justified, as it would have been more expensive to maintain an outdated system over the next decade.

The Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC) launched an investigation after the discovery of DVDs containing images of naked children that had not been acted upon. This led to a disciplined intelligence officer, although the nature of the punishment was not disclosed. Deputy Chief Constable Tim Madgwick stated that the force would "fully accept" the IPCC's recommendations. The report advised the force to implement a more robust and formalised approach to supervising and monitoring intelligence. The Child Exploitation and Online Protection Centre's operation Spade was investigating the supply of these DVDs to the UK from Canada, and had shared intelligence with North Yorkshire Police in November 2013, but it wasn't acted upon until the last week of September 2014. An investigation found that a force intelligence bureau officer had failed to adequately handle the material. Operation Spade had identified 25 suspects, the IPCC reported. IPCC Commissioner Kathryn Stone stated that North Yorkshire Police had promptly implemented new working methods following the investigation. Mr Madgwick also emphasised that the force takes its safeguarding responsibilities extremely seriously.
Avon and Somerset's top cop, Nick Gargan, was suspended amid allegations that he made unwanted advances to female colleagues. The police oversight body claimed he may have broken the Data Protection Act by sending sensitive information to individuals unrelated to the force via email. Gargan, who denies the accusations, is set to be questioned soon. According to Rachel Cerfontyne of the Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC), Gargan has been formally notified that this aspect of his behavior is now under investigation by the authorities. During the probe, investigators sifted through a substantial amount of digital and phone records, with 50 staff members from the force providing valuable insights and information. The IPCC confirmed that this cooperation was instrumental in advancing the investigation. On behalf of Gargan, a spokesperson for the Chief Police Officers' Staff Association stated that the chief constable would continue to cooperate with the investigation. In a statement, Gargan expressed satisfaction that progress had been made regarding the allegations of improper behavior and reassured that the matter was no longer being treated as a serious offense. He expressed his eagerness to return to work and hoped that the investigation could be expedited as soon as possible.
South West Trains plans to open the new Berkshire station to the public as soon as possible, despite the official opening date set for November 14. The project was initially due for completion in August, but ground works issues caused significant delays. Transport Minister Norman Baker will officially open the station. The upgrade includes a modern main building with retail space, lifts, and a footbridge. The old station building will be demolished once the new adjacent facility is ready. According to Robin Ashton from the Wokingham Chamber of Commerce, the town is thrilled with the news, and he anticipates it will be even more welcome when the site is fully completed on November 14. South West Trains notes that Wokingham station handles over two million journeys annually, and the upgrade aims to encourage more people to travel to work and for leisure by rail.
A report compiled for the police and crime commissioner of South Yorkshire identified 105 gangs, with 27 linked to firearms and 44 to drugs. South Yorkshire Police has made tackling firearms gangs its top priority. Assistant Chief Constable Rachel Barber said the actual use of guns in the county is relatively limited. The number of gangs is comparable to the county's population size, and not all are active, Barber added. Most gang-related offenses occur between gangs themselves, she stated. The force aims to investigate how reactivated firearms enter the county. The report also found that gangs are involved in immigration and child sexual exploitation. ACC Barber said the force is collaborating with local authorities to help children with family members in gangs "break the cycle" of crime. Dr. Alan Billings, the South Yorkshire police and crime commissioner, chairs a Public Accountability Board meeting where the report was presented.

I'd like to thank the players leaving us for their hard work and professionalism."
A devastating fire swept through a nursing home within a hospital in Tainan City, claiming the lives of elderly patients who were mostly bed-ridden and reliant on respirators. According to hospital officials, the victims died from inhaling toxic smoke. Residents who were injured were being treated at multiple hospitals. Police have arrested a suspect, who is believed to be a patient at the nursing home, on suspicion of starting the blaze. The nursing home, which housed over 100 people, was particularly vulnerable due to its location and the patients' inability to move independently. "It was pitch black and the heavy smoke was unbearable," said a resident who managed to escape the inferno. "I had to use my wheelchair to find a hiding spot on the second floor until rescuers found me," a 94-year-old woman reported. Thirty fire engines responded to the blaze, which was extinguished just 45 minutes after it was reported, at 04:16 local time (20:16 GMT). Tainan Mayor Lai Ching-te attributed the severity of the situation to the hospital's remote location and the fact that most patients were immobile. Firefighters arrived at the scene within 10 minutes, but residents had to help transport injured patients to other hospitals. The fire is considered one of the deadliest at a Taiwanese hospital in recent years, according to experts. Premier Sean Chen expressed his condolences to the victims' families in a statement.
The UK government has issued an invitation for tenders to provide training for civil servants across Whitehall on trade policy. The aim is to equip staff with a solid understanding of the UK's trade priorities and those of its key partners, as well as relevant terminology. This comes as the UK seeks to finalize a series of free trade deals post-Brexit, but concerns have been raised about the lack of expertise in this area. Former UK ambassador to the EU Sir Ivan Rogers had previously stated that multilateral negotiating experience was in short supply in the UK, citing the EU's decades-long involvement in trade negotiations on the UK's behalf. Many UK specialists in the field have worked in Brussels, leaving a void that the government now seeks to fill. The Foreign Office and the Department for International Trade are looking for a supplier to deliver "short-term civil service training in international trade policy and negotiations." The successful bidder will be expected to provide staff across Whitehall departments with a comprehensive knowledge of the key areas and terminology of international trade policy, as well as the UK's and its key partners' trading priorities. The goal is to equip officials with the knowledge and skills needed to support and deliver the UK's international trade policy priorities and negotiating agenda both domestically and internationally. The KPMG firm is managing the process, which is part of the Cabinet Office's core curriculum. The UK cannot enter into trade deals until it has left the EU, but it is currently exploring potential future opportunities, including bilateral agreements with countries like Australia, New Zealand, and Canada. US President Donald Trump has expressed his desire for an early trade accord with the UK post-Brexit and is set to discuss the issue with Prime Minister Theresa May on Friday. The Treasury has increased funding for the Department for International Trade and the Foreign Office to enhance the UK's trade promotion capabilities. In the past, a senior official noted that the civil service was committed to equipping existing staff for new responsibilities, and may also seek to "buy in" talent from outside Whitehall. The number of trade policy experts in the UK has increased significantly, from 45 at the time of the June referendum to over 100 today.
In February 1957, Moira Anderson, then 11 years old, left her grandmother's home in Coatbridge to run errands but was never seen again. Bus driver and convicted child abuser Alexander Gartshore, who died in 2006, is suspected of her murder. The investigation has focused on the area of Monkland Canal, where police are now searching for her remains. Moira was last seen on February 23, 1957, during a heavy snowstorm, boarding a Baxter's bus driven by Gartshore. Later that year, he was sentenced to jail for raping a 17-year-old babysitter. In 1999, a former friend of Gartshore, James Gallogley, named him as Moira's murderer. Gartshore's own daughter, Sandra Brown, also believed he was responsible and campaigned to have him charged. In 2014, prosecutors announced that Gartshore would have faced prosecution for the schoolgirl's murder if he were still alive. A witness reported seeing a tall man carrying a large sack towards the canal the morning after Moira disappeared, but the sighting was not pursued. In 2018, a grave in Old Monkland Cemetery was exhumed as part of the search, but no evidence was found linking Moira to the site.
Charlie Naysmith stumbled upon a 600g lump of ambergris while searching for seaweed at Hengistbury Head in Bournemouth. Initially, he thought it was just a normal stone, but after consulting the internet with his father, Alex, they discovered it was likely a rare form of vomit from a sperm whale. Ambergris originates from the sperm whale's innards and is used in perfumes after it has been regurgitated. Due to its rarity and ability to float on the ocean for years, it can be highly sought after. Mr Naysmith, from Christchurch, believes the lump his son found is approximately 20 years old and may be worth up to £40,000. He noted that ambergris is extremely rare and usually found in small amounts. Historically, it was used in perfumes and some medicines. While he's unsure if it's worth that much today, he's optimistic. Charlie plans to use the potential earnings to create a nature reserve for animals in the country. For centuries, ambergris has been referred to as "floating gold" by scientists and treasure hunters. It's believed to serve as a digestive aid for sperm whales and is often expelled from their abdomen while hundreds of kilometres away from land. Initially, it's a soft, foul-smelling substance that floats on the ocean, but over time, it hardens into a compact, waxy rock with a sweet smell. Although still used in perfumes, many manufacturers now opt for synthetic alternatives due to its association with whaling and the endangered sperm whale species.
Edinburgh Airport experienced the largest growth, followed by Aberdeen and Glasgow. In February, the capital airport welcomed 607,723 passengers, a 7.8% increase from the same month last year. International passenger numbers surged 11.6% to 269,235, driven by new routes from easyJet and Lufthansa to Frankfurt. Domestic passenger numbers also rose by 4.9%, with 338,488 people traveling through the airport last month. The airport attributed the growth to an increase in travelers heading to Heathrow with Virgin Atlantic Little Red and Gatwick with British Airways. Edinburgh Airport's CEO, Gordon Dewar, expressed satisfaction with the results, saying: "February was another strong month for us, and we're pleased to see a healthy rise in our passenger figures." The airport performed well internationally, with notable successes from Lufthansa and Turkish Airlines. Dewar also announced that the airport is looking forward to a busy summer schedule and the launch of 10 new routes between March and June, including Chicago, Philadelphia, and Doha in May. Aberdeen Airport saw a 6.5% increase in passenger numbers, with 255,416 people passing through last month. The airport reported growth in both fixed-wing and helicopter operations. Aberdeen's managing director, Carol Benzie, welcomed the results, saying: "In the coming weeks, we will begin our summer schedule." Benzie noted that the airport expects an increase in holiday traffic due to new charter destinations, such as Corfu for sunseekers. The airport will also see an increase in scheduled operations with new services to Riga and Oslo. Glasgow Airport saw a 6% increase in passenger numbers, with 448,465 people passing through last month. The airport reported a strong month for international traffic, with a 13% increase to 174,236 due to demand for long-haul services and new airlines such as Jet2.com, Icelandair, and KLM adding capacity. Domestic traffic rose by 2% to 274,229, driven by strong demand for London and regional services. CityJet's new Cardiff service was also popular. Glasgow Airport's managing director, Amanda McMillan, expressed satisfaction with the results, saying: "It's very pleasing to have continued our positive start to 2014." McMillan announced that the airport will launch Citywing's new service to the Isle of Man and will become the first Scottish airport to welcome the A380 in April, operated by Emirates.
Len Allchurch had a distinguished career spanning over 600 League matches from 1950 to 1971, helping Sheffield United secure promotion to the First Division in the 1960-61 season. As the younger brother of Wales international Ivor Allchurch, Len was also a talented player who won 11 caps for Wales, although he didn't get to play in the 1958 World Cup. Former Swansea and Wales teammate Cliff Jones, now a Tottenham Hotspur legend, remembered his friend and fellow player fondly. "We grew up together and played football together," Jones said. "We were in the same Swansea schoolboy team and after leaving school, we worked in the docks. I was an apprentice sheet metal worker, while Lenny was an apprentice coppersmith." Jones continued, "We were closely related in both our working and sporting lives at Swansea Town. Lenny was an exceptional right winger with great control, a wonderful temperament, and excellent company." The 1950s were a golden era for football in Swansea, with Swansea Town producing some of the finest talent in Welsh football history. Other notable players from the club included John and Mel Charles, as well as Spurs legends Jones and Terry Medwin. Notably, John Charles was the only one who didn't play for his hometown team. Allchurch played 276 League games for Swansea before joining Sheffield United in 1961 for £18,000, where he scored five goals in the last seven games of the season to help the team secure promotion to the First Division. He later played for Stockport County, helping them win the Fourth Division title in 1966-67, before returning to Swansea City in 1969. After making 71 further League appearances, he retired in 1971. Although he was part of the 1958 World Cup squad, Len didn't get to play alongside his brother as Wales was knocked out by Brazil in the quarterfinals. However, he did play in the 2-0 win over Israel in the first leg of the play-off, which Wales won to reach the finals for the first and only time.

The pound plummeted to a 37-year low against the dollar, dipping to $1.3058, and a 10-year low against the euro at €1.1703. Analysts attributed this drastic drop to a disappointing UK services sector report and the ongoing uncertainty surrounding the UK's vote to leave the European Union. This anxiety was mirrored on Wall Street, where stocks closed lower. In London, shares in property firms and asset managers plummeted sharply after insurance giant Aviva became the second company in two days to suspend trading in a UK property fund following the Brexit vote. Aviva explained the freeze would take immediate effect due to "extraordinary market circumstances." Just a day earlier, Standard Life Investments had also suspended trading in its UK property fund. Mark Priest from ETX Capital said: "Investors are becoming increasingly anxious, and this level of action hasn't been seen since the financial crisis." Investors are now worried about the investment industry, causing sterling to plummet once again after a brief period of calm following the Brexit vote. The FTSE 100 index initially fell throughout the morning but later recovered after the Bank of England announced new measures to support the economy. The Bank of England has reduced the special capital requirements for banks, which could free up £150 billion for lending. The FTSE 100 closed 23.11 points, or 0.4%, higher at 6,545.37. However, the FTSE 250 - which includes more UK-focused companies - closed 382 points, or 2.4%, lower at 15,734.68. In the property sector, housebuilder Persimmon fell 7.2% despite reporting a rise in half-year revenues. Persimmon expressed confidence in its prospects and said it was too early to determine the impact of the UK's vote to leave the EU on the sector. However, the news from Standard Life Investments and Aviva cast a shadow over the entire property sector. Shares in Barratt Developments fell 9.8%, while Berkeley Group closed 6.4% lower. Standard Life Investments reported an increase in the number of investors asking to withdraw their money following the EU referendum vote. The £2.9 billion fund invests in a mix of commercial real estate in the UK, including office blocks, shopping centres, and warehouses.
The US government spent a tenth of the original sum on the plane to combat the Afghan drug trade, but millions more were spent on upgrades. However, the aircraft remains in storage in Delaware to this day, according to a report by the US Justice Department's Inspector General's Office. The report states that the plane is unlikely to ever be used in Afghanistan, as the programme it was intended for ended in 2015. The ATR 42-500 plane was meant to be part of an anti-drug initiative led by the DEA and Pentagon, but the costs of modifying and housing the aircraft skyrocketed. The US Department of Defence spent over $67.9 million on the plane and a custom-built hangar in Kabul, which was four times more than the original estimate. The report claims that DEA aviation officials failed to consider the significant time and cost required to establish a necessary infrastructure, including pilots, mechanics, trainers, and spare parts. The plane has remained inoperable for over seven years, resting on jacks, and has never flown in Afghanistan. A DEA official initially stated that the plane would be used for flights in the Caribbean and Latin America, but the report notes that this was not the intended purpose of the funding. These revelations come after a US government watchdog criticized a Pentagon agency for wasting millions on "ill-conceived" reconstruction projects in Afghanistan. The Special Inspector General for Afghan Reconstruction, John Sopko, highlighted a programme that spent $6 million on importing rare Italian goats to support the Afghan cashmere industry, raising concerns about the effectiveness of oversight. Last year, Sopko noted that over $43 million was spent on building a vehicle refuelling station in Afghanistan, which was 140 times more expensive than a comparable station in Pakistan. He suspected that fraud and corruption may have contributed to the increased cost.
The Healthcare Environment Inspectorate (HEI) made a request after inspecting Western Isles Hospital in Stornoway, Lewis, in September. Inspectors found that there was no record of the storage temperature for breast milk, nor was there a staff policy in place for handling and storing it. However, the HEI stated that the hospital was otherwise clean and met the required standards. The inspectors only raised this specific concern regarding breast milk storage, without making any recommendations. Jacqui Macrae, head of quality of care for HEI, noted that the inspection had shown a good ward environment and patient equipment, as well as staff compliance with standard infection control procedures. NHS Western Isles chairman Dr Neil Galbraith said the board closely monitors hospital cleanliness due to its strong correlation with patient safety. He welcomed the HEI's findings, which largely matched the hospital's self-assessment report and provided external reassurance that their cleanliness and infection control measures were effective. Dr Galbraith added that the board will produce a policy paper on breast milk storage and implement the new practices immediately.

Currently, the facility supports 2,500 people with terminal illnesses, either as residents or day visitors. The expansion, which includes a new teaching centre, was officially marked by Alan Birchenall, Leicester City club ambassador and LOROS patron, who also cut the first sod at the charity's current site 31 years ago. This time, Birchenall brought the Premier League trophy, a gesture that reflects his long-standing connection with the charity. In addition to the day therapy unit expansion, LOROS is building a professional development centre to share its expertise with others. John Knight, LOROS chief executive, views this new centre as an extension of the charity's existing education programme. "It will be a stunning, bespoke facility where people can learn about palliative care," he said. "By building this centre, we're not only enhancing our own services, but also spreading our influence beyond our existing site."
BBC One's nature documentary has received nine nominations ahead of the Craft Awards, which honour the behind-the-scenes talent in television. Netflix's The Crown follows closely with seven nominations, while the BBC's The Night Manager has six. The winners will be announced on April 23, three weeks before the Bafta Television Awards on May 14. Here's a breakdown of the nominated programmes. The BBC's landmark natural history series has received two nominations each in the editing and sound categories, as well as a nomination for the original music prize. The show is also a shoo-in for the photography: factual category, which guarantees it at least one award. Netflix's The Crown has received nominations for its direction and writing, courtesy of Stephen Daldry and Peter Morgan. It has also been recognised for its costume design, production design, and photography and lighting, with additional nods going to its visual effects and titles. The BBC's adaptation of John le Carre's novel has received a nomination for best director for Susanne Bier, as well as nods for sound and editing. Like The Crown, it has also landed nominations for production design, visual effects, and titles. The BBC's adaptation of Tolstoy's novel is up for best make-up and hair design, thanks to its lavish costumes. Charlie Brooker's satirical series has been nominated for its music, sound, production design, and visual effects. Seamus McGarvey has received a nomination for his photography on the Nosedive episode starring Bryce Dallas Howard. Channel 4's topical drama about a TV personality accused of historical sex crimes has been recognised for its direction, editing, music, and photography. For the full list of nominations, check out the BBC website.
A film shot on a Derbyshire grouse moor in February has sparked outrage after footage appeared to show a gamekeeper targeting a hen harrier. Conservation group Raptor Persecution has called for action, citing concerns that the decoy bird may have been made to look like a male hen harrier to lure another into attack. Derbyshire Police investigated but decided not to take further action, while the National Trust has launched its own probe. The footage, captured by birdwatchers about a kilometre away, was released after police cleared them of any wrongdoing. Raptor Persecution's spokeswoman argued that while nothing illegal occurred, the decoy bird's appearance may have been designed to deceive the hen harrier, which is notorious for defending its territory. "While the bird is distracted by the decoy, the gamekeeper takes aim from a nearby hiding spot," she explained. The Royal Society for the Protection of Birds (RSPB) expressed concern that the footage might demonstrate an attempt to target hen harriers or other protected species. The Peak District National Park's chief executive, Sarah Fowler, described the video as "alarming and suspicious," and pledged her support for the investigation. The Moorland Association condemned wildlife crime but stated that no crime had been committed in this instance. They argued that making assumptions about the video's content or the intentions behind it would be speculative. Derbyshire's Police and Crime Commissioner, Alan Charles, noted that the incident highlighted the need to protect wildlife, particularly hen harriers, which are the most persecuted birds of prey in the UK. According to the RSPB, hen harriers have faced intense persecution in the UK, with five males disappearing from sites in England in 2015, leading to the collapse of nests and the disappearance of their whereabouts.
In August 2014 and April 2015, several significant discoveries were made in Wales. In Wenvoe, Vale of Glamorgan, a Bronze Age find was uncovered, while in Llanarmon-yn-Iâl, Denbighshire, an early medieval piece that would have been part of a brooch was discovered. The same year, a valuable early medieval silver ingot was found in Trelawnyd and Gwaenysgor, Flintshire. These finds were made by Gwyn Rees, who unearthed Bronze Age axe and spearheads that are believed to have been buried in a ritual ceremony around 3,500 years ago. The items are now of great interest to National Museum Wales, with the museum expressing a desire to purchase them. The brooch, discovered by Karina Kucharski, features a gold baseplate adorned with a blue glass bead set in a gold collar. Dr. Mark Redknap, of National Museum Wales, noted that early medieval gold work of this period is rare in Wales, and the discovery adds significantly to the understanding of early medieval jewellery and its circulation, as well as the gold working technologies used at that time. Meanwhile, the silver ingot, found by Francis Hodge, has been coveted by Flintshire museums.
England secured a vital win over Ireland on Saturday, propelling them to the top of the table with their third consecutive victory, relegating Wales to second place. This has set up a highly anticipated showdown between the two unbeaten teams at Twickenham on March 12. "It's a big challenge for us as a pack, but it's something we excel at," said Wasps second row Davies. "We need to outmuscle them at the set-piece and, for me, that's where the game is won or lost. "The English pack tries to overpower you, so we need to raise our game and hopefully outdo them." Davies believes Wales improve as the tournament progresses. Wales had previously won at Twickenham in the 2015 World Cup and had also secured victories there on their way to their 2008 and 2012 Grand Slams. They are now chasing their first consecutive victories at home since 1978. Davies, 29, made his first start in the Six Nations after recovering from a knee injury to Luke Charteris, having previously been on the bench for the opening two games. "In our recent history, we tend to get better the more weeks we spend together," Davies told BBC Wales Sport. "We proved that with a slow start against Ireland, and each week we've improved so hopefully we can do the same at Twickenham. "It will be tough, but we've been in this situation before and come out on top. "We always believe we can beat anyone, wherever we play or whoever we play against. We got written off before the World Cup and it was nice to get a win.
