George Kruis is an under-rated player who's finally getting the recognition he deserves. "Joe Launchbury, a club-mate of mine, is world-class when he wants to be, and Courtney Lawes is as well."
A 79-year-old former FIFA president, Sepp Blatter, was charged in Switzerland last week. Sports Minister Tracey Crouch commented, "The quicker Blatter leaves, the sooner we can initiate large-scale reforms at FIFA." Major FIFA sponsors, including Coca-Cola, Visa, Budweiser, and McDonald's, called for him to resign, but Blatter stated he wouldn't step down until 2016. Coca-Cola stated, "Each passing day that FIFA's image and reputation continue to tarnish," while McDonald's claimed it would be in the best interest of the game for Blatter to step down. Blatter is accused by Swiss prosecutors of signing an unfavorable contract for FIFA and making a disloyal payment to UEFA president Michel Platini, but denies any wrongdoing. In a statement released through his lawyers, Blatter said resigning now "would not be in the best interest of FIFA, nor would it advance the process of reform." FIFA Association chairman Greg Dyke described the developments as a "game-changer." Blatter was re-elected for a fifth term as FIFA president in May but the election was overshadowed when seven FIFA officials were arrested in dawn raids at a Zurich hotel. The US later indicted 14 current and former FIFA officials and associates on charges of "rampant, systemic, and deep-rooted" corruption following a major inquiry by the Federal Bureau of Investigation. Blatter then announced he would step down as president in February 2016. He stated the 2011 £1.5m payment made to Platini was "valid compensation and nothing more." Platini, 60, has said he received the payment for work as Blatter's technical advisor between 1999 and 2002 and has written a letter to UEFA members denying any wrongdoing. The contract described by Swiss prosecutors as "unfavorable to FIFA" refers to a 2005 TV rights deal between FIFA and Jack Warner, the former president of Concacaf. An investigation by Swiss broadcaster SRF found the deal allegedly resulted in a multi-million pound profit for Warner's company. A separate Swiss investigation is looking into the bidding process for the 2018 and 2022 World Cups, which will be held in Russia and Qatar respectively.
The Royal College of Nursing and British Medical Association have sounded the alarm at their respective conferences, warning that the pressures on the National Health Service are putting patients at risk. Delegates at both events stressed that the current situation is unsustainable, citing instances of frail elderly patients being moved at night and treated in corridors and storerooms. Doctors attending the conference in Belfast described the NHS as "bursting at the seams" due to a severe shortage of beds. The situation is particularly concerning, as OECD figures show that countries such as Belgium, France, Austria, Germany, and Romania have significantly more beds per head of population than the UK. In England, the number of hospital beds has decreased by over 20% over the past decade, leaving hospitals dangerously full. Dr Mark Porter, leader of the British Medical Association, stated that the current state of the NHS poses an increased risk of infections and makes it less likely that patients will receive care on the correct ward. Some doctors, including ear, nose, and throat surgeon Michael Hardingham, have gone as far as to say that patients are being harmed because they are being sent home due to the lack of available beds. The motion passed by the doctors was clear: the reduction in bed stock has gone too far. Nurses at the Royal College of Nursing conference in Glasgow highlighted the extreme measures being taken by some hospitals, including moving patients to emergency departments and treating them in less-than-ideal conditions. Janet Davies, general secretary of the RCN, described the situation as an "endless winter" creating chaos, while emergency care nurse Janet Youd from West Yorkshire warned that patients may not receive safe care at any hospital they need to visit. Despite these warnings, the Department of Health maintained that the NHS is "performing well" despite the added pressures, with a focus on making the system "safest and most transparent" in the world.
Lionsgate is remaking the 1987 film starring Jennifer Grey alongside Patrick Swayze. Kenny Ortega, who choreographed the original, will helm the remake, having previously directed Michael Jackson's This Is It and High School Musical. "Directing Dirty Dancing feels like coming home for me," Ortega told Variety. "I'm looking forward to assembling a talented team and a talented cast to bring this classic to the big screen for a new audience." Ortega praised Swayze, saying he set the bar for men dancing on screen, much like Gene Kelly and Fred Astaire before him. The original romantic film, set in the 1960s, tells the story of a teenage girl who falls for her dance instructor while on vacation with her family. The movie's soundtrack was a huge success, featuring hit songs like Time of My Life and Hungry Eyes. The stage show, which ran for five years in the West End and ended in July, is now touring the UK in September. Grey, 51, who played Baby in the original, won US show Dancing with the Stars last year. Swayze, who played Johnny, passed away from pancreatic cancer in September 2009 at the age of 57.
Air traffic at the airport plummeted by 8.1% in May, with just over 300,000 passengers passing through its gates. The drop was also seen in helicopter traffic, which fell by 13.1%, while fixed-wing traffic declined by 7.2%. Airport managing director Carol Benzie stated that passenger numbers had dropped across all three sectors: helicopters, international, and domestic. She attributed the decline to the impact of cost savings being implemented across the oil and gas industry. In contrast, Edinburgh and Glasgow airports reported strong numbers. Glasgow Airport saw a 13.3% increase in passengers, reaching its busiest May in seven years with 785,063 travelers. International traffic grew by 17%, while domestic traffic rose by 9.1%. Glasgow Airport's managing director, Amanda McMillan, praised May as a successful month, citing the launch of 10 new services, including several firsts for Glasgow, Scotland, and the UK. The airport also noted its recognition as one of Europe's fastest-growing airports this year by ACI Europe. Following its busiest April on record, the airport has seen consistent double-digit increases in passenger numbers since November. Edinburgh Airport set a new record, with over one million passengers in May, driven largely by new routes introduced in the past year.
Mr McLaughlin called for change at the Speaker's annual St Patrick's event in parliament buildings. He expressed concern that the building contained art and symbolism that didn't reflect all cultural identities. The Sinn Féin MLA stated he would be the "first to oppose its removal," but suggested more symbols should be added. Mr McLaughlin acknowledged the scale of the challenge and emphasized the need to address the issue "in a consensual way without provoking controversy." He emphasized the importance of achieving "a balance in this society, demonstrating respect to all without being deliberately provocative." The speaker welcomed the fact that Stormont would be "bathed in green" on Tuesday to celebrate St Patrick's Day, following a decision by the Assembly Commission to illuminate the building with green lights for four annual events. This move also includes turning Stormont orange to commemorate the Battle of the Boyne and marking Remembrance Day in November. The Sinn Féin MLA expressed delight that the building would have an orange glow on the 12th of July. He argued that respect for one culture can't be expected if others are not respected, drawing on his recent visit to Flanders to commemorate First World War casualties and his role as President of the Assembly Branch of the Commonwealth Parliamentary Association. In his St Patrick's Day address, Mr McLaughlin stated: "This building contains art and symbolism that doesn't reflect the cultural identities of all our members or communities. However, I will be the first to oppose its removal. The building was shaped by its context and is deeply cherished by many in our community. We cannot ignore our history. It would be disrespectful to try to airbrush that history away. Stormont should better reflect the journey we've made since 1998 and the society this assembly represents, inclusive of all political opinions, faiths, and none, men and women, the whole community. Adding to what we have achieved can be addressed in a consensual way without provoking controversy by focusing on positive outcomes, democratic persuasion rather than confrontation. Achieving that balance, which demonstrates respect to all without being deliberately provocative, should be of no threat to anyone."
Police in Northumbria say a series of pranks involving men dressed as clowns has left youngsters feeling "incredibly distressed". Since Friday, the force has received six reports, prompting concerns from parents. A teenager from Blakelaw has been arrested in connection with one of the incidents, which also involved a bladed article. Fortunately, no-one has been injured, but there have been further reports of clowns chasing people in the streets. Insp Stephen Wykes said: "The perpetrators of these pranks may think they're being funny, but it's having a profound impact on many young people. Parents and teachers are understandably worried and we're working closely with them to address the issue. We believe these incidents may be linked to a trend in America, where similar pranks have led to disturbing incidents, including men in clown costumes luring children into woods or brandishing knives and guns. As a result, we're keen to prevent further incidents from causing distress to others."
PCSO Mike Ober was on foot patrol in Bradford-on-Avon on Saturday when he stumbled upon an open door at a social club. Upon entering, he was met by a group of women who lost their composure, mistaking him for the male stripper they had hired. "The party was just getting underway, and they thought I was an early arrival," he explained. "I had to make a quick exit." The 20-something-year-old PCSO said the social club was usually unused on weekends, so he decided to "pop in and check it out." "The parking lot was empty, and the door was ajar," he noted. "However, as I entered, the atmosphere was electric, with loud cheers and someone shouting, 'Are you the stripper?' After being informed I was too early, I left, passing the actual performer on my way out. 'It's a matter of personal taste,' he said, "but the uniforms aren't the most flattering, so I suppose that's flattering in a way."
British tennis star Andy Murray suffered a setback during his Wimbledon warm-up on Friday, just three days before his title defense. According to BBC Sport's David Ornstein, Murray grimaced several times during practice, but overall appeared comfortable. His coach, Ivan Lendl, described the session as going well. Murray, a two-time Wimbledon champion, will face Kazakhstan's Alexander Bublik on Centre Court on Monday. This will be his second competitive match on grass this summer, after losing in the first round of the Aegon Championships. With his practice session cut short, Murray hadn't been able to train since Tuesday. Ornstein noted that the decision to practice three times in a day so close to the tournament is unusual. Lendl reassured that Murray was "doing great" after the practice, and the pair then moved on to hitting with Belgian Steve Darcis. Murray seemed comfortable during the session, but struggled with his movements and grimaced frequently. He also participated in volleying and serving drills, showing signs of reaching full intensity. Despite his discomfort, Murray was seen joking and smiling with Lendl, who also had a conversation with Wimbledon chief executive Richard Lewis. After the session, Murray hit with 17-year-old Scottish junior Aidan McHugh, looking relaxed and comfortable with his team.
The European Commission unveiled a plan to boost spending on essential assets like drones and helicopters, as well as fund innovative technologies. The initiative aims to reverse a decade-long trend of defence spending cuts by EU members, with a real-term impact of nearly 12%. This comes as US President-elect Donald Trump threatens to scale back the US security commitment to NATO. Trump has stated that he would not defend NATO allies who fail to contribute their fair share to the alliance. In response, Europe is focusing on its own defences due to concerns about Russian aggression and Trump's close ties to Moscow. Brexit has also played a role in the plan's creation, as the UK has traditionally blocked Franco-German initiatives aimed at strengthening the EU's security. European Commission President Jean-Claude Juncker emphasized the need for increased investment and cooperation among member states to ensure collective security. "We must invest in the common development of technologies and equipment that are crucial to our strategic interests - from land, air, sea, and space capabilities to cyber security," he said. "If we don't take care of our own security, no one else will." The plan includes a significant increase in funding for research, with the "research window" expanded from €25m to €90m in 2020. This will cover areas such as robotics and encrypted software. At the same time, the "capability window" will aim to mobilise €5bn in annual funding from member states to purchase equipment like drones and helicopters jointly. The Commission will act as a treasury to manage these tenders. Despite defence spending in EU countries decreasing, military cooperation between them has not improved. The Commission estimates that this failure has cost the bloc between €25bn and €100bn a year. The EU has its own rapid reaction forces, but there is little evidence of unity among the 28 nations necessary to create a European defence union. The European Council, comprising heads of state and government, is set to discuss the defence plan later this month.
The AA's statement offered no explanation for Mr Mackenzie's departure, but a company spokesperson described it as a "personal conduct matter". However, Mr Mackenzie's son revealed that his father had submitted his resignation earlier that morning due to acute health issues. The son described the situation as a "very distressing mental health issue" that had been affecting his father for some time. A consultant clinical psychologist had advised Mr Mackenzie to take at least six months of leave, and he had since been admitted to hospital. Following the announcement, shares in the AA plummeted 14% and the company revealed that trading had been impacted by "erratic workload patterns". Mr Mackenzie, who had been the executive chairman since 2014, would be replaced by non-executive chairman John Leach, with Simon Breakwell taking on the role of acting chief executive. The AA had previously been owned by private equity firms Permira, Charterhouse, and CVC. The company's share price took a hit after the announcement, with some analysts speculating that Mr Mackenzie's departure had created uncertainty and potentially presented an opportunity for short sellers to put pressure on the shares. The AA stated that it would release its half-year results at the end of September, which would be affected by the workload issue and changes to the way it accounts for certain products. The company also acknowledged that it had made changes to how it records the commission paid on the sale of breakdown cover. In light of these developments, the AA reassured investors that it remained confident in the resilience and long-term prospects of the company.
Maria Sharapova, a five-time Grand Slam champion, revealed Monday that she tested positive for the banned substance meldonium in January. The Russian athlete wrote on social media that she had been overwhelmed by the outpouring of support, saying, "I woke up yesterday morning with an inbox full of love and compassion. Your wonderful words put a smile on my face." Sharapova, who faces up to a four-year ban, expressed her determination to return to tennis, stating, "I wish I didn't have to go through this, but I do - and I will." Her friends had created a collage of messages and hashtags, which she had been reading with her dog by her side. Meldonium, developed to treat diabetes and heart conditions, has been linked to improved stamina and endurance by increasing oxygen flow to muscles. It was added to the World Anti-Doping Agency's list of banned substances on January 1, 2016. Sharapova, who has been taking the substance for health reasons for 10 years, has seen some sponsorship deals end or be postponed. The International Tennis Federation announced that she will be provisionally suspended from March 12.
A pigeon was spotted at a car wash in Lower Hollin Bank Street, Blackburn on Wednesday morning. It's unclear whether the bird had accidentally fallen into paint or was deliberately covered, according to an RSPCA spokesperson. Insp Nina Small, who has been working with the RSPCA for 15 years, said she had never seen anything like it. "Only his eyes were clear," she observed. "He was in a car wash, which is a strange place for a pigeon to be - perhaps he was trying to clean himself?" Despite a wash, the pigeon's feathers remained pink-stained. The amount of paint coming off its body was astonishing. If someone had intentionally painted the pigeon, Insp Small would be concerned for other birds and animals in the area. She hopes to be able to remove all the paint and release the bird back into the wild. However, if the feathers have been permanently damaged, the pigeon's ability to fly may be affected. Additionally, it's possible that while trying to clean itself, the pigeon may have ingested toxic paint.

Labour's general secretary Iain McNicol described Cox as a champion of international feminism and a fitting tribute to the mother of two. The programme is a joint initiative between the Labour Party and the Labour Women's Network, aiming to train and mentor women to take on various roles within the party, including MPs, councillors, and mayors. Labour MP Rachel Reeves said that Cox was passionate about women's leadership and politics, and was a trailblazer as the first chair of the Labour women's network. Reeves praised Cox's ability to support and encourage other women to pursue careers in politics, likening her to having one arm wrapped around your shoulder and the other pushing you forward. Leadership rival Owen Smith also accused Corbyn of not doing enough to tackle "intolerance and misogyny". About 65% of five-year-old children from disadvantaged backgrounds struggled with vocabulary tests, compared to 38% of their more affluent peers, according to the 'Ready to Read' Northern Ireland report. The report, released by the Read On Get On coalition, surveyed 1,955 children from 63 council wards across Northern Ireland. The coalition, which includes Save the Children, Libraries NI, Parenting NI, and Early Years, is calling for increased investment in early childhood education and additional support for parents. Save the Children Northern Ireland's head, Fergus Cooper, believes that engaging parents in their child's education is key to developing literacy skills. "Many parents want their children to succeed, but often they're unsure of the best ways to reinforce that learning," he said. "Early language development is crucial for reading, so we need to start investing and engaging much earlier." Black Mountain Primary School in west Belfast, which has almost 150 pupils, has been working with Save the Children for several years. Parents can visit the school during the day to read with their children, and Stacey Brittan's son AJ has seen significant improvement. "I never read - I'd rather watch a film," Brittan said. "Now, AJ can't wait to buy new books and read them, and it's quality time we enjoy together." Elaine Crossman also attends the school to read with her son Riley. "It's brilliant because I don't always get to read with him at home, but it's great when I can spend that time with him," she said. Black Mountain's principal, Jill Black, credits parents' involvement for the improvement in reading skills. "We started encouraging parents to come into school about three years ago, and the enthusiasm for reading has really taken off, especially in Primary One and Two," she said. The initiative has also strengthened relationships between teachers and parents, leading to improved reading test results. "When parents enjoy reading, it has a positive impact on their children," Black added. "We've seen a huge increase in their achievement and attainment in reading." The 'Ready to Read' report features new analysis by University College London of a Northern Ireland sample from the Millennium Cohort Study, a project following the lives of thousands of UK children. The UK-wide Read On Get On organisation aims to have every child reading well by the age of 11 by 2025.
The High Court has rejected an argument by Govia Thameslink Railway (GTR) that a strike by the RMT union would breach customers' rights. This decision clears the way for the first of three planned strike days to take place on Tuesday. Southern has warned that the strike will result in no services on most routes. The RMT union, which has been staging strikes since the summer over the role of conductors, plans further stoppages around Christmas. Aslef, which described the court ruling as a victory, has criticized GTR's action, calling it a waste of money for shareholders, passengers, and taxpayers. Mick Whelan, General Secretary of Aslef, says the company should negotiate with them to do a deal that benefits passengers, staff, and the company itself. A GTR spokesman expressed disappointment at the decision, stating that the judge found the strike would cause massive disruption to the public, but ultimately ruled that the strikes were sanctioned by a lawful ballot and did not breach freedom of movement provisions. The judge also rejected GTR's attempt to circumvent parliamentary immunity given to unions. Nick Herbert, MP for Arundel and South Downs, suggests that the government should consider alternative remedies to counter "unjustifiable" strike action.
Katrice Lee vanished from a shop at a German military base in 1992, where her father was stationed in Teesside. For years, MPs have debated the handling of the investigation into her disappearance. Labour MP Iain Wright has said that the family waited "far too long" and called for action to be taken. Conservative MP Caroline Dinenage has criticized former Prime Minister David Cameron for refusing to meet with the family. Katrice's mother Sharon Lee and sister Natasha, along with her father Richard Lee, firmly believe that Katrice is still alive and was abducted. Defence Minister Mark Francois has promised to meet with them before Christmas, saying: "We don't have a time machine and can't go back to that day in 1992 when Katrice vanished." However, he added that the Royal Military Police are working diligently to apply modern investigative techniques and seek advice from leading international colleagues. This effort aims to uncover the truth about what happened to Katrice. Katrice disappeared on her second birthday while her family was out shopping for party food. On that day, her mother left her at the checkout with her aunt for just a moment to get crisps, but when she returned, Katrice had vanished.
Greece's finance minister, Yanis Varoufakis, told the New York Times that he couldn't release a recording of a meeting with fellow finance ministers due to confidentiality rules. The controversy surrounding his negotiating style at debt talks has been ongoing. The Greek government claims it won't be able to repay €1.5 billion to the International Monetary Fund (IMF) on June 5 without a deal in the coming days. Varoufakis denied that his colleagues had called him names during a meeting in Riga in April. "I categorically deny the reports that I was abused, insulted, or called a time-waster," he said. According to the NYT, Varoufakis had secretly recorded the meeting but was unable to release the tape due to confidentiality rules. In a later statement, he refused to refute the report, simply stating that he values the confidentiality of his conversations with partners, peers, and institutions. The Greek finance minister was replaced as chief negotiator at the debt talks with EU creditors following the meeting amid reports of a disagreement. Varoufakis denied being sidelined. The debt mountain in Greece stands at €320 billion, with a €240 billion European bailout and a €56 billion debt owed to Germany. The country's debt-to-GDP ratio is 177%, with a 25% decline in GDP since 2010 and a 26% unemployment rate. Greece has been locked in negotiations with the EU and IMF over economic reforms that must be implemented before the final €7.2 billion tranche of the country's €240 billion bailout is released. Issues remain over pension reform, taxation, deregulation of the labor market, and the re-hiring of 4,000 former civil servants. Greek Prime Minister Alexis Tsipras is attending the EU Eastern Partnership Summit in Riga, where he aims to discuss a debt deal with other EU leaders. The government has said it will prioritize the payment of salaries, pensions, and state running costs over the IMF repayment on June 5. "This is the moment of truth," Nikos Filis, spokesman for the ruling Syriza party's lawmakers, told Greek television on Wednesday. "If there is no deal by June 5... they won't receive any money," he said. Meanwhile, German Finance Minister Wolfgang Schaeuble said he couldn't rule out a Greek debt default, according to media reports.
A military hearing will decide whether to proceed with court-martial proceedings against Sergeant Bergdahl. If convicted, he could face a life sentence for desertion or potentially up to five years for misbehavior. The US Army announced the charges at its Forces Command in North Carolina, stating that the investigation into Bergdahl's disappearance had been reviewed before the charges were filed. Colonel Daniel King explained that Bergdahl has been charged with desertion with intent to evade duty and misbehavior before the enemy, which could result in a maximum sentence of life imprisonment. A preliminary hearing will determine whether Bergdahl should face a court-martial, a process similar to a civilian grand jury proceeding. The timing of the hearing has not been announced. Fellow soldiers claim that Bergdahl knowingly left his unit in Afghanistan in June 2009, where he was captured by the Taliban and held for five years before being rescued by US special forces. In exchange for his release, five senior Taliban commanders held at Guantanamo Bay were transferred to Qatar. The deal sparked controversy, with Republicans and some Democrats arguing that it put American lives at risk. Top Republicans claimed the deal was a law violation and negotiating with terrorists, but the Obama administration denied these allegations. They also criticized the lack of Congressional notification. Bergdahl's family has faced threats, and a welcome party in his hometown in Idaho was canceled due to safety concerns.

Scotland is bracing for severe weather, with schools shut, homes without power, and transport severely disrupted. The storm has already had a significant impact, with amber warnings now upgraded to yellow for wind and snow affecting much of the UK. The Scottish Environment Protection Agency has issued over 40 flood warnings. The storm raged across Scotland in the morning, with Shetland particularly hard hit by high winds in the afternoon. Loganair has cancelled 11 flights in and out of Shetland on Friday due to the weather, advising passengers to check their website for rebooking information. In Edinburgh, a man in his 30s suffered head injuries after being knocked down by flying debris, and is receiving treatment at the Edinburgh Royal Infirmary. Trains between Edinburgh and London have been delayed, with some services cancelled, following damage to overhead power lines along the route to Berwick. ScotRail has announced significant cancellations and reduced services for Friday. Network Rail reported flooding at Dalguise viaduct near Pitlochry, which has shut the line between Perth and Inverness. The weather has caused major travel disruption, with bridges closed across Scotland. The Forth Road Bridge, Tay, Kessock, Skye, and Erskine Bridges have now reopened to cars, but remain closed to high-sided vehicles. An HGV overturned on the M9 near Stirling, and emergency services responded to lorries blown over on the A96 near Huntly, the M9 near Dunblane, and the A90 between Glamis and Forfar. In Edinburgh, a tree fell onto a bus on Corstorphine Road, but no one was injured. In Aberdeen, drivers had to avoid a trampoline blown onto the Newtonhill flyover near the A90. A landslide closed the A82 between Spean Bridge and Invergarry, leading to a 154-mile diversion. Strong winds and lightning strikes left thousands without electricity, with Scottish Hydro crews restoring power to 11,000 customers but 2,800 homes and businesses remaining disconnected late on Friday. West coast ferry operator Caledonian MacBrayne reported all 26 routes affected by cancellations or delays. All schools on the Western Isles, Orkney, and Shetland are closed, along with a number of schools in the Highlands.

A spokesperson for the government claimed that Mr. Brokenshire had made no decisions on the issue, stating it was a devolved matter and that compensation was just one reason he wanted to revive the Northern Ireland Executive. He urged the parties to seek an urgent resolution to restore the executive, so they could respond to the report's findings as soon as possible. Critics, including Margaret McGuckian of Survivors and Victims of Institutional Abuse, called Mr. Brokenshire's stance "cowardly" and accused him of ignoring victims. Mike Nesbitt, an Ulster Unionist MLA and former Victims Commissioner, expressed confusion over Brokenshire's approach. When faced with budget issues due to the Assembly's lack of functioning, Brokenshire introduced legislation at Westminster. Similarly, when there was no agreement among the DUP and Sinn Féin to restore the executive, he again turned to Westminster to find a solution. Now, he is refusing to provide even an interim payment to victims, despite their pleas. Nesbitt argued that the request for an interim payment was modest, barely noticeable, and that it was the least victims could expect given their suffering. He stated that leaders from the five main Northern Ireland parties agreed that something needed to be done to address the needs of victims. The HIA Inquiry's report recommended compensation, a memorial, and a public apology to survivors. Sir Anthony Hart, the judge who led the inquiry, suggested a tax-free lump sum payment to all survivors, including those in institutions not covered by the inquiry.

AGO Outsourcing has announced a significant £40m five-year investment in the Scottish jobs market, which will see the creation of 90 new roles in its sales division starting from the end of this month. An investigation into Princess Cruise Lines began after a tip revealed that the company had dumped oily waste off the coast of England in 2013. The department alleged that the firm had attempted to conceal the dumping, and it has since agreed to plead guilty to seven charges related to the Caribbean Princess cruise ship's illegal discharges at nine US ports and two territories. The charges stem from the ship's history of making unauthorized discharges since 2005, just a year after its operations started. The department claims that the ship used a "magic pipe" to bypass pollution-prevention equipment, allowing it to evade monitoring of oil levels and separate oil from the water. The probe was sparked by an engineer who reported the dumping in August 2013 and subsequently quit his job upon the ship's arrival at Southampton. The ports linked to the charges include Florida, Maine, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York, Rhode Island, South Carolina, Texas, and Virginia, as well as the U.S. Virgin Islands and Puerto Rico. According to Jeremy Smart, head of enforcement at the Maritime and Coastguard Agency, the case sends a clear message to the industry that such pollution practices will not be tolerated worldwide. Princess Cruise Lines, a subsidiary of Carnival Corporation, is the world's largest cruise company. The US Justice Department noted that this was the largest-ever criminal penalty imposed for deliberate vessel pollution.
The world's deepest-diving vehicle, which has recently been tested in the Bahamas, can now reach depths of 1km (0.6 miles). However, its design serves as a blueprint for a future vessel capable of making the 11km (7-mile) journey to the bottom of the Mariana Trench. At its core is a 15cm (6in) thick glass sphere being manufactured by Rayotek Scientific in San Diego. This sphere will be crucial in keeping the three-man crew safe from the intense pressure of the deep ocean, while also providing a 360-degree view during their descent. Triton's goal is to make the dive commercially viable, with plans to sell tickets to thrill-seekers willing to take the plunge. According to Bruce Jones, the company's CEO, "Thousands of people climb Everest, and Richard Branson is taking deposits for trips to near-Earth orbit. "We believe that some adventurous types could be willing to pay $250,000 for the chance to be one of the select few to visit the ocean's deepest spot." Triton aims to have its craft ready within the next two years.

A report commissioned by the county council found that the 2013 event generated almost £4m more revenue than the previous year. Next week, cabinet members will vote on a £600,000 commissioning fee to bring the festival back in 2017. The remaining estimated £1.8m cost will be covered by an Arts Council England grant, sponsorship, and donations from trusts and local businesses. Council leader Simon Henig noted that the 2015 event was commissioned based on its "sound economic impact" which showed a clear return on investment for the county, local businesses, and residents. Some of the increase was attributed to increased scrutiny of visitor spending. Adam Deathe, business engagement manager at Durham Business District, stated that hotels, restaurants, and shops all benefited from the influx of visitors from across the North East and beyond. "Lumiere brings national attention to Durham and hotels are often booked up months in advance as people come for at least a couple of days," he said. "They often use the city as a base and explore the surrounding area as well." The festival was first held in the city in 2009 and has returned every other year since then. In January 2016, the event was replicated in London with the same organiser, Artichoke.

A groundbreaking review, led by Queen's University and the NSPCC, has confirmed that Northern Ireland's child protection system is effective. According to Dr John Devaney, who oversaw the research, the findings show that the system is robust. The report, Translating Learning into Action, will be released later this week. Researchers analyzed 24 reports of children who died or were severely injured. Dr Devaney noted that in most cases, the deaths were unforeseen. "However, there are key areas where we can improve, such as providing support to families earlier and ensuring professionals stay involved with families for longer to sustain improvements," he said. The report also revealed that the number of children on protection registers has increased over the past five years, but the authors attribute this to the improved protection system in place. The report drew several conclusions: firstly, the rate of non-accidental child deaths in Northern Ireland continues to decline due to the strong child protection system; secondly, the majority of case reviews praised the dedication and professionalism of individual staff working with families; thirdly, the children in these reports were among hundreds living in similar circumstances, known to professionals, and the reviews concluded it was unlikely that these children could have been identified as being at heightened risk; fourthly, services need to become involved with families earlier to prevent problems from becoming entrenched and harder to improve; fifthly, services should stay involved with some families for longer to consolidate improvements in parenting; sixthly, therapeutic services should be provided to address the psychological harm caused by poor parenting; seventhly, professionals should have more opportunities to meet and coordinate assessments and interventions with children and families; finally, senior managers must take greater responsibility for managing individual professionals' workloads and ensuring they are commensurate with their experience.
Irish Water has confirmed that it will take until Thursday to restore water supply to 50,000 homes and businesses affected by a burst on Friday morning. The storage reservoir levels have dropped to a critically low point, according to Irish Water, and repair work on the water main has proven more challenging than initially anticipated. The main, which supplies the Staleen Water Treatment Plant, is located over four metres underground and is 50 years old, with a warped pipe that has added to the complexity of the repair. Additionally, it's one of the highest-pressure pipes, making the job even more difficult. A bespoke solution is currently being engineered. The plant serves drinking water to Drogheda and surrounding areas, as well as parts of south Louth and east Meath. In order to manage the remaining water resource, Irish Water has reintroduced water rationing in certain parts of Drogheda, starting at 08:00 local time on Monday. To supplement the water supply, Louth County Council and Irish Water have brought in large tankers from both Ireland and Northern Ireland to deliver treated water into the water network. This water is being distributed directly into the network to increase the available water supply for the rotating water roster, which involves rationing water to different areas in a planned manner. Locations that will receive water will be posted on the Louth County Council and Irish Water websites, and will be updated regularly. Temporary water stations have been set up in various locations across east Meath and Louth, but consumers are advised to boil water from these stations before use as a precaution. Northern Ireland Water has offered to support Irish Water in liaising with colleagues and providing assistance until the incident is resolved.

Archaeologists unearthed human remains at St Mary's Primary School in Leith after a skeleton was discovered during construction work for a new classroom. The excavation uncovered the outlines of at least nine coffins in the school grounds, which experts say is evidence of Leith's rich archaeological past. Richard Lewis, the City of Edinburgh Council's culture convener, stated that the new findings from the site are proving to be even more significant than initially thought. "The school community has been fascinated by the excavation and its discoveries, with the children eager to learn about the new findings," he said. "As the excavation continues, we'll gain more insights into the former inhabitants of Leith." John Lawson, a City of Edinburgh Council archaeologist, added that the Leith area has a rich archaeological history. "These excavations have uncovered what appears to be a complex cemetery dating from the 15th to the 17th centuries, containing at least nine graves, including adults and young children buried in coffins," he explained. "The discovery of these burials in this location is a surprise, although further excavations will provide more evidence to accurately date the burials and their connection to Leith's history." The area containing the remains has been fenced off, but the school remains operational. The construction of the new classrooms has been delayed due to ongoing archaeological work, which is expected to last until the end of June.

Chris Martin, the 19-year-old son of Michael Martin, was a fit and healthy university student who played rugby for University College Cork. Tragically, he died two days after Christmas in 2015 after waking up gasping for breath. His father, Michael, urged those with mild asthma to manage their condition properly, saying he never thought asthma would be fatal, even for someone as active as his son. However, the Asthma Society of Ireland notes that mild asthma can be deadly, with an average of one death per week in the Republic of Ireland. To correct a common misconception, the charity has launched an awareness campaign, which includes TV and social media ads, highlighting that asthma can kill anyone, regardless of severity. Michael Martin, who has been open about his son's asthma, has participated in the campaign. Chris was diagnosed with asthma at the age of four but had never been hospitalized. He was an active teenager who enjoyed sports like surfing and mountaineering, and always carried his reliever inhaler, but often forgot to take his preventative medication. His father explained that Chris would forget to take his medication between Monday and Thursday, but would remember to take it again when he started feeling unwell. Michael Martin described how his son's asthma went untreated, and he died suddenly at the age of 19, unable to breathe. The Asthma Society of Ireland's chief executive, Averil Power, said Michael's story is not unique, as they have contacted families of other young people who died of asthma in the past 18 months, all of whom were fit and active. Power emphasized that there is a misconception that only severe asthma is deadly, but a 2014 study found that most asthma deaths are caused by mild or moderate asthma, and underuse of preventer inhalers is a major factor. She advised patients to take their medication as prescribed and to inform their doctor of any issues, adding that with proper care, most asthma deaths can be prevented.

In a speech to the David Hume Institute, Tricia Marwick urged a fundamental examination of the committees' operations and suggested electing conveners by their peers. The Scottish Parliament currently has 17 committees, with 132 seats shared among 80 MSPs. Marwick aims to reduce the number to 10 or 11, stating, "I've come to realize that changing our culture isn't enough – we need to consider structural changes." She questioned whether the current committee structure is sufficient, noting the lack of proposed legislation since 2002, apart from procedural matters. Marwick also expressed frustration over the absence of post-legislative scrutiny. "Larger committees could offer a better policy fit, bringing together separate subjects that might work better under one umbrella," she said. "These committees should be focused on what's most important to the parliament in terms of policy priorities." Marwick emphasized the need for the Scottish Parliament to clearly define its policy priorities and shape its committees according to its own needs. "Larger committees can break into smaller subgroups, meeting in a more flexible manner, without all members needing to work on all committee business simultaneously," she said. "We need to think outside the box and be more creative in our approach." Marwick also supports the introduction of elected committee conveners as part of a cultural shift within Holyrood. While parties would maintain a proportional share of conveners, these elected representatives would be directly accountable to parliament through a secret ballot of all MSPs. Marwick advocated for more powerful conveners with a stronger voice, not influenced by government legislation. The Scottish Parliament's committees have faced criticism for lacking teeth in scrutinizing the Scottish government, with opposition MSPs accusing the SNP administration of suppressing dissent.

They advocate for fewer concessions on international nuclear inspections and research and development activities. As the deadline for a final accord approaches, US Secretary of State John Kerry and his Iranian counterpart Mohammad Javad Zarif will meet in Vienna to join the negotiations, which could extend beyond June 30. The P5+1 group, comprising the US, UK, France, China, Russia, and Germany, aims to limit Iran's sensitive nuclear activities to prevent the rapid development of a nuclear weapon. Iran, seeking the lifting of sanctions, insists its nuclear work is peaceful. A bipartisan group of US diplomats, legislators, policymakers, and experts, who have met regularly over the past three years to discuss Iran, published the letter. The letter states, "Most of us would have preferred a stronger agreement." The agreement will not prevent Iran from developing a nuclear weapons capability, nor will it require the dismantling of its nuclear infrastructure, but it will reduce the infrastructure for 10 to 15 years and impose a transparency and inspection regime to deter Iran from building a nuclear weapon. The letter urges President Obama to demand information about Iran's nuclear weapons research, grant the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) access to military sites, and establish strict limits on centrifuge research and development to prevent rapid technical upgrades.
Former RUC Special Branch officer William Matchett, who has also advised on policing in Iraq and Afghanistan, has written a book about Special Branch during the Northern Ireland Troubles. In a recent statement, Matchett highlighted the significant differences in the type of terrorism the UK now faces compared to the Troubles. "When analyzing this new threat, which manifests itself in England in a particularly alarming way, especially with vehicles and knives being used to kill people - it's extremely quick and dynamic, happening within seconds," he said. "It's a vastly different scenario from what I experienced in Northern Ireland, where the terrorist would attack someone, then quickly escape. "In England, in the past three attacks, that's not been the case - the terrorists have lingered, attempting to kill as many people as possible. "Given this, it's crucial to act swiftly and, in many ways, it's becoming increasingly challenging to counter the threat posed by police officers carrying sidearms, even those on duty and off-duty." Last Saturday night's terror attack in London resulted in seven deaths and 48 injuries. Three attackers were shot by police from an armed response unit after driving into pedestrians on London Bridge and stabbing people in Borough Market. An off-duty policeman, an amateur rugby player, was stabbed after tackling one of the attackers and remains critically ill. A British Transport Police officer, armed only with his baton, was seriously injured but is now in a stable condition. In contrast to Northern Ireland, where all police officers are armed on duty and have personal protection weapons off-duty, the Metropolitan Police Commissioner, Cressida Dick, expressed reservations about arming every police officer in the UK. "Personally, I do not support the routine arming of every single police officer on the streets," she said. "The terrorists aim to alter the fabric of our society. "I believe the public and my officers are uneasy with the idea of every officer being armed, and I don't think it necessarily provides better protection for the public or officers. "We've seen this in France, for example, where a lone officer was armed and vulnerable. "I think it's better to have officers who can handle this complex and terrifying threat."
IAEA Director-General Yukiya Amano announced that Iran had provided environmental samples from the Parchin military site, which he had visited on Sunday. However, Amano cautioned that much work remained to be done before the investigation could be completed by the end of the year. Western powers suspect that nuclear weapons research took place at Parchin, but Iran has consistently denied this and maintains that its nuclear program is entirely peaceful. The completion of the IAEA's investigation is crucial to implementing the comprehensive agreement reached between six world powers and Iran in July, which limits the country's sensitive nuclear activities in exchange for the lifting of sanctions. Located south of Tehran, Parchin is a complex dedicated to researching, developing, and producing ammunition, rockets, and explosives. Concerns about its possible involvement in Iran's nuclear program emerged in 2004, when reports surfaced about the construction of a large explosives containment vessel for hydrodynamic experiments. The IAEA considers these developments to be strong indicators of possible nuclear weapon development. Although inspectors did not observe any unusual activities during a 2005 visit and environmental samples did not indicate the presence of nuclear material, suspicions persisted. The Iranian government had repeatedly rejected the IAEA's requests to revisit Parchin, resulting in extensive landscaping, demolition, and new construction. Amano was given a tour of Parchin on Sunday, accompanied by the head of the IAEA's Department of Safeguards, Tero Varjoranta. At a news conference in Vienna on Monday, Amano revealed that they were able to inspect a building previously only observed using satellite imagery, finding no equipment but indications of recent renovation work. Environmental samples were taken from "places of interest" at Parchin last week, with the Iranian side participating in the sampling process. While Amano did not disclose whether IAEA inspectors were present, he emphasized that the procedure was carried out in a way that did not compromise verification processes. "The agency can confirm the integrity of the sampling process and the authenticity of the samples," he stated. "This was carried out under our responsibility and monitoring." The collection of samples at Parchin is believed to be part of a confidential "road map" agreement signed by the IAEA and Iran in July.
The trial has been divided into four phases, each corresponding to a floor in the prison where 111 people were killed. The latest hearing focuses on those accused of killing eight inmates, but their lawyers claim it's impossible to determine who fired the fatal shots. In the previous two phases, 48 policemen were convicted. The latest part of the trial began on Monday with a new jury and is expected to last about a week. The final phase of the trial is set to start on March 17. Last August, 25 police officers received lengthy sentences for their roles in the massacre, with each receiving 624 years for the deaths of 52 inmates. However, they are limited by law to serving no more than 30 years in prison. In April, another 23 officers were convicted. The defense can only challenge the verdicts after the trial is complete. Fifteen police officers still face a jury in the case. The killings occurred when riot police were called to quell a riot on October 2, 1992. The riot began as a dispute between two of the nearly 10,000 inmates, who were housed in one of South America's largest prisons at the time. The situation quickly escalated into a battle between rival gangs. Carandiru prison was closed in 2002, following simultaneous uprisings in 27 jails across Sao Paulo state, during which thousands of visitors were held hostage. In 2001, Colonel Ubiratan Guimaraes was convicted of using excessive force in the operation to regain control at Carandiru, but was acquitted in an appeal hearing in 2006.
News Group Newspapers, which has previously settled a substantial number of cases against its defunct sister publication, News of the World, has long maintained that its sister tabloid was not involved in hacking. However, in a London court on Thursday, Justice Mann allowed the company to contest these claims. Since 2000, News Group Newspapers, now known as News UK, has settled over 1,000 phone hacking cases. The judge's ruling now permits four claimants to include the daily newspaper in their cases for the first time, with two of these cases set to go to trial in July. Chris Hutchings, a lead solicitor for those claiming their private voicemail messages were hacked, said: "This ruling brings the Sun directly into the civil litigation against News Group." "The court has now enabled claimants to include phone hacking allegations against the newspaper for the first time. This decision will also impact up to 50 additional cases that are about to be brought against the Defendant, resulting in further disclosure of documentation that News UK will be required to provide in the future."
A new poll by YouGov for ITV Cymru Wales and Cardiff University suggests that Theresa May's general election support has risen to 29%, with Labour on 35%, Plaid Cymru on 13%, UKIP on 14%, and the Lib Dems on 7%. This marks a significant shift, with Labour's lead over the Conservatives narrowing. Analyst Prof Roger Scully from Cardiff University's Wales Governance Centre believes that May's "honeymoon period" as a new party leader is behind the increase. When projected across the current Westminster constituency boundaries, the findings indicate that Labour would lose Ynys Mon to Plaid Cymru, resulting in a 24-16 split in Welsh seats. However, a new parliamentary map proposed by the Boundary Commission would reduce the number of Welsh MPs to 29. Under these arrangements, Labour's seat projections suggest they would narrowly win the majority of Welsh seats, with 15 MPs, the Conservatives 10, Plaid Cymru four, and the Liberal Democrats one. The poll's findings for assembly election voting follow a similar pattern, with Labour losing three seats and the Conservatives gaining three, making them the largest opposition party in Cardiff Bay. Prof Scully's projections also indicate that the other parties would retain the same number of AMs as in the previous assembly election. The poll's results confirm that the recent Britain-wide polls' trends also apply to Wales. This "honeymoon period" is not unusual for new prime ministers or party leaders, as seen in the case of Jeremy Corbyn's leadership victory last year, which initially boosted his party's polls but soon dissipated. The poll, conducted online by YouGov from September 18-21, 2016, had a sample of 1,001 adults and provides insight into the current political landscape in Wales.
Grandmother Pauline McKee was playing the Miss Kitty game at a casino when she won a significant amount of money, initially 185 credits, or $1.85, but a message on the screen revealed she had also won a massive bonus worth $41,797,550.16. The casino refused to pay out, citing a computer glitch, and Pauline, who has 13 grandchildren, took the casino to court. However, the Iowa Supreme Court ultimately dismissed her case. Pauline expressed her doubts from the start, saying that such a large prize for a penny machine seemed implausible. She hoped to help her children financially, but it wasn't meant to be. The casino argued that the on-screen rules clearly stated that any malfunction would void all pays and plays. At the time of the dispute, the Iowa Racing and Gaming Commission investigated the machine and sent it to an independent testing laboratory for analysis. The investigators found that the software was programmed to allow a bonus of up to $10,000, but couldn't explain how the multi-million bonus message appeared. The machine's maker, Aristocrat Technologies, acknowledged that this type of error in the display was possible due to component degradation over time. The company had warned casinos about this glitch in 2010 and recommended disabling the bonus facility as a precautionary measure. However, the casino had not taken this step. The Iowa Racing and Gaming Commission concluded that the bonus display was not valid and that the slot machine had malfunctioned. Pauline sued the casino in 2012, claiming breach of contract and consumer fraud. The court ultimately sided with the casino in 2013, but the case dragged on when Pauline's lawyers questioned the IRGC's jurisdiction in disputes between casinos and patrons, as well as whether the machine truly malfunctioned and whether Pauline had entered into a contract. There have been similar cases in the US involving erroneous bonus displays. In 2009, a player won a bonus of $1m, but a Mississippi court threw out the claim. In this case, the game rules limited payouts to $8,000. Pauline's case was finally rejected by the Iowa Supreme Court on April 24.
Gordon McLeod's plans were thwarted when the 70-year-old homeowner and his 69-year-old friend overpowered him. At the High Court in Glasgow, McLeod, 32 and a heroin addict, pleaded guilty to assaulting the men. The judge, Lady Scott, commended the bravery of the pensioners. The court was told that McLeod posed as an East Lothian council worker in an attempt to gain access to Ronald Cormack's home in Edinburgh last May. He claimed that he needed to turn off the water inside because of an emergency, wearing a high-visibility vest and flashing a fake identity card. However, when he was refused entry, McLeod forced his way into the house, producing a knife. Mr. Cormack and his friend Jack Cairns then tackled the intruder, one of them pushing him towards the door while the other called the police. Lady Scott stated: "The elderly gentlemen were having a quiet evening when you appeared, pretending there was an emergency and you needed to switch the water off. When this failed, you forced your way into the house, with the intention of robbing the homeowner." She added that when McLeod met resistance from the two elderly men, he gave up and was escorted out of the house. Thankfully, neither of the victims was injured. The court heard that McLeod had a long list of previous convictions, including one for assault and robbery in 2006, for which he received a 42-month jail sentence. Defence counsel Matt Jackson said that McLeod could not express enough remorse for the victims, adding that forcing his way into the house was unacceptable.
Some children are getting better access to educational opportunities, according to a report by the Social Mobility and Child Poverty Commission, which has echoes of concerns raised by Ofsted in its annual report and aligns with government policy on making schools academies the key route to success. However, the evidence suggests the picture is more complex, as schools have converted in different circumstances. As Health Secretary, Alan Milburn introduced Foundation Trust hospitals, which have a similar autonomous public sector status as academies. Nevertheless, there's a note of dissonance over teachers' pay, as the report warns that starting salaries for teachers are falling behind other graduate jobs in the UK, including new teachers in developed economies. The median starting salary for top graduate jobs is £30,000, compared to £27,800 in London and around £5,000 less in other parts of the country for teachers. The commission is urging the government to increase teachers' pay, which comes as the body advising the government on teachers' pay starts to process submissions from schools. The teaching unions are calling for pay rises above the 1% average limit set by George Osborne's spending review, citing struggles with recruiting teachers and anecdotal evidence from head teachers that it's becoming harder to appoint good-quality candidates. The government is unlikely to increase pay, but may face growing discord across the teaching profession. An elite group of 1,500 teachers is to be recruited for underperforming schools, but they may need to be deployed in clusters to be effective. Ministers will try to sell the long-term benefits of the profession, including satisfaction and long-term pay prospects. International research suggests that teachers taking on more responsibility can end up being paid reasonably well in the long term compared to other countries. However, this may not be enough to avoid the pay review body warning of increasing difficulties in recruiting enough teachers to match rising pupil numbers. The government is unlikely to go as far as the unions would like, and may recommend an exception to public pay restraint for teachers.
A study at Leicester University found that stillbirth and neonatal death rates were declining across the UK, but Scotland was leading the way. The rate of stillbirths and deaths within 28 days of birth in Scotland is 4.72 per 1,000 live births, compared to 4.3 in Nordic countries such as Norway, Sweden, Denmark, Finland, and Iceland, which are often considered to have the "gold standard" in terms of neonatal survival rates. The 2015 rate for the UK as a whole was 5.61 per 1,000 live births, a decrease from 6.04 in 2013. In Scotland, the rate has dropped from 5.43 to 4.72, the lowest among all UK nations. Mary Ross-Davie, director of the Royal College of Midwives in Scotland, welcomed the report as "good news" for Scotland. She credited efforts to educate expectant mothers about smoking during pregnancy and encourage them to monitor fetal movements, as well as initiatives to help midwives and obstetricians closely monitor fetal growth and respond promptly to any concerns. Ross-Davie noted that Scotland was ahead of the curve in addressing stillbirths, having established a national stillbirth group in 2013 and the Maternity Care Quality Improvement Collaborative. She emphasized that socioeconomic deprivation is a significant factor in stillbirth and neonatal death rates and that there is still much work to be done in Scotland to reduce these numbers. Ross-Davie pointed to the more equal society in Scandinavia as a model for Scotland, where lower levels of deprivation contribute to lower stillbirth rates. The report's author, Prof Elizabeth Draper, noted that stillbirth rates have decreased by 8% across the UK, with Scotland's rate being lower due to a lower starting point. Draper's goal is to reduce stillbirths significantly and bring all UK countries down to Nordic levels of stillbirth.

Armenia is situated along the historic Silk Road, having been influenced by numerous cultures and empires. Following its independence from the Soviet Union in 1991, the country became embroiled in a bloody conflict with Azerbaijan over the predominantly Armenian-speaking Nagorno-Karabakh region. As one of the earliest Christian civilizations, Armenia's first churches were established in the 4th century. The country has frequently oscillated between various forms of control, including Byzantine, Persian, Mongol, and Turkish rule, as well as periods of independence. With a population of approximately 3.1 million people, Armenia covers an area of 29,743 square kilometers. The country's major languages are Armenian and Russian, with Christianity being the predominant religion. Armenians enjoy a relatively high life expectancy, with men living to 71 years and women to 77 years. The main exports of Armenia include diamonds, machinery, and foodstuffs. Serge Sarkisian has been Armenia's president since 2008, having won a second term in 2013. Before becoming president, Sarkisian served in various roles, including as a Soviet soldier and defence minister. He played a key role in negotiating a historic deal with Turkey in 2009, which aimed to re-establish diplomatic ties. However, this agreement ultimately failed to materialize due to nationalist pressure from both sides. Armenia's media landscape is dominated by television, with 25 private stations operating alongside two public networks. The main Russian networks are widely available, while few Armenians rely on newspapers as their main news source. A media law in Armenia prohibits censorship, but libel and defamation can be punished by prison terms, and journalists have been sentenced under relevant laws. Armenia's history is marked by several key events, including the 1915-1917 massacres of Armenians, the country's independence from the Ottoman Empire in 1918, its incorporation into the Soviet Union in 1922, the beginning of the conflict over Nagorno-Karabakh in 1989, and the failed attempt to normalize diplomatic ties with Turkey in 2009. In 2015, Armenia officially joined the Eurasian Customs Union, choosing instead to pursue a relationship with Russia over the European Union.
A new baby Malayan tapir has been born at the Port Lympne Reserve in Kent, England. The reserve is now home to the 11th Malayan tapir born there since 1989, marking it as one of Europe's top breeders of this critically endangered species. Only around 1,500 to 2,000 of these animals are thought to exist in the wild, primarily in south-east Asia's forests and rainforests. The tapir's decline is largely attributed to hunting, illegal logging, and deforestation. As the largest of the four tapir species, the Malayan tapir is also known as the Asian tapir and is related to both horses and rhinoceroses. Characterized by its four-toed front legs and three-toed back legs, the tapir has distinctive white spots and stripes on its black skin, which darken as it matures to help with camouflage in its native moonlit forests. This remarkable breeding success is a cause for celebration, not only for the tapir's future but also for the dedicated staff who care for them.
In recent weeks, the UK has seen a spate of acid attacks targeting cyclists and moped riders in London. Since then, over 70 Deliveroo riders have reported refusing to make deliveries due to safety concerns. To address these fears, Deliveroo has introduced a new app feature that allows riders to report security concerns, as well as a trial of helmet cameras to gather evidence. In one shocking incident last month, two Deliveroo and UberEats riders were among five people injured in a 90-minute attack by moped riders who threw acid in their faces in north London. What's more, Deliveroo drivers are self-employed and therefore not entitled to sick pay if they are attacked while working. The company has also upgraded its app to allow riders to flag up safety concerns about specific addresses, which are then shared with its headquarters and local authorities. Deliveroo is currently conducting a trial in Hackney, east London, which has become notorious for its problems with riders. To tackle these issues, Deliveroo is hiring 50 new staff focused on rider safety. Jabed Hussein, a delivery rider for UberEats, was the first victim of the acid attacks. Speaking to the BBC's Victoria Derbyshire programme, he described the incident: "It was like getting fire on my face. I was screaming on the street. This shouldn't be a job where your life is at risk." Hussein also revealed that many riders are now ending their shifts at 10 pm due to fear for their safety. "Most of them won't work after this, whereas we used to work until 1 am. Now, I feel like I'm working in some sort of jungle, where anything can happen and I'm not safe," he said. Deliveroo's managing director, Dan Warne, said: "The safety of our riders is our top priority, and it's our responsibility as a company to do everything we can to ensure they feel safe all the time."

The British Film Institute has announced a significant increase in takings, with revenue up 17% from the previous year to £1.24 billion. Cinema admissions also saw a 9% rise, with 171.9 million tickets sold, while spending on film production reached £1.41 billion - the second-highest total on record. Notably, this marks the first year since 2012 that both admissions and box office takings have risen. In 2015, five films earned more than £40 million at the UK box office, a significant improvement from just one, The Hobbit: The Desolation of Smaug, in 2014. The Force Awakens, released in December, took a record-breaking £114 million and is now the UK's highest-grossing film of all time. Spectre, the latest James Bond release, took £94.7 million, followed closely by Jurassic World with £64.5 million. The Avengers: Age of Ultron took £48.3 million, while Minions rounded out the top five with £47.7 million. The BFI also reported that UK independent films took an 11% market share in 2015, the third-highest on record. Eight independent films earned more than £5 million at the box office, with Tom Hardy's Legend taking the top spot with £18.4 million. The Second Best Exotic Marigold Hotel came in second, earning £16 million. In contrast, the UK's film production spend decreased by 6% from 2014's record total of £1.49 billion. Amanda Nevill, chief executive of the BFI, highlighted the positive trend, saying, "With spend from film production over £1.4 billion, audiences flocking to cinemas and UK box office topping £1.2 billion for the first time, today's numbers show a golden age of British film continuing - and underlines film's important contribution to the UK economy." She also noted that, while a wide range of British independent films performed well, the market remains challenging for independent and specialised films. Culture minister Ed Vaizey hailed the figures as "great news".

Kirsty Williams, the party leader, described the situation as "shocking and disappointing". In contrast, the Welsh Government has reported that more people are surviving cancer despite an increase in diagnoses. The government's target is to have 95% of patients suspected of having cancer start definitive treatment within 62 days of diagnosis. This target was first emphasized by Health Minister Mark Drakeford in June 2013, who stated that the key to achieving this level was to ensure patients receive their first hospital appointment within 10 days of referral. Data from the Welsh Government shows that between October 2013 and September 2015, over 22,000 people were referred to the hospital with suspected breast cancer, with 2,249 being diagnosed and 92.3% of these starting treatment within 62 days. However, not all health boards are meeting the target. Betsi Cadwaladr health board reported an average wait of 21 days for an appointment with a cancer specialist at Ysbyty Glan Clwyd and Ysbyty Gwynedd. Powys Teaching health board, on the other hand, refers its patients to hospitals in neighboring health boards. The latest figures show that 83.7% of cancer patients started treatment within 62 days. Kirsty Williams said that the situation is "absolutely desperate" for individuals who spend time worrying about their health and future. However, she also pointed out that it highlights the need for a systematic approach to ensure there is enough capacity within cancer services to see people within a timely fashion. In response, the Welsh Government suggested that one-stop diagnostic clinics for women with suspected breast cancer enable them to start treatment immediately. An Aneurin Bevan health board spokesman stated that their current waiting times have reduced to 19 days, and every patient with a suspected malignancy is being seen and treated within 62 days, meeting the government's target.

A pregnant woman and two survivors, including one from Cameroon and one from the Democratic Republic of Congo, were rescued. The survivors said there had been between 20 and 25 people on board the boat. Hundreds have lost their lives since 2015 attempting to cross from Turkey to Greece. The numbers plummeted dramatically in March last year after Turkey and the EU agreed on a deal to curb the influx of people. However, concerns have grown that the numbers attempting the crossing could rise again if the deal is undermined by deteriorating relations between the two sides. The coastguards found the bodies of six women, a child, and two men in Greek territory, while six men and a child were discovered in Turkish waters. The survivors reported that the boat had capsized on Sunday night. In another incident, an Iranian man died during a high-speed chase involving a van of illegal migrants and Greek police near Greece's north-eastern border with Turkey after the van overturned, reported AFP news agency.
The combined budget for the two events was initially £9.29 billion, but it has since been revised to £8.77 billion. This represents a £151 million increase in savings, largely due to a decrease in policing and security expenses, as well as reduced costs in transport and construction. Sports Minister Hugh Robertson welcomed the revised figures, citing the sound financial management of all parties involved. The updated budget was revealed in a written statement to Parliament by Robertson following the release of the Organising Committee's final report and accounts in May. The original budget, set in 2007, was nearly four times the estimated cost when London first bid to host the 2005 Games. After factoring in previously overlooked expenses such as VAT and increased security costs, the budget was revised upwards. Robertson also highlighted the progress made in delivering a lasting legacy, noting that the transformation of the Olympic Park is well underway. The long-term future of the eight major venues is secure, and the first residents are set to move into the Olympic Village this summer. The Olympic Development Agency is currently transforming apartments in the village into new homes, with the project due to be completed in time for occupation later this summer.
The Ministry of Justice has confirmed that the Grade II listed prison, which closed in 2013, will be made available as a venue for the town's 2016 Year of Culture events. The Year of Culture initiative aims to unite the town's arts groups and make it a cultural destination. Reading UK's Alex Brennan described the prison as a "fabulous" addition to the programme. The MoJ said it will be working with a third party to make the venue suitable for a season of art, literature, and music in the autumn, without any additional cost to taxpayers. Reading East MP Rob Wilson said the prison's use would "help celebrate the town's cultural and historical significance". Brennan added that the prison holds a central place in Reading's history, making it a "marvellous and rather unusual" venue. The prison, which was immortalized in Oscar Wilde's poem The Ballad Of Reading Gaol, is also home to a Scheduled Ancient Monument. Roderick Maclean, Amelia Dyer, and Oscar Wilde were all associated with the prison, as were Arthur Griffith and other notable figures. In November 2015, the MoJ announced plans to sell the site for housing, with archaeological investigations set to begin before a development plan is drawn up. The Reading Borough Council hopes to take over the site and has previously criticized the MoJ for delaying a decision on the prison's long-term future. The abandoned jail, which cost the MoJ approximately £262,000 in the 2014/15 financial year, will be made available without any additional cost to taxpayers.
Sgt Craig Nicholas Jones maintained that he was wrongly convicted by magistrates after two 50mph speed limit signs on the A55 at Old Colwyn were found to be non-functional. However, judges at the High Court in London disagreed, stating that the signs would have been visible in the police car's headlights. As a result, Sgt Jones's 90-day driving ban, initially suspended pending the appeal, has been reinstated. On April 1, 2009, Sgt Jones was clocked driving at 98mph on the road between Llanddulas and Colwyn Bay while on duty. His lawyers argued that the broken lights on the speed limit signs meant he should have been acquitted at magistrates' court, citing a "clear and settled policy" at the time not to enforce the speed limit when the signs were not functioning properly. In response, Mr Justice Charles and Mr Justice Wyn Williams stated that "right-thinking people" would have deemed a decision not to prosecute Sgt Jones as detrimental to the administration of justice. The judges concluded that despite the faulty lights, the signs were "correctly positioned" and "complied fully" with relevant regulations. They ruled that Sgt Jones's appeal had been dismissed, stating that minor breaches of the regulations did not affect the legality or enforceability of the speed limit. Sgt Jones's 90-day driving ban, originally imposed in December 2009, will now take effect on January 31.

Deborah Streatfield of MyBigCareer has expressed her outrage at the fact that students interested in pursuing a career in medicine are being asked to pay for work experience, a requirement for university applications. This experience is being offered through the Chartwell Trust, a private hospital in Essex, as part of its "Chartwell Work Experience For Medical School Applications" program. The trust's directors consider this opportunity to be "remarkable" and claims it provides "in-depth insight" into working in a hospital and the healthcare system, as well as "valuable medical work experience" to support a Ucas application. The program promises "good supervision" from motivated staff and "interview training" by qualified doctors. The trust also operates care homes for children with learning difficulties and the elderly with dementia. Medicine is an extremely competitive course for university entry, and the program's emphasis on "medical school interview techniques" is seen as a valuable asset for students. Mrs. Streatfield's charity aims to provide better career advice to young people, believing that social mobility will improve if they are better informed and aware of their options. However, she believes that charging for work experience creates a barrier to accessing sought-after courses like medicine, particularly for disadvantaged students who lack family connections. "This scheme is completely unfair," she stated. MyBigCareer plans to pilot unpaid work experience placements with 43 GP practices in Hackney and the City of London. In a statement, the directors of Chartwell Trust Care argue that the placement offers one-to-one meetings with senior healthcare professionals, providing an "insight into healthcare" and allowing individuals to develop their "empathy and compassion." The fee charged is to cover administrative costs and coaching, aiming to boost confidence and self-esteem. There have been concerns that the requirement to apply for medicine draws too many applicants from wealthier backgrounds, with research from the Medical Schools Council revealing that half of UK secondary schools and colleges had not produced a single applicant in recent years.
