To help people rethink their parking habits, Cambridge police has released photos of bikes left in random spots as part of its "badlyparkedbike" campaign. The police weren't surprised by the scenes, as cycling is a common mode of transport in the city, with 58% of residents cycling at least once a month. Insp Matt Johnson, of Cambridgeshire police, said, "While the photos are shocking, these kinds of parking incidents aren't uncommon in Cambridge." He warned that such parking can cause problems, including obstructing bike paths, making it hard for others to access their bikes, and even resulting in injuries when trying to retrieve them. Johnson advised using designated bike racks, as they are designed to prevent theft when combined with a good lock. Some people praised the crackdown, while others argued that it highlighted the need for more bike parking spaces. One user, Al Storer, noted that the campaign only showed areas where bike parking was woefully lacking. Insp Johnson pointed out that despite the issue, there is a significant amount of bike parking provision throughout the city, but the abandoned cycles still pose a persistent problem.
The UK's Intellectual Property Office has upheld Swatch's opposition to Apple's trademark application for "iWatch". Swatch had claimed the name was too similar to its own trademarks for "iSwatch" and "Swatch". By the time Apple launched its smartwatch in 2015, the company had decided to call its product "Apple Watch". However, the IPO's decision now allows Apple to use the name "iWatch" for certain products, including software and accessories, but not hardware, monitors, audio and video devices, or cameras. The decision comes after a hearing in April. Trademark lawyer Sharon Daboul noted that the ruling highlights the challenges global brands face when launching new products worldwide. "Trademarks are territorial, meaning they must be available for registration in every country of interest," she said. Apple's inability to secure the "iWatch" trademark globally was a key factor in its decision not to use the name for its smartwatch. Swatch declined to comment on the decision.

On Friday, a tractor overturned on the A55, causing a tailback, and the road could not be cleaned to the required standard, resulting in its partial reopening on Tuesday after resurfacing. Photographer Joanne Coates has spent the past three years documenting the lives of fishermen in Scotland, capturing the rugged men who brave the sea. For Coates, who grew up in northern England, these photographs are a nostalgic reminder of her childhood trips to the coast. Every Sunday, she would visit Scarborough, Whitby, and Bridlington with her brother, where she'd observe the fishermen at work. "I remember seeing them in rubber boots, nets scattered about, and snippets of their conversations," Coates recalls. "Their worn jumpers and wide smiles – I don't know how I developed this connection, but it felt real." These images reveal a world of hard work and long days, men clad in grubby overalls and grey skies. The fishing industry is a perilous one, with a staggering number of fatalities. In fact, more lives were lost at sea in the first six months of 2016 than in the entire previous year. The Fishermen's Mission charity estimates that an average of 15 fishermen are killed or seriously injured each year. In Orkney, where Coates took these photographs, fishing is a vital part of many men's lives. She's documenting the inshore fishermen of the Orkney Fishermen's Society, a cooperative that focuses on brown crab and lobster, with the area also home to Europe's largest lobster hatchery, which releases over 100,000 lobsters annually. Coates' photographs offer a poignant glimpse into the lives of these men, navigating an uncertain future.

Dennis Green, 81, and his wife Doreen, 80, tragically lost their lives in a two-vehicle collision on April 5th at Gate Helmsley, near York. The couple was remembered as cherished parents, grandparents, and great-grandparents. In a statement released after the crash, their family expressed their deep sadness, saying, "We will all miss them more than words can convey." Their relatives described the couple as well-known and respected members of their community in Strensall, where they had lived for over 50 years. They loved sequence dancing, took frequent holidays, and enjoyed an active social life. The family acknowledged that their loss will be deeply felt in the village and amongst their extensive network of friends. Interestingly, the couple had celebrated 60 years of marriage the day before the accident, and a family party had been planned for the weekend. However, their untimely passing left a huge void in the close-knit family. The family found solace in the happy memories they shared with their loved ones and the fact that Dennis and Doreen were together. The collision occurred at around 1:20 pm on Northgate Lane, Gate Helmsley. Dennis Green was driving their Citroen Xsara at the time of the crash, while Doreen was a passenger. The driver of the other vehicle, a man in his 20s, received minor injuries at the scene.
The Hamilton Band served as stretcher-bearers at the Battle of the Somme, lifting spirits with concerts and even using their bass drum as a communion table. The Somme claimed the lives of Lance Corporal Joseph Scott from Moore Street and Rifleman George Mills from Pine Street, with many more to die before hostilities ended. Founded in 1856, the Hamilton Band is one of the oldest in Northern Ireland, forming the nucleus of the 10th battalion's regimental band, the Royal Inniskilling Fusiliers. After training in Ireland, the 10th Inniskillings moved to England and then France, where they encountered combat and the harsh realities of trench warfare. Following the Armistice, surviving members rebuilt the band, but they faced a daunting task, as the battalion band had taken most of the instruments to the front. When the band was revived, many of the drums and most of the flutes were missing, but eventually, the original drums were returned, now hanging in St Columb's Cathedral's Chapter House. The bass drum, used throughout the First World War, had been repurposed as a communion table on the battlefield on Sundays and was later returned to the band, used until the 1970s. The Hamilton Flute Band secretary, Robert McGonagle, praised the men's courage, noting that such circumstances would not be met with the same dedication and bravery today. Those who lost their lives will be honoured at the war memorial in Derry, and this year, a special drum service will be held at St Columb's Cathedral to commemorate the band's contribution. To mark the occasion, a piece of music was commissioned, built around the Battle of the Somme and the band's journey to the war.
A nation in turmoil, the president urged calm after the assassination of Gen Adolphe Nshimirimana in a rocket and gun attack on his car. Blaming the killing on external forces was premature, a presidential spokesman told the BBC. The president had recently won a third term amid deadly protests over his decision to run for office again. Opponents argued this contravened the constitution, and a failed coup attempt had occurred in May. As the regime's number two, Nshimirimana's death was a significant blow to the government. The attackers targeted his car with machine guns and rocket launchers in the Kamenge district of the capital city. Presidential spokesperson Gerve Abehayo rejected suggestions that elements within Burundi's own security apparatus were responsible for the attack. He insisted that the government's support was not waning, and the army remained strong and united. One general's death did not mean the entire military was at risk. President Nkurunziza called for a swift investigation into the general's killing. The African Union, European Union, and US have all condemned the attack, with the AU chairperson calling it a "barbaric act that will further destabilise the country". A prominent Burundian journalist was detained and badly beaten after trying to take photos at the scene. Over 70 people have been killed since the unrest began, and 180,000 have fled the country, according to the UN refugee agency. The country has a history of violence, with 300,000 killed in a 12-year conflict between ethnic Hutus and Tutsis in 2005.

California lawmakers, led by Governor Jerry Brown, had initially included a proposal to cut oil use in a climate change bill but were forced to back down due to mounting opposition. State Senate leader Kevin de Leon, who supported the measure, accused oil companies of using "scare tactics" to sway public opinion. Despite the setback, Brown and his allies vowed to push ahead with other climate reforms, including boosting renewable energy use. "While oil may have won this battle, it's lost the war," Brown declared. "I'm more committed than ever to making California's regulatory system work for its people - cleaning up the air, reducing our reliance on petroleum, and creating green jobs." However, plans to require a 50% reduction in petroleum use in motor vehicles by 2030 faced fierce resistance from business groups and oil companies, who warned of potential economic consequences for the state. De Leon accused the industry of having a "singular motive" to create a "multibillion-dollar smoke screen" to deter lawmakers from voting for the legislation. Republicans, along with some moderate Democrats, had also opposed the proposal. Catherine Reheis-Boyd, president of the Western States Petroleum Association, welcomed the decision to lower the emissions target from the bill, saying it acknowledged the interconnectedness of California's energy future, economy, and environment. As the second-largest producer of carbon dioxide from fossil fuels among US states, California's climate change efforts have been a top priority for Governor Brown during his final term. He is set to attend the United Nations climate change conference in Paris in November.

Brain Injury Matters acknowledges the uncertainty surrounding the figures due to the absence of a dedicated children's brain injury unit. The organization emphasizes the importance of early detection, as difficulties may not be immediately apparent, but rather gradually develop as the brain matures. For example, a mother whose son was eight when he became ill reported that he had made a remarkable recovery, transforming into a completely different boy. Janet McCullough's son, Eric, initially suffered from a kidney infection that progressed to encephalitis, a condition where the brain swells. "Despite being on antibiotics, his body turned against him, targeting the brain," Janet recalled. "He experienced headaches, sensitivity to light, and was severely ill. Initially hospitalized at the Causeway Hospital and later the Royal Victoria Hospital, Eric's condition had improved significantly by the time he was discharged." One year on, Janet's family is actively involved with Brain Injury Matters, aiming to raise awareness about this crucial issue. Janet noted that Eric now exhibits signs of fatigue, both physically and mentally, and struggles with time management. "From a bright and energetic boy, he has become dyslexic and requires substantial support to concentrate," she said. "However, despite his challenges, he remains my loving son, and I am proud of him." Acquired brain injury has become more prevalent due to advances in medical care, leading to a decrease in mortality rates. This condition can result from various causes, including road traffic accidents, falls, infections, lack of oxygen, or strokes. While it can affect people of all ages, it tends to peak in early childhood, adolescence, and young adulthood, with males more susceptible than females. Fiona McCabe, the chief executive of Brain Injury Matters, highlighted the charity's efforts to support an increasing number of families. "Based on evidence from other parts of the UK and anecdotal reports, we suspect that there could be as many as 1,000 new cases of brain injury in children per year in Northern Ireland," she said. "Without a clear understanding of the total number of brain injuries in Northern Ireland, it is challenging to allocate resources effectively. An annual increase in cases makes this task even more daunting." Janet's family is receiving support from both Brain Injury Matters and the Child Brain Injury Trust, particularly in managing the emotional impact on their other children. Kaite Welsh, literature officer at Creative Scotland, described the event as an "essential fixture" on the literary calendar. She praised the event's diverse program of literary events, which she believes will enthuse younger readers and stimulate debate among older ones.
The European Parliament's Budgetary Control Committee is investigating allowances granted to Markus Engels, the adviser to outgoing parliament leader Schulz. In 2012, Engels was allowed to claim residence in Brussels while living in Berlin, a move that has raised questions. The SPD party has been quoted as saying that such travel arrangements are a "normal practice" in Brussels. The EU's independent anti-fraud office, Olaf, is reviewing media reports about the working conditions of certain EU Parliament staff members, but has not named them. Olaf will assess whether there is sufficient evidence to open an investigation into potential fraud, corruption, or illegal activities affecting EU finances. The office will decide whether to launch a probe only after the reports have been analyzed. Schulz chaired the EP from 2012 to this year, when he stepped down to return to Germany for the general election in September. The SPD party has seen a rise in poll ratings under his leadership. Engels now manages Schulz's election campaign. Documents from the EP's Budgetary Control Committee, seen by the BBC, reveal that Schulz asked the Director-General for Communication to authorize a "long-term mission" for Engels in 2012. The committee has asked EP Secretary General Klaus Welle why Brussels was chosen as Engels' place of employment, despite no travel costs being incurred. The committee also wants to know by what process Engels was selected for the post. A response to the committee's questions is expected early next month.
Hundreds of fans packed into Islington's historic Union Chapel to pay tribute to the 69-year-old musician who passed away from cancer on January 10th. The service featured a diverse lineup of artists, including the Magic Numbers and ex-Sex Pistols bassist Glen Matlock, performing the musician's iconic songs. The event coincided with the release of his album Blackstar, which confirmed his passing two days prior. Since then, numerous tribute concerts have taken place around the globe, and his hits have surged back onto the UK charts. As fans filled the small chapel in north London, the musician's most recognizable tunes, accompanied by an organ, resonated throughout the hall. A large screen displayed images of the star in various guises, before the ceremony kicked off with a rousing sing-along of Starman. Maggi Ronson, the sister of late guitarist and collaborator Mick Ronson, joined The Featherz to perform Ziggy Stardust, paying tribute to the musician's profound influence. Guy Pratt, a former bassist for Pink Floyd and Roxy Music, called the musician the "greatest artist I've had the privilege to know," before performing Life on Mars with members of The Feeling. The event, which was streamed live on YouTube, included a marathon five hours of the musician's hits, interspersed with clips of his interviews, music videos, and tributes from journalists, authors, and those who worked with him. The ceremony also featured quirky performances, such as Where Are We Now? on a saw and Wild Is the Wind on the harp. Flowers and candles adorned the dimly-lit, smoky hall, which is a working church and music venue. A wall of remembrance at the back allowed fans to post personal tributes and messages, many sporting the musician's signature Aladdin Sane lightning bolt. One fan said, "Thank you for giving my son the freedom to be himself." Another read, "RIP David, the soundtrack to my life." Comedian David Baddiel told the audience, "I assumed Bowie was immortal. I thought he was a space god who had come to live amongst us." He is, was, the greatest tunesmith we have." Organizers said the event was an opportunity for those inspired, influenced, or touched by the musician's work to pay tribute. Neil Lindsay, one of the organizers, said it was "for fans by fans." Eastleigh stunned Sandgrounders Andy Bishop with a 1-1 draw after a dismal run of four defeats in their last five games, with Burr's defensive solidity preventing further damage in a dull first half. The hosts remained the dominant force, but Eastleigh's persistence finally paid off when a low cross was half-cleared, and Mikael Mandron curled the ball into the top corner in the 51st minute. Eastleigh continued to press, but their failure to score a second goal backfired as James Gray equalized in the 71st minute, converting James Caton's cross after coming off the bench. The hosts' inability to capitalize on their dominance was revealed when Eastleigh took the lead again, with Mandron scoring in the 75th minute. Eastleigh's victory was sealed when Ross White was sent off for Southport in the 90th minute. The match ended 1-1, with Eastleigh earning a crucial point in their bid to escape the relegation zone.
Teachers from Llandrindod High School marched to county hall, carrying banners and chanting slogans. The National Union of Teachers claimed the strike was to protect children's right to a quality education, which they felt was being eroded. Powys council's education spokesperson, Arwel Jones, expressed disappointment with the union's actions. The council is currently consulting on plans to reorganize secondary schools in the mid and south of the county. Jones stated that schools are facing significant challenges and that the strike, while not a solution, could cause disruption to students at a critical time. Nineteen Powys schools have been impacted by the strike, with six fully closed and 13 partially closed. NUT organizer Cai Jones praised the turnout for the march, saying it was "wonderful" and that the community showed strong support. "Parents, children, and teachers walked together, and the town's residents came out to show their support, even stopping their shops and cars to wave," he said. After handing over their demands to Powys council, the teachers met at Llandrindod Pavilion to discuss the changes they hoped to see.
Eleven care workers are set to face sentencing for the mistreatment and neglect of five patients at Winterbourne View. A BBC investigation also uncovered evidence of alleged abuse against former patient Ben Pullar. The National Autistic Society has called for a full investigation into the case, while police have declined to reopen it. Pullar, who is autistic and bipolar with severe learning difficulties, spent almost a year at the hospital from July 2009, when he was 18. Nurse Maxwell Nyamukapa, involved in the abuse, was suspended and later reinstated. Despite repeated attempts to contact Nyamukapa, he has refused to comment. The police initially logged the incident as an assault, stating Nyamukapa had acted instinctively in self-defence. Sarah Lambert, from the National Autistic Society, said: "Any disabled person living in residential care who suffers serious injuries must have their case thoroughly investigated. "The abuse of people with autism and other disabilities by those supposed to care for and protect them is utterly unacceptable and a disability hate crime. "The police must reopen this case to send a clear message that abuse by support staff is not only unacceptable but a serious criminal offence." Daily care notes from Pullar's time at the hospital show that he had an accident with his teeth just weeks after his arrival. His family was told he had bitten the floor, and he was taken to Bristol Dental Hospital, where the alarm was raised. Notes obtained by Inside Out West revealed that the nurse had bitten Pullar and retaliated to remove his fingers, and later, that he had pushed and pulled to get them out. South Gloucestershire Council has declined to comment on Pullar's case. Castlebeck, the hospital's owner at the time, said its new board and management was reviewing the incident and had referred Nyamukapa to the Nursing & Midwifery Council, which declined to comment.

Hoban, he believes, is young and still has much to learn, but Coyle makes an effort to talk to him each day, sharing his experience and knowledge of the game. Hoban is receptive to Coyle's guidance, eager to learn and it's already showing.
A year-long ban was imposed on Croatian tennis official Denis Pitner in August after he revealed sensitive information about a player's fitness and accessed an account used to place bets. Pitner had received his US Open credentials just a few days earlier, but the US Open Tennis Association (USTA) was caught off guard when it discovered he had worked as a linesman during the 2015 US Open while he was banned. Following a Guardian investigation, the USTA acknowledged that a flaw in its credential approval process had allowed Pitner to work during his suspension. The USTA's response to a BBC and Buzzfeed exposé on match-fixing in tennis came after it was revealed that Pitner had also officiated at the ATP Qatar Open in January. The sport's governing bodies have now announced the formation of an independent review panel to investigate corruption allegations, which will take at least a year to complete. The USTA has vowed to work with the panel to prevent similar incidents in the future, taking the matter extremely seriously and prioritizing its investigation into the error that allowed Pitner to work during his suspension.
A plan to cut one of Wrexham's fire engines was scrapped by North Wales Fire and Rescue Authority in March, pending the outcome of the May elections. The new authority has yet to rule out the possibility of eliminating the engine as it develops its budget plan for the next three years. Deputy Chief Fire Officer Dawn Docx emphasized that any cuts would be a last resort. Although there are no plans to slash front-line services, the Fire Brigades Union fears the engine could be axed again due to budget pressures. Cerith Griffiths, of the FBU, warned that cutting the engine would force firefighters to disregard safety protocols to save lives. Under the service's safety guidelines, which dictate how many crews should respond to a specific incident, two fire engines are required for a house fire - a predetermined response. If the second engine were to be eliminated, firefighters would be compelled to choose between waiting for crucial minutes or entering a burning building without backup, putting their own lives at risk. "As seen in Grenfell, London, where firefighters broke numerous protocols, we shouldn't be entering a property without a second engine," Griffiths said. "We've observed firefighters will go to great lengths to save someone. "Waiting half an hour for the second engine is an eternity in a situation where every second counts, it's a long time if you're trapped. "It's putting lives in danger." Proposals to eliminate one of Wrexham's two fire engines and 24 firefighter posts, aimed at filling a £900,000 funding gap by 2020, were withdrawn in March following public opposition. The station is the only one in north Wales with three fire engines - two full-time and one part-time engine. The new authority is now preparing its budget plans and is seeking public input ahead of publishing its draft financial strategy in November. Docx stated that no proposals have yet been made, but the service is exploring ways to make savings without affecting front-line jobs. A fire engine may need to be eliminated from a station, resulting in crews waiting "slightly longer" for backup. "There would have to be a judgment call - do we wait for the second engine or do we go in," Docx said. "I certainly wouldn't want to leave you with the impression that senior fire officers want this to happen," she said. "If we can't close the gap with the budget, this is an option the authority could consider."
Vandals used red spray paint to write "expletive law" early Tuesday morning, according to the National Park Service. Crews are removing the graffiti with a special paint stripper designed for historic stone. US Civil War memorials have become a focal point in the country's debate over racism. Other, similarly indecipherable graffiti was found in silver paint on a nearby sign directing tourists to the Smithsonian Institution on Washington's National Mall. President Lincoln led the Union to victory over the Confederacy in 1863, freeing African-American slaves. He was assassinated just five days after Confederate General Robert E Lee's surrender, effectively ending the war. The statue of General Lee has been a contentious issue, as white supremacists clashed with anti-racist protesters in Charlottesville, Virginia, over its removal. A protester died after being run over, and President Donald Trump faced widespread criticism for not immediately blaming the violence on the Ku Klux Klan and neo-Nazi groups. Many cities across the US, including Charlottesville, have announced plans to remove Confederate monuments following a 2015 hate attack in South Carolina. After Saturday's violence, several cities announced their intention to remove monuments to Confederate leaders, including Baltimore, Maryland; Lexington, Kentucky; Memphis, Tennessee; and Jacksonville, Florida. On Monday, protesters in Durham, North Carolina, toppled a statue to a Confederate leader, prompting police to review the video to identify those responsible.
The mother-of-ten, a Protestant from east Belfast, converted to Catholicism after marrying Arthur McConville. She and her family moved to west Belfast due to intimidation, settling into the Divis Flats on the Lower Falls Road. Her husband died in 1971, and she was taken from her home by the IRA in December 1972. There had been speculation about her abduction, with some believing she was seen helping an injured British soldier, while others thought she was an informer, but this was later disproven. In a 2023 BBC documentary, one of Mrs McConville's daughters, Agnes, spoke publicly about her mother's disappearance for the first time. She recalled her mother's terrified screams as she was dragged away by several men and women and thrown into a van. "That was the last time we saw her," she said. It's believed that Mrs McConville was held in various locations before her death. Her disappearance received extensive media attention in the lead-up to Christmas, with her children being interviewed in an effort to gather information about her fate. However, their pleas went unanswered. Within the community, republicans claimed she was merely 'lying low', and her story gradually faded from the headlines. The IRA denied involvement in her disappearance until the Independent Commission for the Location of Victims' Remains was established in 1999. This commission, set up by the British and Irish governments, aimed to gather information in confidence to help locate the remains of those who had disappeared during the Troubles, known as the Disappeared. In 1999, the IRA admitted that nine of the Disappeared, including Jean McConville, were murdered and secretly buried. It wasn't until 2003 that her body was found on Shelling Hill Beach in County Louth, Ireland. Irish police confirmed that she had died from a bullet wound to the head. In response to her family's suffering, the IRA issued a statement apologizing for the grief caused and acknowledging that their suffering had continued for so long.
